# 老子翼

The Imperially Commissioned *Complete Library of the Four Treasuries*
*Masters Section, Fourteenth Division*
*Wings of Laozi*
*Daoist School*
*Abstract*

We, your ministers, respectfully note: *Wings of Laozi* in three juan was compiled by Jiao Hong of the Ming dynasty. Hong’s *Easy Explanation of the Book of Changes* has already been recorded. This work gathers sixty-four exegetes from Han Fei onward who interpreted *Laozi*, appending Hong’s own notes to form sixty-five entries. Each entry distills the finest insights into a single compilation. Where passages remain intact from beginning to end, forming complete sections, the work follows the example of Li Dingzuo’s *Collected Explications of the Zhou Changes*, marking each with the commentator’s name and placing it after the original chapter. For glosses on pronunciation, meaning, or single words and phrases, it adopts Pei Yin’s *Collected Explications of the Records of the Grand Historian*, weaving them into the text and appending them to the final sentence of the original chapter. The *Upper* and *Lower* chapters each occupy one juan, while the *Appendices* and *Textual Variants* form another, totaling three juan. The work does not impose the titles *Dao Classic* or *Virtue Classic*, nor does it arbitrarily assign chapter names. Its editorial principles are particularly aligned with those of ancient works. Most of the selected commentaries are drawn from the *Daoist Canon*, many of which are not commonly circulated in the world. Hong’s selections are notably precise and judicious, his primary aim being to elucidate the original words and clarify the principles of purity and naturalness. For instance, while figures like Ge Changgeng often conflated Daoist alchemical practices with Chan Buddhist repartee, though their names are listed, their interpretations are largely excluded. Among the various commentaries, the work is both erudite and reasoned. This is because Hong’s scholarship in the teachings of the Two Schools (Daoism and Buddhism) was profound, surpassing even his grasp of Confucianism. Thus, his explanations of Confucian principles often stray into vagueness, while his exegesis of the Two Schools’ doctrines demonstrates discernment and originality.

Respectfully submitted for imperial review in the second month of the forty-fifth year of the Qianlong reign.

*Chief Compilers:*
Minister Ji Yun
Minister Lu Xixiong
Minister Sun Shiyi

*Chief Collator:*
Minister Lu Feichi

*Preface to the Wings of Laozi*

*Laozi* is a book that illuminates the Dao. So too did Wang Zhen of the Tang dynasty...

Those who go so far as to claim it was composed as a treatise on warfare—could it be that even the words on fine soldiers and skillful battle have somehow inspired them? I say: Laozi does not speak of warfare. It illuminates the attainment of suppleness. Of all things under heaven that delight in strength, none surpasses warfare, and yet even warfare is made to yield through suppleness. From this, one may infer that where there is no suppleness, there is no Dao. Suppleness is the opposite of rigidity. The Dao is present in all things, yet why does it uniquely favor suppleness and treat rigidity as secondary?

I say: Laozi does not speak of suppleness. It illuminates non-action. Suppleness is not the Dao itself, but it approaches non-action. Rigidity is not outside the Dao, but it distances itself from non-action. Thus, by seeking through suppleness, non-action may be guarded. That which is non-existent cannot but have existence, just as suppleness cannot be without rigidity. Yet when one establishes it upon the constancy of non-being and being, the world’s benevolence, righteousness, sageliness, and wisdom do not cease to be discarded. This is merely another case of elevating one while abolishing the hundred.

I say: Laozi does not speak of the non-existence of non-being. It illuminates the non-being of existence. The non-existence of non-being is to abandon existence in pursuit of non-being—its name is "severance." The non-being of existence is to use existence to verify non-being—its teaching is "returning to the root." If all things return to their roots, then though the ten thousand beings flourish together, they ultimately cannot be called "existent." This is the ultimate of attaining emptiness and maintaining quiescence.

Scholars know the vessel but not the Dao, hence the *Yi* clarifies the Dao through the vessel. They see form but not emptiness, hence Buddhism clarifies emptiness through form. They grasp being but not non-being, hence Laozi clarifies non-being through being. If one truly understands that being is non-being, then in non-action, affairs are not acted upon, yet nothing is left undone, and nothing can obstruct it. What, then, is there to discard?

Thus it is said: *Laozi* is a book that illuminates the Dao. I was once fond of rigidity and forceful conduct, reading *Laozi* as if feeding through the ears—no different from not having read it at all. At twenty-three, I heard the teachings of masters and friends and gradually turned my mind to learning, though I found it difficult to enter. There were those who guided me with what they called "luminous clarity," and I rejoiced as if it resonated within. Reflecting upon it in my heart was like a horse with bit and bridle, or a door with hinge and pivot. Comparing it with the teachings of recent Confucians was like tallying pieces that fit perfectly.

Believing the Dao to lie therein, I looked back upon twenty years—wherever I turned, obstacles arose. Where there were obstacles, there were doubts. Examining antiquity, I found much that clashed; where there was clash, there was contradiction. Never for a moment did this leave my thoughts.

In recent years, worn down by hardship and long illness, in the stillness of convalescence, I suddenly awoke alone and sighed deeply, saying: In the past, I never failed to reject the discriminating mind, yet sometimes sought to extinguish it in pursuit of quiescence. I never failed to prize the mindless state, yet did not know that the original mind is naturally without knowing. I yearned for purity and stillness, yet did not know that the stainless is not pure. I knew of the true self, yet did not know that where there is no thing, there is no self. All this was led astray by the teachings of others.

From then on, as I pursued my views, they vanished without a trace, and I, too, fortunately became a man of non-action. At that time, my friend Zhai Defo, who was fond of discussing *Laozi*, would occasionally bring it up to test me. I responded with my recent insights, and Defo never failed to applaud them. He urged me to write an exposition, so I gathered my family’s old copies of *Laozi* along with those collected in the Daoist Canon, reading through them all—sixty-four versions in total. How vast their words! Among them, though statements that betray the Dao and depart from the classics were not uncommon, those that accorded were not few.

Reciting and chanting in cycles, I sighed deeply—ah! The five thousand words of *Laozi* are sometimes said to encompass the entire book of Rongchengzi. *Liezi* also claims that the "Valley Spirit" chapter was not written by the Yellow Emperor, hence the saying: "Transmitting but not creating, trusting and cherishing antiquity, I compare myself to Lao Peng." The sages of old could illuminate the Dao without it all necessarily originating from themselves. How much less, then, could I—whose understanding of this matter is but a fraction of a hair’s tip, not even one ten-thousandth—presume to add to it?

Thus, I set aside my own composition and instead gathered the commentaries of earlier scholars, arranging and compiling them into a single volume, with a few superficial insights of my own appended. Defo, meanwhile, was still in the midst of explicating the *Yinfu Jing* and had not yet completed it. When he finishes, we shall present both works together to scholars. For now, I merely record this preface and keep it in my home.

Written in the winter of the *dinghai* year of the Wanli era by Jiao Hong, styled Ruohou.

*Complete Library of the Four Treasuries*
*Wings of the *Laozi*, Fascicle One*
*Ming Dynasty*
*Compiled by Jiao Hong*
*Upper Chapter*

The Dao that can be spoken is not the constant Dao;
The name that can be named is not the constant name.
Nameless, it is the beginning of Heaven and Earth;
Named, it is the mother of the myriad things.
Thus, constantly without desire, one observes its mystery;
Constantly with desire, one observes its manifestations.
These two emerge together yet differ in name—
Together they are called the profound.
Profound and ever more profound:
The gateway to all mysteries.

[The "can be spoken" is like ritual—Dao is not vainly spoken.
"Constant" means enduring and unchanging.
"Mother" signifies that things arise from it.
"Desire" is like the desires of human nature; "之欲" means intention or emotion.
"Manifestations" (徼) are the apertures through which things emerge, or their boundaries, their returns.
Chen Jingyuan says: "At the edge of the great Dao are small paths called *jiao*."
Ding Yidong says: "The nameless is the beginning of Heaven and Earth; the named is the mother of the myriad things." Some read this as "nameless and named," while others read it as "nonbeing and being." Yet Laozi also says, "The Dao is constantly nameless; only when the beginning is instituted does it have a name"—how then can it be read as "nonbeing and being"?
Those who read it as "constantly without, constantly with" and those who read it as "without desire, with desire"—Zhuangzi says, "Establish it in the constant of nonbeing and being," directly pointing to Laozi’s words, so the punctuation of "constantly without, constantly with" seems correct. Yet Laozi also says, "Constantly without desire, it can be named as small"—this cannot be used as evidence from Zhuangzi. Laozi should be read as Laozi.]

Cheng Ju’s Discourse:
The Dao that can be spoken is for regulating conduct;
The name that can be named is for establishing speech.
As for the constant Dao that cannot be spoken,
The constant name that cannot be named—
The sage does not dare to reveal it to others.
Not that it is concealed in secrecy and withheld,
But that it cannot be shown to others.

Thus, the sages of the Western Regions manifest their teachings through three vehicles and expound them into twelve divisions, calling them "teachings." Yet what is transmitted beyond these teachings is precisely that which cannot be spoken and cannot be named. The sages of China—from the lineage of Tang, Yu, Xia, Wen, and Wu—codified the texts of the *Odes*, *Documents*, rites, and music, calling them "classics." Yet what their words tread like footprints that are not the path itself is also that which cannot be spoken and cannot be named. Therefore, Laozi composed his five-thousand-character text to reveal it to the world and guide later generations, not retreating into the obscurity of the Dao to hoard it for himself. Thus, the opening of his words speaks of the Dao that can be spoken and the name that can be named—these are what the five-thousand-character text encompasses. As for what the sages do not transmit, it cannot be put into words.

Su Ziyou’s commentary:
All is the Dao, yet what can be spoken of it cannot be constant. Only what cannot be spoken of is truly constant. Consider benevolence, righteousness, propriety, and wisdom—these are the Dao that can be spoken. Yet benevolence cannot become righteousness, nor can propriety become wisdom; what can be spoken of is thus not constant. Only what cannot be spoken of remains in benevolence as benevolence, in righteousness as righteousness, and so too with propriety and wisdom—these are all inconstant, while the Dao remains unchanging. Such is the constancy of what cannot be spoken. If the Dao cannot be spoken, how much less can it be named? All names belong to what can be spoken. Once a name is established, the differences between round and square, crooked and straight cannot be constant. Before names took form, there were heaven and earth; once heaven and earth were established, names began. From the dissemination of names arose the myriad things, and with the flourishing of the myriad things, names became innumerable. Thus, the nameless is the essence of the Dao, while the named is its function. The sage embodies the Dao to function in the world, entering into the realm of multiplicity while remaining in the constant of nonbeing to observe its subtleties. Embodying the utmost nonbeing while abiding in the constant of being, he observes its manifestations. Yet if one acts within manifestations without knowing the subtleties, one remains coarse and unspiritual; if one dwells in subtleties without knowing the manifestations, one becomes refined but unchanging. In terms of form, being and nonbeing are indeed two—how can one know that nonbeing transforms into being and being returns to nonbeing, never not unified? Though their names differ, their root is one. To know the unity of the root is to attain the profound. Whatever is boundless and without limit must be profound in color; thus, Laozi often used "profound" to signify the ultimate. To speak of the profound is to reach it, yet the mind that clings to the profound still remains. Profound upon profound—only then is it exhausted, beyond further addition. This is the source from which all subtleties arise.

Lǚ Jifu annotates:
All the Ways under heaven that can be spoken of are indeed the Dao, yet there are times when they verge on peril—such are not the constant Dao. All the names under heaven that can be named are indeed names, yet there are times when they depart—such are not the constant names. The myriad things flourish and flourish, each returning to its root, yet they know it not. Stillness is called "returning to destiny"; returning to destiny is called "constancy." To be constant is to attain the Dao. When one reaches constancy, the mind congeals, form dissolves, and self and other are all forgotten. Who then knows what can be spoken as Dao or named as name? Thus, the constant Dao is inherently unspeakable. Therefore it is said: "The Dao buries itself in long obscurity, never imperiled." The constant name is inherently unnameable. Therefore it is said: "From ancient times to now, its name does not depart." Not imperiled, not departed—this is called the constant Dao. The constant nonbeing of name—this nonbeing is the Dao, the origin from which heaven and earth themselves arise. Thus it is said: "Nameless, it is the beginning of heaven and earth." In the Great Beginning, there was nonbeing; there was no nonbeing, no name. When there was no nonbeing, even "one" could not be attained. When there was no name, this was the arising of the one. There was one, yet no form had appeared. To call it "one" is already to have a name, though no form yet exists. The name "one" and that which names it make two; two and one make three. From this, the myriad things in profusion arise. Thus it is said: "The named is the mother of the myriad things." To know constancy is called "clarity"; clarity sees all without obstruction. Only constancy allows one to observe: when there is no desire, one purifies and polishes the profound mirror, leaving no flaw in this observation of the subtle. Thus it is said: "Constantly without desire, one may name it the small." When there is desire, the myriad things arise together in profusion; in this, one observes their manifestations. Thus it is said: "The myriad things all return to it, yet they know not their master—one may name it the great." Only the small reveals the subtle; only the great reveals the manifest. These two emerge from the same source, yet their names differ—nothing more. Though their names differ, their reality has never differed. Whether in desire or the absence of desire, their source cannot be sought. If it cannot be sought, then their source is the same. Thus it is called "profound." Is the color of profundity the same whether black or red? One. Is the color of heaven the same whether in yin or yang? One. Do names arise from the profound in the same way, whether in desire or its absence? One. They are the same.

One is indeed wondrous—yet is it wondrous only as One? Is it not also wondrous as Many? Is it wondrous only as the self? Is it not also wondrous as things? When the profound is pursued to the utmost profundity, even sameness cannot be found. If sameness cannot be found, then neither One nor Many, neither self nor things—all are wondrous without exception. The wondrousness of the myriad things arises from this alone. Thus it is said: "The profound pursued to the utmost profundity is the gateway to all wonders."

Li Xizhai’s Commentary:
"Constant" means unchanging. Things change, but the Dao does not. The changes of things extend to the fleeting of thoughts and the passing of moments, never pausing for even an instant. Yet what is called the Dao has no beginning and no end; heaven and earth may perish, but this Dao does not—this is what is meant by "constant." As the constant Dao, it cannot be traversed to its end, nor can it be named to be attained. If it could be traversed, it would not be the constant Dao; if it could be named, it would not be the constant name. Before the beginning of heaven and earth, the principle of true constancy was already present in the nameless origin. Thus, the nameless is the beginning of heaven and earth. Once heaven and earth were divided, names arose for high and low, and the myriad things multiplied from this point onward. Thus, what can be named is the mother of things.

The sage embodies the Dao of true constancy, moving between being and non-being. The wondrous is the great Dao—non-being. The manifest is the lesser Dao—being. If I wish to observe the wondrous, I enter into wondrousness and return to non-being. If I wish to observe the manifest, I emerge with manifestation and wander in being. The wondrous is the manifest; the manifest is the wondrous. Being is emptiness; emptiness is being. Their root is the same, though their branches differ. Thus, they are both called "profound." And not only this: when the profound is pursued to the utmost profundity, it enables all manifestations to be nothing but wonders. This is called "the gateway to all wonders," for within manifestation and wondrousness, being and non-being, there is no distinction—all are wondrous.

*Bishu*:
The character *jiao* (徼) is read like the *jiao* in "frontier" (边徼), referring to the furthest extent of things. Yanzi said, "Manifestation is the return of virtue." Liezi said, "Death is the furthest extent of virtue." Both refer to the point of exhaustion. When there is non-being, nothing remains to be said. Yet when desire arises, people cling to being as if it were real. But desire must come to an end, and when it is exhausted, having reached its limit with nowhere else to go, it must return to non-being. How is this different from wondrousness? Thus it is said: "These two are called the same profound." Nevertheless, Laozi spoke thus out of necessity for those who have not yet awakened. In truth, he did not advocate abandoning being to seek non-being. If one were to abandon being to seek non-being, then beyond being there would be non-being—how could this be true non-being? When being is present, it has never truly been being—this is genuine non-being. Thus, form is not destroyed to become emptiness; form is emptiness. Affairs are not negated to become emptiness; affairs are emptiness. Otherwise, the so-called non-being would be non-being in opposition to being, and the so-called being would be being in opposition to non-being. How could this be called constant non-being or constant being? Alas, how can one who does not know the constant engage in discussion of this?

When all under heaven know beauty as beauty, ugliness is already present. When all know goodness as goodness, non-goodness is already present. Thus, being and non-being give rise to each other; difficulty and ease complete each other; long and short shape each other; high and low incline toward each other; sound and voice harmonize with each other; front and back follow one another. Therefore, the sage abides in non-action and practices wordless teaching. The myriad things arise, yet he does not reject them; he gives life, yet does not possess; he acts, yet does not rely on it; he accomplishes, yet does not dwell in it. It is precisely because he does not dwell that it does not depart.

Lu Xisheng’s Commentary:
What people call beauty and ugliness arise from emotion: what conforms to emotion is beauty, what opposes it is ugliness. The same applies to goodness and non-goodness. Yet what is deemed beautiful is not necessarily beautiful; what is deemed ugly is not necessarily ugly; what is deemed good is not necessarily good; what is deemed non-good is not necessarily non-good. Why is this? Emotion is the cause. Human nature is fundamentally the same, but emotions differ. When these differing emotions are externally affected by things, likes and dislikes become entangled, and beauty and ugliness have no fixed standard. How can this be corrected? It lies in returning to nature. Why? The arising of emotion must originate from nature. Thus, the sage transforms emotion to return to nature, reaching great unity. What is called "being and non-being giving rise to each other" refers to emotion and nature. The mutual dependence of emotion and nature is like the mutual completion of difficulty and ease. Those who govern by using emotion to transform nature find it difficult to succeed; those who use nature to correct emotion find it easy. What is called "long and short shaping each other" refers to beauty and ugliness. The competition between beauty and ugliness is like the inclination of high and low. What is called "sound and voice harmonizing with each other" refers to goodness and non-goodness. The mutual reliance of goodness and non-goodness is like the following of front and back. Alas, the world’s many troubles arise from these six. This is why the world is not at peace, and the myriad things cannot fulfill their nature. The sage, wishing to restore their nature, first transforms their emotions. Those who are already good are confirmed in their goodness; those who are not good are also led to goodness, so that all become good. Those who are already trustworthy are confirmed in their trust; those who are not trustworthy are also led to trust, so that all become trustworthy. Thus, nothing is discarded in things, and no one is abandoned in teaching. When things have no ugliness and people have no non-goodness, an ungoverned world has never existed. The *Yijing* says: "Its Dao is vast, and the hundred things are not abandoned." This is what it means.

Therefore, the sage embodies the nameless and thus acts without action, and affairs settle themselves. He employs the named and thus teaches without words, and instruction proceeds on its own. He enables the myriad things to fulfill their nature as if nothing had caused it—like heaven and earth giving life to things without possessing them, like the hundred craftsmen making tools without relying on them, like the four seasons completing the year without dwelling in it. Only in this way can his Dao be constant and his name not depart.

Su’s Commentary:
All under heaven speak of beauty and ugliness in terms of form and name. What they call beautiful and good—are these truly beautiful and good? They do not know that being and non-being, long and short, difficulty and ease, high and low, sound and voice, front and back—all arise and compete with one another, and none are in their true state. When one takes oneself to be long, and another longer appears, one becomes short. When one takes oneself to be ahead, and another appears before, one becomes behind. If one follows what is deemed beautiful and trusts it, one strays far from the truth. When acting, one should have the mind of non-action; when teaching, one should have the intention of wordless instruction. Only thus can one transcend the measures of long and short, depart from the calculations of front and back—neither beautiful nor ugly, neither good nor non-good. How could all under heaven know this? The myriad things act for me, yet I do not reject them; I give life to them, yet do not possess them; I act, yet do not rely on it; I accomplish, yet do not dwell in it.

Thus is the reward of non-action and wordless teaching—even the sage does not know it as beauty or goodness. How then could there be evil or non-goodness following it? The sage dwells in poverty and lowliness yet knows no sorrow of poverty and lowliness; dwells in wealth and nobility yet bears no burden of wealth and nobility. This is what is called "not dwelling." If I do not dwell, how can that which is other depart? This is the ultimate dwelling.

**Lü’s Annotation:**
All under heaven know beauty as beauty and goodness as goodness, desiring them; know evil and non-goodness, and despise them. Yet, speaking from the departure from the Dao, even beauty and goodness are far from the Dao. Speaking from the arising of the Dao, even evil and non-goodness are not outside the Dao. From this perspective, beauty becomes evil, and goodness becomes non-goodness—how could this be empty words? Therefore, the things of the world are born from being, and being is born from non-being. This is called the mutual arising of being and non-being. Difficult tasks arise from the easy, yet the easy also arises from the difficult. This is called the mutual completion of difficulty and ease. Only when there is the long leg of the crane does one know the short leg of the wild duck; only when there is the short leg of the wild duck does one know the long leg of the crane. This is called the mutual formation of long and short. If height is affirmed, yet the hundred valleys are the source of rivers and streams, then height has that which topples it. If lowliness is affirmed, yet rivers and streams are the gathering of the hundred valleys, then lowliness has that which topples it. This is called the mutual toppling of high and low. When the Yellow Bell is sovereign, the remaining pitches harmonize with it; when the remaining pitches are sovereign, the Yellow Bell harmonizes with them. This is called the mutual harmony of sounds. Gazing from autumn and winter toward spring and summer, spring and summer come before, and autumn and winter follow; gazing from spring and summer toward autumn and winter, autumn and winter come before, and spring and summer follow. This is called the mutual following of front and back. In all these six cases, when they accord with their time and fit their circumstances, all under heaven call them beautiful and good; when they do not accord with their time and do not fit their circumstances, all under heaven call them evil and non-good. How could they know what is called the ultimate beauty and ultimate goodness? Thus, beauty and evil, goodness and non-goodness, alternately come and go, rise and fall—how could they be constant? Therefore, the sage, knowing this, employs the constant Dao to handle affairs, and affairs arise from non-action; employs the constant name to conduct teaching, and teaching arises from wordlessness. When affairs arise from non-action, one acts all day yet has never acted; when teaching arises from wordlessness, one speaks all day yet has never spoken. Then, how could I harbor concern for beauty or evil, goodness or non-goodness? Being thus, there is nowhere that is not wondrous. If nowhere is not wondrous, then in the arising of the myriad things, I do not see their arising or the one who causes their arising; do not see their birth or the one who gives them birth; do not see their acting or the one who acts. Thus, though they arise, it is as if they do not arise; though they are born, it is as if they are not born; though they act, it is as if they do not act. How could I reject, possess, rely, or dwell? This is why one accomplishes yet does not dwell. Without dwelling, there is nothing to depart—where could one depart to?

**Lu Nongshi’s Annotation:**
When beauty reaches non-beauty, it is the true beauty of the world; when goodness reaches non-goodness, it is the true goodness of the world. Once true beauty departs, all under heaven know beauty as beauty; once true goodness scatters, all under heaven know goodness as goodness. Therefore, being and non-being speak of the Dao; difficulty and ease speak of its power; long and short speak of its form; high and low speak of its position; sounds speak of its mutual resonance; front and back speak of its beginning and end. Such is the nature of things. The sage engages in affairs of non-action and practices wordless teaching to guide people toward true goodness, merge with true beauty, return to simplicity, and become companions with heaven and earth, friends with creation. Being thus, the ceasing of the myriad things enters with them without resistance; the arising of the myriad things departs with them without rejection. How could I harbor concern?

**Wang Wujiu’s Exegesis:**
The sage engages in affairs of non-action and practices wordless teaching, having no intention of acting or speaking, merely following the principles of the nature and destiny of the myriad things. Thus, when the myriad things arise, I arise with them without rejection; when the myriad things are born, I am born with them without possession; when the myriad things act, I act with them without reliance; when the myriad things accomplish, I accomplish with them without dwelling. For their arising, birth, acting, and accomplishment all follow the principles of nature and destiny, aligning with things and time, and are not of me. Then, why must I reject, possess, rely, or dwell? Therefore, it is said: "The myriad things arise together, yet I do not reject them; they are born, yet I do not possess them; I act, yet do not rely on it; I accomplish, yet do not dwell." First there is arising, then birth; first birth, then acting; first acting, then accomplishment—this is their sequence.

**Xizhai’s Annotation:**
The five thousand words of Laozi can ascend to the profound and descend to the subtle; seeking the Dao through them, the Dao is attained; governing the state through them, the state is well-ordered; cultivating the self through them, the self is at peace. Their words always penetrate these three realms—this is why they are subtle, wondrous, and profoundly unfathomable. This chapter speaks of my original nature, which from the beginning has never had things—who then is beautiful, who is evil, who is good, who is non-good? Once life arises and forms and names are established, people all know beauty as beauty yet do not know that the name of evil already arises from beauty; all know goodness as goodness yet do not know that the name of non-goodness already arises from goodness. For there is nothing in the world without its counterpart: being and non-being arise mutually; difficulty and ease complete each other; long and short form each other; high and low topple each other; sounds harmonize with each other; front and back follow each other. There is never one without the other. The sage, knowing this, must stand before things, follow their nature, engage in affairs of non-action, practice wordless teaching, neither grasping goodness nor rejecting evil, never clinging to one, never not being one. Acting all day, yet never acting; speaking all day, yet never speaking. Thus, when the myriad things arise together, I follow and arise with them, arising without rejection; when the myriad things are born together, I follow and am born with them, being born without possession. When they act, it is not my acting—merely following things. Thus, acting without reliance. When they accomplish, it is not my accomplishment—merely responding to things. Thus, accomplishing without dwelling. By not dwelling in the end but dwelling in the beginning, what I dwell in cannot be departed from. Therefore, things cannot depart.

Wu Youqing’s annotation states: "These five all speak of their paired aspects, yet sound is not mentioned as such. For if one only says 'opening and closing, clarity and turbidity,' people would not recognize it as sound. If one speaks of sound, then the paired aspects of opening and closing, clarity and turbidity are naturally understood, so one need only speak of its essence and not its pairing.

The sage, by not engaging in affairs, engages in affairs—thus their affairs involve no action. By not teaching, they teach—thus their teaching involves no words. Without action and without words, though there is beauty and goodness, people do not recognize it. Therefore, their beauty and goodness are uniquely revered and uniquely valued, without anything to oppose them. If there were affairs of action and teachings of words, people would all recognize them as beauty and goodness, and beauty would oppose ugliness, goodness would oppose ungoodness—such beauty and goodness could not be called uniquely revered or uniquely valued, nor could they be named as nameless beauty and goodness. Throughout the *Laozi*, all chapters speak of nothing beyond this."

"Not exalting the worthy prevents the people from contending.
Not valuing rare goods prevents the people from stealing.
Not displaying what is desirable prevents the heart from chaos.
Thus, in the sage’s governance:
Empty their hearts,
Fill their bellies,
Weaken their ambitions,
Strengthen their bones.
Ever cause the people to be without knowledge and without desire,
So that those with knowledge dare not act.
Act through non-action, and nothing is left ungoverned."

*(The pronunciation of 治 is *zhì*; the first 知 in "无知" is read as *zhī*, while the second 知 in "知者" is read as *zhì*; 夫 is pronounced *fú*.)*

Wang Fusi says: "The heart harbors wisdom, the belly harbors food. To be empty is to have wisdom, yet in reality to be without knowledge. The bones, being without knowledge, are thus strong; the ambitions, born of affairs, are thus weakened."

Su’s annotation states: "Exalting the worthy causes the people to feel shame in not matching others, leading to contention. Valuing rare goods causes the people to suffer from lack, leading to theft. Displaying what is desirable causes the people to worry over not obtaining it, leading to chaos. Though the world knows these three as sources of trouble and wishes to abolish them, it remains deluded.

The sage does not act thus: they do not fail to employ the worthy, only they do not exalt them; they do not discard rare goods, only they do not value them; they do not eliminate desire, only they do not display it."

Thus, when the worthy are employed yet the people do not contend, when rare goods and desirable things are all displayed before them yet theft, calamity, and chaos do not arise—is this not to empty the heart without harming the fullness of the belly, to weaken the ambitions without harming the strength of the bones?

Now, if one were to promote the worthy and exalt them, treasure goods and value them, and display what is desirable, then both heart and belly would be filled. If one were to abolish and discard them altogether, then both ambitions and bones would be weakened. When heart and belly are both full, the people contend; when ambitions and bones are both weak, they have nothing with which to stand. If one does not employ these three, the people know not what to admire, remaining tranquil and without desire—even the clever have no craft to employ.

Following the natural course of these three without exalting, without valuing, without displaying—this is what is called "acting through non-action."

Lu’s annotation states: The sage knows that beauty gives rise to ugliness and goodness to its opposite, yet remains without preference. Thus, though they bind the world’s ranks to their service, they merely employ them—worthiness is not what they exalt. Though they gather the world’s wealth to nourish the people, rare goods are not what they value. The people’s contention commonly arises from mutual esteem of worthiness; if worthiness is not what the ruler exalts, they do not contend. Thus it is said: "Promote the worthy, and the people contend with one another." The people’s theft commonly arises from desire for profit; if goods are not what the ruler values, they do not become thieves. Thus it is said: "If the master is without desire, though you reward them, they do not steal." What the noble person desires is worthiness; what the petty person desires is goods. If I do not display what is desirable, the heart is not disordered.

Yet not exalting the worthy does not mean abandoning them in the wilds and not employing them; not valuing rare goods does not mean casting them to the ground and not gathering them. It is merely that inwardly, they are not stored in the heart, and outwardly, their traces are not left behind.

Thus, in the sage’s governance: empty the heart and fill the belly, weaken the ambitions and strengthen the bones. The heart stores the spirit, and the belly is the heart’s dwelling; empty the heart, and the spirit is not diminished while the belly is full. The kidneys store the ambitions, and the bones are the kidneys’ surplus; weaken the ambitions, and the essence is not disturbed while the bones are strong. Empty the heart and fill the belly, and the people are constantly made without knowledge. Weaken the ambitions and strengthen the bones, and the people are constantly made without desire. When the wise know that worthiness is not what the ruler exalts and goods are not what the ruler values, their actions are not profitable, and so they dare not act. Only thus does one act through non-action and yet nothing is left ungoverned.

Li Hongfu’s commentary states:

The origin of contention and theft is opened by the sage. Thus, the highest contend over goodness, and the next steal the state—all arise from the sight of what is desirable. When the ear hears what is desirable, and such desires are many, the people’s ambitions are thrown into chaos. How can there be governance? As for the Supreme, does he truly possess a means to govern this? It is merely that he does not display what is desirable.

The belly is not full because the heart is burdened. Now, if there is nothing seen that can be desired, exalted, or valued, then the heart is empty and the belly naturally full. The bones are not firm because the ambitions are broken. If one dares not engage in rebellious contention or thievery, then the ambitions are weakened and the bones naturally strong. The reason for this is the absence of desire.

The people are born with desires and without knowledge, yet the sage daily leads them to acquire knowledge. He arrays benevolence, righteousness, ritual, and music, guides them with laws, regulations, and prohibitions, and establishes palaces, clothing, carriages, horses, caps, marriages, defenses, and sacrifices—awakening their boundless knowledge, only then to restrain their endless desires. This is like flooding waters overwhelming the heavens, then attempting to block them with a single reed. How can this succeed?

The Supreme acts otherwise: he constantly keeps them in a state of primal chaos, without knowledge and without desire. Even if those with intelligence and understanding emerge, wishing to act, they dare not do so, and so the world all returns to non-action. Non-action arises from the absence of desire, and the absence of desire arises from the absence of knowledge. How can one person cause the people to be without knowledge? The Supreme simply claims he himself has never possessed knowledge. He does not display what can be governed, and the people are thus made to follow the desires of his own heart.

The Dao flows emptily, yet its use is never exhausted—profound as an abyss, it seems the ancestor of the myriad things. It blunts their sharpness, unravels their tangles, harmonizes their radiance, and mingles with their dust. Deep and still, it seems to abide—yet I know not whose child it is, for it appears before the Lord of Creation.

*(The character 冲 was originally written as 盅, signifying the emptiness of a vessel. Li Yue notes: "The Lord is the master who gives birth to things; all transformations follow after, yet only the Dao alone dwells before them. 象 means 'to resemble.' The nature of the Dao is humility, so it does not claim a fixed place before all things but says only that it 'seems' to be so.)*

*(Su's commentary: The Dao is utterly empty, yet when applied to the manifold existences, even the vastness of heaven and earth or the breadth of mountains and rivers cannot contain it. Because it is formless, it seems never full—profound and boundless, I know it as the ancestor of the myriad things, yet dare not speak of it directly, thus saying only that it 'seems' the ancestor of the myriad things. All people possess the Dao, yet only the sage can preserve it whole. Blunting its sharpness prevents it from straying into recklessness; unraveling its tangles prevents it from clashing with things. Freed from recklessness and conflict, external troubles vanish, and radiance arises. Yet the sage further harmonizes this radiance, lest it set itself apart from things. Radiance is most pure; dust is most mixed. Though mingled with dust, nothing is excluded, lest it abandon the myriad things. Only thus preserved whole does it abide, deep and constant. Though it abides, none recognize it, so it is said only to 'seem' to exist. The Dao, though ever-present, can never be named, yet neither can it be called nothing. Thus it is said: How could this not precede the Lord? It precedes the Lord, and yet nothing precedes it.)*

Lü’s Commentary:
All things bear yin and embrace yang, harmonizing through the qi of the Unfathomable—this "unfathomable" is the harmony of yin and yang. Yin is emptiness; yang is fullness. The body of the Dao is unfathomable, yet in its function it may not be full—its body is unfathomable. Thus, whether with desire or without, it is equally called "mysterious"; in its function, it is not full. Therefore, empty the heart and weaken the will, ever causing the people to remain without knowledge or desire. Only thus does it become profound, seeming the ancestor of the myriad things, yet those who seek to make it their ancestor assuredly cannot obtain it—it only *seems* so.

How then may one attain this Dao? By blunting its sharpness, unraveling its tangles, harmonizing its radiance, and mingling with the dust—nothing more. When the heart projects outward and enters things, this is sharpness; blunt its sharpness and do not act. When things arrive and the heart engages, this is entanglement; unravel its tangles and do not disturb. With sharpness blunted and tangles unraveled, the clarity of constant knowing arises.

Heavenly radiance—radiance lies beyond the dust, yet its light is not dazzling, so one harmonizes it without distinction. Dust lies within radiance, yet it is not obscured, so one mingles with it without differentiation. Only thus does that which is called the "ancestor" abide, deep and seemingly present. "Profound"—this speaks of its depth.

Deep—this speaks of its clarity. That which "may not be full" cannot be deemed a fixed emptiness; that which "seems to abide" cannot be deemed a fixed presence. In fullness and emptiness, presence and absence, I hold no preference—how then could I know their origin? I see only their arising as Heaven, as spirits, as the Lord-on-High. Thus it is said: "I know not whose child it is—it seems to precede the Lord of Images." For where there is "I" and "knowing," the Dao is already concealed. If I know not who it is, then I also know not myself—this is the true source from which the Dao emerges.

That which precedes the birth of Heaven, that which begins before the Lord of Images and spirits, its priority to all forms and vessels may be known. **Xizhai’s Commentary:** The Dao is empty and subtle, so close it seems unattainable—thus it is said, "may not be full." Yet the deeper its subtlety, the more inexhaustible its use. All things arise from the Dao, yet the Dao never becomes a thing—thus it is said, "seems the ancestor of the myriad things." Those of old who studied the Dao never set their minds on advancement, yet inevitably embodied it. To "blunt its sharpness" is not a deliberate act; to "unravel its tangles" is to act without hindrance. To "harmonize its radiance" is not to seek goodness; to "mingle with the dust" is not to reject the bad. Abiding in clarity without clinging to clarity—thus it "seems to abide." I know not its beginning, so I know not whose child it is; I know not its priority, so it seems to precede the Lord of Images.

**Shaobian’s Commentary:** Sharpness is what leads to contention—blunting sharpness thus unravels tangles. Radiance is what enables distinction—harmonizing radiance thus mingles with the dust. The first two "its" refer to oneself; the latter two "its" refer to others.

**Hongfu’s Commentary:** That which is empty and boundless yet never full is the Dao. But those who employ it may perceive it as full, thereby losing its boundless emptiness. Thus, profound and still, though all streams return to it, no fullness is seen. The sage embodies the Dao in his person—deep, tranquil, and immeasurable, like the ancestor of the myriad things, yet without any intent to rule them. Thus he constantly blunts his sharpness to appear incapable, unravels his tangles to appear inept, harmonizes his radiance to move through the world, and mingles with the dust to blend with the common. Profoundly still, seeming to vanish yet abiding—ah! Yet whose child is this, truly?

I fear this Dao—even the Yellow Emperor would find it difficult to fully grasp. Perhaps its origins lie before the Emperor’s time? The sea is the ancestor of all streams, yet the sea itself is nothing—one sees only its profound depth. The sage is the ancestor of the myriad things, yet the sage himself is nothing—one sees only his stillness. Those who gallop in ability, flaunt their talents, and dazzle the crowd do so by relying on what they *have*, seeking to connect with things through possession. They are not the ancestor of the myriad things. Only one who has no such ancestor can truly be the ancestor of all things—yet who would believe this?

Heaven and Earth are not benevolent; they treat the myriad things as straw dogs. The sage is not benevolent; he treats the people as straw dogs. The space between Heaven and Earth—is it not like a bellows? Empty yet inexhaustible, the more it moves, the more it yields. Much speech leads to frequent exhaustion; better to hold to the center.

**Su’s Commentary:** Heaven and Earth are impartial, allowing the myriad things to follow their nature. Thus, things live and die of themselves—death is not their oppression, nor life their benevolence. It is like binding straw into dogs for sacrifice: adorned and offered with care, yet not out of love. Once the ritual ends, they are discarded, trampled underfoot—yet not out of hatred. It is simply the way of things. The sage’s relation to the people is the same: so long as he does no harm, the people preserve their nature. Life and death proceed without his interference. Though he has never acted with benevolence, his benevolence is vast.

Consider the bellows and its pipe: when set in motion, its breath reaches everywhere, and the unknowing marvel at its ingenuity. Yet what does the bellows itself do? It remains empty and unobstructed, thus the more it moves, the more it yields. The workings of Heaven and Earth—giving life, bringing death, carving the myriad forms—are no different. Seeing only its movement and abundance, one fails to recognize that it is the emptiness within that responds. Thus, it is said: much speech leads to frequent exhaustion; better to hold to the center, which knows no end.

**Lü’s Commentary:** The Dao existed before the image of the Emperor, and none can trace its origin. How then could one who embodies this Dao be named by benevolence? Benevolence is merely a human sentiment. Heaven and Earth embody the Dao, and thus do not act with benevolence, treating the myriad things as straw dogs. The sage embodies the Dao, and thus does not act with benevolence, treating the people as straw dogs. Straw dogs are things without benevolence, yet those who keep them do so without acting with benevolence. The myriad things share the same body as Heaven and Earth; the people share the same body as the sage. If Heaven and Earth regard themselves as straw dogs, then their view of the myriad things is no different. To give life, to nurture, to raise, to cultivate—where is the need for benevolence? Only by being without benevolence does one achieve true benevolence.

Thus, the space between Heaven and Earth is indeed like a bellows. The bellows, by being empty and unobstructed, moves and yields ever more. So too the one who embodies the Dao: his words arise from non-words, and thus his actions are non-action. Whether speaking or acting, in essence, they are one. Why? Because the body of speech and action is thus. Without this understanding, words do not arise from non-words, and thus differ from the emptiness of the bellows. Once set in motion, they multiply and quickly reach exhaustion—is this not inevitable?

Confucius said: *"Who among those singing today truly understands?"* Knowing this, one knows that words arise from non-words. Words arise from non-words—none do otherwise. Yet they cannot do so because their minds cling to *having*. Clinging to having, they cannot align with the Dao. Only by neither having nor lacking, neither grasping nor rejecting, can one align with the Dao. This is called *holding to the center*. Holding to the center without cease, one understands the source of words. Thus, much speech leads to frequent exhaustion; better to make holding to the center one’s duty.

**Wang Chunfu’s Commentary:** Straw dogs illustrate the sage’s wondrous transformation; the bellows illustrates the sage’s wondrous preservation of spirit. *"Much speech leads to frequent exhaustion; better to hold to the center"*—these are Laozi’s words of caution. The Dao cannot be spoken, yet he rebukes it as "not benevolent." The Dao cannot be named, yet he describes it as a bellows. The wise grasp the meaning and forget the words, but the unwise fail to perceive the truth and merely argue endlessly. Thus, my words reach exhaustion. The *center* is the center—emptiness, nothingness, that which cannot be spoken or named.

Holding to this, the mind’s thoughts cease, the path of words is severed—what exhaustion is there to fear? Laozi wrote a book expounding the Dao, yet his words are thus: how profound was his sense of being compelled! Later scholars, failing to grasp the meaning of his words about straw dogs and common folk, instead suspect him of harboring contempt for the people, even claiming that the cruelty of Shen Buhai and Han Fei originated in the *Daodejing*. Sima Qian already held this view—how much more so others? This is precisely one instance of "much speech leading to frequent exhaustion." Laozi had foreseen it.

---

The valley spirit does not die—
This is called the Mysterious Female.
The gate of the Mysterious Female—
This is called the root of Heaven and Earth.
Subtle, it seems to endure;
In use, it is not exhausted.

**Gu Shen’s Commentary:** The valley is a metaphor: because it is empty, it can receive; receiving, it does not possess. Subtle and unfathomable, it is called the "valley spirit." The female gives birth to things, as in the previous chapter’s "mother." Calling it the "Mysterious Female" also conveys its profound, unfathomable nature. Xue Juncai says: Laozi’s text often varies its phrasing to harmonize rhyme, not to derive meaning from individual characters. For example, in "是谓玄牝," *pin* (female) is read as *bin* to rhyme with the preceding line. When it says "玄牝之门," the extra word is added to rhyme with the following line. Some interpretations arise from the flow of language, but if one both rhymes *xuanpin* and then designates a specific place as the "gate of the Mysterious Female," this is mistaken.

**Su Zhe’s Commentary:** The valley is utterly empty yet still has form; the valley spirit is empty and formless. Empty and formless, it has no being—how could it have death? Calling it the "valley spirit" describes its virtue; calling it the "Mysterious Female" describes its function. The female gives birth to the myriad things, yet it is called "mysterious" because one sees that it gives birth but not how it does so. The "gate of the Mysterious Female" means the myriad things emerge from it. The "root of Heaven and Earth" means Heaven and Earth arise from it. "Subtle, it seems to endure"—subtle and unceasing. "Seems to endure"—existing yet not visibly so. If one can be thus, though using it all day, one does not labor.

**Lü Huiqing’s Commentary:** The valley has form. Because it attains the One, it is empty yet able to be full. Spirit has no form. Because it attains the One, it is still yet able to be numinous. If one can hold to the center and attain the One, the body with form can be made empty like a valley, and the mind without form can be made still like spirit. Then form and formlessness merge, and one does not die. The ancients spoke of the body uniting with the mind, the mind with *qi*, *qi* with spirit, and spirit with nothingness—this is correct. United, one does not die; not dying, one does not live. Not living, one can give life—this is called the Mysterious Female. "Mysterious" is the union of being and non-being; "female" is that which gives life. Thus it says, "The valley spirit does not die—this is called the Mysterious Female." The Dao’s generation of Heaven and Earth arises from this alone. Thus it says, "The gate of the Mysterious Female—this is called the root of Heaven and Earth." If one thinks it perishes, it is subtle and never ceases; if one thinks it endures, how can one perceive its endurance? It seems to endure yet is not enduring; it seems to perish yet is not perishing. Thus, in using and preserving it, one remains effortless, perfectly attuned—what labor is there?

**Du Daojian’s Commentary:** The *Liezi* also contains this chapter, but it does not attribute it to Laozi, instead calling it a text of the Yellow Emperor. This shows that Laozi’s *Five Thousand Characters* draws extensively from ancient classics and sayings. For example, phrases in the text like "Therefore the sage," "The ancients said," "It is said," "The military has its sayings"—all clarify that he is transmitting the words of ancient sages. Thus Confucius "transmitted but did not create," and in this, Laozi may be compared to him. Only those who trust and love antiquity can discuss this Dao.

---

Heaven is long, Earth is enduring.
The reason Heaven and Earth can be long and enduring
Is that they do not live for themselves—
Thus they can live long.
Therefore the sage
Places himself last and finds himself first,
Sets himself aside and finds himself preserved.
Is it not because he is selfless
That he can fulfill himself?

**Su Zhe’s Commentary:** Though Heaven and Earth are vast, they have not transcended form and number, so their long endurance is still measurable. Yet Laozi speaks of long endurance in terms of Heaven and Earth because he is referring to what humans perceive. As for the ultimate in long endurance, it is what he calls "the beginning of Heaven and Earth." Heaven and Earth give life to things but do not live for themselves, standing beyond the myriad things—thus they live long. The sage places himself last and puts others first, sets himself aside and benefits others, standing beyond the crowd—thus he finds himself first and preserved. If Heaven and Earth competed with things for life, and the sage vied with people for gain, then Heaven and Earth would be but one thing, and the sage but one person—how could they surpass others? Though their selflessness is not aimed at fulfilling themselves, fulfillment naturally follows—this is the Dao.

**Lü Huiqing’s Commentary:** "Long" pertains to form; "enduring" pertains to time. Heaven moves through time, so "long" is insufficient for its form—thus it is called "long." Earth moves through form, so "enduring" is insufficient for its time—thus it is called "enduring." The root of Heaven and Earth emerges from the Mysterious Female; the essence of the Mysterious Female resides in the valley spirit’s not dying. Not dying, it does not live; not living, it can give life. The reason Heaven and Earth can be long and enduring is that they do not live for themselves—thus they live long. This is like the valley spirit. Then how can the sage consider his body a burden? It is due to necessity that things cannot stop him. Thus it says, "Places himself last and finds himself first." Standing in nothingness, things cannot harm him—thus it says, "Sets himself aside and finds himself preserved." The body is one’s private concern. Placing himself last and setting himself aside, he is public and selfless—yet it is through selflessness that he fulfills himself.

**Cheng Ju’s Discussion:** Heaven, Earth, and humans share a single origin. What makes Heaven Heaven, Earth Earth, and humans human is fundamentally the same. Yet Heaven and Earth can be long and enduring, while humans alone cannot—why? Heaven does not know it is Heaven; Earth does not know it is Earth. Once one receives form and becomes human, one clings to it as "me"—"me," one says. Believing one cannot live without things, one pursues endless desires. Believing one cannot but cherish the body, one schemes for its adornment. Indulging life, life is harmed; nurturing the body, the body falls ill. To not become a prematurely dead Heaven is already fortunate. Laozi’s teaching is thus, yet those who do not reflect on it assume the followers of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi are all fear death and seek long life—how deluded!

Man, possessing no Way of life—where can he find support? Yet those in the world who ruin their lives do so precisely by treating their existence as a burden. Even those who possess life are deeply cautioned against by Laozi—how much more so those who seek life outside the Dao? They have not reflected on this.

**Xizhai’s Annotation:**
The Way of Heaven’s bestowal and Earth’s generation—Heaven and Earth never for a day cease their giving. Their bestowal of things is unceasing; their generation of things, unfathomable. Heaven does not cherish its bestowal; Earth does not rest its generation. This is called "not living for oneself." Because it does not live for itself, the myriad things rely on it to live—thus it attains long life. The sage is likewise: knowing this heart does not change or decay, vast and flowing in unison with Heaven and Earth, he finds nothing in this body worth cherishing. The movement of this single heart knows no action that is not good, no conduct that is not beneficial. Because he takes what is not the body as the body, none in the world who possess a body can compare to him. This is called "putting the self last, yet the self comes first; treating the self as external, yet the self endures." What prevents people from perfecting things is their self-interest. I have no self-interest, thus I can perfect the self-interest of others. Things attain their self-interest; I attain my attainment. This is called "mutual attainment."

**Supreme goodness is like water.**
Water benefits the myriad things yet contends not;
it dwells where the multitude abhor,
thus it is close to the Dao.
In dwelling, it is good at abiding in the earth;
in the heart, it is good at depth;
in giving, it is good at benevolence;
in speech, it is good at trustworthiness;
in governance, it is good at order;
in affairs, it is good at ability;
in movement, it is good at timing.
Only by not contending does it remain free from blame.

**Su’s Annotation:**
The *Yi* says: "The alternation of yin and yang is called the Dao. What continues it is goodness; what completes it is nature." It also says: "Heaven uses the One to generate water." The Dao moves and becomes goodness, just as qi moves and generates water. Thus it is said: "Supreme goodness is like water." Both begin from nothing and take form, so their principles are the same. The Dao is present everywhere, benefiting all without exception—and water is likewise. Yet once bound to form, it becomes distinct from the Dao. Thus it is said to be "close to the Dao." However, no goodness can be named that surpasses this. Thus it is called "supreme goodness." Avoiding the high and tending toward the low, it never opposes—this is good at abiding in the earth. Empty, still, and silent, deep and unfathomable—this is good at depth. Benefiting and moistening the myriad things, giving without seeking repayment—this is good at benevolence. Round, it must revolve; square, it must turn; blocked, it must halt; unblocked, it must flow—this is good at trustworthiness. Washing and cleansing all things, leveling high and low—this is good at order. Encountering things, it takes their form yet does not linger in one—this is good at ability. Freezing in winter, melting in spring, drying up or overflowing without losing its season—this is good at timing. Though possessing goodness, it is not immune to human fault—because it contends. Water alone does not contend, thus it combines the seven virtues without blame.

**Lü’s Annotation:**
The *Commentary* says: "The alternation of yin and yang is called the Dao. What continues it is goodness." To call it "continuation" is already to depart from the Dao and no longer be its essence. "Supreme goodness" refers to the goodness spoken of by the Dao—not the goodness that all under Heaven recognize as good. Thus it is like water. Water benefits the myriad things yet contends not; it dwells where the multitude abhor—and supreme goodness is likewise. Though not yet sufficient to be the Dao, it is close to the Dao. The reason rivers and seas can be kings of the hundred valleys is that they lower themselves. Thus, in dwelling, it is good at abiding in the earth. Still water is deep; flowing water is deep—thus, in the heart, it is good at depth. It pours forth without filling, is drawn from without being exhausted—thus, in giving, it is good at benevolence. Traveling through peril without losing its trustworthiness—thus, in speech, it is good at trustworthiness. Its branches become streams and valleys; its end becomes ditches and seas—thus, in governance, it is good at order. Nothing in the world is softer or weaker than water, yet in attacking the hard and strong, none can surpass it—thus, in affairs, it is good at ability. The source springs forth ceaselessly day and night, filling the hollows before advancing—thus, in movement, it is good at timing. In sum, it arises from not contending, and its foundation is dwelling in the good earth. Thus it is said: "Only by not contending can none in the world contend with it," and so it remains free from blame.

**Wu’s Annotation:**
"Supreme goodness is like water"—water’s goodness lies in its irrigation and cleansing, benefiting the myriad things without contention. While others seek the high and pure, it dwells in the low and filthy places the multitude abhor—thus it is close to the goodness of those with the Dao. The multitude’s goodness lies in dwelling where they deem good, their hearts’ goodness in stillness and depth, their giving’s goodness in befriending the benevolent, their speech’s goodness in trustworthiness, their governance’s goodness in order, their affairs’ goodness in ability, their movement’s goodness in timing. All seven virtues are chosen from what the multitude prefer—this can be called goodness, but not supreme goodness. Only the supreme goodness of those with the Dao does not contend, willingly dwelling below, resembling water. Thus no one blames it. *You* means resentment and fault. The multitude abhor dwelling below and prefer dwelling above, seeking to surpass others—this is contention, and contention brings blame.

**Hongfu’s Annotation:**
Of all goodness in the world, none is as good as water—how then is the sage’s goodness like it? Generally, what benefits things may not be able to do so without contention, and what can avoid contention may not necessarily moisten things. Water’s goodness is precisely its benefiting the myriad things without contention. How is its non-contention evident? While others dwell above, it alone dwells below; while others dwell high, it alone dwells low; while others dwell in ease, it alone dwells in peril; while others dwell in compliance, it may dwell in opposition; while others dwell in purity, it may dwell in filth. Its dwelling is entirely where others abhor—who would contend with it? Without contention, there is no blame. This is why it is called supreme goodness. The seven lines from "dwelling in the good earth" all describe the sage’s benefiting the myriad things without contention.

**Holding and filling it, it is better to stop.**
Sharpening and honing it, it cannot be preserved long.
When gold and jade fill the hall, none can guard them.
Wealth and honor with arrogance—one brings misfortune upon oneself.
When merit is achieved and fame attained, withdraw the self—
this is the Dao of Heaven.

Su Ziyou’s Annotation:
To know that fullness must overflow and thus hold it fast is not as secure as not being full at all. To know that sharpness must break and thus measure it beforehand is to not know that such measurement cannot be relied upon. As for the sage, possessing yet not possessing—how could there be fullness? Following principle and then acting—how could there be sharpness? Without fullness, there is no need to hold; without sharpness, there is no need to measure. The sun at its zenith shifts; the moon at its fullness wanes. The revolutions of the four seasons and the departure of those who have accomplished merit—even Heaven and Earth must yield to this, how much more so for man?

Lü Huiqing’s Annotation:
Holding is meant to prevent overflow, yet filling it only compounds the overflow. If one wishes to avoid overflow, it is as if one should not fill it at all—thus it is said, "Holding and filling it, it is better to stop." Measuring is meant to anticipate loss, yet sharpening it only compounds the loss. If one wishes to avoid loss, it is as if one should not sharpen it at all—thus it is said, "Sharpening and honing it, it cannot be preserved long." To be full yet not overflow is how to long preserve wealth; holding and filling it leads to halls filled with gold and jade that none can guard. To be exalted yet not imperiled is how to long preserve honor; sharpening and honing it leads to wealth and honor with arrogance—one brings misfortune upon oneself. How then to avoid this calamity? Simply follow the Dao of Heaven. For when merit is achieved and fame attained, withdraw the self—this is the Dao of Heaven, and herein lies the absence of self-interest that accomplishes one’s private good. The border guard’s admonition to Yao—"Withdraw yourself"—is this not what it means to follow the Dao of Heaven?

Yuan Ze’s Annotation:
Holding and filling it arises from the intention to possess, and thus one loses it. Only by forgetting that possession is possession and possessing it as if it were not possessed can one avoid loss and overflow. Measuring is skill in assessing circumstances; sharpness is advantage in engaging with things. Yet things are inexhaustible, and if one confronts their changes with a single self, the skill of measuring and sharpening will at times be confounded—how can it be preserved long? Thus, the perfected person follows the moment and rides the principle, meeting all with selflessness. Their emergence is without fixed direction, and their responses are inexhaustible. Cold and heat alternate; when things reach their extreme, they return. Yin and yang succeed one another—this is the inherent pattern of Heaven’s Dao. Yet the foolish of the world, once they encounter change or once they attain a name, cling to it for life, taking it as their own, never realizing the subtle shifts of creation or the hidden interdependence of fortune and misfortune. Thus, they end by sitting in vigilance against calamity, with no means to sustain themselves. Only the perfected person is not thus: they store gold and jade yet do not treasure them; they dwell in wealth and honor yet do not glory in them. Whatever comes to lodge with them—like shadows gathering on the body—is met with profound indifference, unaware whether it lies in the other or in the self. How then could they hold fullness as caution or measure sharpness as skill? If not for the subtlety of selflessness, how could one attain this? The Dao of Heaven is vast—nothing surpasses it.

Liu Shili’s explanation:
["When full, it must empty—beware of fulfillment.
When sharp, it must dull—beware of advancement.
Gold and jade will encumber—beware of greed.
Wealth and honor breed excess—beware of arrogance.
Success achieved and fame attained bring peril—
only in knowing when to stop can one not lose the upright."
These words deeply seek to save others, for one need not dwell in mountains and forests, cutting off human affairs, to enter the Dao. Even amidst success, fame, wealth, and honor, one may diligently practice it.]

Xi Zhai’s annotation:
[To know fullness and yet hold to it is not as good as never having been full.
To know sharpness and yet measure it is not as fitting as never having been sharp.
To know fullness and hold to it, to know sharpness and measure it—this is already not good,
how much worse to be full and not hold, sharp and not measure?
Fullness breeds greater greed; sharpness breeds greater pride.
Gold and jade fill the hall, yet one knows no limit.
Wealth and honor breed arrogance, but before one can make others proud, one’s own heart is already lost—
this is to invite one’s own fault.
The turning of the four seasons: when achievement is complete, it retreats.
Such is the Dao of Heaven—how much more so for humans?]

"Can you load your vital essence and embrace the One without separation?
Can you concentrate your qi and attain suppleness like a newborn child?"

"Can you cleanse and purify your profound vision without flaw?
Can you love the people and govern the state without action?
Can you open and close the gates of Heaven while remaining receptive?
Can you comprehend the four directions without knowledge?

It gives life and nurtures,
gives life yet does not possess,
acts yet does not rely,
leads yet does not dominate—
this is called profound virtue.

[載 means "to carry" or "to bear." 营 resembles the movement of managing or encircling, as in the *Baihutong*, where it describes an unsettled appearance. Though 营魄 may seem stagnant, to "carry and bear" it implies non-attachment. To gloss 营 as "hun-soul" or as "defense" does not align well with the meaning; while "hun carries po" is plausible, the text does not say "hun carries po" but "carries 营魄," leaving later readers no way to discern its intent as referring to the hun-soul. Moreover, to set one against the other and divide them contradicts the caution of embracing the One. 涤 means cleansing like water, 除 means removing like waste. 天门 refers to this heart-mind; 开阖 describes the movement and transformation of the heart-mind. *Zhuangzi* says, "Entering and exiting without revealing its form"—this is called the gate of Heaven. 畜 is read as *xù* in the sixth tone, meaning "to nurture." 长 is read in the upper tone, meaning "to lead" or "to dominate."]"

Su's Annotation:
What distinguishes *po* from *hun* is that *po* pertains to the material while *hun* pertains to the spirit. The *Yijing* states, "Essence and *qi* constitute things; the wandering *hun* effects transformation." Thus we know the conditions of ghosts and spirits: *po*, being material, is mixed and stationary; *hun*, being spirit, is unified and transformative. To speak of "carrying *yingpo*" is to speak of its station. The Dao is omnipresent; in humans, it is called nature, and the marvel of nature is spirit. When pure and undivided, it is called the One; when gathered and undispersed, it is called the Uncarved Block—all ultimately return to the Dao, each described according to its reality.

The sage’s nature is settled and spirit congealed, unshaken by things. Though *po* serves as its dwelling, wherever the spirit wishes to go, *po* follows without fail—thus the spirit constantly carries *po*. The common people, enslaved by things, suffer a clouded spirit that cannot govern itself; then the spirit listens to *po*. Eyes and ears are wearied by sounds and colors; nose and mouth are taxed by scents and flavors. Wherever *po* wishes to go, the spirit follows—thus *po* constantly carries the spirit. Therefore, the teaching is to embrace the spirit and carry *po*, so the two do not separate. This is indeed the essence of the sage’s self-cultivation. As for the ancients’ "true persons" who "deepened their roots and firmed their stems" to "live long and see far," their Way likewise proceeded from this.

When the spirit is ungoverned, *qi* becomes chaotic: the strong delight in fighting, the weak in fear, all unconsciously. When the spirit is governed, *qi* does not act recklessly; joy and anger each follow their measure—this is called "focusing *qi*." The ultimate in spirit’s emptiness is softness; the beginning of *qi*’s fullness is hardness. To purify one’s nature and eliminate *qi* is called "attaining softness." The infant knows neither good nor evil; thus its nature is whole, its *qi* subtle, its body soft. To focus *qi* and attain softness like an infant is the utmost. The sage, outwardly not carried by *po* and inwardly not driven by *qi*, has cleansed and removed all dust and grime. Then the spirit, vast and darkly perceptive, knows all things as issuing from nature, viewing purity and filth equally without blemish.

Having cultivated the self, one extends the surplus to others. Though governing the people and ordering the state, one meets all with non-interference. If one acts with intention, then loving the people harms them, and ordering the state disorders it. The "Gate of Heaven" is the source of order and disorder, rise and decline. Having taken the world upon oneself, when the gate opens and closes, when changes and transformations occur, the common people value gain and fear loss, seeking blessings by acting preemptively. The sage follows principle and understands Heaven’s decree, waiting for the song before harmonizing. The *Yijing* says, "Preceding Heaven, Heaven does not oppose; following Heaven, one complies with its timing"—not that one truly precedes or follows Heaven, but that one’s actions always accord with Heaven’s decree. Otherwise, those who act early will be premature, those who act late will be tardy—all will miss the mark. Thus, what is called "being able to be the female" is merely not losing the proper time. Inwardly for self-cultivation, outwardly for governing the state, facing change—there is a Way for all. How could one do this without being "lucid and penetrating"?

"Lucid and penetrating" describes the heart-mind. This heart-mind knows all things yet never possesses a heart-mind that knows. The great heart-mind is simply One; if there were another that knows, then it would be two. From One to two, delusion arises, and stupidity begins. Now, a mirror responds to things as they come—nothing more. How could it know the one that responds to things? Originally, there is nothing; to impose intention upon it is the source of delusion. The Dao suffices to give life to and nurture the myriad things, yet it does not possess, does not rely, does not dominate. Though it possesses great virtue, things do not recognize it. Thus it is called "dark virtue."

Lu's Annotation:
People have possessed their bodies for a long time, yet to desire to withdraw and embody Heaven’s Way without being burdened by merit or fame—is this not difficult? Yet there is a Way.

At the inception of human life, it is called *po*; when *po* unites with essence, there is only stillness. Once *po* is born, yang is called *hun*; *hun* and spirit come and go, while *po* guards the ingress and egress of essence—thus *po* develops fissures and ceases to nurture. Once separated, they no longer embrace.

"Carrying" refers to the cyclical end and beginning; "nurturing" refers to the seamless encircling. Though one has already become human, if in carrying and nurturing the *po*, embracing the One, one remains profoundly inactive, as at life’s inception, then one can avoid separation. Able to avoid separation, one concentrates *qi* without division, attains suppleness without resistance, and can become like an infant. Able to become like an infant, one washes away regrets and resentments, gazes into the mysterious and observes the subtle—every slightest movement, I necessarily know, yet remain without flaw.

Thus, in cultivating the inner, though one loves the people and governs the state, one is not burdened by affairs and can practice non-action. Within, one washes away and gazes into the mysterious without flaw; without, one loves the people and governs the state through non-action—then the Gate of Heaven opens and closes always at my command, and one can act as the feminine. Neither pursuing nor welcoming, responding without hoarding, one becomes luminous and all-pervading, yet able to be without knowledge. When the Dao reaches the point of no-knowledge, this is true knowledge—this is how one’s human form becomes Heaven.

What burden of merit or fame could there be? To give life and nurture, to give life yet not possess, to act yet not rely, to lead yet not dominate—this is how one becomes Heaven. Dark virtue is none other than the virtue of Heaven.

The Xizhai Annotation states:
At inception, there is construction—*hun* is the yang of a person, *po* the yin. From the first formation of *po*, it embraces *hun*; within *po*, it enables *hun* and *po* to embrace as one, never to part. The infant’s yang *qi* has not dispersed; within, it harmonizes with softness. This is not the infant’s own ability but the effect of concentrating *qi*. Without seeking attainment, attainment comes—thus, concentrating *qi* achieves harmony, unity without admixture. Can one be like a child?

Transcendent and mysterious observation is not without merit, yet if this heart is not forgotten, it is insufficient to speak of the Dao. Therefore, one must wash away and gaze into the mysterious without flaw. To love the people with love is to begin with all-encompassing care; to govern the state with affairs is to begin with disorder. With clarity and stillness, one presides over the people, and they transform themselves—thus, one can practice non-action.

Yang moves and opens; yin is still and closes. From this alternation of opening and closing, transformations emerge. Yet ceaseless movement must reach exhaustion; ceasing movement and guarding stillness nurtures the dynamic—thus, one can act as the feminine.

Within and without, luminous and clear; the center, utterly open. Though not engaging in affairs or action, one may still be burdened by the dust of stillness. Only by knowing the self as no-self can one know that nothing is an obstacle—thus, one can be without knowledge.
