The sage, in relation to things, gives life as a parent and nurtures as a child or grandchild—giving life yet not claiming possession, acting yet not relying on it as merit, leading yet not assuming mastery. Without embodying dark virtue, this cannot be achieved.

The Hongfu Annotation states:
People know that *po* carries the spirit, yet do not know that the spirit carries *po*. When *po* carries the spirit, the spirit is cultivated; when the spirit carries *po*, it is not spirit—yet it is one spirit. Alas!

To embrace the Origin and guard the One is for spirit and *po* to naturally remain undivided—thus one may come to know what spirit truly is. The *ying* and *wei* circulate as protectors. The infant knows nothing, yet its qi is utterly focused; it can do nothing, yet its qi is utterly supple. To focus the qi and attain suppleness, to be as an infant—this is to embrace the One.

If flaws remain unwashed away, the Gate of the Mysterious will not open; if the Gate of the Mysterious does not open, form and substance remain obstructed. Only by cleansing and beholding the Mysterious, attaining flawlessness, can one embrace the One.

To love the people and govern the state—who but spirit could accomplish this? Yet one cannot do so through non-action. Thus, those who know to embrace the One do not divide their minds to love the people, while those who strive to love the people inevitably exhaust their spirit to govern the state—this is duality. How can one embrace the One without separation?

The opening and closing of the Gate of Heaven—who but spirit could preside over it? Yet one cannot master it oneself. Thus, when it opens, it rises of its own accord without compulsion; when it closes, it remains settled without disturbance—this is inner dissipation. How can one embrace the One without separation?

This arises from nothing other than ignorance of what spirit does. Spirit is utterly empty—emptiness is naturally luminous and clear. Spirit is utterly numinous—numinosity is naturally all-pervading. Who could separate from it? Yet precisely because it has knowledge, it is only through non-knowledge that one may know it. With knowledge, *po* carries spirit; without knowledge, spirit carries *po*. When spirit carries *po*, there is oneness; when *po* carries spirit, there is duality. Thus, one must not rely on knowledge, nor can one be without it. To know this is to embody dark virtue.

Dark virtue runs deep and far. Therefore, it can give life but cannot be possessed; it can act but cannot be relied upon as merit; it can lead but cannot be mastered. Alas! This is its fullness.

The *Brush Jottings*:

In ancient times, *hun* and *po* were sometimes spoken of as one. The *Zuozhuan* states: "The essence and clarity of the heart—this is called *hun* and *po*." At other times, they were spoken of as distinct. The *Zuozhuan* says: "At the beginning of human life, the transformation is called *po*; once *po* is born, the yang is called *hun*."

Generally, when clear and empty, *po* becomes *hun*; when attached and fixed, *hun* becomes *po*. It is like water congealing into ice or melting back into water—essentially, they are one and the same.

Now, *po* busies itself daily, tending toward existence. Yet when it is said to "carry," this means knowing that the seven emotions have no substance and the four great elements are fundamentally empty—like a person riding a carriage, a boat, or water, drifting without destination.

Thus, transforming existence into nothingness, cleansing emotions to return to nature: the multitude separates them into two, while I alone embrace them as one. What could be more essential to entering the Dao than this?

"Concentrating qi to attain suppleness"—Laozi said: "When the heart commands qi, it is called force." Zhuangzi said: "Do not listen with the heart but listen with qi." Qi is emptiness that awaits things.

The heart has judgments of right and wrong; qi makes no distinctions. Thus, when the heart commands qi, it is forceful. Concentrating on qi without the heart’s interference makes one supple. This is what is meant by "guarding the purity of qi." Who but an infant could achieve this? It is the vision of the *Profound Mirror* and the *Mysterious Wondrous*.

Flaws and illnesses:
The flaws of the multitude are coarse and easily removed; the flaws of the scholar are subtle and difficult to dispel. Why? What the Dao calls a flaw, the scholar clings to as unique insight.

Gold dust, though fine, becomes a film in the eye. Taking awareness as an obstacle and understanding as a defense—how can one not be afflicted? Therefore, one must cleanse and remove them.

Laozi’s teachings to humanity could not be more thorough.

Yet the wise eliminate the heart but not affairs, while the deluded eliminate affairs but not the heart. Should one misconstrue the earlier words and mistake restraint for the scholar’s defense, Laozi further clarifies:

When I speak of "bearing the *po* and *hun*," I do not mean mere silent inaction—it is loving the people and governing the state while practicing non-action. This is what is meant by "acting through non-action."

When I speak of "concentrating qi to attain suppleness," I do not mean stifling closure—it is the opening and closing of the Heavenly Gate while abiding as the feminine. This is what is meant by "the masculine guarding the feminine."

When I speak of "cleansing to reveal the *Profound Mirror*," I do not mean obscuring darkness—it is clarity extending in all four directions while embodying unknowing. This is what is meant by "knowing the unknowing."

Loving the people and governing the state, the opening and closing of the Heavenly Gate, clarity extending in all four directions—none of these abandon the nurturing, rearing, leading, and maturing of life. Yet through non-action, abiding as the feminine, and unknowing, one gives life without possession, acts without reliance, and leads without domination. What is this if not *profound virtue*?

Guan Yinzi said: "Within oneself, take no residence, and the forms of things will naturally manifest."
Zhuangzi said: "Laozi takes emptiness and non-destruction of the myriad things as the true reality." His intent is abundantly clear.

Those who dismiss Laozi claim he abandons the practical affairs of humanity to rely solely on emptiness and nothingness. They have not examined his words but first imposed their bias to slander him—how could this not be delusional?

】Thirty spokes unite in a single hub—it is where there is nothing that the use of the cart lies. Clay is shaped to form a vessel—it is where there is nothing that the use of the vessel lies. Doors and windows are cut to make a room—it is where there is nothing that the use of the room lies. Thus, what is present is considered advantageous, but it is where there is nothing that the function resides.【*Gǔ* (毂): pronounced as "gǔ" in ancient wood rhyme, the part of the wheel where the spokes converge. The *Kao Gong Ji* states: "The *gǔ* is that by which the wheel turns to advantage." *Dāng* (当): pronounced in level tone. *Shān* (埏): pronounced as "shī rán." *Zhí* (埴): pronounced as "shì lì." The *Kao Gong Ji* states: "*Shān* is the blending of clay; it is kneaded with water and earth, then fired to make pottery." A single door is called a *hù* (户); a window is called a *yǒu* (牖).】

Su Ziyou’s commentary:【Exhausting knowledge and employing things to make vessels, the function of the vessel always lies in the nothingness within. Without the presence of something, there would be no nothingness to achieve its function; without nothingness, there would be no presence to apply its advantage. Thus, the sage constantly observes the wondrous in nothingness and the boundary in presence. Knowing that the two are one and indivisible—this is perfection.】

Lü’s commentary:【Thirty spokes unite in a single hub—it is where there is nothing that the use of the cart lies. The cart is what I ride. Clay is shaped to form a vessel—it is where there is nothing that the use of the vessel lies. The vessel is what I use. Doors and windows are cut to make a room—it is where there is nothing that the use of the room lies. The room is where I dwell. When riding, one observes the cart’s use; when using, one observes the vessel’s function; when dwelling, one observes the room’s purpose. Their function is never not in nothingness, and the principle is not far. Yet when it comes to the self, one does not know: what is it that I employ? Thus, what is present is considered advantageous, and it is where there is nothing that the function resides. If presence alone were advantageous and nothingness had no function, then what is called advantage would be abandoned and unused. If nothingness alone had function and presence had no advantage, then what is called function would become harmful and not advantageous. Therefore, the sage enters without ever possessing things—this is how nothingness achieves its function. The sage emerges without ever lacking things—this is how presence secures its advantage. Hence it is said: "Refining wisdom to the spiritual to achieve function; utilizing advantage to secure the self and exalt virtue."】

Wu's Annotation:
The spokes are the flanges of the wheel, the hub its center—where there is no hollow emptiness. *Shan* means to mix earth; *zhi* refers to the sticky clay used to make vessels, meaning to blend water with adhesive clay to form pottery. Generally, in front of a house lies the eastern door and western window: doors serve for entry and exit, windows for light and air. Carts bear heavy loads and travel far; vessels store goods; rooms provide dwelling—thus, these carts, vessels, and rooms all serve to benefit the world. Hence it is said: "Presence is for advantage." Yet without the hollow emptiness of the hub and linchpin, the axle cannot turn, and the cart cannot traverse the earth. Without the hollow emptiness within, a vessel cannot hold things. Without the hollow emptiness of doors and windows, a room cannot allow entry, exit, or illumination. The cart’s function lies in turning the axle; the vessel’s in containing objects; the room’s in enabling passage and light—all residing in emptiness. Thus it is said: "Nothingness is for function." The human belly, filled with qi, sustains life—this is what is meant by advantage. The empty heart, devoid of things, generates qi—this is what is meant by function. Therefore, these three objects are taken as analogies.

Xue Juncai’s Exegesis:
Though the chapter alternately discusses presence and nothingness, its intent is to elucidate the value of nothingness through presence. For while all recognize the advantage of presence, the function of nothingness is universally overlooked and unexamined. Thus, Laozi employs these examples to enlighten.

The five colors blind the eye;
The five tones deafen the ear;
The five flavors dull the palate;
Racing and hunting madden the heart;
Rare goods impede conduct.
Therefore, the sage attends to the belly, not the eye—thus discarding that and embracing this.

*(Note: "令" is read in level tone; "爽" in level tone, meaning "difference"; "行" in departing tone.)*
Kumārajīva says: Not knowing the emptiness within form, the emptiness within sound—how does this differ from blindness and deafness? "Attending to the belly" resembles the meaning of "stabilizing the back" in the *Yi Jing*; "not attending to the eye" resembles the *Classic of the Dark Warrior*’s "the mechanism lies in the eye." Li Yue says: The eye is insatiable; the sage does not attend to it. The belly knows contentment; the sage attends to it. The eye gazes outward—hence "that"; the belly is filled within—hence "this."

Su’s Annotation:
Seeing colors, hearing sounds, tasting flavors—all originally arise from nature. When nature is in its pristine state, prior to the existence of things, it is perfect. Yet when the eye clings to the five colors, the ear to the five tones, the mouth to the five flavors, they are seized by their objects and forget their origin. Thus, though one sees, one is truly blind; though one hears, truly deaf; though one tastes, truly dulled. The sage sees colors, hears sounds, and tastes flavors just as others do. As for racing and hunting, they have never refrained from such pursuits; as for rare goods, they have never ceased to use them. Yet while all others deem these harmful, the sage alone regards them as blessings. Why? The sage attends to the belly, while the multitude attends to the eye. The eye is greedy and cannot be satisfied; the belly receives yet is never greedy. Those are things that come from without; this is nature congealed within.

Lü’s Annotation:
What enables the eye to be the eye is color—yet color is not color. When it clings to the five colors, it loses what enables it to be the eye and becomes no different from blindness. The ear...

The ear’s capacity to be the ear lies in sound—yet sound is not sound. When it clings to the five tones, it loses what enables it to be the ear and becomes no different from deafness. The mouth’s capacity to be the mouth lies in flavor—yet flavor is not flavor. When it clings to the five flavors, it loses what enables it to be the mouth and becomes no different from dullness. Nothing in the myriad things can disturb it; this is why the mind is tranquil and sagely. Pursuing externals leads to deluded thoughts and madness. There is nothing that does not follow this principle, yet racing and hunting are especially egregious. Knowing sufficiency brings no disgrace; knowing when to stop brings no danger. This is how one preserves integrity. Seeking externals leads to disgrace, danger, and harm to one’s conduct. There is nothing that does not follow this principle, yet rare goods are especially egregious. The belly is without awareness; the eye possesses perception. Thus the sage attends to the belly, not the eye—abandoning that pursuit of perception and desire to embrace this state of unknowing and desireless emptiness.

Xi Zhai’s Annotation:
The eye can see colors, yet the eye is blinded by color. The ear can hear sounds, yet the ear is deafened by sound. The mouth can taste flavors, yet the mouth is dulled by flavor. Racing, hunting, and the insatiable pursuit of goods all arise from the mind’s capacity to grasp, yet they also arise from that very capacity to destroy. For spirit is stored within, while the ear, eye, mouth, and mind each divide its function—seeing, hearing, tasting, and knowing. If seeing does not transcend color, then color seizes sight. If hearing does not transcend sound, then sound seizes hearing. If tasting is confined to flavor, then flavor seizes the mouth. If knowing is confined to grasping, then grasping seizes the mind. When these four are seized by things, the eye is as if blind, the ear as if deaf.

The mouth is as if numbed, the mind as if maddened. How much better to turn inward and seek it—let seeing not see through color, but let seeing be the eye; let hearing not hear through sound, but let hearing be the ear; let tasting not taste through flavor, but let tasting be the mouth; let knowing not know through the mind, but let knowing be the mind. Thus the sage acts for the belly, not for the eyes. The belly receives without grasping, takes in without retaining, and is easily satisfied, free from emotion—not like the eyes, which are never sated, seeing more yet feeling more wanting. The eyes race outward, while the belly rests within. The sage focuses on the inner and forgets the outer, thus abandoning that to take this.

Wu’s commentary: All external desires harm the body. The sage merely fills the belly to nourish himself, not to please the eyes and be enslaved by things. Thus he completely discards those external illusions and solely embraces this inner truth. Above, it speaks of the eye’s blindness, the ear’s deafness, the mouth’s numbness, and the mind’s madness—all obstructing the five senses. Below, it speaks only of not acting for the eyes, taking one to encompass the other four. Dong Sijing said: The previous chapter spoke of the function of emptiness within; this one warns against being filled by external perversions. The eye must see, the ear must hear, the mouth must taste, the form must act, and the mind must perceive—none can be forced to stillness. Only when movement does not depart from stillness can one move without being attached to things, remaining serene and desireless. To be stained by dust and pursue external realms is to lose one’s true nature, and the root of this lies in the eyes—hence it is spoken of from beginning to end. Confucius’s four prohibitions begin with “seeing,” and in Buddhism’s six senses, eye and form come first—all conveying this meaning.

Favor and disgrace are both like shock; valuing great trouble is like valuing the body. What does “favor and disgrace are both like shock” mean? Favor is inferior: gaining it is like shock, losing it is like shock—this is what “favor and disgrace are both like shock” means. What does “valuing great trouble is like valuing the body” mean? The reason I have great trouble is because I have a body. If I had no body, what trouble would I have? Thus, one who values the body as if it were the world can be entrusted with the world; one who cherishes the body as if it were the world can be relied upon with the world.

Su’s commentary: The enlightened of old were shocked by favor as if by disgrace, knowing that favor precedes disgrace. They valued the body as if it were great trouble, knowing that the body is the root of trouble. Thus, they abandoned favor and disgrace did not reach them; they forgot the body and trouble did not arise. What is called “favor and disgrace” are not two separate things—disgrace arises from favor, yet the world does not realize this, taking favor as superior and disgrace as inferior. If one knows that disgrace arises from favor, then favor is inherently inferior. Thus, the enlightened of old gained favor as if shocked, lost favor as if shocked, never resting in favor or being shocked by disgrace. What is called “as if shocked” is not truly shocked—merely appearing so. “Valuing” means difficulty. The body is the root of great trouble, yet the world’s scholars find it difficult to endure great trouble but not difficult to possess a body. Therefore, the sage, knowing their difficulty in enduring trouble, teaches them the difficulty of having a body. Once they know the difficulty of having a body, great trouble departs. Human nature cannot be increased by life or diminished by death; its greatness can fill heaven and earth, its essence can tread water and fire, enter metal and stone—no thing can trouble it. Yet the world constantly suffers the loss of its original nature, seeing only the body and loving it deeply, so that things begin to trouble it. Changes like life and death, illness and disease attack from within; encounters like favor and disgrace, gain and loss assail from without—there is not a single thing that is not trouble. Only the enlightened know that nature cannot be harmed while the body is not real. Suddenly forgetting the body, all troubles in the world vanish, and then one can engage with the world without burden. People chase after power and profit, drown in wealth and nobility, face danger without regret—all to enrich their bodies. Now, if one is entrusted with the world and charged with its weight, yet forgets the body to this extent, then even the greatness of the world cannot trouble them.

Lü’s commentary: Favor is what one is kept by; it is the lower Way. If one possesses favor, then disgrace follows. The reason I have disgrace is because I have shock—if I had not gained it, I would be shocked by gaining it; if I had gained it, I would be shocked by losing it. If I had no shock, what disgrace would I have? Thus, the disgrace that comes with favor is merely like this. Valuing is what keeps others; it is the higher Way. If one possesses valuing, then trouble follows. The reason I have great trouble is because I have a body—fortune is my trouble, misfortune is my trouble. If I had no body, what trouble would I have? Thus, the great trouble that comes with valuing is merely like this. Speaking of the body reveals that shock is of the mind; speaking of shock reveals that the body is a burden. Without the mind, there is no shock; without shock, there is no disgrace. Without the body, there is no burden; without burden, there is no trouble. In the past, Shun befriended the Son of Heaven as a common man—this could be called favor, yet he treated it as if it were nothing—what disgrace could there be? Towering above, possessing the world could be called valuing, yet he did not consider himself as possessing it—what great trouble could there be? Thus, one who values the body as if it were the world can be entrusted with the world. If favor brings disgrace, then one demeans the body and cannot be entrusted with the world. If valuing brings trouble, then one endangers the body and cannot be relied upon with the world. Only one who possesses favor without being possessed by it, possesses valuing without being possessed by it—like Shun—can truly be entrusted with the world.

Yuanze’s commentary: Valuing means being above things and possessing a state, yet if one cannot forget it, then trouble is great. It is like a person having a body and cherishing it—cold and heat, illness and pain, a thousand threads all arise—is this not great trouble? The Master of the Southern Market said: Yao was not possessed by others, nor did he appear to be possessed by others. Those who value are possessed by others; those who are favored appear to be possessed by others. Only the sage can avoid these two. When the myriad things and I are one, then one is in harmony with the profound Dao, and the ten thousand changes are all forgotten—good and ill cease. Yet the foolish cannot free themselves, clinging to form as self. Thus, whatever the form encounters, trouble arises along the way. Laozi first clarifies the burdens of favor and valuing, and these burdens all arise from having a body. Hence, he uses the analogy of valuing as if it were the body and proceeds to the wonder of having no body. Zhuangzi said: “Forget what should not be forgotten, and do not forget what should be forgotten”—this is called true forgetting, also clarifying this meaning. Confucius’s “Do not impose on others what you yourself do not desire” shares the same principle. The goal of learning lies in this alone. Yet what is called “having no body” does not mean casting it aside and abandoning it.

Yet to possess while never truly possessing—this is not burdensome. Moreover, nothing is more exalted than wealth and honor. If one can hold them as if holding nothing, then this is how the sage serves the world—what trouble could there be? As for favor, it is no different.

*Brush Jottings* states: Human nature invariably esteems favor and despises disgrace, not realizing that disgrace does not arise on its own but is born of favor. Thus, favor becomes disgrace. Since favor is but disgrace, to gain or lose it is equally startling—startled because one is touched by things yet unattached to them. When the touch passes, emptiness remains.

To value and honor is to deem something difficult. Human nature invariably clings to the body and fears suffering, not realizing that suffering does not arise on its own but is born of the body. Without this body, there would be no suffering. From this, we see that the world’s esteem for favor is in truth esteem for disgrace. If alarm at favor and disgrace is the same, what disgrace remains? To have a body is to have great suffering. If valuing the body and valuing suffering are the same, what suffering remains?

One who does not regard the body as the body but regards it as great suffering—this is to have no body. Only then can one be free of suffering. This is what is meant by "putting the self last, yet the self comes first; setting the self aside, yet the self endures."

To put it another way: one who does not lightly offer the self for the world finds the world can be entrusted to them; one who cherishes the self for the world finds the world can be relied upon. Thus, we know that not possessing the self ensures the self is preserved.

Zhuangzi said: The people of Yue assassinated their ruler for three generations. Prince Sou, fearing this, fled into a cinnabar cave. The people of Yue smoked him out with mugwort, then bore him away in the royal carriage. Prince Sou grasped the reins, mounted the chariot, and looking to heaven, cried: "Ruler! Ruler! Could you not spare me alone?"

Prince Sou did not despise being a ruler—he despised the perils of rulership. This was precisely what the people of Yue desired: to be their ruler. Yet the more Prince Sou shunned the throne, the more the people of Yue pressed him to take it. Thus, not possessing the self ensures the self is preserved—what doubt remains?

Look for it and it cannot be seen—this is called "the Level."
Listen for it and it cannot be heard—this is called "the Rare."
Grasp for it and it cannot be held—this is called "the Subtle."
These three cannot be fathomed, so they merge into one.
Above, it is not bright; below, it is not dark.
Endless, unceasing—it cannot be named, yet returns to nothingness.
This is called the formless form, the imageless image—what we call "elusive and evanescent."
Approach it and you do not see its head; follow it and you do not see its tail.
Hold fast to the ancient Dao to steer the present realm.
To know the ancient beginning is called the thread of the Dao.

The graspable, too—merged into one.
To see, use the eyes; to hear, use the ears; to grasp, use the heart—merged into one, then the ears are like the eyes, the eyes like the ears, the heart like the ears and eyes.
Those who lose the Dao see light above and earth below—what I attain is neither.
Above, it is not light, so it is not bright; below, it is not earth, so it is not dark.
Endless, ever-straight—trustworthy, yet though trustworthy, it cannot be named, so it returns to nothingness.
Though nothingness, this is called the formless form, the imageless image—yet it has never been without thingness.
This is called "elusive and evanescent."
Elusive, then not bright; not bright, then suspected of nothingness—yet not nothingness.
Evanescent, then not dark; not dark, then suspected of thingness—yet not thingness.
Its beginning has no prior, so approaching it, you do not see its head; its end has no after, so following it, you do not see its tail.
Without prior, without after—neither ancient nor present.
Though neither ancient nor present, it has never been without ancient and present.
Thus, it is older than high antiquity yet does not age.
I attain it in daily use.
Therefore, it is said: "Hold fast to the ancient Dao to steer the present realm."
The so-called "ancient" is not different from the present—by knowing the ancient’s beginning, one knows the present’s origin.
The so-called "present" is not different from the ancient—by knowing the present’s source, one knows the ancient’s origin.
Truly knowing the ancient’s beginning, one knows the present’s source.
The beginning has no origin, no source—this is called the "thread without end."
The thread is the thread of the Dao.
The Dao cannot be grasped, but attaining this, one may grasp it as virtue.
To grasp virtue is called the thread.

Yuan Ze’s commentary:
These three are originally one body, yet people seek it sometimes by seeing, sometimes by hearing, sometimes by grasping—thus, they force names according to circumstances. Nevertheless, the means of seeking and what is sought have never been separate. Only by realizing our oneness does it merge with that oneness, becoming one. Pushing further to the beginning without beginning, one knows things have no source—thus, the nature of the Dao is attained.

Li Rong’s commentary:
These three—Rare, Subtle, Level—are all beyond sound and form, transcending shape and name. Neither existence nor nonexistence suffices to fathom them; neither length nor shortness can be discussed. Undifferentiated, chaotic—we lodge the name "one." Yet this one does not arise from itself; the three do not arise from the one. Thus, the one arises from the three, and the three arise from the one. The one is three; the three are one. The one does not become one; the three do not become three. The three not becoming three means there are no three; the one not becoming one means there is no one. Without one, without three—naturally harmonizing the principle beyond words. Clinging to three or one obstructs the teaching of mysterious penetration.

Xizhai’s commentary:
Seeing yet not seeing—it cannot be sought by color. Hearing yet not hearing—it cannot be seized by sound. Grasping yet not grasping—it cannot be sought by form. No longer confined by color, form, or vessel, what is called "original clarity" is but a single luminous essence. Before it scatters, merged into one, though lodged in clarity, it cannot be called clear—thus, "above, it is not bright." Though it cannot be called clear, it also cannot be called unclear—thus, "below, it is not dark." Never for a moment can it be separated or abandoned. Therefore, it is said: "Endless, it cannot be named, yet returns to nothingness." Though returning to nothingness, it cannot be called nothingness—thus, "the formless form, the imageless image," called "elusive and evanescent." Elusive and evanescent—entering and exiting, transforming without fixed abode, hence its constancy. Its coming has no beginning, so approaching it, you do not see its head; its going has no end, so following it, you do not see its tail. Try holding fast to the ancient Dao to steer the present realm—then the present is like the ancient. By the present being like the ancient, one knows the ancient is like the present—this is called the "thread of the Dao." The thread of the Dao means no going, no coming—this is the meaning of ancient and present.

The ancients who were good at being scholars were subtly mysterious, profoundly penetrating, deeply unfathomable. Only because they were unfathomable did they force themselves to adopt appearances:
Hesitant, as if crossing a river in winter;
Cautious, as if fearing neighbors on all sides;
Reserved, as if a guest;
Dispersing, as if ice about to melt;
Simple, as if uncarved wood;
Vast, as if a valley;
Turbid, as if muddy.
Who can make the turbid still and slowly clear?
Who can make the tranquil stir and slowly come to life?
Those who preserve this Dao do not desire fullness.
Only by not being full can they be worn yet not newly made.

Those who act only after caution are called "prepared"—what they desire to do is compelled yet responsive, prepared as if crossing a river in winter, hesitating as if unable to proceed. Those who do not act due to doubt are called "hesitant"—what they do not desire is delayed and resisted, hesitant as if fearing the gaze of neighbors on all sides. Like a guest, they show reverence in all things, never negligent. Like ice about to melt, they know the myriad things arise from delusion and never cling to anything. Like uncarved wood, human artifice is exhausted and their nature restored. Like a valley, they are empty and receptive to all. Like turbid water, they harmonize their light and merge with the dust, not differing from things.

Worldly scholars, drowned by things, become turbid and never clear afterward. Those who wither in stillness extinguish their nature and find peace, never reviving. Now, knowing turbidity disturbs nature, one quiets it—quieting it, it slowly clears. Knowing the extinction of nature is not the Dao, one stirs it—stirring it, it slowly comes to life. The *Yi Jing* says: "Still and unmoving, yet when stimulated, it penetrates all causes under heaven." What is called "stirring" here is like this.

Fullness arises from extreme turbidity and cannot clear; from stagnation and cannot live—this is why it is full. No thing does not wear out. Only by not being full does its weariness depart without needing renewal.

---

Lü’s Commentary:
The ancients who were good at being scholars aimed to become sages and fully realize the spirit. Though they had not yet attained sagehood or spiritual mastery, what enabled them to become sages and fully realize the spirit was already complete within them. Subtle, wondrous, mysterious, and penetrating—deeply unfathomable—this is how they became sages and fully realized the spirit. Subtle, then wondrous; wondrous, then mysterious; mysterious, then penetrating—thus deeply unfathomable. Only by being unfathomable could their appearances not be imitated. They forced themselves to adopt appearances: prepared, like crossing a river in winter, acting only when compelled, rising only when unavoidable; hesitant, like fearing neighbors, guarding against deceit to preserve sincerity, so that if things did not provoke them, their minds did not emerge; reserved, like a guest, not presuming to take the lead; dispersing, like ice about to melt, ending with the heart congealed, form released, flesh and bones fused; simple, like uncarved wood, returning to their origin; vast, like a valley, responsive yet not hoarding; turbid, like muddy water, without distinctions of right and wrong or self and other.

People are all bright—who can make the turbid still and slowly clear? Slowly clearing, nothing is not illuminated. People all seek to be first—who can remain tranquil and slowly come to life? Slowly coming to life, nothing is not brought forth. To still the mind, one must calm the qi; to spiritually transform, one must follow the heart. To act appropriately, one must respond to necessity—calming the qi and following the heart are how one slowly clears. Responding to necessity is how one slowly comes to life. This is how scholars become sages and fully realize the spirit.

The body of the Dao is empty and harmonious, the balance of yin and yang, the preservation of fullness and emptiness. Those who preserve this Dao do not desire fullness—only emptiness. Yet it is not called "emptiness" but "not full" for fear of being burdened by emptiness. Burdened by emptiness, one is not empty. Thus it says: "Perhaps not full." It means not desiring fullness—merely this. All things under heaven are new, then worn; worn, then broken. Few can avoid weariness. Only by not being full do newness, weariness, completion, and ruin find no place in the heart. Thus, though worn, they are not worn; not worn, they do not break; not worn, not broken, they are not new, not complete.

---

Yuanze’s Commentary:
"Scholar" is the name for one who serves the Dao. Subtle and wondrous, mysterious and penetrating—possessing this Dao, they retreat and conceal it in secrecy. Secrecy lies within the original nature, so no traces are visible. The scholars of that time differed from this. Thus, the ancients who were good at being scholars were called "prepared"—those who caution before acting. The perfect person has no mind for things, yet acts only when compelled. "Crossing in winter" means hesitating before action, as if unable to proceed. *Zhuangzi* says: "Not engaging in affairs, one need not be cautious," yet they are cautious—this is "hesitant." The perfect person is still, secret, deep, and does not emerge from the dwelling of nature, so they are always like one fearing neighbors, restraining their conduct and not indulging. Reserved like a guest, not taking affairs as their own responsibility, their demeanor is tranquil.

At the beginning of human life, all is one with the great emptiness. Congealed into our bodies like water into ice, when matters reach this point, their appearance no longer clings to one form—dispersing, they are about to melt. I have privately considered this: human nature is like water, congealed by creation, thus bound and unflowing. Those who melt return to the origin. Unworked material is called "uncarved wood"—this metaphor describes the wholeness of nature. After the ice melts, one can preserve the wholeness of heavenly nature without being carved by human artifice, thus like uncarved wood. Nature is whole and unharmed, yet does not possess its wholeness, so it is vast and empty like a valley, receptive and responsive.

The nature of water is originally clear, but turbidity arises from mixing with things. The perfect person does not purify themselves apart from things, so they are turbid like muddy water. When the nature is stirred, it becomes increasingly turbid; only by stilling it can it slowly clear. The scholar of the Dao, whom no thing can disturb, does so by stilling and slowly clearing, returning to tranquility. Stimulated, they penetrate all causes, slowly coming to life—thus there is an end and a beginning. Stirring and slowly coming to life, they change and move without abiding—what thing can stop them? Who can make them tranquil? Those who do this trust the principles of yin and yang, ride the movement of nature, and have no mind in between. Thus, all is said to be "slow." Turbidity knows tranquility clears it; tranquility knows movement revives it. This aligns with the previous chapter’s "subtle and wondrous" passage.

"Not full" is the true fullness. After seeing the Dao, to be full and possess it is to depart from the Dao. The Dao has no form and cannot be contained by vessels. When used, it is inexhaustible; when sought, it cannot be obtained. Thus, those who attain the Dao are never full, yet their use is endless. Those who attain the Dao are never new, for the Dao’s function pervades the myriad things without weariness—without weariness, without newness, without completion, without failure. Weariness arises from newness, failure from completion. Though the scholar completes the Dao, they are always like one worn and failed. If, upon attaining the Dao, they pride themselves on newness and completion, they differ from the Dao—this is not great completion.

The *Classic* says: "Great completion seems deficient; its use is unwearying." This passage proceeds step by step to completion—completing yet seeming worn is its culmination.

】 Attain utmost emptiness and firmly preserve profound stillness—then the myriad things arise together, and I observe their return. Now, things flourish in abundance, each returning to its root. Returning to the root is called stillness; stillness is called returning to life-destiny. Returning to life-destiny is called constancy. Knowing constancy is called clarity. Not knowing constancy, reckless action brings misfortune. Knowing constancy, one is all-embracing; being all-embracing, one is impartial; being impartial, one is kingly; being kingly, one accords with Heaven; according with Heaven, one accords with the Dao; according with the Dao, one endures long—even to the end of life, one does not perish.

[Kaiyuan Commentary: *Zhi* means to cause something to come of itself, as in the *Spring and Autumn Annals* where *zhi shi* means "to engage in battle." *Zuo* means "to act," as in "the sun rises and one acts." *Yun yun* describes the state of their arising. *Chang* is the constancy of the constant Dao. Because it is the master of the ten thousand images, it is called "kingly"; because it is the father of all, it is called "Heaven."]

Su Che’s Annotation:
If one does not attain utmost emptiness, something remains unvanished. If one does not firmly preserve stillness, movement remains unvanished. Though mountains and hills may be removed, the slightest speck of dust remains—this is not yet "utmost" or "firm." Now, to attain emptiness is still to retain emptiness, which has not yet departed from being; to preserve stillness is still to guard against movement—how much more so with other things? Without attaining utmost emptiness or firmly preserving stillness, to demand the function of emptiness and stillness is difficult indeed. Only by attaining utmost emptiness and firmly preserving stillness can one observe the transformations of the myriad things and not be confused by their changes, knowing that all arising must return. If I, too, am caught up in the arising of the myriad things, I cannot know this.

All things arise from their nature and return to their nature—like blossoms and leaves that grow from the root and return to the root, or waves that rise from water and return to water. If one has not yet returned to one’s nature, merely ceasing movement and stilling thoughts to seek stillness is not true stillness. Only by returning to the root is there stillness. *Ming* (life-destiny) is the wonder of nature; nature can be spoken of, but when it comes to *ming*, it cannot be spoken of. The *Book of Changes* says: "Exhaust principle, fulfill nature, to reach *ming*." The sage’s study of the Dao must begin with exhausting principle, proceed through fulfilling nature, and culminate in returning to *ming*.

Humaneness, rightness, ritual, and music are how the sage engages with things, yet the function of humaneness, rightness, ritual, and music must have a source. Those who act without knowing their source are vulgar scholars; those who know their source and then act are noble persons. This is called "exhausting principle." Yet even after exhausting the mind to exhaust principle, one must seek to obtain it—without seeking, one does not obtain. When affairs and things daily confront one, only by seeking can one respond; thus, the effort is laborious, and the achievement is slight.

The sage, apart from being obscured by things, maintains a nature that is profoundly clear, effortlessly centered, unthinking yet attaining, responding to things as they arrive—this is called "fulfilling nature." Yet even so, this is still my nature, and there remains a distinction between self and things, which guards against delusion. The ruler’s *ming* is called *ming*; Heaven’s *ming* is called *ming*. Engaging with things through nature yet not knowing it as oneself is why it is entrusted to *ming*—this is called "returning to *ming*."

When things arise, even the vastness of heaven and earth, mountains and rivers, have nothing that does not change and decay, nothing that is not impermanent. Only by returning to nature does one abide in profound constancy. If one does not regard returning to nature as clarity, it is merely worldly wisdom—though one may call it clarity, it is not true clarity. Not knowing to return to nature, one moves with things, and there is no action that is not misfortune. Though one may gain for a time, one loses in the long run.

When deluded by delusion, one is self-righteous and denies others; all things become one’s enemies—how can one be a guest? If one knows that all is delusion, then even enemies will be pitied and cherished—what cannot be embraced? With nothing not embraced, the feelings of self and other are exhausted—whom would one favor? With nothing not impartial, the world will come and submit. With nothing not cherished, even Heaven cannot add to it. Heaven still has form; as for the Dao, it is the ultimate. Yet even the Dao cannot advance beyond this.

Lü’s Commentary:
Those who preserve this Dao do not desire fullness; they attain emptiness without extremity, maintain stillness without rigidity—this is not the ultimate of non-fullness. As for the multitude and the myriad things, only when they rest do they reveal their return; only when they decline do they reveal their return to the root. Yet I, through the ultimate of emptiness and stillness, perceive the arising of the myriad things and their flourishing—this lies within me, not in them. What causes their arising is precisely what causes their return. When they flourish, this is precisely what causes their return to the root. Thus, observing their arising together is to observe their return: as they flourish, each returns to its root. Therefore, the so-called emptiness is not emptiness made by emptying, but simply non-fullness that results in emptiness. The so-called stillness is not stillness made by stilling, for as the myriad things flourish and each returns to its root, none can disturb the heart—thus it is still. Hence, returning to the root is called stillness. The Mandate is what we receive to live. Only through stillness can one return to that which gives life and thereby command things. Thus, stillness is called returning to the Mandate. When the Dao reaches the point of commanding things, it abides constantly without departing. Hence, returning to the Mandate is called constancy. Viewed from constancy, fortune and misfortune, regret and shame are ever apparent in the subtlest movements—what clarity could surpass this? Thus, knowing constancy is called clarity. Those who do not know constancy act in opposition to it, and all their actions are but delusion. Those who know constancy and embody it find the myriad things and the self as one. Thus, knowing constancy allows all things to be embraced, and the self and the myriad things become one—no longer cherishing the self in private. Embracing is thus impartial; the myriad things and the self as one, without partiality, are vast. Vastness is sageliness. Inner sageliness and outer kingship—thus impartiality is kingship. Sageliness then reaches the divine; thus kingship is Heaven. Heaven is then the divine. The Dao is what completes sageliness and exhausts the divine. Thus, Heaven is the Dao. Practicing the Dao to attain constancy is to exhaust it. Thus, the Dao endures, and the body does not perish. Enduring to the point where the body does not perish—this is what constancy means.

*Brush Records*:
Attaining emptiness without knowing that reality is itself emptiness—emptiness is not yet ultimate. Maintaining stillness without knowing that movement is itself stillness—stillness is not yet profound. Those who observe nothingness in nothingness have never observed it in being. Thus, observe the myriad things as they arise together: though they seem to move and be real, in truth, not even the slightest movement or reality exists. To observe thus is to observe return. Return is returning to the beginning. Those who cannot observe return yet halt movement to seek stillness—desiring stillness, they only stir further. When things flourish and return to their root, all thoughts naturally cease in their place. Not seeking stillness, they attain true stillness. Stillness is returning to the Mandate. It is called Mandate, not nature, because nature can be spoken of, but the Mandate cannot. The *Book of Changes* says: "Exhaust principle, fulfill nature, to reach the Mandate." Principle and nature are not without subtlety, yet there is still subtlety beyond them. To exhaust and fulfill them is to find them inexhaustible—this is the ultimate Mandate. Thus, the Mandate transcends nature. Where there is action, there is change; returning to the Mandate is action without action—this is true constancy, which only the enlightened can know. Knowing constancy brings stillness and fortune; not knowing constancy brings delusion and misfortune. When deluded by delusion, one is self-righteous and denies others—incapable of embracing. Knowing constancy, one forgets both good and evil, leaving no trace of right or wrong—what cannot be embraced? Embracing is impartiality; impartiality is kingship; kingship is Heaven; Heaven is the Mandate. At this point, the Dao is complete, and one may endure without peril—this is but a secondary matter.

The Highest does not let them know of his existence; the next is loved and praised; the next is feared; the next is scorned. Where trust is insufficient, there is distrust. How cautious he is in his words! When his work is accomplished and his affairs completed, the hundred clans all say, "We are naturally so."

Lu Xiusheng’s Commentary:
The rulers of highest antiquity, possessing virtue, acted without trace, so the people only knew of their existence above—saying, "What has the emperor’s power to do with me?" As virtue declined, benevolence and righteousness governed the world. The people, embraced by benevolence, loved them; cherished by righteousness, praised them. When benevolence and righteousness were insufficient to govern the heart, punishments and laws were used—thus the people feared them. When punishments and laws were insufficient to control the will, cunning and deception were employed—thus the masses scorned them. Ah! That which is true in the heart is called sincerity; words that can be fulfilled are called trust. When sincerity is not true, words cannot be fulfilled—yet one still values and esteems them. Can this be called sincerity and trust? When the Dao is hidden, benevolence and righteousness become manifest; when benevolence and righteousness fail, punishments arise—yet one still exalts these later arts. Can this be called the Dao? The sage does otherwise: holding to antiquity, guiding the present, carving simplicity from the uncarved—accomplishing work without attachment, completing affairs without action. Following the times, he establishes his work, allowing the hundred clans to fulfill their natures, all saying, "We are naturally so." Thus, the hearts of love, praise, fear, and scorn do not arise in the world.

Su’s Commentary:
The Highest rules the world through the Dao’s all-pervading influence without governing, so the people do not know why things are so—they only know of his existence. The next rules through benevolence and righteousness; his virtue can be embraced, his achievements seen—thus the people love and praise him. Though his name is fair, the distinction between favor and disfavor begins here. The next governs through policies to order the people; though the people do not lack fear, where power does not reach, they scorn him. If one truly trusts oneself, ruling through the Dao is sufficient. Only when one lacks self-trust, adding benevolence and righteousness, then punishments and policies, do the people begin to distrust. The sage trusts himself abundantly; in his words, he values them carefully, not lightly uttering them—thus the people trust him. When his work is accomplished and his affairs completed, the people daily move toward goodness and away from wrongdoing without realizing it.

Lü’s Commentary:
Holding to the great image, the world comes. As the world is about to release me, it forgets me—whose trace can be seen? Thus, the people only know of his existence—nothing surpasses this, so it is called the Highest. The next is loved and praised; the next is feared and scorned—this can be known. How to discuss it? Consider the love and reverence between father and son—it is self-evident without words. As for the relationship between host and guest, or friends, to convey reverence, one must adorn it with words to be understood. Why? It is merely a matter of trust being sufficient or not. Thus, love and praise already arise from insufficient trust and distrust—how much more so fear and scorn?

Yet if one wishes to make trust fully sufficient and eliminate distrust, what should be done? Should one not value speech to return to the Dao? Valuing speech means practicing the teaching without words. Practicing the teaching without words, one engages in affairs of non-action—this can be known. When merits are accomplished and affairs completed, the hundred clans say, “We are thus of ourselves,” unaware of who brought it about—then who could love and praise them?

Wu’s Commentary:
*Tai shang* means “the highest,” referring to the age of the Great Dao, where all forget non-action and the people do not know their superiors exist. The next refers to rulers of benevolence and righteousness, whom the people love as parents; when benevolence and righteousness become more manifest, the people not only love them but also praise them. The next refers to rulers of wisdom, whom the people fear as divine; when wisdom is exhausted, the people not only fear them but also scorn them. Trust is the substance of the Great Dao. When the substance of the Great Dao is insufficient and cannot match the age of antiquity, rulers no longer treat the people with its substance, and daily incline toward ornamentation. Thus, with one descent, they employ benevolence and righteousness; with a second descent, they employ wisdom. The preceding speaks of the *tai shang* not being known—this is like the earlier chapter’s “valuing treasures.” Yet even so, treasures are held in their words, not lightly uttered, like a dog hesitating and retreating. The sage does not speak, acting without action, so the people subtly receive his gifts and each lives in peace. When his merits are complete and affairs accomplished, the hundred clans all say, “We are thus of ourselves,” unaware of the ruler’s gift.

When the Great Dao is abandoned, benevolence, righteousness, and wisdom emerge; when great falsity arises, the six relations are not harmonious, and filial piety appears; when the state is in chaos, loyal ministers appear.

Wang Fuzhi’s Commentary: The six relations are father, son, elder brother, younger brother, husband, and wife. Filial piety and compassion refer to filial sons and compassionate grandsons.

Su’s Commentary:
When the Great Dao flourishes, benevolence and righteousness operate within it, yet the people do not notice. Only when the Great Dao is abandoned do benevolence and righteousness appear. The world does not know that the Dao suffices to satisfy all things, so it imposes wisdom upon them, and thus the people begin to repay with falsity. When the six relations are harmonious, who is not filial or compassionate? When the state is well-governed, who is not loyal? Yao was not unfilial, yet only Shun is praised—because he had no blind father. Yi Yin and the Duke of Zhou were not disloyal, yet only Longfeng and Bigan are praised—because they had no Jie or Zhou. Fish in a drying marsh blow foam at one another, moisten one another with wetness—how much better to forget one another in rivers and lakes!

Wang Jiefu’s Commentary:
The Dao flows in formlessness; names arise from insufficiency. When the Dao flows in formlessness, there is no distinction between great and small. When names arise from insufficiency, there are gradations of benevolence, righteousness, and wisdom. Benevolence involves partiality; righteousness involves distinction. Because of this partiality and distinction, the Great Dao is abandoned.

The wise possess knowledge; the perceptive possess discernment. Because they have knowledge and discernment, this is how great falsity arises. The filial each cherish their own parents; the compassionate each dote on their own children—this is why the six relations are not harmonious. The loyal are loyal to their own lord and call it loyalty; loyal to another’s lord, they call it treason.

Lü’s Commentary:
The Dao cannot be named; to name it as Dao is already not the Dao. Then to further divide it into benevolence and righteousness—how can this be the whole of the Dao? Thus, those who possess benevolence and righteousness are surely those who have abandoned the Great Dao. No harm is greater than virtue with intent, intent with eyes, and eyes that turn inward. Inward gaze leads to ruin. Thus, the emergence of wisdom and discernment is precisely what gives rise to great falsity. Falsity is the opposite of virtue. Without benevolence and righteousness, their absence extends to the six relations becoming disharmonious, and so filial piety and compassion arise. With great falsity, its effects extend to the state falling into chaos, and so loyal ministers arise. Therefore, only with the obstinacy of Gusou and the arrogance of Xiang did Shun emerge; only with the tyranny of Jie and Zhou did Longfeng and Bigan arise. There is no other reason—it is simply that they have drifted ever farther from the root.

Xizhai’s Commentary:
When the Dao scatters, it descends and gives birth to wrongdoing; when falsity prevails, it reverses and reveres the Dao. Descending is the Dao’s decline; reversing is the extremity of lament. When the Dao has not yet scattered, benevolence and righteousness lie hidden within it, undivided and unenumerated. But when one acts with tender affection as benevolence, people respond with tender affection, cherishing that benevolence; when one acts with rigid integrity as righteousness, people respond with rigid integrity, cherishing that righteousness. If the Great Dao were practiced and the world held in common, how could benevolence and righteousness ever stand before it? Unfortunately, there are those with petty wisdom and cunning who usurp benevolence and righteousness to put them into practice—thus, falsity begins to flourish, and disorder begins here. This is what is meant by descending and giving birth to wrongdoing. As it flows to its end, human falsity multiplies, and nothing is not false. Amidst the clamor of falsity, those who slightly distinguish themselves are then revered—thus, only when the six relations are disharmonious does one know of filial piety and compassion; only when the state is in chaos does one know of loyal ministers. This is what is meant by reversing and revering the Dao. When the Dao scatters, one sees its semblance and forgets its wholeness; when it declines, it is utterly exhausted, yet what is revered is only its semblance. This is why it descends ever lower.

Abandon sagacity, discard wisdom, and the people will benefit a hundredfold. Abandon benevolence, discard righteousness, and the people will return to filial piety and compassion. Abandon skill, discard profit, and thieves and bandits will cease to exist. These three are insufficient as cultural refinements, so let them be directed toward something: manifest plainness, embrace simplicity, reduce selfishness, diminish desires.

Su’s Commentary:
Without sagacity and wisdom, one cannot know the Dao. If sagacity and wisdom were employed to govern the world, could there be any who would not use the Dao to guide things? Yet the people of the world are insufficient to grasp the root of sagacity and wisdom, seeing only their branches and mistaking them for cunning that overcomes things. Thus, they gallop along the branches’ currents, and the people begin to suffer their harm. Therefore, abandon sagacity and discard wisdom, and the people will benefit a hundredfold. There has never been benevolence that neglects one’s kin, nor righteousness that betrays one’s lord. Benevolence and righteousness are what give rise to filial piety and compassion. Yet when they decline, people usurp the names of benevolence and righteousness to seek profit in the world—thus, children defy fathers, and fathers abuse children. This is the doing of the traces of benevolence and righteousness. Therefore, abandon benevolence and discard righteousness, and the people will return to filial piety and compassion. Skill is for facilitating tasks; profit is for aiding others. Neither is intended to foster theft, yet without them, thieves and bandits cannot act. Therefore, abandon skill and discard profit, and thieves and bandits will cease to exist. The world reveres these three as the cause of its disorder, for cultural refinements are insufficient. Thus, some direct them toward sagacity and wisdom, some toward benevolence and righteousness, and some toward skill and profit—all to refine governance. Yet the world grows ever more disordered. Why not return to the root? Manifest plainness, embrace simplicity, reduce selfishness, diminish desires, and the world will each return to its nature. Even with these three, there will be no use for them. Thus it is said: “I practice non-action, and the people transform themselves; I cherish stillness, and the people rectify themselves; I engage in no affairs, and the people enrich themselves; I have no desires, and the people return to simplicity.” This is the ultimate of sagacity and wisdom, the perfection of benevolence and righteousness, the extremity of skill and profit. Yet Confucius governed the world with benevolence, righteousness, ritual, and music, while Laozi abandoned and discarded them. Some may think them different. The *Yi* says: “That which is above form is called the Dao; that which is within form is called the vessel.” Confucius, deeply concerned for later generations, showed people the vessel while obscuring the Dao, so that those below the middle level would hold to the vessel and not be misled by the Dao, thus not failing to be gentlemen. Those above the middle level would naturally progress upward from there. Laozi, however, was not like this. His aim was to illuminate the Dao and urgently open people’s minds, so he showed them the Dao while slighting the vessel. If scholars only know the vessel, the Dao is lost. Thus, he abandoned benevolence and righteousness, discarded ritual and music, to clarify the Dao. The Dao cannot be spoken; whatever can be spoken is only its semblance. The enlightened recognize the real through semblance, while the benighted cling to semblance and fall into falsity. Thus, later generations have used Laozi’s words to disrupt the world, while those who study Confucius commit no great errors. Many have used Laozi’s words to grasp the Dao, yet those who seek it in Confucius often find no entry point. These two sages both acted out of necessity. To perfect one is to neglect the other.

Lü’s commentary: The sage knew that the world’s chaos begins with losing one’s original nature through delusion of the root. Only the uncarved simplicity of the nameless can settle it. Thus, "cut off sageliness and abandon wisdom, sever benevolence and discard righteousness, break craft and reject profit"—these are the means to restore our nameless simplicity and settle it.

To cut off sageliness and abandon wisdom, to sever benevolence and discard righteousness, is to prevent beauty and goodness from burdening the heart. To break craft and reject profit is to prevent evil and ungoodness from burdening the heart. When nothing burdens the heart within and no traces are left without, the people’s benefit increases a hundredfold, they return to filial piety and kindness, and thieves and bandits vanish—this is the inherent principle.

Now, "cutting off sageliness and abandoning wisdom, severing benevolence and discarding righteousness" is the fullest expression of "not exalting the worthy." To cut off and discard them is not merely to refrain from exalting them but to eliminate them entirely. "Breaking craft and rejecting profit" is the fullest expression of "not prizing rare goods." To break and discard them is not merely to refrain from prizing them but to abolish them entirely.

Human life is such that all things are complete within me—possessing the utmost abundance of wealth. If one can cut off sageliness and abandon wisdom to return to the beginning, their benefit increases a hundredfold. When the people return to filial piety and kindness, the six relations all harmonize without awareness of filial piety and kindness. When thieves and bandits vanish, the state is well-governed without awareness of benevolent ministers.

"Not exalting the worthy" causes the people not to contend, and their benefit increases a hundredfold; when they return to filial piety and kindness, it is not merely that they do not contend but that contention ceases entirely. "Not prizing rare goods" causes the people not to steal; when thieves and bandits vanish, it is not merely that they do not steal but that theft ceases entirely. Sageliness and wisdom, benevolence and righteousness, craft and profit—these are the very things to be cut off and discarded.

These three—when taken as embellishments rather than substance, as insufficiency rather than wholeness—must be cut off and discarded, directing one instead to what truly belongs: to perceive the plain and embrace the unhewn, to diminish self-interest and reduce desires—this is what truly belongs.

To perceive the plain is to know it is free from admixture and not mere embellishment; to embrace the unhewn is to know it is undivided and not mere insufficiency. Plain and unadulterated, unhewn and undivided—this is to return to one’s nature, so that external things cannot delude, and self-interest and desires diminish. Only then can one speak of the ultimate Dao of cutting off learning.

**Xizhai’s Annotation:**
The sage does not regard himself as sagely; the wise do not regard themselves as wise. If sageliness and wisdom could be cut off, they would not be true sageliness and wisdom. The benevolent do not regard themselves as benevolent; the righteous do not regard themselves as righteous. If benevolence and righteousness could be cut off, they would not be true benevolence and righteousness. Great skill does not display its skill; great profit does not display its profit. If skill and profit could be cut off, they would not be true skill and profit.

The sage employs substance, not embellishment, so what appears externally has no fixed form. The multitude, stealing semblances, confuse the real, so what appears externally has an attachment—yet without fixed form, it benefits things without their awareness. Having an attachment is merely enough to disorder the world. The sage detests falsehood for its power to confuse the real, so he seeks to sever its root to remedy the ills of its branches, ensuring the world’s people no longer harbor profit-seeking minds, nor feign sagely wisdom in conduct, nor borrow benevolence and righteousness while abandoning the reality of filial piety and kindness, nor employ thieves and bandits while discarding the convenience of skill and profit. Only by placing plainness and unhewn simplicity before the people—so that seeing his plainness and unhewn simplicity, they dare not deceive with embellishment, nor display private desires—do they, seeing his diminished self-interest and reduced desires, dare not deceive themselves with embellishment.

**Bisheng’s Notes:**
*Shu* (属) is like Zhuangzi’s “attaching to one’s nature.” Benevolence and righteousness are *shu*—mere attachments. Sageliness, wisdom, benevolence, righteousness, skill, and profit—these three arise as the world’s Dao daily trends toward embellishment, hence their names. From the perspective of one who knows the Dao, these are embellishments. Embellishment is insufficient to govern the world; better to direct one’s intent to *shu*.

Perceive the plain and embrace the unhewn—then self-interest and desires diminish, and the world is at peace. Plainness is unpainted; unhewn is uncarved—this is the beginning of nature, the real. Roaming in the beginning of nature, though one has a body, it is as if one has no body, so self-interest is minimal. Though one has a mind, it is as if one has no mind, so desires are few. Thus, perceiving the plain and embracing the unhewn is the essence of sageliness, wisdom, benevolence, and righteousness—why employ embellishment?

Laozi cuts these off there precisely to attach them here. Scholars who fail to grasp his intent, seeing only that he cuts and discards, rashly claim Laozi’s teachings are reckless and lawless—this is what is meant by “not attaining the words.”

**Cutting off learning brings no care.**
How far apart are *wei* and *a*?
How far apart are good and evil?
What others fear, one must also fear—
vast and boundless, it has no end!

The multitude bustle and rejoice,
as if feasting on the grand sacrifice,
as if ascending the terrace in spring.
I alone remain still, without sign,
like an infant who has not yet smiled,
adrift, as if with no place to return.
The multitude all have more than enough;
I alone seem lacking.
My mind is that of a fool—how dull!
The common folk are bright;
I alone am dim.
The common folk are sharp;
I alone am muddled.
Vast, like the boundless sea,
ceaseless, as if without rest.
The multitude all have purpose;
I alone am stubborn, like a rustic.
I alone differ from others,
yet I value feeding from the Mother.

The signs are like the cracks in a tortoise shell—subtle movements indeed. *Hai* means an infant’s smile; once it smiles, emotions stir and consciousness arises. To have a destination is to halt one’s carriage and not ride on—"adrift, as if with no place to return" means having no fixed abode.

Ma Jiuji said: "Nature admits no excess or lack; all surplus is beyond one’s portion." Feasting on the grand sacrifice or ascending the spring terrace—all such gains are beyond one’s portion, hence "the multitude all have more than enough." *Yi* means loss.

*Dun* is like the primordial chaos—unaware. Some texts write it as *chun*. "Small brightness" refers to *zhao* and *cha*, petty and meticulous. *Men* (pronounced *moben*) means dull, numb to pain. In ancient times, the capital was called *beautiful*, and the countryside *rustic*. *Shi* (pronounced *si*) means to feed; *Mother* refers to the nursing mother, as seen in the *Liji*’s "Nei Ze" chapter.

Su’s commentary: Cultivation through learning daily increases; cultivation of the Dao daily diminishes. Not knowing the proper course of nature and destiny, one seeks to augment through learning, piling up what one has not heard until it cannot be unified—thus the square harms the circle, the straight harms the crooked, and the mind becomes entangled, overwhelmed with cares. This is the calamity of scholars, hence "cutting off learning brings no care." As for the sage, though he has never ceased learning, he takes the Dao as his master—learning without deficiency, learning without disorder, vast and carefree. Why then cut off learning?

Scholars drown in what they have heard, unable to unify it, so their *wei* becomes arrogance and their *a* becomes sloth—these cannot be spoken of in the same breath. How much less can the opposition of good and evil be compared? Only the sage knows that the myriad things all emerge from nature yet are completed in delusion—like painting horses and oxen, like carving tigers and swine, none of which are real. Pitying this, he makes no distinctions of right and wrong, same and different. Who then knows how far apart they are?

If one knows this, then the myriad things grow together without harming one another, and the Dao flows in parallel without conflict—nothing is strange. The sage equalizes self and other, unifies same and different, leaving no residue in his mind. Yet how could he thereby neglect worldly laws, violate social roles, or disrupt order? What others fear, I too fear; what others do, I too do. Though I stand among rulers and ministers, fathers and sons, moving within the realms of ritual, music, punishment, and governance, the world does not perceive my difference. What keeps me untouched by things is only the mind.

People all follow what they know, rigidly confined to their paths. The sage encompasses both being and non-being, entering all without obstruction—vast and boundless, it has no end. Each person drowns in their preferences, their beauty like feasting on the grand sacrifice, their joy like ascending the spring terrace, clamoring to follow without knowing its falsity. Only the sage deeply investigates delusion, meeting it with stillness, unmoved like an infant who has not yet smiled. Riding the principle of the myriad things without selfishness, he seems as if with no place to return.

The multitude cling to what they know, each considering themselves abundant. The sage embraces the myriad things without favoring one, transcending as if lacking. *Dun dun*, like a fool yet not a fool. The world takes discrimination as knowledge; the sage knows the futility of distinguishing among delusions. Thus outwardly he seems dim, inwardly muddled, vast like the boundless sea, drifting without rest. People each have their abilities, so the world can employ them all. The sage’s talent is complete and virtue perfect, yet he seems to have nothing to offer—hence he is suspected of being stubborn and rustic.

The Dao is the Mother of the myriad things. The multitude pursue things and forget the Dao, but the sage sheds all things and takes the Dao as his foundation. Like an infant with no mixed diet, he feeds only from the Mother.

Lü’s commentary: Above, he cuts off the goodness of sagely wisdom, benevolence, and righteousness; below, he cuts off the evil of cunning and profit. Not burdening the mind with these is to cut off learning. Cutting off learning is non-action; non-action is spirit. The spirit is the drum of the myriad things, not sharing the sage’s cares, hence "cutting off learning brings no care."

*Wei* and *a* arise from the same sound—how far apart are they? Good and evil diverge from the one Dao—how far apart are they? This is why even sagely wisdom is cut off and discarded, not burdening the mind. Regret and remorse lie in petty distinctions; freedom from blame lies in vastness. Regret is what people fear, and one must fear it. Vast and boundless, it has no end—this is not only fearing what others fear.

Why? What others fear must be feared to be in accord with people. Vast and boundless, it has no end—to be in accord with Heaven.

The multitude, bustling and joyous, do not know to block their openings and shut their gates. Like feasting on grand sacrifices, they do not know the blandness of flavorlessness; like ascending a terrace in spring, they do not know the insufficiency of what meets the eye. I alone am cautious, as yet without sign, like an infant who has not yet learned to smile—blocking my openings, shutting my gates, finding no flavor to crave, no sight to delight in.

Adrift, adrift, as if with no place to return—this speaks to how the myriad things drift, yet in myself, I have no fixed abode. The multitude, like feasting on grand sacrifices or ascending a terrace in spring, thus all have surplus. I alone am cautious, as yet without sign, like an infant who has not yet learned to smile, thus alone seem lacking. All this speaks to relinquishing things and departing from form.

My heart is that of a fool—this speaks to its lack of knowledge. Pure, pure—this speaks to its lack of admixture. The vulgar are bright and clear; I alone seem dim. The vulgar are sharp and discerning; I alone seem dull. Vast and indistinct, like darkness—utterly without sight. Not merely dim and dull, but in motion, adrift, adrift, as if with no place to return; in stillness, silent, as if with no place to rest. The vulgar are bright and clear, the vulgar are sharp and discerning, thus all have means. I alone seem dim, I alone seem dull, thus seem crude and rustic. All this speaks to casting off knowledge and forgetting the heart.

For sight, hearing, thought, and reflection arise from the Dao. Thus, in relation to the Dao, they are children, while the Dao is their mother. The multitude pursue things, enslave their knowledge, and employ sight, hearing, thought, and reflection—nourishing only their children. But I relinquish and abandon these, for I value nourishing the mother. Thus it is said: "I alone differ from others and value feeding on the mother."

How spirit-like is Laozi! What need has he for nourishment, yet he differs from the multitude and the vulgar? He desires those who practice the Dao to know this, that they may thereby attain the Dao. Thus, the great purport of "cutting off learning" may be known. Yet earlier scholars, believing that one who does not learn is no better than a beast, have utterly failed to grasp the meaning of "cutting off learning brings no care."

**Xizhai’s Commentary:**
Learning that does not reach the point of "nothing to learn" is not "cutting off learning." The Dao takes "no attainment" as attainment; learning takes "no learning" as true learning. If the Dao could be attained, it would all be benevolence and righteousness; if learning could be learned, it would all be names and numbers. Thus, the sage takes "no attainment" as attainment of the Dao and "no learning" as true learning. Hence, "cutting off learning brings no care." If one has not yet reached "no learning," then the learning of the world is endless—grasping one and losing two, gaining here and failing there—necessarily taking gain as joy and loss as sorrow, without respite.

The root of the Dao is originally not different. If one does not grasp the root but seeks to equalize the branches, the branches will inevitably diverge greatly. Moreover, "reverence" and "flattery" arise from the same sound—how far apart are they? Facing principle is good; turning from it is evil—how far apart are they? The sage has observed their beginnings and knows their root is the same, thus turning flattery into reverence and evil into good in an instant. If one knows that reverence and flattery, good and evil, share the same root, then attaining "cutting off learning" is not difficult.

Yet when the sage emerges to respond to the world, how can he easily change what the world desires to follow his own desires, or align the world’s views with his own? What others delight in, he also delights in; what others fear, he also fears. But what differs from the multitude is this: the multitude’s joy is boundless, bustling and joyous, their delight like ascending a terrace in spring, their beauty like feasting on grand sacrifices. Bright and clear, they deem themselves wise; sharp and discerning, they deem none their equal. Their hearts truly believe in joy, in beauty, in brightness, in wisdom—blind to their own nature, knowing only what is tangible. Thus, their hearts see nothing beyond.

The sage’s heart roams in the Dao; his responses to things are not real. Thus, cautious, as yet without sign, like an infant who has not yet learned to smile; adrift, adrift, as if with no place to return; swift as the sea, drifting, as if with no place to rest. The multitude all have surplus; I alone seem lacking. The multitude all have means; I alone seem crude and rustic. Is this truly the heart of a fool? It is that I differ from the multitude—knowing the root, reaching the origin, not flowing into the branches. This is called "valuing feeding on the mother."

**Bisheng’s Notes:**
People’s learning is troubled by not attaining goodness. If I can cut off learning, what sorrow is there? Yet this is not forced cessation. It is knowing that human nature is originally without goodness. Those who practice goodness, though different from evil, are equally distant from nature—their slight difference is like that between reverence and flattery.

Though good and evil share the same root, the sage does not abandon goodness, for what people fear cannot but be feared—this is what is meant by "fortune and misfortune shared with the people." As for the heart’s return to the original nature, it is vast and boundless, without limit—how could it be like goodness, which has shores and bounds? Thus, people delight in goodness like feasting on grand sacrifices or ascending a terrace in spring, while I alone am tranquil, like an infant who has not yet learned to smile, without sign. Adrift, adrift, with no place to return, no anchorage.

People’s attainment of goodness all has surplus, while I alone seem lacking, like a fool, dull and without knowledge. People are bright and clear, all as if having means, while I alone seem dim and dull, vast and indistinct, like the sea, drifting without rest. Is this truly the sage’s crudeness and rusticity? It is that the multitude all pursue their children, while I alone value the mother—thus I cannot but differ from the multitude.

For nature is neither good nor evil, yet the myriad phenomena of good and evil all arise from it—thus it is called "feeding on the mother." Dong Sijing [remarks]: "Some say that *wei* and *a* both emerge from sound, good and evil both arise from action. The great observer sees their root as fundamentally not different—just like the lost sheep in the tale of Zang and Gu."
