This is indeed what the Most High means by "forgetting emotions," where right and wrong are both dissolved—only thus can one act. The learner must fix their gaze precisely where good and evil cannot be named; to simply equate good and evil would be to abandon the world to evil without regard—how could this be reasonable?

Chunfu’s annotation: The heavens, without the sun, cannot illuminate things; people, without learning, cannot attain the Dao. Thus it is said: "Learning is the white sun of the mind." Yet the sage declares, "Abandon learning, and there will be no worries"—how could this truly mean reckless neglect of the mind? What must be abandoned is worldly learning, while what must be valued is the learning of "feeding on the mother." What is the mother? Virtue is the mother of the myriad things, and the Dao is in turn the mother of virtue—thus what the sage calls "mother" encompasses both Dao and virtue. To "feed" is to savor for one’s own nourishment. Savoring Dao and virtue to nourish oneself is to act without acting yet accomplish all, and its joy is measureless. Why then trouble oneself with vulgar learning, only to invite worry? This is how the sage differs from others.

"The bearing of great virtue follows only the Dao.
As for the Dao—it is a thing elusive and evanescent.
Evanescent, elusive, yet within it are images;
Elusive, evanescent, yet within it are things.
Abstruse, obscure, yet within it is essence;
Its essence is most true, and within it is trustworthiness.
From ancient times to now, its name has never departed,
By which we witness the origins of the multitude.
How do I know the nature of the multitude’s origins?
By this."

When Dao circulates and becomes virtue, it takes on form. Thus, virtue is the manifestation of Dao. Extending this principle, the forms of all things are Dao’s manifestation in the phenomenal world. Dao transcends being and non-being, hence it is described as "elusive and evanescent." Yet when it moves to form images and coalesces into things, nothing fails to emerge from this elusiveness. At the undetermined stage between being and non-being, it remains evanescent and invisible; but at the intersection of being and non-being, its profound obscurity becomes apparent. Though wood takes shape, its essence abides within.

Once things assume form, truth and falsehood intermingle. While essence remains, falsehood cannot intrude. But when truth and falsehood mingle, one becomes two, two becomes three, and a tangled profusion arises—no longer to be trusted. Yet while essence endures, it does not deceive us.

Though ages differ, Dao never departs; thus it is named "the Unchanging." Precisely because it never departs, it can witness the transformations of all beings. All things, however beautiful, are subject to change. The sage’s knowledge of why things are as they are comes from embodying Dao without departure.

---

Virtue’s vast bearing is the fullest expression of Dao. When one abandons things, detaches from form, relinquishes knowledge, and forgets the mind—approaching the state of a rustic—what then remains to follow? Only Dao.

Dao as a thing is both elusive and evanescent. In its elusiveness and evanescence, it is neither obscure nor distinct—neither obscure nor distinct, and thus luminous. This luminosity gives the impression of something substantial. Yet within it lies image—an impression of substance that is not substance. Hence it is called "the image of no-thing" and "the great image without form."

In its evanescence and elusiveness, it is neither bright nor dim—neither bright nor dim, and thus obscure. This obscurity gives the impression of nothingness. Yet within it lies thing—an impression of nothingness that is indeed something. Hence it is called "the form of no-form" and "a thing uncarved, complete."

Elusive and evanescent, it is unfathomable—unfathomable, and thus numinous. Profoundly obscure, it is numinous beyond measure. Numinous beyond measure, it attains essence. Thus it is said: "Abstruse, obscure, yet within it is essence." Essence is the undiluted attainment of Dao.

Among all things under heaven, nothing is truer, more trustworthy, more constant, or more unchanging. The origin of all things lies herein, and thus we witness their beginnings. Therefore it is said: "Its essence is most true, and within it is trustworthiness. From ancient times to now, its name has never departed, by which we witness the origins of the multitude."

Dao is neither bright nor obscure; it preserves its elusiveness. Without seeing, without hearing, it enters profound obscurity. There is image—there is thing; there is thing—there is essence; there is essence—there is trustworthiness. When Dao attains trustworthiness, it aligns with the heart, and all things are attained.

To know how heaven and earth and the myriad things become what they are, one must begin here. Thus it is said: "How do I know the nature of the multitude’s origins? By this."

---

"Hole" signifies vastness. The vastness of virtue is commensurate with its bearing, yet it ultimately depends on Dao. Thus the text begins with Dao. I do not know its arising—Dao gives rise to it. I do not know its cessation—Dao brings it to rest.

Dao as a thing is elusive, evanescent, profoundly obscure, and not constant. Yet within its elusiveness lies image; within its evanescence lies thing; within its obscurity lies essence. Thus, within nothingness, nothing is not present.

What appears to be something within being is not true being, for being’s root is not substantial. What appears to be nothing within being is actually something. Conversely, what appears to be something within nothingness is not true nothingness, for nothingness’s root is not empty. What appears to be nothing within nothingness is actually something. Being’s something is but the coarse aspect of existence.

Only the something within nothingness is the true aspect of being. Precisely because it is true and not artificial, it neither persists through being nor perishes through non-being. This is called "trustworthiness." From ancient times to the present, it does not change or alter; its essence does not depart. The sage’s ability to observe the beginnings of all things and know their causes stems from embodying this ultimate nothingness, thereby witnessing the multitude of beings.

---

The bearing of vast virtue corresponds to the preceding chapter’s "still and formless" passage.

Only the Dao is to be followed—this means that the bearing of vast virtue and all appearances arise from the Dao. This is why the sage values "feeding from the mother."

Below, the text repeatedly depicts the marvel of the Dao’s essence: "elusive and evanescent," "dim and obscure," all conveying the meaning of profundity, subtlety, and formlessness. "Things" refer to forms, "true" to essence, and "trustworthy" to what is real. The variations in phrasing harmonize with poetic rhythm; to parse each line rigidly would be forced. "Experience" means traversing. "The multitude of beginnings" refers to all things under heaven and earth that issue from the Dao. Though the multitude of beginnings transform and flow, the Dao remains eternally unchanged—hence, "from ancient times to the present, its name does not depart." The phrase "experiencing the multitude of beginnings" refers to traversing, while the earlier "elusive and evanescent, dim and obscure" describes the Dao’s nature. How do we know that the multitude of beginnings are traversed by the Dao? Precisely because the Dao’s essence—elusive, evanescent, dim, and obscure—cannot be altered or destroyed. Thus, while the multitude of beginnings pass away, the Dao abides, like an inn that hosts transient guests.

Su Zizhan said: "That which passes is like this stream, yet it never truly departs; that which waxes and wanes is like the moon, yet in the end it neither increases nor diminishes." This aligns with the present meaning. A Chan master put it even more plainly: "There is a thing prior to heaven and earth, formless and fundamentally quiescent; it can be the master of all images yet does not decay with the four seasons."

*Brush Jottings* states: The Dao is without form; whatever can be described by form belongs to virtue. Yet by knowing the bearing of virtue, one may also come to recognize the Dao—as in the so-called "elusive and evanescent, dim and obscure." Those who study the Dao prefer action, and when they encounter the elusive, evanescent, dim, and obscure, they find it difficult to approach or settle into. They do not realize that the elusive and evanescent, though formless, *are* form; though without thing, *are* thing; the dim and obscure, though without essence, *are* essence. As the Buddhist scriptures say: "If one sees all forms as non-form, one sees the Tathāgata." The provisional is called false, the constant true. The elusive, evanescent, dim, and obscure neither persist through being nor perish through non-being—what could be more real and trustworthy than this? Hence, "from ancient times to the present, its name does not depart."

Those who are benighted claim that within the elusive, evanescent, dim, and obscure, there truly exists some thing. But the elusive, evanescent, dim, and obscure transcend boundaries—what could such a thing adhere to? Moreover, if it had a presence, it would also have a departure—how then could it endure through all time? If so, what does it mean to "experience the multitude of beginnings"? "Beginnings" means origins. People cling to the multitude of beings as real yet cannot fathom the subtle and wondrous between presence and absence because they have never experienced their origin. To experience the origin of the multitude of beings is to know that there was never a true beginning. Knowing that there was never a true beginning, one sees that all beings are wondrous, and their elusive, evanescent, dim, and obscure nature is one. Thus, one knows that the multitude of beings are true emptiness precisely because one has experienced and known them.

Buddhist teachings often guide people to awakening through contemplative methods, yet Laozi’s words are no different. To experience the origin of beings is to see the emptiness of the past; to observe their subtleties is to see the emptiness of the future. When the myriad things arise, observe their return—then one sees the emptiness of the present. When a single thought returns to the root, the three temporal realms are forever severed. Yet all this depends on the ability to observe. For those who truly seek to "know the constant," they must begin here.

】"To bend is to be whole,
To twist is to be straight,
To hollow is to be filled,
To wear out is to be renewed,
To have little is to gain,
To have much is to be confused."

Thus the sage embraces the One and becomes the model for all under heaven.
Not asserting oneself, one becomes illuminated;
Not self-righteous, one becomes distinguished;
Not boasting, one achieves merit;
Not self-aggrandizing, one endures.
It is precisely because one does not contend that no one under heaven can contend with them.

What the ancients called "to bend is to be whole"—how could this be empty words? Truly, wholeness is what they return to.

Su Zhe’s commentary:
When the sage acts, they necessarily follow the principle. Wherever the principle lies, whether straight or bent, the essential matter is to achieve harmony. Harmonious, they do not conflict with things; not conflicting, they remain whole.
If one is straight but not in accord with the principle, it is not true straightness. Following the principle, though bent, is the ultimate straightness under heaven.
What the multitude returns to is the lowly—though one may wish not to be filled, it cannot be avoided.
To be dazzlingly discerning is not the Dao;
To be dull and seemingly worn out is where daily renewal arises.
The Dao is simply the One. Attaining the One, there is nothing one does not attain. To learn much without unifying it with the One is to be confused.
To embrace the One is to return to one’s nature. For "to bend is to be whole, to twist is to be straight, to hollow is to be filled, to wear out is to be renewed, to have little is to gain"—all are the overflow of embracing the One.
Thus, it concludes with embracing the One.

The eye does not see itself, yet it can see things;
The mirror does not illuminate itself, yet it can illuminate things.
If one were to see or illuminate oneself, one would be too occupied with oneself to attend to other things.
"Not asserting oneself, not self-righteous, not boasting, not self-aggrandizing"—all are the overflow of not contending. Thus, it concludes with not contending.

The world takes the straight as right and the bent as wrong. If one were to follow the principle in the world, there would inevitably be times when one must bend. Therefore, at the end of the chapter, it reiterates: "How could this be empty words? Truly, wholeness is what they return to."

What is called "wholeness" is not merely preserving the self. Internally, it preserves the self; externally, it preserves things. Both self and things are preserved, and one returns to their nature—then their straightness is truly great.

Lü’s Commentary:
To know the origins of the myriad things is to be able to embrace the One and attain suppleness. Embracing the One and attaining suppleness, one can then bend, yield, hollow, and wear out.
Bending is the natural state of bending; yielding is bending made so by force. Among all things under heaven, only water aligns with the Dao. Flowing west or east, nothing can harm it—this is how bending preserves wholeness. Avoiding obstacles, it winds a thousand turns yet must flow east—this is how yielding becomes straight. Humbling itself, all valleys gather to it—this is how hollowing becomes fullness. Receiving the world’s filth, yet none can sully it—this is how wearing out becomes renewal. Only those who attain the One are sufficient for this. Thus, it is said: “Less is gained.” The reason the multitude cannot do this is their lack of oneness. Hence, it is said: “More is confusion.” In regulating finances, there must be a model; in transmitting earth and wood, there must be a model. What is held is simple, yet what is addressed is vast.
The sage embraces the One as the world’s model, and it is no different. Thus, one can bend, yield, hollow, and wear out—everywhere, nothing departs from the One. Therefore, by following what the world sees, one sees, yet does not see oneself. Thus, nothing seen is unexamined, and it is said: “Not seeing oneself, one is clear.” By following what the world deems right, one deems right, yet does not deem oneself right. Thus, nothing deemed right can be surpassed, and it is said: “Not deeming oneself right, one is distinguished.” By attributing achievement to the world, one does not claim it for oneself. Thus, merit belongs to the myriad things, and it is said: “Not boasting, one has merit.” By relying on the abilities of the myriad things, one does not pride oneself on superiority. Thus, one endures, and it is said: “Not self-aggrandizing, one endures.” In this way, there is nothing else—attaining the One, there is no self; without self, there is no contention. Only without contention can none under heaven contend with one.
The essence of the ancient saying “bending preserves wholeness” is this alone. Knowing how bending preserves wholeness, one knows how yielding becomes straight, hollowing becomes fullness, and wearing out becomes renewal. Not seeing oneself, one is clear; not deeming oneself right, one is distinguished; not boasting, one has merit; not self-aggrandizing, one endures—all arise from this. Is this not what is called “wholeness is what they return to”?

Xizhai’s Commentary:
Things cannot remain forever bent, thus bending preserves wholeness. Things cannot remain forever yielded, thus yielding becomes straight. Hollowing must lead to fullness; wearing out must lead to renewal. Less is easily attained; more is easily confused—this is the ultimate principle of fullness and emptiness.
The reason the sages of antiquity could be the ancestors of the myriad things is their embrace of the One. Embracing the One, they constantly align with the Dao. Thus, they do not see themselves but follow others’ sight; they do not deem themselves right but follow things’ rightness; they do not claim merit but follow others’ merit; they do not pride themselves on superiority but follow others’ superiority. Only by standing alone among things and not contending with them can none under heaven contend with them. The sage moves in accordance with principle, and one cannot find where they are not whole. Yet they can also attain bending to nurture it—thus their wholeness is perfect. This is called “truly, wholeness is what they return to.”

Hongfu’s Commentary:
Everyone desires wholeness, yet those who lose the way of preserving it often seek wholeness through wholeness and ultimately fail to attain it—this is the common folly of the world. They do not know that the five viscera, six bowels, four limbs, and hundred bones are utterly bent, yet people rely on them for wholeness. Without bending, wholeness cannot be achieved. Stretching a rope makes it straight, thus taking the rope as straight, yet not knowing its yielding. Without yielding, how could it be straight? The sea is hollow, yet always full; the sun and moon are old, yet always new. Drinking wine and eating meat in moderation brings satiety and comfort, but pursuing excess without end leads to vomiting and discomfort—this is confusion.
Knowing this, one knows the sage’s way of embracing the One. The One’s capacity for multiplicity is like the few’s capacity for attainment, the worn-out’s capacity for renewal, the hollow’s fullness, the yielded’s straightness, the bent’s wholeness—this is the principle of moving against the current. The sage’s model for the world lies herein; it is lamentable that people do not know it. From this perspective, those who study the Dao can turn back.
The eye is most clear, yet cannot see itself. If it were to see itself, it would not see clearly—how then could it be clear? Thus, not deeming oneself right, others must deem one right; not claiming merit, others must attribute merit; not priding oneself on superiority, others must regard one as superior; not contending, others must not contend with one—all are the way of bending and preserving wholeness. Alas! Who indeed can attain wholeness and return to it?

The *Brush Jottings* states:
To bend is to attain wholeness, to yield is to become straight, the hollow is filled, the worn-out is renewed—all illustrate how the few may obtain. The One is the extremity of the few. Embracing the One, the world takes it as a model, and thus its gains are many. Yet if one state has three rulers, none knows whom to follow; if ten sheep have nine herders, how can they find fodder? Those who lose their lives do so by pursuing many paths; those who lose their sheep suffer from too many forks in the road.

Rare words arise naturally.
A whirlwind does not last the morning;
a sudden storm does not last the day.
What brings these about? Heaven and Earth.
Even Heaven and Earth cannot endure them—
how much less can man?
Thus, those who engage with the Dao:
those who align with the Dao become one with the Dao,
those who align with virtue become one with virtue,
those who align with loss become one with loss.
Those who become one with the Dao—the Dao delights in obtaining them;
those who become one with virtue—virtue delights in obtaining them;
those who become one with loss—loss delights in obtaining them.
Where trust is insufficient, there will be distrust.

*(Annotations: "Whirlwind" denotes swift wind; "sudden storm" denotes violent rain. From dawn to dusk is "lasting the morning"; from early to late is "lasting the day." Wind and rain shake and surge, fleeting and impermanent—they cannot endure. As the *Yellow Emperor* said: "Excess brings harm; response brings control." "Delights" is read in the entering tone.)*

Su’s commentary:
Words that arise from the natural are simple and hit the mark; those not arising from the natural, when forced, become verbose and hard to trust. Thus it is said: "The Dao in speech is bland, without flavor—seen, it is not enough to be visible; heard, it is not enough to be audible; used, it cannot be exhausted." This is what is meant by "rare words."
Yin and yang do not contend; wind and rain arrive in their time, neither too swift nor too slow, exhausting their momentum before ceasing. But when yang is excessive above and yin is suppressed below, blocked and unable to release, there arise whirlwinds and sudden storms, as if unable to endure—yet their force cannot last the day. The sages of old spoke sparingly and acted plainly, all following the natural, and thus endured without exhaustion. Yet the world may grow weary of them, deeming them less pleasing than clever rhetoric or shocking conduct, unaware that such things cannot last. Confucius said: "If one is truly devoted to benevolence..."

Those who harbor no malice—this is what is meant by "the faults of the benevolent are easily forgiven." If one devoted to benevolence is thus, how much more so for one devoted to the Dao? If one truly engages in the practice of the Dao, then what one does in accord with the Dao attains the Dao; what one does in accord with virtue attains virtue. Even if one errs in misfortune, though the error lies in action, there must still be attainment in Dao and virtue. Those who do not understand the Dao, though they profess faith in it, do so without conviction; when they encounter failure, they grow doubtful, and thus their disbelief only deepens. Only those who understand the Dao do not allow success or failure to sow doubt in it.

**Lü’s Commentary:**
What is heard yet inaudible is called "rare words." To know the source of such speech is to speak from wordless speech, and thus what is heard becomes inaudible. Therefore, "rare words" are words spoken through the Dao. Hence it is said: "naturally." Whirlwinds and sudden storms arise from violence and disorder, not from the constant and regular. Thus, though heaven and earth produce them, they cannot last even a morning or a day. If human speech does not arise from the natural, it will be excessive and quickly exhausted—no wonder. Only those who engage in the practice of the Dao can achieve selflessness. Selflessness is the Dao, is virtue, is even loss.

I see no difference in them—thus those who align with the Dao are one with the Dao, those who align with virtue are one with virtue, and those who align with loss are one with loss. The strange, the cunning, the bizarre, and the wondrous all become one. Only when one perceives no difference and unites with them does their strangeness, though present, never depart from me.

Thus it is said: those who are one with the Dao, the Dao embraces; those who are one with virtue, virtue embraces; those who are one with loss, loss embraces. Only when trust is insufficient does one distinguish between Dao, virtue, and loss.

**Bi Sheng’s Commentary:**
The Dao regards spontaneity as supreme, yet the world rarely speaks of it, preferring contrivance. Where there is contrivance, there must be cessation—how could it endure? Even whirlwinds and sudden storms cannot last long; this is evident. Those who engage in the Dao are not so. To engage in the Dao is to be spontaneous. Being spontaneous, there is fundamentally nothing to gain, nor anything to lose. Without gain or loss, one follows the world’s gains and losses—thus whether it becomes virtue or loss, one trusts what arises without reservation. There is no difference, and thus no lack of joy—this is the principle.

When there is no difference, even seeking trust becomes impossible, let alone distrust. If one departs from the Dao to pursue virtue, one cannot be one with loss. If one departs from virtue to pursue loss, one cannot be one with virtue. Unable to be one with virtue or loss, how can one hope to be one with the Dao? This is like seeking ears on grain—precisely what is meant by "trust is insufficient, and thus there is distrust."

All are like the fleeting whirlwind and sudden storm. Some may ask: *"The Śūraṅgama Sūtra speaks of neither causation nor spontaneity, yet the Laozi takes spontaneity as its core—is there a difference?"* I say: there is no difference.

What is despised in spontaneity is that it has a source from which it arises and a reason for which it is so. If it has a source from which it arises and a reason for which it is so, then it is not spontaneity—it exists above things and emerges below the non-thing, which is what the Śākya school condemns. Yet the Old Master clarifies spontaneity, saying: *"Nameless, it is the beginning of Heaven and Earth."* To know the nameless is to know its spontaneity. There is no "self" in its spontaneity, no "self" in its "thusness." Without a "self" in its "thusness," how can causation or conditions confine it? Thus it is said: *"Pure awareness, wondrous and luminous, is neither cause nor condition, neither spontaneous nor non-spontaneous—detached from all appearances, it is all dharmas."* This is what is meant by *"the constant Dao that cannot be spoken."*

One who stands on tiptoe cannot stand firm; one who strides cannot walk. One who displays himself is not enlightened; one who asserts himself is not distinguished; one who boasts achieves no merit; one who is self-conceited does not endure. In the Dao, these are called *"leftover food and parasitic growths"*—things that others despise. Therefore, one who embodies the Dao does not abide in them.

*(Annotations in the original: "跂 is interchangeable with 企, meaning to stand on tiptoe. 薛云: lifting the heels is called 跂; stretching the feet is called 跨. To stand, one seeks greater height, yet this harms one’s stability; to walk, one seeks greater stride, yet this harms one’s progress. 赘 is an excrescence; 行 here should be read as 形, as the characters were interchangeable in ancient script. Leftover food is despised by others; parasitic growths are deemed unsightly. As the Zuozhuan says: *'People will not eat my leftovers.'* Zhuangzi speaks of *'hanging growths and attached tumors—emerging from form yet exceeding nature.'* 恶 is read in the falling tone; 处 in the rising tone.)*

Su Dongpo’s commentary:
*"No one is incapable of standing or walking. Yet if one deems standing insufficient and adds tiptoeing, or deems walking insufficient and adds striding, one will inevitably lose one’s ability to stand or walk. Those who display themselves, assert themselves, boast, or are self-conceited are no different. It is like eating: when one is just full, that is enough; excess leads to illness. Or like the body: when it is whole, that is enough; growths become burdens."*

Lü Huiqing’s commentary:
*"Standing on tiptoe is not the natural way of standing; striding is not the natural way of walking. Thus, they cannot endure. Though one may stand, one does not truly stand; though one may walk, one does not truly walk."*

The Dao is fundamentally without self; without self, there is no contention. Thus, those who display themselves, assert themselves, or are self-conceited do not abide in the constant Way. Therefore, their so-called "Dao" is but surplus food and superfluous conduct—what the common people all crave. Yet if I, too, were to take it up, it would be surplus food indeed. Nature originally has no such thing, and to indulge in it is superfluous conduct. Surplus food and superfluous conduct—some things detest them, so the Dao should not reside in them. The Dao resides where the multitude detests, yet it is said, "Some things detest it," so why does the man of Dao not reside there? It is because humility, emptiness, softness, and weakness are what the multitude detests and shuns. Thus, the man of Dao resides in them. Those who are self-displaying and contentious are what the multitude detests and competes with, so he does not reside in them. Whether he resides or not, his non-contention remains the same.

*Xizhai’s commentary:*
Stone stands without feet; wind moves without feet—this is because the one who stands does not know he stands, and the one who walks does not know he walks. Feet not touching the ground is called *qi*; feet overstepping in walking is called *kua*. Standing on tiptoe cannot last; striding cannot endure. The ancients who studied the Dao preserved their heavenly nature, and when they encountered things, their responses arose without deliberation—like the hands, feet, ears, and eyes, which inwardly respond to the heart without being commanded. If one were to make the hands, feet, ears, and eyes think before responding, then in every action, one would not know what to do. Thus, those who display themselves are not enlightened; those who assert themselves are not illustrious; those who boast achieve no merit; those who are self-conceited do not endure—all because they have a self-centered mind. To study the Dao while having a self-centered mind is to be like surplus food and superfluous conduct. Eating is for satiety; walking is for purpose. Once full, the excess of fine meats and grains before one only makes one fear they cannot be taken away. When walking does not serve its purpose, even the integrity of Zhongzi or the fidelity of Weisheng become detestable. Thus, the surplus of food and the superfluity of conduct—these two are what some things detest, yet the man of Dao always follows what is natural. Therefore, his eating is not excessive, and his conduct is not superfluous.

There is a thing, formless yet complete,
Born before heaven and earth.
Silent and void,
It stands alone and does not change,
Goes round and does not weary.
It may be called the Mother of all things.
I do not know its name;
I style it "Dao."
Forced to give it a name, I call it "Great."
Great, it passes on;
Passing on, it becomes remote;
Remote, it returns.
Thus, the Dao is great, heaven is great, earth is great, and the king is also great.
Within the realm, there are four greats, and the king occupies one of them.
Man models himself on earth;
Earth models itself on heaven;
Heaven models itself on the Dao;
The Dao models itself on nature.

*Annotations:*
- *Hun* (混): blended, undifferentiated.
- *Xian* (先): before, prior.
- *Xi* (悉): entirely.
- *Jian* (荐): to offer (here, *fan* 反 as variant).
- *Qiang* (强): forced (falling tone).
- Jiefu (介甫) says: *Ji* (寂) means stillness; *liao* (寥) means distant.
- Kumarajiva says: The wondrous principle is ever-present, hence "there is a thing." The myriad ways cannot divide it, hence "formless yet complete."
- Zhong Fang says: Vast and without peer is called "stands alone." Unchanging through the ages is called "does not change." Present everywhere is called "goes round." Ever-pervading is called "does not weary."

*Su’s commentary:*
The Dao is neither clear nor turbid, neither high nor low, neither coming nor going, neither good nor evil—it is blended into a single body, and in man, it is called nature. Thus, it is said, "There is a thing, formless yet complete." No one knows its origin, for it abides in stillness and constancy, and heaven and earth are born within it. Silent and without sound, void and without form, it stands alone without peer and never changes. It moves through all things and never wearies. Bending down, it nurtures and fosters the myriad things, and thus may be called their Mother. The Dao is originally nameless, but the sage, seeing that nothing does not proceed from it, names it "Dao." Seeing that nothing surpasses it, he forces a name upon it and calls it "Great." Yet in truth, it cannot be named. Seeking it from the great, it passes on and departs. Seeking it from departure, it becomes remote and unattainable. Though it passes on and becomes remote, returning to seek it, one finds it in the heart alone. Speaking of the Dao, even heaven, earth, and the king are not truly great. Yet the world is accustomed to knowing the greatness of these three and does not believe in the greatness of the Dao. Therefore, it is stated plainly: man does not surpass earth, earth does not surpass heaven, heaven does not surpass the Dao, and the Dao does not surpass nature. Yet if man were to recover his nature for a single day, these three would all be fully realized within him.

*Lü’s commentary:*
There is *qi*, there is form, there is substance—and thus the positions of heaven, earth, and man can be heard, seen, and grasped.

The faculties of ear, eye, and heart distinguish things, yet they have never perceived *qi*, form, or substance. *Qi*, form, and substance are undifferentiated and inseparable—seen yet unseen, heard yet unheard, grasped yet unobtainable. Thus, their form cannot be perceived. Therefore, I know not its name and designate it by its meaning, calling it the *Dao*. I know not its name, yet with the heart I comprehend it; I call it the *Dao* because its meaning can be spoken.

As a thing, the *Dao*: when employed, it fills the six directions; when abandoned, none know its whereabouts. How could "greatness" suffice to name it? Yet we force a name upon it—calling it "great." Greatness pervades all without ceasing, not confined to this body alone. Thus, "great" is called "passing on." Passing on, it becomes remote and uncontainable; thus, "passing on" is called "remote." Remote and uncontainable, I seek its bounds yet cannot find them. Yet returning to its root, it has never departed from this body. Thus, "remote" is called "return."

The *Dao* is great, heaven is great, earth is great, and the king is also great—these are the "Four Greats" within the realm. The king is one who restores destiny and knows constancy; being accommodating, he becomes impartial; being impartial, he becomes kingly. Thus, within the realm there are Four Greats, and the king occupies one of them. The king is the fulfillment of the human *Dao*. Man, possessing form, unites with the formless; so too does earth. Where earth reaches, man reaches as well. Thus, it is said: "Man models himself on earth." Where heaven reaches, earth reaches as well. Thus, it is said: "Earth models itself on heaven." Where the *Dao* reaches, heaven reaches as well. Thus, it is said: "Heaven models itself on the *Dao*." The *Dao* is self-originating and self-rooted; before heaven and earth existed, it was already firmly established from ancient times. That which takes "no-method" as its method—this is spontaneity alone. Thus, it is said: "The *Dao* models itself on spontaneity."

*Xizhai’s commentary:*
To call it "a thing" is unnameable; to call it "undifferentiated" is unmodifiable. Born before heaven and earth, it shows no beginning; showing no beginning, its end is unknown. Without beginning or end, it stands alone yet unchanging. Though it stands alone, it is never truly alone—it pervades the ten thousand things, entering all without peril or exhaustion. The things of the world are born through it without cease; thus, it may be called the Mother of the World. Yet to call it "standing alone" is not standing alone; to call it "pervading all" is not pervading all; to call it the "Mother of the World" is not the Mother of the World. I know not its name, so I call it the *Dao*, forcing the name "great" upon it. Yet its greatness is never seen as great, for it is ever-transforming. Thus, it is called "passing on." To call it "passing on" is not passing on, for it never departs and pervades all without exception. Thus, it is called "remote." To call it "remote" is not remote, for in a single thought it contains all. Thus, it is called "return." From its greatness it can pass on; from its remoteness it can return. Therefore, it is neither great nor minute, neither remote nor near—all names fail to capture it. This is called the *Dao*.

When the *Dao* descends to generate things, heaven and earth divide, and the Four Greats within the realm emerge. By worldly standards, the king’s greatness does not match that of heaven and earth, and heaven and earth’s greatness does not match that of the *Dao*. Yet here it is said the Four Greats include the king as one—thus equating the king with the *Dao*, as if it were effortless. If even the emotions of a single person—joy, anger, sorrow, and delight—can align with heaven and earth and govern the ten thousand things, how much more so for a king? If one can turn and model oneself: man on earth, earth on heaven, heaven on the *Dao*—then how could it be difficult for the king to match the *Dao*?

*Li Yue’s commentary:*
"The *Dao* is great, heaven is great, earth is great, and the king is also great"—these are the Four Greats within the realm. The king models himself on earth, heaven, and the *Dao*, governing the world through spontaneity. The world finds peace through this, hence it is called "modeling." When speaking of "man," it refers to the king; its meaning is to model earth (earth’s impartial bearing), model heaven (heaven’s impartial covering), and model the *Dao* (the *Dao*’s impartial generation and completion)—like the examples of ruler as ruler, minister as minister, father as father, son as son.

Later scholars have mistakenly transmitted that "man models earth, earth models heaven, heaven models the *Dao*, the *Dao* models spontaneity," making the Four Greats into Five. How could the king only model earth and not model heaven or the *Dao*? Can heaven and earth, being without intent, also model one another? Moreover, if earth models heaven, heaven models the *Dao*, and the *Dao* models spontaneity, then the *Dao* becomes the father of heaven and earth, and spontaneity its child—fragmented, disjointed, and far from coherent principle.

Stillness is the root of motion; tranquility is the lord of restlessness. Therefore, the sage travels all day without departing from the heavy supply wagon. Though possessing splendid vistas and serene dwellings, he remains detached. How could the lord of ten thousand chariots treat his person lightly before the world? Lightness loses the root; restlessness loses the lord.

Su's Annotation:
All things light cannot bear weight; small cannot restrain great; the unmovable makes the movable move; the unmoving governs the moving. Thus, light takes heaviness as its root, and restlessness takes stillness as its lord. In travel, one may desire lightness but must not depart from the heavy supply wagon. Though there be splendid vistas and serene dwellings, one must have a place of tranquil repose. The importance of heaviness and stillness cannot be lost in this way. If the ruler of the world treats his person lightly while bearing its weight, he is insufficient to bear the world. Lightness and restlessness are never admissible. If the lord is light, his ministers know he is not to be relied upon; if the lord is restless, his ministers know his mind is set on profit. Thus it is said: "Lightness loses the ministers; restlessness loses the lord."

Lü's Annotation:
The light responds first, the heavy responds later. The response arises from what is felt, so heaviness is the root of lightness. Stillness employs things; restlessness is employed by things. Restlessness is always employed by stillness, so stillness is the lord of restlessness. Therefore, though the noble person’s conduct throughout the day may be carried out with minimal provisions, he still does not depart from the heavy supply wagon—lightness cannot exist without heaviness. Though there be splendid vistas sufficient for contentment, he must have a place of supernal repose—restlessness cannot exist without stillness. If even daily conduct and splendid vistas are thus, how much more so for the lord of ten thousand chariots, who bears heavy responsibility and travels a long path in overseeing the world? Should he not be still and heavy? Only when compelled does he move; only when stirred does he respond; only when unavoidable does he rise—this is heaviness. Without action, he is still. If his movements are always at the point of being avoidable and he cannot be without action, then he treats his person lightly before the world and does not hold it in weight. Without weight, he is restless and not still. Thus it is said: "Above, there is no action; below, there is also no action." If below is in accord with above in virtue, then the ministers do not act as ministers. If below acts, then above also acts. If above is in accord with below in the Way, then the lord does not act as lord. For if the lord is light, he takes on the labor of his ministers and replaces them, while the ministers, acting without action, are in accord with the lord in the Way—thus they do not act as ministers. If they do not act as ministers, this is to lose the ministers. If the lord is restless, he forgets the dignity of his position and is used by the world. Then the lord also acts and is in accord with below in the Way—thus he does not act as lord. If he does not act as lord, this is to lose the lord. Thus it is said: "Lightness loses the ministers; restlessness loses the lord."

Xi Zhai's Annotation:
Lightness must return to heaviness; restlessness must return to stillness. Thus, heaviness is the root of lightness, and stillness is the lord of restlessness. The sage travels all day without departing from the heavy supply wagon, reaching everywhere without departing from his root. Though there be splendid vistas and tranquil repose, he remains supernally detached, acting without action. How can the lord of ten thousand chariots fail to measure his own weight and merely drive his person through the world’s trifles? If he entangles himself in trifles, a single thing is enough to employ him—how then can he govern the world? Though both lightness and restlessness are sufficient to cause harm, the harm of restlessness is more severe than that of lightness. For lightness employs the mind shallowly, while restlessness employs it deeply. The loss of lightness is limited to what is treated lightly, but the loss of restlessness disturbs the lord within, losing the wholeness of stillness. Thus, "lightness loses the ministers; restlessness loses the lord."

Hongfu's Annotation:
With the heavy supply wagon, though one travels all day, one is not light—why? Because heaviness is its root. With constant tranquil repose, though there be splendid vistas, one is not restless—why? Because stillness is its lord. Thus, lightness loses the root of heaviness; restlessness loses the lord of stillness.

In good conduct, there are no wheel tracks;
In good speech, there are no flaws;
In good planning, no counting rods are used;
In good closure, no bolts are needed yet it cannot be opened;
In good binding, no cords are used yet it cannot be undone.
Thus, the sage is always good at saving people, so there are no outcasts;
Always good at saving things, so there is nothing discarded.
This is called inheriting illumination.
Therefore, the good person is the teacher of the not-good;
The not-good person is the resource of the good.
Not to value one’s teacher,
Not to love one’s resource—
Though knowing, this is great delusion.
This is called the essential subtlety.

Su's Annotation:
Moving in accord with principle, there are no tracks. Speaking at the proper time, one’s words fill the world without fault. The numbers of the myriad things are all arrayed before one—knowing without calculation, what need is there for counting rods? The person of complete virtue, in relation to the myriad things, is like a mother to her child: though she lets them go, they do not depart. Thus, without bolts, he can close; without cords, he can bind. Those who rely on counting rods to plan, set bolts to close, and use cords to bind—their strength reaches only so far.

The sage, in relation to people, not only accommodates them but also skillfully saves them. If I do not cast people aside, how can they not return to me? To save people in times of danger and difficulty is not the greatest form of saving. When they are flowing and turning in the cycle of life and death, obscured by things, and I extend my utmost illumination to them, so that the dark all become bright—like a lamp passed on, transmitted without end—this is called skillfully saving people.

The sage has no mind to teach, so he does not love his resource; the world has no mind to learn, so it does not value its teacher. The sage not only forgets himself but also enables the world to forget him. This is the subtlety of the sage, which even the wise may not fully comprehend. Thus it is called the essential subtlety.

Lü's Annotation:
Where there are chariots, there are wheel tracks; where there are pedestrians, there are footprints—thus conduct cannot be without tracks. The knowing do not speak; the speaking do not know—thus speech cannot be without flaws. Knowing the source of conduct, conduct arises from non-conduct—thus, in good conduct, there are no wheel tracks. Knowing the source of speech, speech arises from non-speech—thus, in good speech, there are no flaws. One and speech become two; two and one become three. From this point onward, even the most skillful reckoner cannot calculate. Only one who attains the One and forgets speech can reach the numbers. Reaching the numbers, his plans cannot be exhausted. Thus it is said: "In good planning, no counting rods are used."

The gate of heaven has no opening or closing—it is within me. If I do not open it, who can open it? Thus it is said: "In good closure, no bolts are needed yet it cannot be opened." The world has constant principles. Binding does not rely on ropes and cords; it knots according to these constant principles. Thus it is said: "In good binding, no cords are used yet it cannot be undone." Therefore, if conduct does not proceed from this, it cannot be without wheel tracks; if speech does not proceed from this, it cannot be without flaws. This is the calculation.

Closing and binding—if not proceeding from this, then even with counting rods there is chaos; even with bolts there is opening; even with cords there is unraveling. What resides within oneself cannot be without decay—how then could one save others? The sage alone embodies the Dao to master these five arts, and thus is ever adept at saving people without abandoning anyone, ever adept at saving things without abandoning anything.

Why? These five arts share the same principle of inherent nature and destiny. Only the sage, with the clarity of one who knows the constant, saves others at the very point of their shared nature.

In walking, speaking, planning, closing, and binding—none know their underlying principle. Thus their clarity is concealed and cannot be perceived. This is called "concealed clarity." Only through such saving is it so.

Therefore, the good person is the teacher of the not-good; the not-good person is the resource of the good. How could the wise abandon those who are not good? Yet though one aspires to the Dao, the mind is forgotten. Though the good person may be the teacher of the not-good, I do not know that such teaching is to be prized. Though the not-good person may be the resource of the good, I do not know that such a resource is to be cherished. If one does not prize the teacher nor cherish the resource, then even the wise will have realms they cannot know and will wander far astray.

Only by seeking with wisdom and not attaining does one grasp the subtlety of this Dao. Thus it is said: "Even the wise are greatly deluded—this is called subtlety."

**Dong’s Commentary:**
Wheels rolling on earth leave tracks—how could one united with the Dao leave traces? Faults arise from deviation. Only by harmonizing with heaven’s natural articulation is there no error of speech. The Dao is one—it encompasses the myriad existences. This is called "good planning has no gates, no chambers, yet extends in all four directions." Even the vastness of heaven and earth cannot lie beyond it—this is called "good closure." The wondrous root is profoundly still, neither bound by dharmas nor seeking liberation from them. It is open and luminous, utterly without gaps—what cannot be departed from for even a moment.

If one is mired in deliberate effort, esteems conduct, clings to words, or stagnates in phrases, employing the mind to calculate and measure in pursuit of the Dao yet failing to reach the gateway of expedient means, then one is instead bound by doctrinal appearances and cannot thoroughly perceive the root of all dharmas. This is what is meant by "good binding"—it is simply non-action.

Saving people from peril and hardship merely rescues their form, and even then may not succeed—how could this be truly good? Only when others are attached to contrivance and deluded about their nature, lost in darkness, does one embrace them with all-embracing capacity, awakening them with prior awakening so that their own celestial light naturally emerges. Like the transmission of a bright lamp, it is inexhaustible. What one gives to others only increases, and thus its clarity knows no limit. Thus it is said: "Ever adept"—meaning there is no time when this is not so.

The *Xizhai Annotation* states:

In action, there are no traces without footprints; in speech, there are no flaws without utterance; in planning, there are no schemes without thought; in closure, there are no barriers without bolts; in binding, there are no knots without restraint. Only those who attain the Dao act without relying on their feet—thus leaving no wheel tracks. They speak without relying on their mouths—thus incurring no faults or censure. They plan without relying on their minds—thus making no calculations. They close without relying on bolts—thus remaining unopenable. They bind without relying on restraint—thus remaining insoluble. This is because they take the ever-constant as their essence and master its wondrous application. Thus, they are capable of perfect goodness in all things.

The Dao of the ever-constant is possessed by every person and attained by every thing. Since it is called "constant," it does not increase with people nor diminish with things. Though both people and things possess this constancy, they lose its true essence. Therefore, the sage always employs true constancy to save them. By using true constancy to save people, no person is abandoned; by using true constancy to save things, no thing is abandoned. Yet how could the sage alone accomplish this? It is because both people and things inherently possess this clarity, and the sage merely reveals their original luminosity. Thus, it is said: "This is called inheriting clarity."

When inheriting clarity, all are equal in oneness. Since people are neither inherently good nor inherently not good, the good person becomes the teacher of the not-good—meaning the not-good can become good. The not-good person becomes the resource of the good—meaning the not-good originally share the same nature as the good.

If one does not honor their teacher nor follow their goodness, does not cherish their resource yet willingly embraces evil, then even with great wisdom, they only deepen their delusion—turning away from the root and returning to the origin is called the essential subtlety.

Cheng Ju’s Discourse:
Good conduct, good words, good plans, good closures, good knots—all are constant goodness. Only constant goodness can save people without abandoning anyone, save things without abandoning anything. Could deliberate goodness achieve this? Only by having no accumulation can one give to others while growing oneself, give to others while increasing oneself. Can offerings bound by form achieve this? Extending this Dao to aid the world and ferry all beings—what distinction then remains between Confucianism, Buddhism, and Daoism?

Hongfu’s Annotation:
To claim possession of a method to save others is to abandon them. The sage, having no "saving," thus saves well. Therefore: without barriers, one excels in closure; without constraints, one excels in knotting; without defenses, one excels in planning; without deceit, one excels in speech; without trace, one excels in conduct—all can be known.

Know the masculine, yet guard the feminine, becoming the world’s valley-stream.
As the world’s valley-stream, constant virtue does not depart, returning to the state of an infant.
Know the white, yet guard the black, becoming the world’s model.
As the world’s model, constant virtue does not err, returning to the boundless.
Know glory, yet guard disgrace, becoming the world’s valley.
As the world’s valley, constant virtue is fulfilled, returning to simplicity.
When simplicity scatters, it becomes vessels; the sage employs it to become a leader.
Thus, great regulation is not division.

[Annotations: *守* means to preserve; *复* means to return; *溪* and *谷* are where all waters gather, signifying the world’s utmost receptivity; *式* means model; *忒* means deviation; *足* means fulfillment; *长* is read in the rising tone; *制* means to regulate; *割* means to divide.]

Lü’s Annotation:
The masculine moves while the feminine is still; the masculine is firm while the feminine is yielding; the masculine initiates while the feminine harmonizes. To know the masculine yet guard the feminine is to be profoundly still, utterly yielding, and non-initiating. Thus, one becomes the world’s valley-stream—a thing that receives from the valley and channels to rivers and seas, accepting without refusal, discharging without accumulation, capable of unimpeded flow. Such capacity ensures constant virtue does not depart.

At birth, humans possess complete constant virtue, unobstructed by things. Yet when swayed by external things, they daily drift further from it. Only by being profoundly still, utterly yielding, and non-initiating can constant virtue remain undeparted, returning to the state of an infant.

White, in color, is receptive; in things, it is brightness; in conduct, it is metal; in numbers, it is four. Black, in color, is untainted; in things, it is obscurity; in conduct, it is water; in numbers, it is one. To know the white yet guard the black is to remain unstained by the myriad things, as if obscure, as if water. Ultimately, this is to embrace the One. Embracing the One, one can be flexible, yielding, hollow, and lowly—thus becoming the world’s model. As the world’s model, there is nowhere the One is not present, so constant virtue does not err.

"Not departing" means not leaving one’s original state, yet this does not necessarily prevent error. "Not erring" means no deviation.

The infant, as a thing, concentrates its qi to achieve suppleness, preserving only the harmony of its singular body—yet to return to the limitless is beyond even the infant’s state.

The flourishing of plants is their glory; their withering, their disgrace. What humans call glory and disgrace is no different. Knowing glory yet guarding disgrace, shedding ornament to return to the root—even if burdened with what the world most reviles, one cannot be encumbered. Thus, one becomes the world’s valley. The valley, as a thing, is hollow yet able to fill, responsive yet not hoarding—whence the source of rivers and seas arises. To be the world’s valley is to return to one’s source.

Thus, constant virtue is not merely unerring but returns to the uncarved block. The uncarved block is the wholeness of the real, the undifferentiated state of things. Only in its undifferentiated state, not yet shaped into vessels, can it be great or small, bent or straight, short or long, round or square—nothing it cannot become. Even the limitless is insufficient to describe it.

Yet to guard the feminine, guard the black, guard disgrace—this is enough. Why seek to know the masculine, the white, or glory? For guarding is the mother, knowing is the child; guarding is the constant, knowing is the variable.

When the uncarved block scatters, it becomes vessels. Vessels, as things, can be great but not small, bent but not straight, short but not long, round but not square. Thus, the sage employs them as officials and leaders—this is not tolerance but impartiality, impartiality the Dao of kingship.

As for embracing the uncarved block to govern the world, one regards the world’s principles as a butcher regards an ox—never seeing the whole ox. Acting where there is no affair, how vast is the space for the blade’s play!

To what end would one carve it? Thus it is said: *"Great governance requires no carving."*

**Xizhai’s Annotation:**
The masculine moves and acts; the feminine remains still and abides. Movement must return to stillness; the masculine must return to the feminine. Thus it becomes the valley of the world. The white seeks knowledge; the black seeks non-knowledge. To possess knowledge yet value non-knowledge, to know the white yet guard the black—this is wisdom. Thus it becomes the model for the world. Glory is what I impose upon others; disgrace is what others impose upon me. If I impose upon others and they can bear it, the benefit lies with them. If others impose upon me and I can bear it, the benefit lies with me. Thus it becomes the valley of the world. Yet how could the constant Dao truly possess what we call masculine and feminine, white and black, glory and disgrace?

To know and to guard are not the constant virtue. When the uncarved block scatters into virtue, and one conducts oneself by this virtue—if one employs the masculine, the white, or glory, one loses the constant virtue. But if one employs the feminine, the constant virtue does not depart, and one returns to the state of an infant. If one employs the black, the constant virtue remains unerring, and one returns to the boundless. If one employs disgrace, the constant virtue becomes complete, and one returns to the uncarved block. What we call the infant, the boundless, and the uncarved block—these are the true and constant.

Thus, the true and constant cannot be known or guarded. Only by making what can be known and guarded return to the constant can one attain the Dao. When the uncarved block scatters into vessels, the sage governs these vessels by the Dao, never losing the Dao. Therefore, employing them as officials and leaders means regarding the world as officials and leaders do—not treating it as a private household. Thus, to govern without partiality, to lead without domination—this is called *"great governance requires no carving."*

**Chunfu’s Annotation:**
This speaks of the sage’s way of wielding the insubstantial to govern the substantial, the subtle and wondrous Dao of the mysterious. "Knowing" refers to the knowledge of the child; it carries the meaning of application. "Guarding" refers to the guarding of the mother; it carries the meaning of mastery. Masculine and feminine are spoken of in terms of hardness and softness; black and white in terms of brightness and obscurity; glory and disgrace in terms of nobility and baseness. The valley lies below, where all streams converge—this is the meaning of "model," the sense of returning to it. "Constant virtue" is the truly constant virtue. The infant speaks of harmony; the boundless speaks of emptiness; the uncarved block speaks of substance.

All these refer to constant virtue. This chapter varies its phrasing and harmonizes its rhymes, repeatedly chanted and intoned—it thus aligns with poetic form. Having said "guard the feminine to become the valley," it further states "constant virtue does not depart," reiterating that there is no additional effort of non-departure *after* becoming the valley. Having said "constant virtue does not depart," it further states "return to the infant," elaborating that beyond constant virtue, there is no separate infant to return to. The two examples below follow this pattern.

For the affairs of the world cannot be managed by softness alone—there are times when hardness must be employed. They cannot be governed by obscurity alone—there are times when clarity must be used. They cannot be accomplished solely by baseness and lowliness—there are times when one must be above. This is why the sage must know the masculine, know the white, know the glorious. Yet hardness does not arise from hardness but from softness; clarity does not arise from clarity but from obscurity; nobility does not arise from nobility but from baseness. Thus, hardness, clarity, and nobility are the branches of things, the child—far from the Dao. Softness, obscurity, and baseness are the roots of things, the mother—close to the Dao. This is why the sage must guard the feminine, guard the black, guard the humble.

The sage guards these, and thus the mother of the world resides within. Where then would the child go? This is why it is called the valley, the gorge, the model—why the world returns to it. It is precisely because this truly constant virtue has never departed. When constant virtue does not depart, even when employing utmost hardness to decide the world’s affairs, utmost clarity to illuminate its circumstances, or utmost nobility to oversee its lowly, one acts without presumption, leads without domination, achieves success without dwelling in it—never failing to return to the root.

The sage’s possession of the world without attachment stems from this. The infant, the boundless, the uncarved block—these are the inherent Dao of humanity, hence the emphasis on "returning." Officials and leaders are the superiors of all administrators. The uncarved block can be carved into vessels, but vessels cannot become the uncarved block. Leaders can govern all administrators, but administrators cannot become leaders. The sage acts as the mother, not the child—just as they act as the uncarved block, not the vessel; as the leader, not the administrator. They merely secure the root. Otherwise, pursuing the child while forgetting the mother, endlessly chasing the masculine, the white, the glorious, clashing and grinding against things without cease—how could one then govern oneself, let alone the world’s return?

**"Those who would take the world and act upon it—I see they will not succeed. The world is a sacred vessel; it cannot be acted upon. Those who act upon it ruin it; those who grasp it lose it."**

Thus, things may proceed or follow, warm or blow, strengthen or weaken, uphold or destroy. Therefore, the sage eliminates excess, eliminates extravagance, eliminates extremity.

**Su’s Annotation:**
The sage’s possession of the world is not a matter of seizing it—all things return to them, and they accept it out of necessity. In governing the world, they do not act upon it but remove its harms by following the natural course of all things. If one seeks to seize and act upon it, it cannot be attained. Nothing can be forced. Even a gathering of a hundred people, if not following its natural course but acting recklessly, will inevitably face discord and resistance—how much more so with the world?

Though small things with few people may still be seized by force or cunning, when it comes to the vastness of the world, with its divine essence, if one does not await its natural return, there will be rebellion; if one does not allow its natural governance, there will be chaos. Yin and yang interact, high and low incline, great and small influence one another—some lead, some follow; some warm, some chill; some strengthen, some weaken; some uphold, some destroy. These are the natural tendencies of things, the inevitable course of events.

The foolish, driven by self-interest and desire for gain, seek to resist and defy this course—disaster will befall them, either ruin or fracture. Only the sage knows it cannot be opposed and thus awaits it with compliance. They eliminate excess, eliminate extravagance, eliminate extremity, preventing things from going too far and harming others—thus the world remains free of calamity. This is the ultimate non-action.

Even Yao and Tang could not avoid floods and droughts, yet they never faced ruin—this is the reason. The *Yi Jing*’s *Tai* hexagram states: *"The ruler, in accordance with the Dao of heaven and earth, assists and harmonizes their proper order, thereby guiding the people."* With three yang lines within and three yin lines without, things reach their utmost flourishing. The sage fears excess and harm, so they regulate and assist, ensuring it does not go too far—this is what is meant by eliminating excess, extravagance, and extremity.

**Lü’s Annotation:**
The sage embraces simplicity in governing the world, thus *"great governance requires no carving."* Their way of taking the world is always through non-interference. To "take" means to win the hearts of the world so they do not depart. Thus, those who would take the world by acting upon it do not truly take it. Those who take it without such means—I see they will not succeed.

What is above form is called the Dao; what is below form is called the vessel. The world as a vessel is a sacred vessel. Only the divine Dao can govern the sacred vessel. The divine is without thought, without action—acting upon it is not the proper way to govern it. Thus, it cannot be acted upon. Those who act seek completion but only bring ruin; those who grasp seek attainment but only incur loss.

Yao did not possess people, yet people saw him as majestic. Shun and Yu possessed the world yet did not claim it. All these truly knew how to take the world—not by acting or grasping. Thus, all things have those that proceed and those that follow, those that warm and those that blow, those that strengthen and those that weaken, those that uphold and those that destroy—the mutual arising of circumstances leaves no alternative. How then can one grasp and act upon it?

Therefore, the sage eliminates excess, eliminates extravagance, eliminates extremity—merely assisting the self-so of all things, never daring to act.

**Yuanze’s Annotation:**
The perfected person embodies spirit and merges with change, becoming one with things. Though ruling all under heaven, they never possess—thus they act with effortless ease, and no undertaking fails. They mold emperors and kings as if shaping chaff or clay. But those who stubbornly cling to possession and impose their will upon things—even a body of six feet becomes cumbersome, as if it could scarcely move. How much more so for all under heaven? Those who seize do so by taking things as their own; those who act do so by contriving—both are bound to the self and its doings. Such people dwell amid constant anxiety; how could they govern others?

The sage’s mind transcends being and non-being, never objectifying things. Thus, when yin and yang alternate, their principle remains unchanged. If one insists on possession, things become adversaries, and the myriad transformations tangle beyond reckoning. Therefore, to walk alone ahead without knowing followers trail behind is like form and shadow never parting. To exhale in warmth without knowing the blower’s chill arrives is like cold and heat arising in turn. To know only strength is to invite weakness in time; to know only bearing is to summon collapse in response. All this is the great truth of creation, the constant pattern of dawn and dusk. Those who cling to possession do not understand its origin, nor do they comprehend its nature—thus they suffer these afflictions and contend with them. In this way, even the slightest defense against a single thing fills heaven and earth with vast anxiety. How could one wield the sacred vessel without burden?

The sage’s mind merges with the non-being that responds to the myriad changes. In their actions, they do not rely on fixed plans, like a mirror reflecting forms—just enough, then stopping. Matters beyond their scope are left undone. Those who cling to possession deludedly perceive appearances, pride themselves on their abilities, and delight in endless doing—thus their affairs always exceed bounds. This stems from ignorance of the reciprocity between proceeding and following, exhaling and blowing, strengthening and weakening, bearing and collapsing.

---

**Xizhai’s Annotation:**
The sage embodies the Dao as their essence and, unavoidably, takes form within heaven and earth. Since the world cannot be governed without the sage, they unavoidably take up its rule. Yet the sage regards this body as a temporary lodging, and all under heaven as entrusted to their care. How could they force what they lack and lose the true, constant Dao?

Those who do not understand the Dao treat the world as something real and themselves as its ruler—thus they act, disturb it with possession, and cling to things, unaware that their actions only bring ruin, and their grasping only incurs loss. For in the world, some things proceed while others follow, some exhale warmth while others blow cold, some grow strong while others weaken, some bear while others collapse—this is the nature of things, their inherent condition. If one insists on acting and grasping, forcing the proceeding to follow, the exhaling to blow, the strong to weaken, the bearing to collapse—even heaven could not endure it, let alone a person.

The sage follows the natural, knowing what they receive cannot be altered. They only eliminate excess, extravagance, and extremity, ensuring that what can proceed, exhale, strengthen, or bear does not go too far, while what follows, blows, weakens, or collapses does not fall short. This is called using one’s naturalness to assist the naturalness of things.

---

**Xue’s Annotation:**
Things each have their natural disposition—how could contrivance harm them? Thus, the sage eliminates excess, extravagance, and extremity, merely following their self-so. What the sage calls "excess," "extravagance," and "extremity" does not refer to the extravagant excesses of later ages. Anything that adds contrivance beyond simplicity falls into this category. The *Book of Han*’s *Biography of Huang Ba* states, "In governing, one must eliminate what is extreme and excessive." Though the words derive from this, the meaning differs. If a matter is slightly excessive, remove the excess—small and harmless, it need not be altered. This is the intent of the Han scholars. Things have their inherent nature; one cannot force them. Affairs have their proper measure; one cannot exceed it. This is the original meaning of Laozi.

---

One who assists a ruler with the Dao does not use weapons to dominate the world, for such affairs swiftly return. Where armies camp, brambles grow; after great wars, years of famine follow. The good achieve their aim and stop—daring not seize strength. Achieving their aim, they do not boast; achieving their aim, they do not vaunt; achieving their aim, they do not arrogate; achieving their aim, they act out of necessity; achieving their aim, they do not force. Things in their prime inevitably age—this is called "not the Dao." What is not the Dao perishes early.

---

**Su’s Annotation:**
When the sage uses weapons, it is always out of necessity. If not out of necessity but seeking to dominate the world by force, though they may prevail, calamity will inevitably return. King Ling of Chu, Duke Min of Qi, the First Emperor of Qin, and Emperor Wu of Han—some died by the sword, others brought disaster to their descendants. What people abhor, ghosts detest—none have escaped. Where armies go, the people’s affairs are neglected, so fields lie fallow. After war, killing energy prevails, so harvests fail. This is true of all warfare, but how much more so for those who dominate the world by force?

"Achieving their aim" means resolving what virtue cannot pacify and governance cannot subdue—only then, out of necessity, do they use weapons to decide it. "Do not boast, do not vaunt, do not arrogate, act out of necessity"—these four are how one avoids forcing. Things in their prime inevitably age—nothing is exempt. Only the man of the Dao, though complete, seems lacking; though full, seems empty. He has never been in his prime, so he has never aged, never dominated the world by force. To dominate the world by force is extreme primacy—how could it not age? How could it not perish?

---

**Lü’s Annotation:**
The ruler is one who does not act; the one who assists the ruler is one who acts. To take the world cannot be done without action, and if this action is unceasing, it leads to dominating by force. Though the assistant acts, they still do not follow the Dao—how much less the ruler, who embodies the non-acting Dao?

The reason is that such affairs swiftly return. If one uses the Dao to serve the world, the world dares not resist. But if one uses weapons to dominate the world, others will resist.

What emerges from you will return to you. Where armies encamp, brambles grow; in the wake of great armies, famine years follow. The poison of armies spreads thus through the world—therefore, the skilled commander achieves only decisive victory. "Decisive" means overcoming the enemy. To overcome the enemy is to launch an attack from Mingtiao, as when Tang overcame Jie from Bo. This is what it means to overcome the enemy. Such action arises from necessity, not from reliance on force. Be decisive but do not boast of your ability; be decisive but do not vaunt your achievement; be decisive but do not grow arrogant in your power. Such decisiveness always arises from necessity—this is the Dao of decisiveness without force. If, however, you are decisive and boast of your ability, decisive and vaunt your achievement, decisive and grow arrogant in your power, then your decisiveness arises from force, not from necessity.

All things, when young, grow strong; when strong, they age—this is the nature of things. The Dao values holding to softness as strength, for this is how one endures without peril. If you dominate the world by force, you abandon softness and employ strength. But strength must age—this is merely the way of things. How could it be the Dao’s way of shaping things? Thus it is said: "When things grow strong, they age. This is called not following the Dao. What does not follow the Dao perishes early."

**Xizhai’s Annotation:**
If you kill a man’s father, his son will kill yours; if you kill a man’s elder brother, his younger brother will kill yours—this is called "what goes around comes around." The harms of war cannot be easily enumerated. Even if, by chance, you prevail, the vengeful qi of slaughter will ensure the earth cannot make things grow and heaven cannot bring harmony. Thus, brambles grow where troops have camped, and famine follows in the wake of armies—how much greater, then, are the harms that cannot be overcome. Therefore, the skilled commander acts only out of necessity, decisive in a single strike but never relying on this to gain strength. "Decisive" means not prolonging the conflict. Internally, he holds to the mind of necessity; externally, he executes a single, conclusive strike. Thus, he never boasts, never grows arrogant, never vaunts his achievement, never relies on force—all arises from necessity. If he could have refrained but did not, his strength will age and his qi decline, just as a man’s strength must age. This is called "not following the Dao." A man who does not follow the Dao may not live out his years—how much less can an army’s strength endure?

**Hongfu’s Annotation:**
The Dao of heaven favors return, yet those who assist rulers by dominating with force do not understand the Dao. Knowing that force cannot be relied upon, they should desist—but without resolute decisiveness, they cannot. Only the truly skilled can be decisive. Thus, the text repeatedly warns against the dangers of decisiveness, guarding deeply against them. "When things grow strong, they age"—this is the Dao of heaven. To rely on strength without knowing that decline will follow is called "not following the Dao." Affairs that do not follow the Dao cannot but end early.

Now, fine weapons are inauspicious tools—some despise them. Therefore, the man of the Dao does not dwell with them. The noble man, in peacetime, values the left; in war, he values the right. Weapons are inauspicious tools, not the tools of the noble man. When he must use them, he does so with utmost restraint. Victory is not to be celebrated; to celebrate it is to delight in killing. Those who delight in killing cannot achieve their will in the world.

Auspicious affairs value the left; inauspicious affairs value the right. The deputy general stands on the left; the supreme general stands on the right—this means that, in positions of power, they are treated with mourning rites. When many are killed, mourn them with sorrowful weeping. When victorious, treat it with mourning rites.

**Su’s Annotation:**
Using weapons to resolve crises but not making them a constant practice—this is called "not dwelling with them."

**Lü’s Annotation:**
Heaven and earth reveal civil virtues while concealing martial ones—this is the Dao of heaven and earth, the principle of yin and yang. To admire weapons is to treat inauspicious tools as fine, and things that some despise as desirable. Therefore, the man of the Dao does not dwell with them. Thus, the noble man, in peacetime, values the left; in war, he values the right—his values differ accordingly. In times of peace, he still does not treat weapons as fine tools, for they are not the tools of the noble man. He uses them only when unavoidable, valuing restraint above all. Thus, even in victory, he does not celebrate it—this is not how one admires weapons. Heaven protects and defends with compassion, for compassion is what the world delights in and never tires of. How could one who kills delight in it? To celebrate killing is to delight in it. Those who delight in killing cannot achieve their will in the world.

Therefore, auspicious affairs value the left; inauspicious affairs value the right. The deputy general stands on the left; the supreme general stands on the right—this means treating them with mourning rites. When many are killed, mourn them with sorrowful weeping; when victorious, treat it with mourning rites. If you treat it with mourning rites, it is clear that these are inauspicious tools and that victory is not to be celebrated. If you mourn with sorrowful weeping, it is clear that you do not delight in killing.

Laozi’s understanding of ritual is profound—how could those who claim he sought to abolish ritual truly grasp his intent?

**Xizhai’s Annotation:**
Weapons cannot be admired, just as men cannot be killed without cause. Only one who does not delight in killing can speak of warfare. Sunzi and Wu Qi’s treatises on war discuss assessing emptiness and fullness, distinguishing the orthodox and the unorthodox—their words are detailed. Yet the root of emptiness and fullness, the orthodox and the unorthodox, they surely understood. Laozi said: "Value restraint; even in victory, do not celebrate it." To speak of restraint in war may seem inadequate, but without restraint, stillness—stillness is the root of victory—gives way to recklessness, and recklessness is the foundation of defeat.

When Liang Xiang asked Mencius, "How can the world be settled?" Mencius replied, "It will be settled through unity." "Who can unify it?" "One who does not delight in killing." If one truly does not delight in killing, unifying the world would not be difficult. From ancient times to the present, those who do not delight in killing have always risen, while those who delight in killing have always perished. Those who delight in killing may achieve temporary success, but none have endured for long. Therefore, the noble man, when victorious, treats it with mourning rites—inasmuch as weapons are inauspicious tools, the man of the Dao does not dwell with them.
