The eternal Dao has no name. Though the uncarved block is small, none in the world dare treat it as a subject. If lords and kings can hold to it, the myriad things will submit of their own accord. Heaven and earth unite to send down sweet dew; people, without command, find it evenly distributed. Only when the system of names begins do names arise. Once names exist, one must also know when to stop. Knowing when to stop is how one avoids peril. The Dao’s presence in the world is like valleys flowing into rivers and seas.

Wang Fusi’s commentary:
The Dao has no form and is not bound to constancy, so it cannot be named. Since the nameless is its constancy, it is said, “The eternal Dao has no name.” The uncarved block, as a thing, is without intent and without name; thus, to attain the Dao, nothing is better than holding to the uncarved. The wise can be made subjects; the brave can be employed in warfare; the skilled can be tasked with service; the strong can bear heavy burdens. But the uncarved block, as a thing, is simple and impartial, approaching nothingness—hence it is said, “none dare treat it as a subject.” Embracing simplicity and non-action, not allowing things to burden one’s true nature, not permitting desires to harm one’s spirit, the myriad things will submit of their own accord, and the Dao will naturally be attained. When heaven and earth unite, sweet dew descends without being sought. If I preserve my true nature and practice non-action, the people, without command, will find it evenly distributed.

“When the system begins” refers to the time when the uncarved block scatters and officials and leaders first emerge. If one then relies on names to designate things, the mother of governance is lost. Thus, knowing when to stop is how one avoids peril. Valleys seek rivers and seas, yet rivers and seas do not summon them. Those who practice the Dao in the world do not command, yet it is evenly distributed; they do not seek, yet it is naturally attained. Therefore, it is said, “The Dao’s presence in the world is like valleys flowing into rivers and seas.”

Su’s commentary:
“Uncarved” is nature.

The Dao is eternally nameless, and thus nature likewise cannot be named. As a thing, when it unfurls, it is present everywhere; when it contracts, it does not fill even a hair’s tip—this is why, though small, it cannot be subjugated. Thus, when a commoner of lowly station preserves it, even dust, chaff, and husks suffice to mold and forge a Yao or Shun; yet when a lord or king of exalted rank fails to preserve it, the myriad things will not submit. When the ascending and descending qi of the void converge as one, sweet dew descends, moistening all things without exception. The sage embodies the supreme Dao to respond to all existence, like dew that reaches everywhere—this is how one makes the myriad things submit.

The sage scatters the uncarved block to form vessels and, based on these vessels, establishes names. How could one pursue names and forget the uncarved, chase the branches and lose the root? One must know to return to nature; thus, riding the ten thousand changes, one remains free from peril.

The rivers and seas are where water gathers; the valleys and streams are where water divides. The Dao is the ancestor of the myriad things; the myriad things are the branches of the Dao. All are water. Thus, valleys and streams return to where they gather; all are the Dao. Thus, the myriad things submit to their ancestor.

Lü’s commentary:
The Dao is eternally nameless; to name it as “Dao” is already to depart from it. When it is nameless, it has not yet begun to be a thing. Its uncarved state may be called small, yet the world does not dare subjugate it. Why? Heaven and earth rely on it to begin; the myriad things depend on it to live. Who in the world would dare subjugate that from which they originate or by which they are born? This is called the true ruler. The myriad things all possess a true ruler—this is what is meant.

If lords and kings can preserve it, they thereby use the true ruler to rule the myriad things. Which of the myriad things, possessing their true ruler, would not submit? Thus it is said, “When the sage arises, the myriad things behold.” The utmost yin is solemn; the utmost yang is blazing. Solemnity issues from heaven; blazing radiance emerges from earth. When the two intermingle, harmony is achieved and things are born. The horse serves as their regulator, yet its form is unseen. When heaven and earth unite to descend sweet dew, this is the perfection of their intermingling and harmony. If lords and kings hold to the Dao as their regulator, the myriad things will submit—just so, yet who can see its form? Thus, people are not commanded, yet all is evenly distributed.

The nameless uncarved block is without thought, without action. As soon as the craftsman or steward moves, the system begins, and names come into being. At this time, one must know when to stop, so that what is at peace is easily maintained, what is unformed is easily planned, what is fragile is easily broken, and what is subtle is easily dispersed. Act before there is disorder; govern before there is chaos—then what peril could there be? This is the way for kings and lords to preserve it.

The Dao’s presence in the world is like valleys and streams in relation to rivers and seas—is there any that does not return to them?

Xizhai’s commentary:
This speaks of the Dao and vessels circulating in mutual succession, their weight and lightness alternating. The Dao is eternally nameless and cannot be seen, yet even a commoner who attains it—though the uncarved is small—the world does not dare subjugate it, knowing that nothing can be added to its reverence. Though lords and kings are great, if they cannot preserve it, the myriad things will not submit. That which makes them submit lies here, not there.

For example, though heaven and earth differ in height, when they unite to descend sweet dew, nothing is not evenly moistened—this shows that heaven and earth are not truly separate. From this, we see that the Dao, though small, need not be considered light; lords and kings, though great, need not be considered noble; heaven and earth, though divided, need not be considered apart. Though the Dao scatters into things and each thing has its name, heaven has never truly abandoned things. Only because heaven still gives life to them do things stand between heaven and earth without peril. Things do not diminish the Dao by scattering it; the Dao does not disperse by giving life to things.

It is like valleys and streams forming clouds and rain, rivers and seas providing moisture: the qi of valleys and streams is never cut off from rivers and seas, nor is the qi of rivers and seas cut off from valleys and streams. Viewed through the Dao, there is never a day without circulation; viewed through vessels, the divisions of water and land differ. Yet all is one.

*Bisheng*:
The “eternal Dao” refers to the “constant Dao” mentioned in the first chapter.

The "nameless" refers to the "nameless" spoken of in the first chapter. Because it is neither carved nor polished, it is called "uncarved wood"; because it is called "subtle" and "minute," it is called "small." Yet few are those who can perceive the small and abide by it. If lords and kings could abide by this and perceive the small, they would be called "illuminated." To know the child is to guard the mother. Thus, stillness becomes the lord of movement, and movement its minister; the One becomes the host of the myriad, and the myriad its guests. Who then would have a minister coarser than the uncured wood?

In the beginning, the nameless was the origin of heaven and earth. The "maker" carves the uncured wood and divides it. The beginning was originally nameless; once carved, it acquires names. If one pursues names without cease, then one generates two, two generates three, and even the most skillful reckoner cannot calculate the endless proliferation of names and forms, all taken as real and engaged in contention. What could be more disastrous than the calamities of yin and yang? This is what is meant by "not knowing the constant and recklessly acting—misfortune."

If one truly knows that the nameless can accommodate the named, then the named can also return to the nameless. Therefore, value the cessation of cessation, anchoring it in the nameless uncured wood. Knowing when to stop prevents being swept away by things; in tranquil self-sufficiency, peril has no place to arise. This is not forced. Things are born by the Dao and perish by the Dao; all things arise from the Dao, and all things return to the Dao. The nature of the self resides within; I myself restore it—what difficulty is there in this?

Thus, the rivers and seas are the source of water, while valleys and streams are its branches. Though branches differ, they are all contained within the source; though names vary, they are all governed by the Dao.

One who knows others is wise; one who knows oneself is enlightened.
One who overcomes others has force; one who overcomes oneself is strong.
One who knows contentment is rich; one who strives resolutely has will.
One who does not lose their place endures; one who dies yet does not perish is long-lived.
*(Not losing one’s place is what the *Yi* means by "abiding in one’s place." Luojia said: "To live without being bound by life is called endurance; to die without perishing is called longevity.")*

Su’s commentary:
Distinguishing things leads to wisdom, but obscuration is exhausted in illumination. The mind that discriminates is not yet eliminated, so one may know others but not oneself. When obscuration is exhausted, there is no more discrimination, so one can know oneself and extend that knowledge to others. Force may reach others but not oneself; overcoming the self and returning to one’s nature is beyond force, so it may be called strength. One who knows contentment finds sufficiency in whatever they encounter and is never poor. Though one may possess the world, if they constantly harbor a sense of insufficiency, they remain poor all their life. Not contending with things yet ceaselessly striving—nothing can wrest their will from them. Though things change endlessly, the mind never loses its place, so it endures. The changes of life and death are vast, yet one’s nature remains serene and undiminished—this is how the ancients’ highest persons could be neither born nor die.

Lü’s commentary:
One who knows others is wise; one who knows oneself is enlightened. Only after knowing oneself can one know others, so enlightenment is surely the source of wisdom. One who overcomes others has force; one who overcomes oneself is strong. Only after overcoming oneself can one overcome others, so strength is surely the source of force. Returning to the mandate is called constancy; knowing constancy is called enlightenment. One who cannot know oneself cannot know constancy, so knowing constancy is the way to know oneself. Enlightenment that reaches self-knowledge—what difficulty would there be in knowing others? Guarding softness is called strength, but contending with things is daily strife of the mind, not guarding softness. Thus, guarding softness is the way to overcome oneself. Strength that reaches self-overcoming—what difficulty would there be in overcoming others? With the enlightenment of self-knowledge, one knows that the myriad things are complete within oneself and has no need to seek outside. Thus, one who knows contentment is rich. With the strength of self-overcoming, one diligently practices the Dao and need not seek elsewhere. Thus, one who strives resolutely has will. Knowing one’s sufficiency and striving resolutely, one can preserve what they preserve and not be swayed by things. Thus, one who does not lose their place endures. Preserving what they preserve, though they die, they have not perished. Thus, one who dies yet does not perish is long-lived.

Xi’s commentary:
Knowledge directed outward is wisdom; directed inward, it is enlightenment. Victory directed outward is force; directed inward, it is strength. Wisdom and force are illusory; enlightenment and strength are real. The gateway to the Dao lies here. People fail to enter the Dao because they see themselves dimly and are overcome by things. If one is inwardly enlightened, they will not chase after externals; not chasing externals, they gradually overcome things. With time, they naturally enter the Dao. All discontent arises from not knowing what one possesses. The myriad things are complete within me; turning inward and reflecting, one knows that taking from oneself is sufficient—is this not wealth? Knowing contentment, the mind gradually departs from existence. Though force is not yet complete, if one cannot fulfill their vision, they must have a resolute will to strive. Seeing the root of tranquility, they gradually return to the Dao. In emptiness, they verify reality; what they attain does not shift, transcending past and present, vast and ever-abiding—this is called not losing one’s place. Regarding life and death as morning and evening, living without possession, dying without perishing—this is called longevity.

The Farmer’s commentary:
Liezi’s "not transforming," Zhuangzi’s "not dying," and the Buddha’s "not perishing" all share the same meaning as "dying yet not perishing." Thus, the sage’s life is the same as death—this is called "spirit"; the sage’s death is the same as life—this is called "longevity." This speaks of their life and death being no different. Only because life and death share the same form and the myriad things reside in one storehouse does the body become like the cicada’s shell or the snake’s slough—a temporary dwelling. The cicada’s shell is already dead, yet the cicada has not perished; the snake’s slough has already decayed, yet the snake has not been lost. Why? Because the true does not perish even in death.

It is said: The highest person is not burned by fire, not drowned by water; tigers cannot seize them, rhinoceroses cannot gore them. Riding the void, they do not fall; striking rocks, they are not obstructed. Yet it is also said they "die yet do not perish." Why? The sage merely follows the times. When the times call for action, the sage does not forcibly avoid it; when the times call for cessation, the sage does not forcibly act. They merely observe heaven’s decree. Thus, there are those who can act yet do not, so there are those who live yet do not die, and those who die yet do not perish.

The great Dao flows freely, available to left and right. The myriad things rely on it to live, yet it does not refuse. Its work is accomplished, yet it does not claim possession. It nurtures and nourishes the myriad things yet does not act as their master. Always desireless, it may be called "small." The myriad things return to it yet do not know their master—it may be called "great." Thus, the sage never acts as great and thereby accomplishes greatness.
*(Flowing freely means without attachment.)*

Su’s Annotation:
["Flowing freely" means it is neither permissible nor impermissible, thus it reaches everywhere—left and right, above and below, in all directions. Those in the world who give life to things yet do not refuse them will surely claim possession in the name of the self. Those who avoid things and do not possess them will surely refuse things and not give life. Only the Dao gives life without refusal, accomplishes without possession. If one acts as great with a mind to be great, one becomes small.]

Lü’s Annotation:
[It may be left but not right, or right but not left—what adheres to things in a partial manner is not the great Dao. The great Dao is nowhere absent, thus it flows freely and may be left or right. All things that are great cannot be called small, and what is small cannot be called great—this is the Dao. Because it may be left or right, the myriad things rely on it to live yet it does not refuse. It accomplishes success yet does not dwell in it, clothes and nourishes the myriad things yet does not act as their master. Precisely because it does not dwell or act as master, it is always desireless, and in this desirelessness lies its most wondrous quality. Thus, it may be called "small." The myriad things return to it yet do not know their master—this is its utmost capacity. Thus, it may be called "great." Yet if it is already great but may still be called small, it is not truly great. If it is already small but may still be called great, it is not truly small. Neither great nor small, the Dao thus remains hidden in namelessness. Then wherein lies the Dao’s greatness? It does not reside in being great. The sage embodies the Dao, so how could their accomplishment of greatness come from acting as great?]

Xizhai’s Annotation:
[The great Dao flows freely, filling the eight directions. In its application, it is as if present to the left and right. The myriad things do not live without the Dao, yet the Dao never speaks of its ability. The myriad things do not accomplish without the Dao, yet the Dao never names its achievement. The myriad things are not nourished without the Dao, yet the Dao never acts as their master. When it is small, no trace of it is seen; when it is great, it never acts as master over the myriad things, yet all things return to it. The sage is likewise—never acting as great, yet nothing in the world surpasses them. Only by not claiming greatness do they accomplish greatness.]

*Brush Notes*:
[It may be called small, yet one cannot call it small; it may be called great, yet one cannot call it great. Precisely because it may be left or right, it is neither small nor great. Neither small nor great, it thereby accomplishes greatness.]

*Grasping the Great Image*:
[All under heaven come, yet it does no harm; there is peace, tranquility, and ease. Music and bait may stop passing travelers, but the Dao’s words, emerging from the mouth, are bland and without flavor—seen, they are not worth seeing; heard, they are not worth hearing. Yet their use cannot be exhausted.]

[Lin Xiyi says: The "great image" is the image of no-image. "All under heaven come" means grasping this and acting accordingly throughout the world. "Exhausted" means complete.]

*Silent Harmony* Annotation:
[The "great image" is the Dao. Those who can grasp the ancient Dao to govern the present being will see all things under heaven return to them. The sage regards the people as infants, fearing only harm to them—how much more so would they cause harm through action? Never causing harm, those who come and return suffer no injury. Suffering no injury, all under heaven abide in their peace and tranquility. Music pleases the ear, and bait suits the mouth, so travelers linger in delight and satisfaction. Yet these are not their true abode, and they cannot stay long. Thus, the *Book of Changes* says: "The bird burns its nest; the traveler first laughs, then wails." Those who grasp the great image are not thus—they do not display desires or pleasures to others. Their words, emerging from the mouth, are bland and without flavor; their teachings, entering the heart, are serene and unadorned. Silent and tranquil, though not enough to be seen or heard, their use is inexhaustible, their draught unending, filling a thousand myriad years without exhaustion.]

Su’s Annotation:
[The Dao is neither being nor non-being, thus it is called the "great image." If it were manifestly formed, it would have similarities and differences—similarities would be loved, differences would be hated. Loved, they come; hated, they leave. This is insufficient to make all under heaven come. With love and hate, there is benefit and harm. When love and hate are exhausted, there is no harm to the myriad things. Thus, those who arrive are all at peace, all tranquil, all at ease. Creating music and setting bait to await visitors—are these not enough to stop passing travelers? Yet when the music ends and the bait is gone, they will leave. As for those who grasp the great image to await all under heaven, how would all under heaven know to love it, let alone hate it? Though it lacks fragrance, flavor, form, color, or sound to please others, its use cannot be exhausted.]

Lü’s Annotation:
[The Dao in all under heaven is like rivers and valleys flowing into the Yangtze and the sea. The myriad things return to it yet do not know their master—this is formlessness. Formlessness—this is the Dao.]

Those who grasp the great image—who would cling to their own ways and refuse to follow? Thus it is said: "Grasp the great image, and all under heaven will come." If one loses the Dao and all under heaven comes, then their coming is but departure, and such coming cannot be without harm. But if one grasps the Dao and all under heaven comes, though they may forget one another in the arts of the Dao, they never truly part. Thus, to come without harm, to be at peace, to be tranquil, to be at ease—peace is the utmost of tranquility, and tranquility is the utmost of ease. Where there is music to delight and bait to tempt, those who stop are but passing travelers. The Dao’s words emerge insipid, utterly flavorless—no bait could tempt them. Look and there is nothing to see; listen and there is nothing to hear—no music could delight them. If this is so, how could its use ever be exhausted? When passing travelers stop, they dwell in a mere travelers’ lodge—nothing meant to last. But when its use cannot be exhausted, the people use it daily without awareness, and from it arises their peace, tranquility, and ease.

Xizhai’s Annotation:
When the Dao descends and takes form, things follow it. The foolish follow and do not return; the wise follow and suffer no harm. Those who follow and do not return have lost the Dao and chase after things. Those who follow and suffer no harm move with the Dao. Having moved with the Dao, they never leave it, and there is nothing that is not at peace, nothing that is not tranquil, nothing that is not at ease. They unite with the Dao in heart, yet do not know the Dao or the heart. If one knows the Dao and acts, there will be unrest, disquiet, and unease. The sage’s relation to forms and vessels is like a passing traveler’s stay in a roadside inn—brief and fleeting, without attachment or regret. If one is lured by desires and pleasures, the traveler lingers where they lodge and does not depart, and there is no trouble they will not bring. Thus, the sage grasps the great image and follows, though they move with things, their heart ever abides with the Dao. They savor the flavorless, see the colorless, hear the soundless, and employ the useless—within forms and vessels, they fully realize the Dao’s use. This is how they attain peace, tranquility, and ease.

"To gather, one must first expand; to weaken, one must first strengthen; to abolish, one must first establish; to take, one must first give." This is called subtle clarity. The soft overcomes the hard; the weak overcomes the strong. Fish cannot leave the depths; the state’s sharpest tools must not be shown to others.

Su’s Annotation:
To seize without first giving is to fail in force and fall short in principle. If force is insufficient, taking is difficult; if principle is lacking, things will not submit. Yet this verges on the use of cunning, no different from Guan Zhong or Sun Wu. The sage’s traces may resemble the worldly, but the sage rides principle while the worldly employ cunning. Riding principle is like a physician’s skill in treating illness; using cunning is like a merchant’s skill in seeking profit. The sage knows that hardness and strength cannot be relied upon, so they abide in softness and weakness. While the world’s hardness and strength vie and clash, they alone remain soft and weak, waiting. When the great are wounded and the small perish, they sit unmoving and await their end—this is what is called victory. Yet does the sage intentionally act thus to overcome things? They know the natural course of power and dwell in its spontaneity. Fish, though lacking claws and teeth to overcome things, dwell in the depths, and even the strong cannot seize them. But once they leave the depths for land, they are but helpless creatures—what can they do? The sage abides in softness and weakness, and the hard and strong cannot harm them. Not only can they not be harmed, but they will ultimately preserve and control what follows—is this not the state’s sharpest tool? Only when fish leave the depths can others control them. The sage, ever abiding in softness and weakness without weariness, ultimately tames all under heaven—how could this be shared with the common people?

Lü’s Annotation:
"To gather, one must first expand; to weaken, one must first strengthen; to abolish, one must first establish; to take, one must first give"—the Dao of heaven, the principle of things, and the affairs of humans never fail to follow this pattern. From expansion, one knows gathering; from strength, one knows weakness; from establishment, one knows abolition; from giving, one knows taking. Who but the perceptive can grasp this? Thus it is called subtle clarity. Yet those who gather, expand, weaken, strengthen, abolish, establish, take, or give are formless and soft; what they gather, expand, weaken, strengthen, abolish, establish, take, or give are formed and hard. Thus, the soft overcoming the hard is evident. One cannot depart from softness and weakness, just as fish cannot leave the depths. If fish leave the depths, they are caught; if one departs from softness and weakness, they perish. The softest under heaven gallops through the hardest, entering where there is no gap. Galloping through the hardest and entering where there is no gap—this is the sharpness of tools. Wielding sharp tools to govern the state and its people, their strategies and movements always lie in the formless—how could one let others know their source? Thus it is said: "The state’s sharpest tools must not be shown to others."

Yuanze’s Annotation:
The nature of yin and yang is like a cycle: when going reaches its limit, it returns; when flourishing reaches its peak, it declines. Observe the waning of the full moon and the decline of the sun at noon, and the unity of all things becomes clear. Only the perfected person deeply comprehends prevention, understanding the traceless. Thus, in nurturing life, they are at ease in bending and stretching; in conducting themselves, they are attuned to growth and decline; in managing affairs, they know the calculations of success and failure; in confronting enemies, they grasp the art of capture and release. The ancients responded to myriad changes with equanimity, free from disturbance, through this Dao. Even ghosts and spirits cannot fathom it—how much less can people? The *Book of Changes* says: "The inchworm bends to extend itself; the dragon and snake hibernate to preserve themselves." Seeing form, one knows the hard and strong control the soft and weak; understanding principle, one realizes the soft and weak overcome the hard and strong. The perfected person deeply grasps the meaning of subtle clarity, so they are humble without arrogance, empty without fullness. They do not contend with things, yet nothing can contend with them. Though this Dao originates in words, its transformations are endless—now soft, now hard, now weak, now strong—who can fix them? Yet even when hard and strong, if softness and weakness cannot overcome them, their movement accords with...

Thus, while the heart accords with principle, it never departs from softness and weakness. From this perspective, we again recognize that softness and weakness overcome hardness and strength—this is what is called the "sharp instrument." The fish, by concealing itself in the depths through softness and weakness, preserves its life. The sage retreats into seclusion, wielding ultimate power to turn the myriad things as if by unseen hands. Though their strength moves heaven and earth, the world does not perceive their vigor; though their might subdues the realm, none name them martial. How could they violently overpower spirits and allow the masses to discuss them?

I have privately considered this: what sets the sage apart from others is their mastery of concealment. When one meets things with hardness and strength, the hardness and strength of those things cannot be overcome. The world all relies on hardness and strength to conquer things, yet I alone lodge in the realm of softness, weakness, and non-contention. When I act, who can resist? Observe the Way of Heaven: autumn and winter become spring and summer—this is but one proof. The sage hides in profound obscurity, taking softness and weakness as their outward form. Thus, they guide the myriad things within their art, and no spirit can fathom their actions. Is this not what is called "concealed application and solitary transformation"?

The *Book of Changes* says: "Concealment is the means to exercise power." Zhuangzi says: "The fish has its strategy." The meaning aligns with this.

**Chunfu’s Annotation:**
"About to" is the phrase of impending action; "must certainly" is the phrase of accomplished fact. Creation and transformation have their cycles of growth and decline, fullness and emptiness; human affairs have their principles of fortune and misfortune, interdependence and latent potential. Thus, when things are about to become such, they must have already been so. Though the "about to" has not yet taken form, the "already" is visible. If one can grasp the "already" and inversely perceive the "about to," then even what seems obscure is in truth perfectly clear. Hence it is said: "This is called foresight and clarity." That softness overcomes hardness and weakness overcomes strength is precisely this principle.

Yet while it may be called "subtle clarity," it is permissible for the subtle to be clear, but not for the clear to be subtle. What is "subtle yet clear"? It is concealing this principle to nurture oneself—remaining profoundly still, deeply withdrawn, at ease and self-content, like a fish that never leaves the depths. What is "making the subtle clear"? It is displaying this principle to others, provoking conflict, inviting trouble, aiding bandits and teaching thieves—like showing a sharp weapon to others.

Zhuangzi’s *Chapter on Opening the Satchel* elucidates this meaning. The "sharp instrument" is a weapon; the metaphor speaks of the principle of subtle clarity. When the sage employs it, it becomes the Great Dao; when a villain usurps it, it becomes the art of strategic expansion and contraction, whose harm surpasses that of blades. Thus, the sage compares it to a "sharp instrument."

**Xizhai’s Annotation:**
This is the sage’s method of restraining the heart and seizing the passions. The heart, as a thing, moves in and out without fixed time or place. To forcibly stop it is to make it stop all the more. The sage, knowing it cannot be forcibly stopped, seeks to close it by opening it, to weaken it by strengthening it, to abolish it by arousing it, to seize it by yielding to it. Attempting to stop movement by stopping only leads to more stopping; one must instead reverse course and seek stillness through movement. By observing false movement, one exhausts its falsity and abolishes it, returning to the natural and restoring true stillness. Though movement may stir, the stirring heart does not arise; when the heart does not arise, stillness is not born. This is the sage’s way of closing the heart, weakening the will, abolishing passions, and seizing desires—subtle and difficult to perceive. Hence it is said: "This is called foresight and clarity."

This foresight and clarity, being both soft and weak, can overcome the world’s hard and strong desires because it does not depart from the Mother of the Dao. If it departs from the Mother of the Dao, it is like a fish leaving the depths. Just as a fish cannot leave the depths, so too must the state’s sharp instrument not be shown to others. If shown to others, they will not believe it.

Those who interpret this passage in the world often fail to follow its root, mixing in the military strategies of Sun and Wu. This is why the *Book of Songs* and *Book of Rites* were composed to enlighten the ignorant.

】 The Dao is eternally non-action, yet nothing is left undone. Should lords and kings abide by this, the myriad things will transform of themselves. When transformation stirs and desires arise, I shall press them down with the nameless uncarved block. The nameless uncarved block itself will then harbor no desire, and with no desire, the world will settle into stillness and right itself.

The Dao’s constancy lies in its celestial norm. As Jiefu notes: "It speaks of the Dao’s governance, hence ‘the myriad things will naturally submit’; it speaks of the Dao’s transformation, hence ‘the myriad things will naturally transform.’" "Arise" denotes movement; "press down" means to stabilize and render unmoving. Kumarajiva says: "When the heart attains unity and emptiness, its resources are never lost, the ten thousand spirits submit to transformation, and deviance returns to rectitude."

**Hisheng’s Annotation:**
The Dao’s constancy arises from its formless essence, hence its non-action; its function is form-bound, hence its all-accomplishing nature. Should lords and kings preserve this primordial technique of origin and mother, the principles of the myriad things will be realized, and the world will be rectified—this is what is meant by "I practice non-action, and the people transform themselves." If the slightest trace of profit or desire should sprout, one must immediately ground and rectify it with the substance of the great Dao, ensuring no movement takes hold.

The highest virtue practices non-action and does not act for any purpose, thus aligning with the Dao. Lesser virtue acts with purpose, thus aligning with virtue. Those who attain virtue inevitably lose the Dao, for where there is a heart of non-action, there must also be traces of non-action. Later generations, tracing these traces, may forget their root, practicing non-action to the point of action. Thus it is said: "The nameless uncarved block itself will harbor no desire"—this is to induce the forgetting of both heart and trace, advancing into ever deeper mystery.

When both heart and trace are forgotten, and the subtleties of affairs and principles are profoundly guarded, the world will each rectify its nature and destiny, unburdened by the traces of things. The opening chapter takes the constant Dao as essence and constant name as function, culminating in "profound and more profound." This chapter takes non-action as essence and all-accomplishing as function, unifying them through the forgetting of both beginning and end, exhausting essence and function in their entirety.

**Su’s Annotation:**
The Dao’s constancy means it accomplishes all without the intention of acting. The sage transforms things through non-action, and the myriad things, once transformed, begin with non-action and gradually progress to action—like the growth of an infant, where human artifice arises day by day. Thus, the decline of the Three Dynasties saw the transformations of human sentiment grow daily more severe. When desires first stir and those above and below the world all yield to them, the changes become inexpressible. If, at the moment of stirring, one does not act upon them but instead presses them down with the nameless uncarved block, there may yet be hope of halting them.

The sage harbors no念 of restraint within and leaves no trace of the uncarved block without, thus preserving the uncarved block in its entirety and wielding great efficacy. If the desire to preserve the uncarved block still lingers in the heart, one strays far from the truth.

**Lü’s Annotation:**
All things possess names, yet the Dao is eternally nameless—thus, all that is named becomes its guest. Hence it is said, "The myriad things will naturally submit," which is to speak from the perspective of the nameless. Yet this merely grasps non-action; to practice non-action without failing to accomplish all is not to cling even to non-action. Thus, "the myriad things will naturally transform," and the self and the myriad things become nothing but the Dao. Who then knows whether the myriad things are guests or the reason for their submission?

When they first submit, names are established. Once names exist, one must also know restraint—knowing restraint means returning to the nameless uncarved block. When they transform and desires arise, I press them down with the nameless uncarved block, and the nameless uncarved block itself will harbor no desire. If the nameless uncarved block harbors no desire, then not only is there non-action, but even the notion of non-action ceases to exist. The world’s movements are rectified by unity. Should lords and kings guard the Dao to this point, they may be said to "harbor no desire and still the world." Could the world then fail to right itself?

Laozi was a true person, yet he did not disdain to earnestly address lords and kings regarding the world’s affairs. Why? The Dao, when cultivated in the self, is authentic; when extended to the world, it becomes universal. If lords and kings understand and abide by it, then cultivating the world is indeed universal. To not hoard the Dao but desire to share it with the world is benevolence; to desire to share it with the world yet first address lords and kings is righteousness. Yet scholars, seeing his words seemingly reject benevolence and righteousness, claim Laozi denigrates and diminishes them—this is not what I accept.

Alas! They fail to perceive the intent behind his rejection. No wonder they do not accept it.

The Xizhai Annotation states: The Dao emerges from nonbeing into being, beginning with the sprouting of joy, anger, sorrow, and pleasure, and culminating in the full establishment of ritual, music, punishment, and governance. When this culmination fails to return [to its source], transformations become endless, and the Dao is increasingly lost. Thus, the sage, when things are about to flow away, restores them with simplicity to stabilize them. Once stabilized with simplicity, simplicity itself becomes nameless. Though employing nameless simplicity, it should appear as if one does not desire it. If there is even a trace of intent to use simplicity, then it is no longer true simplicity. Without desire, the people will right themselves.

*Laozi Yi*, Volume One

The lower text states:
The highest virtue does not act as virtue, and thus possesses virtue.
The lesser virtue does not lose virtue, and thus lacks virtue.
The highest virtue acts without contrivance and without purpose.
The lesser virtue acts with contrivance and with purpose.
The highest benevolence acts without contrivance.
The highest righteousness acts with contrivance.
The highest ritual acts, yet when none respond, it rolls up its sleeves and forces compliance.
Thus, when the Dao is lost, virtue follows; when virtue is lost, benevolence follows; when benevolence is lost, righteousness follows; when righteousness is lost, ritual follows. Ritual is the thinning of loyalty and trust and the beginning of chaos. Foresight is the flower of the Dao and the origin of folly. Therefore, the great person dwells in thickness, not thinness; resides in substance, not its blossoms. Thus, they discard that and take this.

Su’s Annotation:
The sage follows the heart’s desires without overstepping the bounds, not by intentionally cultivating virtue, but because virtue naturally suffices. Those below, knowing the value of virtue, strive to preserve it, merely completing themselves—what virtue do they possess? To act without contrivance yet with purpose is still to act. Only acting without contrivance and without purpose can be called nonaction. Those below may not fail in their actions, yet they still act with purpose—not mere empty doing but with method. Benevolence and righteousness cannot escape acting, yet what distinguishes them is that benevolence excels in acting without purpose, while righteousness achieves its ends through purposeful action. Virtue has higher and lower, but benevolence and righteousness have only higher forms—why? The lesser virtue lies between benevolence and righteousness, while what is below righteousness is not worth discussing. From virtue descending to ritual, the means by which the sage governs the people reach their limit. If one acts and none respond, they resort to force; if force fails, punishments and warfare arise—mere empty doing without method. Loyalty and trust without ritual remain unseen; when ritual is established, the beauty of loyalty and trust shines outwardly. Between ruler and minister, father and child, husband and wife, friends—externally splendid, yet internally hollow. Thus, following it brings order; opposing it brings chaos. The difference between order and chaos is finer than a hair’s breadth, hence it is called the beginning of chaos. The sage’s profound observation of the myriad things discerns right and wrong, all laid bare before them, like a mirror reflecting forms without omission—what is earlier or later? Common people’s vision is limited to the eye, hearing to the ear, thought to the heart, and conduct among the ten thousand things. They exhaust their intellect to seek knowledge and occasionally glimpse something, mistaking it for clarity while remaining fundamentally foolish from the start. The world’s petty people delight in their small gains below while forgetting what is above, favoring thinness over thickness, plucking blossoms while discarding substance. Who but the great person can discard that and take this?

Lü’s Annotation:
If the Dao that can be spoken is not the constant Dao, how can the mediocre grasp and possess it? The highest virtue takes non-possession as possession; only through non-possession does one truly possess. Thus, it is said: the highest virtue does not act as virtue, and thus possesses virtue. The lesser virtue takes non-loss as possession; only by not losing does one possess, yet it is not true virtue. Thus, it is said: the lesser virtue does not lose virtue, and thus lacks virtue. The highest virtue acts without contrivance, not deliberately acting without purpose but naturally without contrivance. The lesser virtue, not understanding this, acts and thus cannot avoid purpose. Virtue without purpose suffices; yet if there is benevolence, it is still acting. However, the highest benevolence does not engage in benevolence but embodies it—acting without purpose. Benevolence cannot stand alone; it must be paired with righteousness. Even the highest righteousness cannot avoid acting—acting with purpose. Benevolence is merely bestowing, while righteousness selects the proper recipient, not yet demanding reciprocity. Ritual, however, involves reciprocity: not responding to a gesture is improper, just as responding without a prior gesture is improper. The principle of ritual is thus. The highest ritual acts, yet when none respond, it rolls up its sleeves and enforces compliance. If even the highest benevolence, ritual, and righteousness are thus, their lower forms are self-evident. From this, we see that when the Dao is lost, virtue follows; when virtue is lost, benevolence follows; when benevolence is lost, righteousness follows; when righteousness is lost, ritual follows—was this said in vain? Ritual’s mutual demands lead to the thinning of loyalty and trust, the rise of chaos, and the use of cunning. Thus, ritual, though meant to govern chaos, becomes its very origin. Foresight, though meant to employ wisdom, becomes the beginning of folly. Why? People’s governance often arises from thickness; thickness preserves their nature, while thinness fosters artifice. To depart from nature and fabricate is to invite chaos. People’s self-awareness often lies in brightness; brightness reveals substance, while blossoms detach from substance. To abandon substance and pursue blossoms is to invite folly. Therefore, the great person dwells in thickness, not thinness; resides in substance, not its blossoms. Thus, they forget benevolence and righteousness, reject ritual and learning, set aside wisdom, and aspire to the great completeness of the Dao—this is what is meant by discarding that and taking this.

The Xizhai Annotation states: The highest virtue does not act as virtue, thus it resides in benevolence, yet benevolence resides in righteousness, and righteousness follows its course without fixation. The lesser virtue does not lose virtue, thus benevolence cannot be acted upon, righteousness cannot be acted upon as ritual—once acted upon, it cannot be altered. The highest virtue does not lose the Dao; the Dao is constantly nonaction, and the mind of nonaction is absent. The lesser virtue does not lose virtue; virtue involves action, and the traces of action remain.

The highest virtue is nonaction, and this is benevolence. Benevolence is close to the Dao, thus it is said: "Benevolence is humanity," and when combined, it is the Dao. Since benevolence is close to the Dao, it is also nonaction and nonaction-in-action. Descending further, it becomes righteousness—righteousness is appropriateness. To act appropriately toward things is to act with purpose; acting without losing appropriateness is acting without having lost it. Descending further, it becomes ritual—ritual is principle. To govern things with ritual, if none respond and one insists on governing them, there will be those who roll up their sleeves and resist. That which cannot be spoken of as attainable is called the Dao; that which can be spoken of as attainable is called virtue. Virtue in humanity is called benevolence; benevolence that does not lose its appropriateness is called righteousness; righteousness that rectifies things is called ritual. Thus, losing the Dao leads to losing virtue; losing virtue leads to losing benevolence; losing benevolence leads to losing righteousness; losing righteousness leads to losing ritual—this is the natural order of descent. From the Dao, five descents bring us to ritual; beyond these five descents, there is nothing worth observing. Ritual arises from chaos, for where there are elders and juniors, without ritual there is chaos, so ritual is established to remedy it. Yet when one rolls up their sleeves and resists, ritual too is lost. Thus, ritual is the beginning of chaos.

Much knowledge is insufficient to embody the Dao, for excessive knowledge confounds and disrupts one’s original true knowing. Knowledge that does not arise from truth is superficial and lacks substance; thus, much knowledge is the beginning of foolishness. Therefore, ritual is the attenuation of loyalty and trust, and knowledge is the blossom of understanding the Dao. The sage dwells in thickness, not thinness, values substance, not blossoms, and thus never loses the root of the Dao.

Hongfu’s Annotation: Nonaction—and yet also no nonaction.

This is called "supreme virtue"—the Yellow Emperor exemplifies it. Next is one who acts yet in reality does not act; this is called "supreme benevolence"—Yao’s benevolence, vast as heaven, exemplifies it. Further next is one who not only acts but also harbors the intent that action must be taken; this is "supreme righteousness"—the sages from Shun and Yu downward exemplify it. When the Dao is lost, virtue is lost; when virtue is lost, benevolence is lost; when benevolence is lost, righteousness is lost; when righteousness is lost, only then does ritual arise—thus, the means by which one acts reach their extremity.

Therefore, when action elicits no response, one resorts to rolling up their sleeves; when even that elicits no response, punishments, armor, and weapons inevitably follow in succession. This is the beginning of chaos and the attenuation of loyalty and trust. All these arise from placing knowledge and cleverness before the Dao, not recognizing virtue, benevolence, righteousness, or ritual. These are but the blossoms of the Dao and the beginning of the foolishness of the common people—those with true wisdom do not dwell in them. For blossoms are insubstantial, while substance is thick; how can a true person of greatness not know to dwell in thickness?

Those who wish to dwell in thickness must abandon the pursuit of knowledge and cleverness; only then can nonaction and the great Dao be approached.

Yan Junping’s Exegesis:
Emptiness, nonbeing, and nonaction that open and guide the myriad things—this is called the Dao.
A person’s clarity and stillness, responding without being confined to any particular action—this is called virtue.
A person’s universal love for the myriad things, bestowing boundlessly—this is called benevolence.
A person’s ordering of names, rectifying reality, and handling affairs appropriately—this is called righteousness.
A person’s humility, deference, reverence, and harmony in maintaining order—this is called ritual.
These five are all but the vestiges of the Dao, not the ultimate attainment. The ultimate attainment transcends even oneness—how could there be five? Thus, the root of emperors and kings lies in the Dao as their origin and virtue as their beginning. When the Dao is lost, virtue follows; when virtue is lost, benevolence follows; when benevolence is lost, righteousness follows; when righteousness is lost, ritual follows; when ritual is lost, chaos follows. These five are the Dao embodied as one, yet the duration of a ruler’s reign depends on them.

Thus, what they do is not what they desire; what they seek is not what they attain. They do not devote themselves to the natural but instead to the petty and thin. Ritual as an affair is contradictory within and without; its blossoms flourish while its substance wanes, its branches thrive while its root declines. Ritual is thinner than loyalty, lighter than authority, weaker than trust, inferior to righteousness, and does not reach the level of virtue or benevolence. It is the dregs of governance and the origin of chaos, the source of deceit and pretense, the cause of resentment and conflict. Thus, Zhuangzi said: "The sage practices the teaching without words. The Dao cannot be attained; virtue cannot be reached. Benevolence can be practiced; righteousness can be diminished; ritual is mutual pretense." This is what he meant.

Han Fei’s Explanation:
"Benevolence" refers to the heart’s spontaneous delight in loving others, its joy in others’ good fortune and aversion to their misfortune—an impulse the mind cannot suppress, not pursued for recompense. Thus it is said: "The highest benevolence acts without purpose."

"Righteousness" denotes the proper order between ruler and minister, superior and inferior; the distinctions between father and son, noble and base; the conduct between acquaintances and friends; the boundaries of closeness and distance, inner and outer. A minister serves his lord fittingly when the inferior embraces the superior; a son serves his father fittingly when the base honors the noble; acquaintances and friends fittingly assist one another; the close are fittingly within, the distant without. "Righteousness" means acting as is fitting. Since it is fitting, one acts accordingly. Thus it is said: "The highest righteousness acts with purpose."

"Ritual" is the means by which inner feelings are expressed outwardly, the patterned ornament of collective righteousness, the interaction between ruler and minister, father and son, the distinction between noble and base, worthy and unworthy. When the heart harbors feelings that remain unexpressed, one hastens with hurried steps and humble bows to make them clear. When genuine love is not perceived, one employs fine words and elaborate phrases to affirm it. Ritual is the external adornment that clarifies the internal. Thus it is said: "Ritual is the outward expression of inner feeling."

In all things, when people are moved by external objects, they do not recognize the ritual that pertains to themselves. The multitude practices ritual to honor others, and thus their enthusiasm waxes and wanes. The noble person practices ritual for the sake of his own person, and thus the spirit regards it as the highest ritual. The highest ritual is spiritual, but the multitude is divided in purpose and cannot respond in kind. Thus it is said: "The highest ritual is acted, yet none respond." Though the multitude is divided, the sage restores reverence and exhausts the rites of hand and foot without decline. Thus it is said: "One rolls up one’s sleeves and persists in it."

Ritual is the outward expression of inner feeling; ornament is the embellishment of substance. The noble person embraces feeling and discards outward form, favors substance and dislikes adornment. Those who rely on outward form to assess feeling reveal their own foul feelings; those who require adornment to judge substance reveal their own weak substance. How can this be demonstrated? The jade of He Shi is not adorned with five colors; the pearl of Duke Sui is not embellished with silver or gold. Their substance is supremely beautiful, and no object suffices to adorn them. Objects that require adornment to be esteemed possess an inherently unbeautiful substance.

Thus, between father and son, though ritual is fully observed, it remains unclear. Thus it is said: "Ritual is thin." All things cannot flourish simultaneously—this is the case with yin and yang; principle seizes and bestows—this is the case with authority and virtue; where substance is thick, outward form is thin—this is the case with the ritual between father and son. From this perspective, the more elaborate the ritual, the more the sincere heart declines.

Thus, those who practice ritual seek to communicate the unadorned heart of others. The multitude practices ritual: if others respond, they rejoice lightly; if others do not respond, they resent and blame. Now, those who practice ritual seek to communicate the unadorned heart of others while relying on distinctions that invite reproach—how can there be no conflict? Where there is conflict, there is chaos. Thus it is said: "Ritual is the thinness of loyalty and trust, and the origin of chaos."

*Brush Notes*:
The origin of chaos begins with folly; this is an extreme statement on the pernicious effects of ritual and cunning. Zhuangzi cited these words of Laozi and discussed them, saying: "Now that one has become a thing, to return to the root—is it not difficult? Yet it is easy for the great person. Nevertheless, having returned to the root, what is a thing and what is not a thing?" Thus he further said: "What cannot but be concealed is affairs; what cannot but be distant is righteousness; what cannot but be broadened is benevolence; what cannot but be accumulated is ritual; what cannot but be exalted is virtue; what cannot but be made easy is the Dao. Though these several matters differ in rank and sequence, when the sage employs them, they are all the Dao. For the sage, a hundred concerns converge in a single return, and both boundaries dissolve—how could there be any distinction of this and that, any rejection or selection?"

*Attaining the One*:
Heaven attains the One and thereby becomes clear; earth attains the One and thereby becomes stable; spirits attain the One and thereby become numinous; valleys attain the One and thereby become full; the myriad things attain the One and thereby come to life; lords and kings attain the One and thereby become the standard for the world. The essence of their attainment is the same.

If heaven were not clear, it would fear splitting; if earth were not stable, it would fear erupting; if spirits were not numinous, they would fear vanishing; if valleys were not full, they would fear drying up; if the myriad things were not alive, they would fear perishing; if lords and kings were not the standard and exalted in nobility, they would fear stumbling.

Thus, nobility takes humility as its root, and height takes lowliness as its foundation. Therefore, lords and kings refer to themselves as "the orphaned," "the widowed," and "the unworthy." Is this not taking humility as their root? Is it not so? Thus, to attain the utmost in chariots is to have no chariots. One does not desire to be like jade, lustrous and fine, or like stone, coarse and rough.

Su’s Commentary:
The One is the Dao. All things that become things do so through the Dao. The people of the world see things and forget the Dao. Heaven knows only its clarity, earth only its stability, spirits only their numinosity, valleys only their fullness, the myriad things only their life, and lords and kings only their role as the world’s standard. They do not know that what enables them to attain these is the presence of the Dao.

"Attaining" means reaching the utmost.

Heaven, without the One, would not suddenly split; earth, without the One, would not suddenly burst forth; spirits, without the One, would not suddenly vanish; the myriad things, without the One, would not suddenly perish; and lords and kings, without the One, would not suddenly fall. Yet their ultimate end must reach this point.

The greatness of heaven and earth and the loftiness of lords and kings all arise from the attainment of the One. But what, after all, is this One? It cannot be seen when looked for, nor grasped when sought—it is the most subtle of heaven and earth. This is what is called "base and lowly," the root. Thus, when lords and kings refer to themselves as "the orphaned," "the widowed," and "the unworthy," they are merely elevating the root and disregarding the branches.

The wheel’s rim, spokes, canopy, axle, crossbar, yoke, hub, and linchpin come together to form a chariot. Each part can be counted, yet the chariot itself cannot be counted—only then do we realize that "nothingness" is what makes the chariot. This is what is meant by "using the absence of things as their function."

Are heaven and earth then made by calling heaven "heaven" and earth "earth"? Are lords and kings made by calling nobility "nobility"? Within the great and the lofty, the One resides. This is what makes them heaven and earth, lords and kings, yet no one knows it.

Thus, the One dwells in the noble yet is not noble; it dwells in the base yet is not base—unlike jade, which is lustrous and fine yet cannot be base, or stone, which is coarse and rough yet cannot be noble.

---

Lü’s Commentary:
The Dao is merely the One. Those who attain it make it separate from the Dao, no longer one. Only by attaining it without attachment is it called "attaining the One." All things that attain the One are no different.

Look up, and it is heaven—attaining the One with clarity, it covers all without collapsing. Look down, and it is earth—attaining the One with stability, it bears all without sinking. Spirits, formless and utterly still, attain the One and thus become wondrously responsive. Valleys, formed yet utterly empty, attain the One and thus respond to all that fills them. The myriad things, in their multitude, attain the One and thus live without end. Lords and kings, in their loftiness, attain the One and thus govern the world’s movements as its standard.

Thus, the One cannot but be attained. For this reason, the One is what enables heaven’s clarity, earth’s stability, spirits’ numinosity, valleys’ fullness, the myriad things’ life, and lords and kings’ role as the world’s standard. If heaven had no clarity, it would split; if earth had no stability, it would burst; if spirits had no numinosity, they would vanish; if valleys had no fullness, they would dry up; if the myriad things had no life, they would perish; and if lords and kings had no standard, would they not fall, knowing only lofty nobility?

Therefore, nobility takes humility as its root—without the base, there would be no nobility to stand upon. Loftiness takes lowliness as its foundation—without the low, there would be no height to rise from. Thus, when the noble is without its nobility and the lofty without its loftiness, is this not how lords and kings serve as the world’s standard without falling?

This is why they call themselves "the orphaned," "the widowed," and "the unworthy"—titles despised by others as base—yet use them as their noble and lofty designations, for their root lies therein. Thus, when one pursues multiplicity to its utmost and reaches the One, nobility becomes no different from baseness, and lowliness no longer separates from loftiness. Praise arises from the absence of praise; when praise is no praise, blame is no blame. When praise is no praise, it cannot be made noble; when blame is no blame, it cannot be made base. Jade can be made noble by polishing it, and stone can be made base by its coarseness—yet one does not desire to be lustrous like jade or rough like stone, for such things cannot be made noble or base.

---

Xizhai’s Commentary:
Confucius said, "My Way is threaded through with a single One." What is this One?

Xizhai’s Commentary:
Heaven’s clarity, Earth’s tranquility, the spirit’s numinosity, the valley’s fullness, the myriad things’ vitality, and the lords and kings’ steadfastness—when one pursues these to their utmost, there is nothing that does not become the same. To know why Heaven is clear is to know why Earth is tranquil; to know why the spirit is numinous is to know why the valley is full; to know why the myriad things are vital is to know why lords and kings are steadfast. The *Yi* says: "The world’s paths diverge yet converge as one; its thoughts are many yet reach the same end." What thought, what deliberation is there? It speaks of that which has never not been one.

If one does not know the One, one must set oneself apart. Setting oneself apart, one severs from things. Lords and kings sever from things, and things in turn sever from them. Thus, nobility takes lowliness as its root, and loftiness takes lowness as its foundation. Lords and kings call themselves "the orphaned," "the widowed," and "the unworthy"—is this not knowing that nobility takes lowliness as its root? The hub, spokes, canopy, axle, crossbar, yoke, and nave assemble to form a chariot. Each part has its name, yet the chariot cannot be named. Benevolence, righteousness, ritual, and wisdom combine to form the Dao. Benevolence and righteousness can be named, yet the Dao cannot be named. Rewards, punishments, penalties, and governance combine to form order. Rewards and punishments can be named, yet order cannot be named. If there is something to grasp, such that its traces appear externally, then the noble—like jade—and the base—like stone—can both be pointed to and named, and people thus begin to distinguish between noble and base.

Hongfu’s Commentary:
Lords and kings, not knowing the Way of attaining the One, are no different from commoners, yet they cannot avoid exalting themselves as noble. Those who exalt themselves will stumble, for they undermine their foundation; those who esteem themselves as noble will stumble, for they reject their root. Why? The principle of attaining the One is neither lowly in commoners nor lofty in lords and kings. In commoners, one may speak of nobility; in lords and kings, one may speak of lowliness—it is only that they do not know this. Now, the wheel, spokes, canopy, axle, crossbar, yoke, hub, and linchpin assemble to form a chariot. People see these many parts—when has there ever been a chariot? Yet they name it "chariot" and do not call it "wheel, spokes, canopy, axle, crossbar, yoke, hub, and linchpin." From this, one may understand what is meant by high and low, noble and base. People see that there is noble and base, high and low, yet do not know that these attain the One. When has there ever been high and low, noble and base? Those who exalt themselves cannot be lowly; those who esteem themselves as noble cannot be base. If they cling to what they see and cannot attain the One, then they are merely lustrous like jade or rough like stone.

"Reversion is the movement of the Dao; weakness is the function of the Dao. The myriad things in the world are born of being; being is born of non-being."

Xu Qi’s Note:
"Reversion" means that when movement reaches its extreme, it must return—this is its reversion. It is precisely because of movement that there is reversion.

Su’s Commentary:
When one restores their nature, they become still. Yet in their quiescence, they do not move, and when stimulated, they penetrate all things under Heaven—this is the origin of movement. The Dao is formless and soundless. Nothing in the world is weaker than the Dao, yet nothing in the world is stronger than it. This is how it can function through the myriad things. The world does not know that stillness is movement or that weakness is strength, so it is told of the origin of things: all things under Heaven hear of the mother controlling the child; none have heard of the child controlling the mother.

Jiefu’s Explanation:
The function of the Dao lies in weakness, which is nothing but emptiness. Observing it in Heaven: the wind that surpasses me, the wind that yields to me—both prevail over me. Its movement through the great void may be called weak, yet there is no thing it does not stir, no form it does not sway. Thus, the wind’s function lies in weakness. Observing it on Earth: if you breach it to the east, it flows east; if you breach it to the west, it flows west. Water, entrusting itself to the deep abyss, may be called weak, yet it dwells where others despise and attacks the firm and strong—none can surpass it. Thus, water’s function lies in weakness.

It also says: reversion is not the same as movement, yet there is what may be called movement—movement through reversion. Weakness is not the same as strength, yet there is what may be called strength—for weakness is what enables strength. Though it speaks of reversion without mentioning stillness, and of weakness without mentioning strength, when it speaks of movement, one knows that reversion is stillness; when it speaks of weakness, one knows that function is strength. The myriad things are born of being; being is born of non-being—it is like this.

Lü’s Commentary:
The Dao circulates through the myriad things, not that it does not pass away, but its movement always lies in reversion—this is what is meant by "the pivot beginning to grasp the center of the ring to respond endlessly." Its movement through Heaven and Earth is not that it is not strong, but its function always lies in weakness—this is what is meant by "the world’s softest galloping through the world’s hardest, entering where there are no gaps." Thus, the myriad things are born of being; being is born of non-being. Only being can generate the world’s things, yet non-being can generate the world’s being. From this, one knows that the Dao’s movement lies in reversion and its function lies in weakness. If one wishes to revert and be weak, there is nothing but to attain the One and pursue it to the utmost of non-being.

Zhao Zhijian’s Commentary:
"Reversion" means returning to the root. Ordinary people take change and novelty as movement, but those who follow the Dao abandon the branches and return to the root—thus it is said, "the movement of the Dao." It is like the body at ease and the mind at rest, not moving. Abandoning being to return to non-being is called movement—just as distance is called "returning" when one has not gone far, for the name of distance arises from non-being. Now, one has also not come near, yet it is called "returning," for the name arises from the body’s presence. The Dao has no coming or going, yet it has the name of "distance" and "return." The mind is originally at peace, yet it has the meaning of "reversion" and "movement." Those whose inner minds are empty and still, and whose outer conduct is soft and weak, practice the conduct of returning to the root—thus it is said, "weakness is the function of the Dao." Being is qi; though things are not yet formed, this qi already exists—thus it is said, "being." Heaven, Earth, and the myriad dharmas are born from the one qi—thus it is said, "born of being." Non-being is the Dao; the one qi is born from the Dao—thus it is said, "born of non-being." Tracing the source of generation to its utmost points to the Dao as the extreme. It desires scholars to return their minds to what precedes this.

Xizhai’s Commentary:
The world’s myriad things are born and become being; only by returning from being to non-being do they become the Dao. Thus it is said, "reversion is the movement of the Dao." The world’s being must be strong; returning from strength to weakness allows one to gradually seek the Dao. Thus it is said, "weakness is the function of the Dao."

*Bisheng*:
The world’s things are born of being—this is what is meant by "the named, the mother of the myriad things." Being is born of non-being—this is what is meant by "the nameless, the beginning of Heaven and Earth." Non-being must give birth to being, so it esteems reversion. Reversion is returning to non-being. Being is born of non-being, so it esteems weakness. Weakness is the semblance of non-being.

The superior person hears the Dao and diligently practices it; the middling person hears the Dao and treats it as if it exists and yet does not; the inferior person hears the Dao and laughs greatly at it. If it is not laughed at, it would not suffice to be the Dao. Thus the established saying has it:

The luminous Dao seems obscure;
Advancing in the Dao seems like retreating;
The level Dao seems uneven;
Supreme virtue resembles a valley;
Great whiteness seems sullied;
Abundant virtue seems insufficient;
Established virtue seems indolent;
True essence seems altered;
The great square has no corners;
The great vessel is late in completion;
The great tone has scarce sound;
The great image has no form.
The Dao is hidden and nameless.

Only the Dao is good at bestowing and yet accomplishing.

*Annotations:*
建立: to establish. 偷: indolent, negligent. 渝: *yú* (羊朱反), to change, alter. *Zhuan Yi* 音义 states that the ancient text reads *shū* (输). *Guangya* says *shū* means foolish; some write it as *yú* (揄), and Dong Yu writes it as *yáo* (揺). Here, following Wang Bi and Fu Yi, it is written as *yú* (渝). 贷: to lend temporarily, not to give permanently; Zhao Zhijian says, "To *dài* is to borrow briefly, not to bestow forever." 且: provisional, not enduring. It is desired that those who receive the loan do not remain long, and those who attain completion do not abide permanently, so that they may swiftly return to the Dao.

*Commentary on "Scarce Sound":*
The *Yijing* says, "That which is above form is called the Dao"—the Dao pervades beyond form. "That which is below form is called the vessel"—the vessel is confined within form. The superior person knows the subtle and the manifest, pervades beyond form, and thus hears the Dao and believes it, then diligently practices it. The middling person stands at the threshold of the subtle and the manifest, between Dao and vessel, and thus hears the Dao with doubt and half-belief, treating it as if it exists and yet does not. The inferior person knows the manifest but not the subtle, is confined within form, and thus hears the Dao and laughs greatly at it—not only laughing but also rejecting it.

Now, the Dao is subtle, wondrous, profound, and unfathomable. If it were not rejected and laughed at by the inferior person, it would not suffice to be the Dao. Those who embody the Dao unite with the sun and moon in their steadfast brightness, yet their light does not dazzle—this is why the luminous Dao seems obscure. They unite with Heaven and Earth in their simplicity and ease, yet their function cannot be reduced to fixed patterns—this is why the level Dao seems uneven. They unite with the four seasons in their cyclical movement, yet their activity must return to the profound and wondrous—this is why advancing in the Dao seems like retreating. They constantly dwell in lowliness and cannot be altered—this is why supreme virtue resembles a valley. They constantly reside in turbidity and cannot be stained—this is why great whiteness seems sullied. They clothe and shelter all under Heaven yet do not claim their benevolence—this is why abundant virtue seems insufficient. They give life to and nurture the myriad things yet do not display their merit—this is why established virtue seems indolent. They restore their nature to govern their emotions—this is why true essence seems altered. They rectify the internal and act in accordance with...

The external aligns with the great square that has no corners. Like a clumsy artisan who shapes the world through pottery yet does not seek immediate results—this is why the great vessel is slow to complete. Through wordless teaching, they move and inspire the myriad things without resorting to petty speech—this is why the great sound is faint. In acting without action, none perceive their function—this is why the great image has no form. In doing without doing, none recognize their essence—this is why the Dao is hidden and nameless. Only those who skillfully aid and lend to the myriad things without demanding repayment—thus do the myriad things receive their life and completion yet do not know this virtue. Therefore, when lesser scholars hear of this Dao, they laugh at it, not believing it could be so.

Su's Annotation:
The Dao has no form and cannot be seen; it has no sound and cannot be heard. Without first knowing the vastness of the myriad things, unbounded and unobstructed, one’s vision remains clouded, and even with insight, disbelief may persist. Thus, when lesser scholars hear of the Dao, they deem it empty, absurd, and fanciful, and laugh at it. Middling scholars hear of the Dao and waver with it—appearing and disappearing, advancing and retreating—ever doubtful. Only those who see it clearly and completely then practice it diligently, serving it without weariness. Confucius said, *"To speak to him without his growing weary—was it not Yan Hui?"* This is what is meant by the superior scholar.

To establish and set forth: The ancients who expounded teachings held such views, and Laozi adopted them in what follows. It illuminates all without being investigative. Though it does not act, none in the world can surpass its swiftness. Whether leveling or filling, wherever it reaches becomes even, yet it never erodes. Supreme virtue does not assert virtue, like a valley’s emptiness. The purest white appears tainted—this describes the scholar who scorns impurity, not the sage. Vast virtue seems insufficient, for when it is boundless and cannot be further augmented, it simply remains thus, not truly vast. Established virtue appears negligent, for it follows the natural course of things without imposing itself—outwardly seeming indolent, yet inwardly firm. True substance appears mutable, for the sage embraces the spirit, adapting to change without losing constancy—outwardly seeming altered. The great square has no corners, preserving its fullness without sharp edges. The great vessel is slow to complete, for its magnitude precludes immediate use. The great sound is beyond hearing, not something the ear can grasp. The great image has no form, not something the eye can perceive. The Dao resides in all these, manifesting everywhere. These twelve examples reveal the Dao in concrete affairs, yet the Dao in its totality remains hidden in namelessness. Only by dwelling in it can one extend its abundance to supply what is lacking, so that all things depend on it to reach completion.

Lü’s Annotation:
Confucius said, *"To hear the Dao in the morning, one may die in the evening without regret."* Thus, for one who has heard the Dao, life and death cannot burden the mind—how could mere knowledge suffice? The superior scholar truly hears the Dao. Having truly heard it, doubt vanishes, and only diligent practice remains. But what does diligent practice entail? It means observing within the heart to align with what has been heard. The middling scholar hears the Dao but does not fully grasp it, wavering between presence and absence. Such uncertainty prevents steadfast practice, leading only to sporadic effort.

The lesser scholar hears the Dao but does not believe in it. Thus, he laughs greatly at it. The reason for his great laughter is that he seeks it through human emotions and fails to attain it. If the Dao could be sought through emotions, he would not laugh at it. Therefore, it is said: *"If it were not laughed at, it would not suffice to be the Dao."* Those who established teachings in ancient times all declared that the Dao cannot be sought through emotions. Only in profound obscurity does one alone perceive clarity; only in utter silence does one alone hear harmony. This is called *"illuminating the Dao."*

If one obscures harmony with right and wrong and rests in it, then in the celestial balance, nothing is not so, and nothing is not right. This is called *"the level Dao."* If one acts for the Dao by daily diminishing, diminishing further until reaching non-action, this is called *"advancing the Dao as if retreating."*

The highest virtue is non-action without contrivance. Yet its responses are like echoes—is this not like a valley? The purest white is cleansed and examined without blemish. Yet it bears the world’s filth—is this not like disgrace? The most expansive virtue is vast, containing all without limit, yet it never displays itself, never asserts itself, never boasts, never exalts itself—is this not like insufficiency?

One who excels in establishing virtue cannot be uprooted, for it is established in the constant of non-being, and thus appears steadfastly present. Therefore, it is said: *"Establishing virtue seems negligent."* Holding to one’s nature and embracing the spirit, one wanders among worldly customs without being startled. Therefore, it is said: *"Authenticity seems mutable."*

The great square has no corners. Its vastness dissolves the four directions into the unfathomable, without east or west, beginning in profound obscurity and returning to great penetration—this is the cornerless vastness of the great square. Great accomplishment seems deficient. Daily reckoning finds it insufficient, yet yearly reckoning finds it abundant—this is the late completion of the great vessel.

The great sound is inaudible when listened to, hence it is called *"rare sound."* The great image is invisible when looked upon, hence it is called *"formless."* All these are the Dao. Yet it is called *"illuminated yet seeming obscure,"* *"level yet seeming uneven,"* *"advancing yet seeming retreating,"* even to the point where sound is rare and image is formless—name and reality constantly appear opposite.

Only the Dao is capable of this. Thus, when one uses it to benefit others, one’s own abundance increases; when one gives it to others, one’s own wealth multiplies. To give life, to nurture, to grow, to cultivate, to mature, to ripen, to nourish, to shelter—none of these are apart from the Dao. Therefore, it is said: *"Only the Dao excels in bestowing and completing."*

Yuanze’s Annotation:
I have privately discussed this chapter, saying that the Dao is not distant from people, yet the world cannot perceive it because they lack illumination. Thus, it begins with *"illuminating the Dao."* The great Dao is utterly level, yet only the illuminated can follow it, so next it speaks of *"the level Dao."* Advancing in the Dao, one returns to the constancy of nature and destiny—this is the virtue of non-virtue. Thus, it next speaks of *"the highest virtue."*
