The highest virtue is self-attained and differs from the myriad things. Thus, it is unstained and white. Because its whiteness naturally distinguishes it from things, how can it be like a valley? Therefore, it next speaks of *"seeming disgrace."* If one is not disgraced yet remains pure, this is the narrowness of Boyi. How can it be expansive? Thus, only after *"seeming disgrace"* does it speak of *"expansive virtue."*

With the highest virtue and expansiveness, one can walk with others yet serve as their lord and teacher. Therefore, it says: *"Establishing virtue seems negligent."* Negligence means not being anxious. There are those who establish virtue, yet in establishing it, they depart from the root and approach falsity. Thus, the essential point lies in not altering one’s true nature. Therefore, it next speaks of *"authenticity seeming mutable."*

By this sequence, the embodiment of the Dao within oneself is complete. Therefore, it says: *"The great square has no corners."* With the great square as one’s body, one can respond to the boundless transformations and extend one’s influence to others. Therefore, it says: *"The great vessel."*

The great vessel is achievement. The perfected person treats chaff and dregs as vessels, yet the vessel is never not great. When Confucius deemed Guan Zhong a *"small vessel,"* was not the achievement of emperors and kings the great vessel? Though the Dao acts in this way, its essence is that it never acts, never possesses.

---

Thus it says: *"The great tone,"* *"the great image."* Yet having exhausted all this, one can unite with the Dao and fulfill the needs of the myriad things, completing their natures.

Alas, this Dao—how can it be known by knowledge, let alone expressed in words? Yet what I have discussed here is but its rough outline.

**Xizhai’s Commentary:**
The superior person, upon hearing the Dao, though the multitude may mock and deride, cannot be made to cease their diligence. The middling person, upon hearing the Dao, though they may not lack belief, cannot be made to cast off doubt. The inferior person, upon hearing the Dao, even if a sage were to rise again, could not be made to refrain from laughter. For those fixated on being are everywhere under heaven. They take being as real, and now they are told that being is not real while non-being is true—who would not doubt and laugh? Thus, that they do not laugh is insufficient to prove the Dao; it is but the natural principle of things and not cause for surprise.

The diligence of the superior person is not diligence by effort but diligence born of insight. The laughter of the inferior person is not laughter by choice but laughter born of blindness. Those who neither rise to diligence nor sink to laughter cannot avoid seeing yet cannot fully trust what they see; even if one wished them not to doubt, it could not be achieved.

Of old, those who established teachings were thus: One who illuminates the Dao illuminates it from within, not through the brightness of form, so that externally nothing of its illumination may be seen. Thus it says: *"Its brightness seems dim."* One who advances in the Dao advances of itself, not through force, so that externally nothing of its advance may be known. Thus it says: *"Its advance seems retreat."* The Dao of the level makes nothing uneven; what others deem level is but their own leveling. Thus it says: *"It seems uneven."* The supreme virtue contains all; though others may embrace the strange and bizarre, one regards them with unity. Thus it says: *"It seems like a valley."* Great whiteness does not stain its nature; if the nature remains unstained, harmonizing with dust is possible. Thus it says: *"It seems disgraced."* Broad virtue must perceive the great; if one perceives the great, one cannot be made to cease. Thus it says: *"It seems insufficient."* One who establishes virtue does so by inwardly confirming their nature; with inner nature established, one’s response to things is necessarily simple. Thus it says: *"It seems negligent."* One who embodies authenticity does not pursue externals; not pursuing externals, one goes only where one wills. Thus it says: *"It seems mutable."* The great square has no corners; it cannot be examined by edges. The great vessel is late in completion; its completion cannot be exhausted. The great tone is scarce of sound; it cannot be known by sound. The great image has no form; it cannot be grasped by form or vessel.

The middling and inferior persons, bound by what they see, judge the traces of the Dao as if they were real—real dimness, real retreat, real unevenness, real disgrace, real negligence, real mutability. Those who do not mock and ridicule are few indeed.

For the great Dao is without name, hidden and unseen. When it bestows through partial completion, that is but its smallest aspect, not its reality. The superior person, knowing the Dao is nameless, disregards its partial bestowals and perceives its greatness, and thus cannot be judged by its name. The middling and inferior persons cling to its partial bestowals and completions, using them to judge the whole—this is why the more they judge, the further they stray.

**Bisheng’s Notes:**
The superior person is tranquil, silent, empty, and non-active. Diligence in this is the utmost of diligent practice, yet in truth, there is nothing practiced. This is what is called *"natural release from bondage."* How could the inferior person comprehend it? Junping said: What the middling person hears is not the utmost beauty; what the inferior person sees is not the utmost goodness. What the middling person doubts and the inferior person laughs at—these are the true beauty and goodness.

*"The Dao gives birth to the One; the One gives birth to the Two; the Two gives birth to the Three; the Three gives birth to the myriad things. The myriad things carry yin and embrace yang, blending their qi to attain harmony. What people despise are orphanhood, loneliness, and unworthiness, yet kings and lords take these as their titles. Thus, things may be diminished yet thereby increased, or increased yet thereby diminished. What others teach, I also teach: 'The strong and violent do not meet a natural death.' I will take this as the father of teachings."*

**Annotations:**
All moving creatures have their backs turned toward the rear—yin, stillness—and their ears, eyes, mouth, and nose positioned in front—yang, movement. Thus it says *"carry yin and embrace yang."* Plants turn away from cold and toward warmth, with their qi circulating between. Wood cut off from water is called a *liang*; a wooden beam bearing a roof is also called a *liang*, taking its name from its strength. Thus it says *"strong and violent."* The *Inscription of the Golden Man* states: *"The strong and violent do not meet a natural death; the competitive inevitably encounter their match."* The ancients used this as a teaching, and I also teach it. Yet Laozi alone venerates it as the *"father of teachings,"* just as he calls the Dao the *"mother of the myriad things."* The mother governs nurture; the father governs instruction. Thus, when speaking of generation, it is called *"mother";* when speaking of teaching, it is called *"father."*

**Su’s Commentary:**
The Dao is neither one nor two, yet when it pairs with things, the Dao is one while things are not. Thus, the Dao is named *"One,"* yet the Dao is not one. One and one make two; two and one make three. Proceeding thus, the myriad things are born. Though things number in the tens of thousands, none do not carry yin and embrace yang, blending their qi to attain harmony. This is because things are born from the Three, and the Three are born from the One—such is the natural principle. People of the world, not knowing the origin of the myriad things, all despise the small and lowly while prizing the great and noble. Yet kings and lords, in their exalted position, refer to themselves as *"orphan,"* *"lonely,"* and *"unworthy."* The enlightened of antiquity already understood this.

**Lü’s Commentary:**
The Dao in the world has nothing to pair with; if something pairs with it, that is but the One. Thus it says *"the Dao gives birth to the One."* Having called it *"One,"* the one who names it and the named become two. Thus it says *"the One gives birth to the Two."* With one and two, there is three. Thus it says *"the Two gives birth to the Three."* If one insists on naming it, it inevitably reaches three, and with three established, the myriad things are born. Thus it says *"the Three gives birth to the myriad things."*

All that is dark and unfathomable is yin; all that is bright and visible is yang. Living things all turn their backs to the dark and unfathomable yin and face the bright and visible yang. *"Carry"* means to turn one’s back; *"embrace"* means to face. Yet there must be the blending qi to harmonize them, for yin and yang are two, while the blending qi is one. The myriad things cannot be born without the one. Thus, what people desire are carriages and caps, wealth and nobility, while what they despise are orphanhood, loneliness, and unworthiness. Yet carriages and caps are not one’s nature or destiny; they are but transient, borrowed things. To possess them as if they were fixed is to face yang without knowing how to return—this is the gravest error. The ancients who established names used what people despise as titles for kings and lords, wishing them to value what is lowly and not forget humility, to hold what is high and not forget descent, to embrace and know what they carry, to face and know what they return to, so as not to lose the proper balance.

Thus, it is solely the harmony of the blending One. Therefore, some things, when diminished, are augmented; when augmented, are diminished. Fullness invites loss; humility receives gain—this is the Dao of Heaven. What others teach, I too teach as my principle; what I teach is the Dao. Teaching without adhering to my principle is merely not the Dao. Only those who penetrate the Dao attain the One. Attaining the One, there is no self; without self, there is no contention. Precisely because there is no contention, none in the world can contend with them. Those who oppose this are but followers of death. Though the teachings of the noble person may vary, they all return to the One and the absence of self—this is the wellspring from which teaching arises. Thus it is said: "The strong and overbearing do not meet a natural death; I shall take this as the father of teaching."

**Xizhai’s Annotation:**
The Dao generates the One, and as Dao, it is One. Before the One is born, how can there be two? Without two, the One does not scatter, hence it is called "not two," signifying that it has not yet become One. When it becomes One, then there is two; with yang, there is yin; with yin and yang, there arises their interaction. Thus, with two, there is three, and with three, there is nothing that does not exist. The myriad things embrace yang as one, bear yin as two, and through the interaction of yin and yang, the blending qi harmonizes them as three. What thing does not possess these three? Yet, in essence, all must return to the Dao. The Dao is what is called "not yet One." Since its root arises from the Dao, its branches do not lose the Dao. Therefore, some things in the world, when diminished, are augmented; when augmented, are diminished. Orphanhood, loneliness, and unworthiness become titles for kings and lords—this is the augmentation born of diminution. The strong and overbearing do not meet a natural death—this is the diminution born of augmentation. Diminution and augmentation share the same source: to diminish oneself is to be augmented; to augment oneself is to be diminished. The sage never augments yet naturally takes orphanhood, loneliness, and unworthiness as titles, wishing to make people understand that the strong and overbearing do not meet a natural death. This is what is meant by "what others teach, I too teach"—it is but a response to the world, unavoidable.

The softest in the world rides and commands the hardest; "nothing" enters where there is no gap. Thus I know the benefit of non-action. The teaching of no-words, the benefit of non-action—few in the world can attain them.

**Su’s Annotation:**
To oppose hardness with hardness is to break or shatter; to oppose hardness with softness leaves the soft unyielding and the hard unharmed. In things, water exemplifies this. To enter the existent with the existent is to meet resistance and incompatibility; to enter the existent with the non-existent is to labor not and remain unperceived. In things, spirit exemplifies this. Thus, the sage, being capable of non-action, can command the strong and move through all existence.

**Lü’s Annotation:**
The softest in the world rides and commands the hardest—observed in things, water exemplifies this. "Nothing" enters where there is no gap—observed in things, qi exemplifies this. Water, by its softness and weakness, nearly approaches the Dao, yet cannot be without form. Still, it overcomes hardness where none can surpass it. Qi, by its lack of substance, verges on the numinous, yet cannot be without qi. Still, it enters where there is no gap. How much more so with the formless utmost softness and the unseen qi of the Great Simplicity? To ride and command the hardest in the world and enter where there is no gap—what could not be moved, and where could one not find ease? Thus it is said: "Thus I know the benefit of non-action." Yet the teaching of no-words and the benefit of non-action are rarely attained in the world—this is merely not knowing to return to one’s root.

**Xizhai’s Annotation:**
Things are not originally things; hardness is an artificial form. Though the hardest in the world, in the end, it must return to nothing. If the softest in the world can already ride and command the hardest, how much more can the true nothingness shatter the artificiality of existence? Thus, "nothing" can enter where there is no gap—this means treading through water and fire, entering metal and stone, yet its essence remains unscathed. The action of non-action is action through the Dao; the teaching of no-words is teaching through Heaven. None can attain these.

Which is dearer, fame or life? Which is weightier, life or wealth? Which is more harmful, gain or loss? Therefore, excessive love incurs great expense; hoarding invites heavy loss. Knowing contentment avoids disgrace; knowing when to stop avoids danger—thus can one endure long.

**Xia’s Annotation:**
"More" means weightier. Xia says: "Knowing contentment delights in what one already has as sufficient, without seeking more." Without seeking, how could there be disgrace? "Knowing when to stop" fears that further advance may bring loss—this is knowing the subtle. Knowing the subtle, how could there be danger?

**Su’s Annotation:**
To place fame after life, to value life and devalue wealth, is still not to forget the self. Those who forget the self do not even possess life, let alone fame or wealth. Yet to value life for the sake of the world is something only those who forget the self can do. Thus, the world is made to understand that fame is not worth cherishing, wealth not worth prizing, and only then does one know to value life. Knowing to value life, one then knows to guard the self—this is Laozi’s intent. Those who do not attain it see loss as harmful; once attained, the fear of losing it becomes a harm even greater than loss. Only by equalizing having and lacking, gain and loss, can one be free from harm. Excessive love leads to pursuing all means without restraint—how can there be no expense? Hoarding invites many attackers—how can there be no loss?

**Lü’s Annotation:**
The ardent scholar pursues fame, yet in doing so, maims life and harms nature, unaware that life is dearer than fame. Thus it is said: "Which is dearer, fame or life?" The greedy pursue wealth, yet in doing so, maim life and harm nature, unaware that life is weightier than wealth. Thus it is said: "Which is weightier, life or wealth?" What the scholar pursues is called "noble"; what the greedy pursues is called "base." Though the noble and base differ in their pursuits, both lose what they preserve. Thus, gaining fame or wealth while losing what one preserves is to not know that loss is more harmful than gain. Therefore, it is said: "Which is more harmful, gain or loss?" Thus, loving fame desires to honor one’s life.

To love excessively is to lose even what is most noble—this is great waste. To hoard wealth is to seek enrichment of one’s life, yet through excessive accumulation, one harms even one’s utmost abundance—this is profound loss. Only those of virtue know that the highest nobility resides within and does not depend on fame. Thus, knowing contentment, they avoid disgrace; knowing that utmost abundance resides within and does not depend on wealth, they avoid peril. Without disgrace and without peril, one may endure long.

**Xizhai’s Annotation:**
Fame is not dearer than life, nor is wealth weightier than life. Gain does not compensate for loss, yet世人 invariably covet fame, drown in wealth, and chase after gain—this stems from their lack of insight. When I love external things excessively, I deplete myself; when I hoard possessions, I burden myself. A little invites disgrace; much invites danger—regret becomes inescapable. The noble person acts otherwise: recognizing that all things are complete within oneself, they turn inward and find sufficiency. Thus, they seek nothing from without, and disgrace cannot befall them. Knowing where to stop—stopping at the utmost Dao—they act only in accord with the Dao. Thus, peril cannot befall them. This is constancy, and constancy enables endurance.

Great accomplishment seems deficient, yet its use never wears out. Great fullness seems empty, yet its use never exhausts. Great straightness seems bent; great skill seems clumsy; great eloquence seems inarticulate. Restlessness overcomes cold; stillness overcomes heat. Only clarity and stillness can serve as the norm for all under heaven.

**Su’s Annotation:**
The world deems wholeness as accomplishment, thus accomplishment must decay. It deems fullness as abundance, thus abundance must deplete. The sage aspires to great accomplishment yet does not guard against deficiency; aims for great fullness yet does not abhor emptiness. Thus, accomplishment does not decay, and fullness does not deplete.
Straightness that does not bend will break; following principle in action, though crooked, is straight. Skill that does not appear clumsy will exhaust itself; entrusting things to their nature, though clumsy, is skill. Eloquence that does not appear inarticulate will reach its limit; speaking in accord with principle, though inarticulate, is eloquence. Thus, accomplishment without deficiency, fullness without emptiness, straightness without bending, skill without clumsiness, eloquence without inarticulateness—these are like restlessness that cannot be still, stillness that cannot be restless.
Restlessness can overcome cold but not heat; stillness can overcome heat but not cold. Both are confined to one extreme and are not the true norm. Only through serene clarity, untainted by partiality—neither accomplishment nor deficiency, neither fullness nor emptiness, neither straightness nor bending, neither skill nor clumsiness, neither eloquence nor inarticulateness—can one overcome all and serve as the norm for all under heaven.

**Lü’s Annotation:**
All things begin with *shi* and end with *shi*—this is great accomplishment. Yet the beginning has no true beginning, and the end has no true end, thus it seems deficient. Only because it seems deficient does its use renew daily and never wear out. All things draw from it without exhaustion—this is fullness. Yet adding to it does not increase it, thus it seems empty. Only because it seems empty does its use provide daily without exhaustion.
Great straightness bends to preserve wholeness and yields to become straight, thus it seems bent. Great skill carves myriad forms yet does not claim skill, thus it seems clumsy. Great eloquence does not speak yet persuades, thus it seems inarticulate. There is no other reason—all stem from having a root.
What is this root? Consider cold and heat, which are the workings of heaven and earth, unavoidable by all forms. Yet a moment of restlessness can overcome cold; a moment of stillness can overcome heat. If transient restlessness and stillness can overcome the workings of heaven and earth, how much more can embodying the clarity and stillness of non-action serve as the norm for all under heaven? Thus, in its accomplishment, it seems deficient yet never wears out; in its fullness, it seems empty yet never exhausts. Its straightness seems bent yet extends; its skill seems clumsy yet excels; its eloquence seems inarticulate yet clarifies. This is called having a root.

**Xizhai’s Annotation:**
Accomplishment and deficiency, emptiness and fullness, straightness and bending, skill and clumsiness, eloquence and inarticulateness—these are all semblances of things. Only the Dao is nameless; seeking it through form yields nothing. Thus, though accomplished, it seems deficient; though full, it seems empty; though straight, it seems bent; though skillful, it seems clumsy; though eloquent, it seems inarticulate. Its accomplishment does not rely on form; its fullness does not rely on vessels; its straightness does not rely on strength; its skill does not rely on the mind; its eloquence does not rely on speech. Thus, the world, seeking it through semblances, cannot grasp it.
Most who speak of the Dao and its arts are partial: restlessness overcomes cold but not heat; stillness overcomes heat but not cold. Each has its limits. Only clarity and stillness, free from action, though not seeking to overcome things, cannot be overcome by anything under heaven. Thus it is said: "Clarity and stillness serve as the norm for all under heaven."

**Ye Mengde’s Annotation:**
Cold and heat are the *qi* of heaven and earth. If one here is restless, one can still overcome cold; if still, one can overcome heat. If one knows what overcomes what, there is nothing one cannot achieve. Clarity overcomes turbidity; stillness overcomes movement—its effects are evident. Yet if one fails to see this and seeks only what overcomes, one will never find the true norm. Only clarity and stillness serve as the norm for all under heaven.

**Wu’s Annotation:**
Taking wholeness as accomplishment, fullness as fullness, straightness as straightness, skill as skill, eloquence as eloquence—this is petty. Seeming deficient yet not whole, seeming empty yet not full, seeming bent yet not straight, seeming clumsy yet not skillful, seeming inarticulate yet not eloquent—these are the great accomplishment, great fullness, great straightness, great skill, and great eloquence.
When the Dao prevails under heaven, warhorses are retired to manure the fields. When the Dao is absent under heaven, warhorses breed at the borders. No crime is greater than indulging desire; no disaster is greater than not knowing contentment; no fault is greater than coveting gain. Thus, the contentment of knowing contentment is constant contentment.

**Xisheng’s Annotation:**
In an age when the Dao prevails under heaven, though armor and weapons exist, they are unused; though strong horses exist, they are unridden. In an age when the Dao is absent, the Son of Heaven wages war against the four barbarians, and feudal lords invade neighboring states. Thus, weapons move within borders, warhorses gallop across the countryside, mulberry and catalpa are felled for firewood, and brambles grow in the fields.
When a ruler lacks the Dao, his poison afflicts all under heaven. Tracing the source, his evils are three: seeing what he desires, he seeks it against principle—thus no crime is greater. Seeking without cease, he must harm others—thus no disaster is greater. Desiring and obtaining, his heart burns ever brighter—thus no fault is weightier. Yet without being a sage, one cannot be free of desire. With desire, one cannot be free of seeking. Seeking without knowing contentment—this is the gravest disaster.
Alas! All that is not true nature is external things—how can one possess them? Everyone has utmost sufficiency. Knowing the sufficiency of utmost sufficiency, there is nothing one lacks.

Su's Annotation:
When all under heaven abides in its proper place, there is no contention and self-governance arises—thus horses are turned back to manure fields. To display what is desirable to others is already a fault, yet those who never know contentment bring even greater disaster. Those who must obtain what they desire incur the gravest blame. Even a common man who harbors but one of these will suffer its affliction; how much more so for lords and kings who indulge in them—this is the very source of warhorses’ galloping. Only those who know contentment find sufficiency in their dwelling, and thus nothing is lacking.

Lü's Annotation:
When the world follows the Dao, the people’s wisdom and skill are exhausted in plowing and eating, and thievery and strife vanish in the absence of desire—even death is cast off. Thus it is said: "Horses are turned back to manure the fields." When the world lacks the Dao, the sight of what is desirable becomes the root of weaponry; even with fortified borders, order cannot be secured. Thus it is said: "Warhorses are born in the suburbs." Yet what is the origin of these faults? Merely the presence of desire. Thus it is said: "No crime is greater than what is desirable." From desirability arises discontent; even with surplus, one does not cease. Contentment is fortune, surplus is calamity. Thus it is said: "No disaster is greater than not knowing contentment." From not knowing contentment arises the desire to obtain; obtaining what is desired invites resentment and blame, and thus warfare never ends. Thus it is said: "No fault is weightier than the desire to obtain." Those who do not know contentment, though they may have enough, never feel sufficient; but those who know the sufficiency of sufficiency are always content—this is evident.

Xizhai's Annotation:
When the world follows the Dao, it can turn weapons into tools for the people; when the world lacks the Dao, it turns the people into weapons. One who knows the Dao can transform form into emptiness; one who does not know the Dao turns emptiness into form. What is desirable is attachment; not knowing contentment is grasping; the desire to obtain is possession. From attachment arises grasping, from grasping arises possession, and from manifold possession arises endless blame. If one knows that grasping need not extend beyond oneself, this is called knowing contentment. Knowing contentment, nothing is lacking.

Without stepping outside, one knows all under heaven; without peering through the window, one sees the Dao of heaven. The farther one goes, the less one knows. Thus the sage knows without traveling, names without seeing, and accomplishes without acting.

Su's Annotation:
The essence of inherent nature pervades the cosmos, undifferentiated by distance, past, or present. The sages of old, though they did not step outside their doors or windows, knew all things because their nature was whole. The people of this world, obscured by external things, divide their nature between ears and eyes, inwardly disturbed by body and mind, outwardly blocked by mountains and rivers. Their sight does not extend beyond their gaze, their hearing does not reach beyond their doors and windows—these slight barriers can sever and isolate them. Unaware that the sage restores nature to its fullness, they seek knowledge by going out, and thus the farther they go, the less they know. What nature reaches is not merely knowing or naming; it can also bring about accomplishment through the natural course of things without effort.

Lü's Annotation:
What makes all under heaven "all under heaven"? Seeing what makes all under heaven "all under heaven," one knows it without stepping outside. What makes the Dao of heaven "the Dao of heaven"? Seeing what makes the Dao of heaven "the Dao of heaven," one sees it without peering through the window. Now, the vastness of all under heaven is boundless; if one must go out to know it, the reach of one’s feet is limited—how much can one know? The remoteness of the Dao of heaven is unfathomable; if one must peer to see it, the reach of one’s eyes is limited—how much can one see? Thus it is said: "The farther one goes, the less one knows." Therefore, the sage, knowing what makes all under heaven "all under heaven," knows without traveling; seeing what makes the Dao of heaven "the Dao of heaven," names without seeing. Why? Because it is complete within oneself. Knowing what is not traveled, naming what is not seen—thus one accomplishes without acting.

Zhang'an's Annotation:
Stepping outside, one knows only what can be known; peering through the window, one sees only what can be seen. The vastness of all under heaven, the breadth of the Dao of heaven—how can they be encompassed by knowing or seeing? Stepping outside, one leaves this and gains some knowledge—how much can one know? Peering through the window, one focuses on that and gains some sight—how far can one see? The sage operates in silent transformation, not relying on knowing to know, thus nothing is unknown; not relying on seeing to see, thus nothing is unseen. All under heaven is where things reside, made so by circumstances. The Dao of heaven is where the Dao resides, naturally so. Its coarse aspect lies in things, its subtle aspect in the Dao—both are fully realized without leaving the present state. What need is there for stepping outside or peering through? Stepping outside implies movement; peering through implies seeing. The sage does not travel but relies on wisdom, does not see but relies on the mind. Thus the affairs of all under heaven can all be known, the subtleties of the Dao of heaven can all be named. Being able to know and name, one accomplishes without acting, bringing about what naturally comes to be.

Xizhai's Annotation:
Going out to seek heaven and earth is to seek their form. Heaven and earth cannot be exhausted by form but can be exhausted by principle. Thus the farther one goes, the less one knows. If one knows that their principle resides here, even with doors closed, it is sufficient. The sage knows that knowledge does not lie in form, thus need not travel; names do not lie in appearance, thus need not see; accomplishment does not lie in affairs, thus need not act.

In learning, one accumulates daily; in cultivating the Dao, one diminishes daily. Diminish and further diminish until reaching non-action. Through non-action, nothing is left undone. Thus, to take all under heaven, one always does so through non-affair. When there is affair, it is insufficient to take all under heaven.

[Annotation from the *Kaiyuan Commentary*: "Taking" is like governing and transforming. Non-affair is non-action. Through non-action, self-transformation occurs; through tranquility, self-rectification arises. Thus it is said: "To take all under heaven, one always does so through non-affair." Acting is ruinous; grasping is loss. Thus it is said: "When there is affair, it is insufficient to take all under heaven."]

Kumarajiva's Annotation:
To diminish is to eliminate all that is coarse until nothing remains unabandoned. Abandonment reaches the point of guarding against evil, then nothing subtle remains unremoved. Removal reaches the point of guarding against both good and evil—evil is not, good is so. Having diminished what is not, one further diminishes what is. Thus it is said: "Diminish and further diminish." Both is and is not are guarded against; emotions and desires are severed, and virtue unites with the Dao, reaching non-action. Though non-action, one allows the self-acting of the myriad things—thus nothing is left undone.

Su's Annotation:
Not knowing the Dao but pursuing learning, one’s hearing and seeing accumulate daily, yet without unity, they inevitably become burdens. Confucius said: "Hear much, select what is good and follow it; see much and remember it—this is secondary knowledge." If one knows the Dao in a single day, one regards the myriad things as nothing but illusion. Abandoning illusion to seek the restoration of inherent nature—this is called diminishing. Confucius said to Zigong: "Do you take me for one who learns much and remembers it?" "Yes," he replied. "It is not so," said Confucius. "I unify it with one thread." Abandoning illusion to seek the restoration of inherent nature can be called diminishing, yet the mind that abandons illusion still remains. Only when this mind is fully guarded, and inherent nature is pure and without residue, can one act without departing from non-action. People all have the desire to take all under heaven, thus they create affairs to seek it. Their minds manifest externally, and things resent them—thus they ultimately cannot obtain it. The sage, through non-action, has no affairs; their mind manifests externally, and things are at peace. Though they do not take all under heaven, all under heaven returns to them.

The Lü commentary states: Those who engage in learning have yet to hear the Dao. Those who have not heard the Dao and seek it cannot but be broad, hence the saying "daily increase." Those who practice the Dao have already heard the Dao. Having heard the Dao and practicing it, they aim only at non-action, hence the saying "daily diminution." Yet even in this diminution, one remains within the realm of action. Only by diminishing even the act of diminution itself does one attain non-action. Non-action means having nothing, yet "nothing is not done" is precisely how one achieves the absence of absence. This is called "abandoning learning and ending sorrow." To be without sorrow is to be spirit-like. The spirit-like is that which treats all things as things yet is not a thing itself—how then could it have any difficulty in taking all under heaven? From this perspective, taking all under heaven is always accomplished through non-action; when there is action, it is insufficient to take all under heaven. Observing how Zhuang Zhou responded to the emperor and kings, and deeply inquiring into it, one may come to understand this.

The Huang Maocai commentary states: Only when one knows all things can one speak of learning, hence learning seeks daily increase. Only after transformation is complete can one speak of the Dao, hence the Dao seeks daily diminution. Yet increase can be advanced, while diminution cannot be forced. If one has not fully guarded against the act of diminution, it is not yet true diminution. One must suddenly break free, severing past and future, to reach the state of non-action. Only then can one act without limitation, responding endlessly, and apply this to all under heaven with ease.

The Xizhai commentary states: Learning is for the sake of seeking knowledge, hence the saying "daily increase." Practicing the Dao is for the sake of eliminating illusion, hence the saying "daily diminution." If knowledge is not exhaustive, diminution is not complete, hence "increase is the means to daily diminution." If one diminishes and diminishes again until illusion is gone and inherent nature is whole, one attains non-action. Upon reaching non-action, all actions are in fact non-action—this cannot easily be discussed in terms of increase or diminution. When non-action is perfected, even taking all under heaven as one’s own would not appear as an affair. If one were to take it through action, not even a single thing could be obtained, let alone all under heaven?

The sage has no constant mind but takes the minds of the common people as his mind. To the good, he responds with goodness; to the not good, he also responds with goodness—thus his virtue is goodness. To the trustworthy, he responds with trust; to the untrustworthy, he also responds with trust—thus his virtue is trust. The sage in the world is apprehensive, blending his mind with the world. The common people all fix their ears and eyes upon him, and the sage treats them all as children.

[No constant mind: the mind has no fixed attachment. Apprehensive: pronounced *dédé* (協反), *wéi* in the departing tone (去聲). Blending: pronounced *hún* (胡本反).]

The Su commentary states: The empty and formless takes the forms of the myriad things as its form—if it were square where things are square, round where things are round, how could the formless give form to the myriad things? Thus the sage has no mind but takes the minds of the common people as his mind. Whether good or not good, he responds with goodness; whether trustworthy or not, he responds with trust. Goodness and its absence lie with them, but my way of responding with goodness never changes—this may be called the virtue of goodness. Trustworthiness and its absence lie with them, but my way of responding with trust never alters—this may be called the virtue of trust. Otherwise, if one were to favor the good and abandon the not good, trust the trustworthy and abandon the untrustworthy, how could this be called "constantly good at saving people, hence abandoning none"? The world’s judgments of good and evil, trust and deceit, are each self-righteous, leading to mutual opposition and harm, with no resolution in sight. The sage worries over this, hence his apprehensiveness, blending his mind with the world. Without good or evil, without trust or deceit, he treats all with unity. While the people fix their ears and eyes to observe the sage’s judgments, he meets them all as infants. Toward the good, he feels no delight; toward the evil, he feels no resentment. Thus, the good do not become proud, the evil do not become resentful, and all are transformed, bringing peace to the world.

The Lü commentary states: The human mind—how spirit-like it is!

Alas! Hold to it and it remains; release it and it vanishes. It comes and goes without fixed time, and none know its direction. Since holding preserves and releasing loses it, it has no constant form to be measured; since it comes and goes without fixed time, it cannot be sought in past or present; since none know its direction, it cannot be found in any place or location. Such is the nature of the spirit—so too is the human mind. The sage first attains the fullness of this mind of mine, and thus has no constant mind but takes the minds of the common people as his mind. Like a mirror, it has no constant form but takes the form of whatever it reflects. The sage regards his own mind in this way, and so regards the minds of the common people likewise. Then what constancy can there be in good or evil, trust or deceit? Therefore, the good I treat as good, and the not-good I also treat as good, knowing that the goodness or not-goodness of their minds is inconstant and thus I respond with the virtue of goodness. The trustworthy I trust, and the untrustworthy I also trust, knowing that the trustworthiness or untrustworthiness of their minds is likewise inconstant and thus I respond with the virtue of trust.

That by which things are enabled to live is called *virtue*; that by which the form and body preserve the spirit, each according to its proper pattern, is called *nature*. When nature is cultivated and returns to virtue, and virtue reaches the point of unity with the origin, then it is clear that virtue is what nature arises from, and there is no ungoodness or untrustworthiness.

The sage in the world is apprehensive without cease, blending the minds of the common people. Blending their minds means ensuring that the good and trustworthy do not consider themselves exceptional, and the not-good and untrustworthy do not abandon themselves. The common people all fix their ears and eyes on the sage’s seeing and hearing, yet the sage treats them all as infants. Treating them as infants means meeting them with kindness and serving them with generosity. Though there be the not-good and untrustworthy, he still treats them as good and trustworthy, knowing that the inconstancy of their minds is no different from his own.

Yuanze’s commentary states: Good and evil arise from partial perception; partial perception arises from self-interest. When one is impartial toward the Great Dao, though the eyes see good and evil, the mind harbors no distinction. Thus, the sage follows the world’s sentiments, forcibly establishing praise and blame, yet knows that good and evil are not their true reality. Therefore, treating the not-good as good is not out of pity or forbearance but arises from not perceiving any difference. Guarding against the reality of good and evil is true goodness. Thus, though the myriad dharmas differ, they are equally real; trust and distrust are blended into one. Knowing that all appearances are partial, he can regard the not-good as good; knowing that all appearances are real, he can regard the untrustworthy as trustworthy. When partiality knows reality, and reality knows partiality—this is how the sage’s wisdom differs from that of the common people.

Xizhai’s commentary states: How extreme it is!

The heart as a harm to the world—Zhuangzi said, "No thief is greater than virtue with a heart, and a heart with eyes." For the heart, as a thing, permeates heaven and earth, reaching everywhere without limit. Yet世人 merely take discrimination as the heart; the discriminating heart gives rise to partiality, and with partiality comes seeing, thus obstructing wholeness. This is why Zhuang Zhou called it a thief.

The sage’s heart constantly abides with the Dao. The Dao is nowhere absent, and thus his heart is likewise nowhere absent. Therefore, he has no fixed heart. When he emerges to respond to the world, he knows that all appearances follow from partiality, so the heart of acceptance and rejection never arises. What is good, he follows as good; what is not good, he also follows as good. In the world, nothing is not good—this is called "virtue-goodness." What is trustworthy, he follows as trustworthy; what is untrustworthy, he also follows as trustworthy. In the world, nothing is not trustworthy—this is called "virtue-trust." Yet he remains ever vigilant, fearing that the world’s people, with virtue possessing a heart and the heart possessing eyes, will carve and chisel, grasping and rejecting, failing to see the great whole. Thus, they take what their ears and eyes encounter as clarity, unaware that it is not true clarity.

The sage treats all as infants, nurturing them so they are not disturbed by false clarity. Then, is this false clarity not what Śākya meant by *avidyā*?

Hongfu’s commentary states: The common people have good and not-good, trustworthy and untrustworthy, yet the sage treats all as good and all as trustworthy. How could the sage ever have a heart of goodness or trust? He simply takes the hearts of the people as good and trustworthy—this is called "shared-virtue goodness," not the goodness of one person, and "shared-virtue trust," not the trust of one self. Thus, it is said: "virtue-goodness, virtue-trust." The people of the world have long been divided, each with their own heart. The sage unites the hearts of all into one. The common people fix their ears and eyes, observing others through themselves and viewing themselves through others, each asserting right and wrong, unable to unify. The sage, seeing this, neither rejoices at one nor angers at another, treating all as infants. Thus, they neither boast nor resent, all dissolve in harmony, and the world is at peace. The sage has no fixed heart, taking the hearts of the people as his own. Such is the great barrier by which the Highest governs the world, enabling effortless rule.

Emerging from life, entering death: Of those who follow life, there are thirteen; of those who follow death, there are thirteen; and of those who, while living, move toward the realm of death, there are also thirteen. Why is this? Because they cling excessively to life. It is said that those skilled in nurturing life walk on land without encountering rhinoceroses or tigers, and enter battle without avoiding armor or weapons. The rhinoceros has nowhere to thrust its horn, the tiger nowhere to place its claws, and weapons nowhere to lodge their blades. Why is this? Because they have no realm of death.

*(Emerging refers to moving from non-being into being; entering refers to returning from being into non-being. Zhuangzi said, "The myriad things all emerge from the mechanism and enter the mechanism." Elsewhere, he said, "Their emergence brings no joy; their entrance brings no reluctance." Again, he said, "There is emergence, there is entrance"—all taking emergence as life and entrance as death. The term *she-sheng* (nurturing life) is like *she-zheng* (regency) or *she-guan* (acting official)—not claiming life as one’s own possession, but temporarily overseeing it, as in provisional governance. *Encounter* means unplanned meeting. *Si* (rhinoceros) is pronounced like *si* in *Shanhaijing*: "Rhinoceroses emerge south of the Xiang River, dark in color." The *Erya* states: "In form like a wild ox, with one horn, weighing a thousand pounds.")*

Su’s commentary states: Nature has no life or death. Emergence is life; entrance is death. Those who use things to extract essence and nourish themselves belong to the realm of life. Those who harm themselves with sounds, colors, scents, and tastes belong to the realm of death. These two are already divided into the paths of life and death. I also know action but not rest, speech but not silence, thought but not prevention, exhausting oneself to the end—these are what is meant by "moving toward the realm of death." The paths of life and death are spoken of in tens: three belong to each. Is it not that the paths of life and death are nine, while the path of neither life nor death is one alone? Neither life nor death is what the *Yi* calls "serene and unmoving." Laozi speaks of the nine but not the one, allowing people to discover it for themselves, lodging the marvel of non-thought and non-action. Where there is life, there is death, so those who follow life are also those who follow death. The more people rely on life, the more the path of death dominates. The sage constantly abides in neither life nor death—how could there be a realm of death for him?

Lü’s commentary states: Those who live are followers of death; death is the beginning of life. Thus, life and death are but mutual emergence and entrance. Of those who follow life, thirteen are those who attain life through life, not by mere luck. Of those who follow death, thirteen are those who attain death through death, not by misfortune. Of the people who, while living, move toward the realm of death, thirteen are those who nourish their inner like leopards yet are devoured externally like tigers, or fortify their outer like armor yet are attacked internally by illness. They do not fail to pursue life, yet by not grasping the Way, they move toward the realm of death. Why is this? Because they cling excessively to life. Those who attain life through life or death through death need not be discussed. But those who pursue life yet reverse into death—this is where their error lies.

It is said that those skilled in nurturing life walk on land without encountering rhinoceroses or tigers, and enter battle without being struck by armor or weapons. The rhinoceros has nowhere to thrust its horn, the tiger nowhere to place its claws, and weapons nowhere to lodge their blades. Why is this? Because they have no realm of death. Laozi has already embodied this in his words, yet he speaks in doubtful terms, testing with questions. Is this not what Zhuangzi called "weighty words"? Let us examine it: People encounter tigers and rhinoceroses or are struck by armor and weapons because they have a self. If I observe my heart and know not its whereabouts, then my heart cannot be grasped. If my heart cannot be grasped, then my self and things also cannot be grasped. Internally, no self is seen; externally, no things are seen. Then what is the realm of death? What are tigers, rhinoceroses, armor, or weapons to thrust their horns, place their claws, or lodge their blades? How could one who nurtures life surpass this?

Yuanzhe’s commentary states: The perfect person knows neither death nor life, thus neither can die nor live. Therefore, it is said: "They have never died, never lived." Those who incline toward yin and yang and follow life and death, as well as those who indulge desires and invite misfortune, moving toward the realm of death—these three groups differ in their views, yet the source of their delusion from the supreme correctness of the great Dao, leading them to be bound by life and death, lies in their partiality toward life and excessive attachment to it.

Should one ask: "Those who pursue the Dao and reach emaciation—do they not contradict the profound nurturance of life?" I reply: What I call "I" is neither dead nor alive, abiding in constancy and unity; one must only cease attachment, whereas they cling to their views and thus become withered. Though their aspiration inclines toward emptiness and stillness, they remain unaware that this is the height of self-grasping.

One who has no realm of death does so because they have no life. One who has no life abides in constant life yet does not regard it as "life"—thus they have never died, never lived. The Dao reaches this point.

Here, though the form may appear to change, the spirit has never altered—how could death occur? These are the divine sages of the Middle Kingdom and the Buddhas of the Western Lands. In such a state, transformations are boundless; water and fire cannot scorch or drown, blades and blows cannot wound. Illness may exploit emptiness and strike the solid, yet nothing obstructs their passage. Desires may seek to possess them, yet how could they cause harm?

Should one ask: "Then why speak of nurturing life?" I say: To descend even one level is to be subject to life and death. Thus, nurturing life must attain this state before life abides eternally. Compared to those who embody profound virtue, this is the ultimate.

**The Brush Chariot**

Of those who follow life, thirteen in ten are those who refine the form to dwell in the world. Of those who follow death, thirteen in ten are those who indulge desires and obstruct life. Of those who move toward the realm of death, thirteen in ten are those who sever their inherent nature. In these ten parts, nine are generally those of abundant life. Since having life necessarily entails death, life is inherently the ground of death. How can one evade rhinoceroses, tigers, armor, and weapons? Only by nurturing life to attain the state of no-life. Thus, the rhinoceros’s horn finds no target, the tiger’s claws have no place to strike, and the blade’s edge has no opening to enter—why? Because there is no ground to receive them. Nine are those of abundant life; one is of no-life. Laozi, among these ten, omits one to subtly align with this principle. Yet the sage does not attain no-life by deliberately diminishing it—it is inherently no-life.

As the ancients said: "Those who cling to life may be slain; those who cling to purity may be defiled; those who cling to honor may be humiliated; those who cling to wholeness may be broken." If there is inherently no life, who can slay it? If there is inherently no defense, who can defile it? If there is inherently no honor, who can humiliate it? If there is inherently no wholeness, who can break it? Knowing this, one may enter and exit creation, playfully roaming through life and death.

---

**Cheng Ju’s Discourse**

Of all transformations in the myriad things, none is greater than life and death. For one who follows the Way and transcends the boundary of life and death, nothing remains. Does life truly come? Does death truly depart? If life is taken as real arrival, then the source from which I come should be knowable—whether from north or south, east or west, above or below. Yet these cannot be spoken of; how can one call it real arrival? If death is taken as real abiding, then the destination to which I depart should be knowable—whether mind, thing, person, or heaven. Yet these cannot be spoken of; how can one call it real departure?

Thus, my life arises without knowing its beginning, ceases without knowing its end, yet spans the ages in unbroken continuity—serene and constant. Only then does one clearly realize that I have never been born, never died. Why then be confined to the realm of death?

---

**The Dao gives them life, its virtue rears them; things give them form, circumstances complete them.** Thus, the myriad things none fail to honor the Dao and esteem its virtue. The Dao’s honor and virtue’s esteem arise not from conferred rank but are ever naturally so. Therefore, the Dao gives life, rears, nurtures, matures, refines, nourishes, and shelters them—yet claims no possession, acts without reliance, and leads without domination. This is called **profound virtue**.

---

**Hisheng’s Annotation**

Only after things are born are they reared; only after being reared do they take form; only after taking form are they completed. What gives them life is the Dao; what rears them is virtue. What shapes their material is human effort; what fulfills their function is circumstance. The myriad things honor the Dao because it gives life, and esteem virtue because it rears. Since the Dao and virtue give life and rear, they are naturally revered by the myriad things—how could this honor and esteem depend on conferred rank?

Yet the Dao is the essence of true purity, while virtue is the function of wondrous things. Essence can encompass function, but function cannot encompass essence. The Dao can embody virtue, but virtue cannot embody the Dao. Thus, receiving its essence is called life; containing its qi is called rearing; developing its form is called growth; nurturing its material is called cultivation; adjusting its completion is called refinement; measuring its use is called tempering; preserving its harmony is called nourishment; preventing its harm is called shelter. This is the Great Dao.

Having given life, it does not claim possession; having acted, it does not boast reliance; having led, it does not dominate. This is called **profound virtue**. The preceding chapter speaks of humans aligning with the Dao and virtue; this chapter speaks of the Dao and virtue aligning with humans. Thus, the wording is similar, yet the principles are interconnected.

---

**Su’s Annotation**

The Dao is the mother of the myriad things; thus, what gives life to the myriad things is the Dao. When it operates as virtue, it herds and nurtures the multitude without refusal—thus, what rears the myriad things is virtue. Yet the Dao and virtue cannot take form on their own; they manifest only through things. Things cannot complete themselves; through the interplay of near and far, hard and soft, circumstances accumulate into momentum, and then the transformations of rise and fall, order and chaos are accomplished.

Though form arises from things and completion from circumstances, without the Dao, there is no life; without virtue, there is no rearing. Thus, the Dao is honored like father and elder brother, and virtue is esteemed like lord and king—because the Dao has no rank, while virtue has a name. Those who rely on rank for honor and esteem are not truly honored or esteemed.

---

**Lü’s Annotation**

The life of the myriad things always originates from the nameless, and their rearing always lies in the One before form. The nameless is the Dao; the One before form, from which things receive life, is virtue. When they become things, they merely take form—this is not what gives life or rears. Once form exists, the naked cannot but be naked, the scaled, shelled, feathered, and furred cannot but be scaled, shelled, feathered, and furred, and they cannot but progress through youth, prime, aging, and death—all the inevitable momentum of circumstance.

Thus, it is said: "The Dao gives them life, its virtue rears them; things give them form, circumstances complete them." Circumstance arises from form, form from virtue, virtue from the Dao. The Dao and virtue are the root; form and circumstance are the branches. The root is honored, the branches base; the root is esteemed, the branches lowly. Thus, the myriad things none fail to honor the Dao and esteem its virtue. The Dao’s honor and virtue’s esteem arise not from conferred rank but are ever naturally so. This is why it can, through the softness and weakness of non-action, overcome the hardness and strength of form and circumstance. The reason kings and lords transform the myriad things lies here, not there.

Yet, though it is said that the Dao gives life, its virtue rears, things give form, and circumstances complete them, in essence, to give life, rear, nurture, mature, refine, temper, nourish, and shelter—all is the Dao. And the Dao ultimately has no name. Thus, it is said: "Gives life yet claims no possession, acts without reliance, leads without domination—this is called **profound virtue**."

---

**Yuanzhe’s Annotation**

These four—life, rearing, form, and circumstance—are all the Dao. Because each attains its proper Dao, they are merely called virtue. As virtue, it only rears. Yet the rearing that constitutes virtue, when it attains its fulfillment and takes form, becomes merely things. Once things have form, near and far interact, hard and soft engage, each following its circumstance to complete its state. Thus, virtue is the Dao’s division; things are virtue’s vessels; circumstance is the principle of things.

Understanding the Dao and virtue, one sees that form and circumstance are not worth knowing, and responding to all things becomes effortless. When commanded by heaven, one is the Son of Heaven; when commanded by the Son of Heaven, one is a lord. Having received a command, the one who issues it can demean the recipient. Only the Dao precedes the myriad things and controls their destiny—who can usurp this? Thus, it is ever naturally so.

息斋’s Annotation:
Things do not arise without the Dao, nor are they reared without virtue. From their having form to their flourishing in circumstance, all take the Dao and virtue as their master. The Dao’s exaltation and virtue’s nobility reach their utmost here—yet they do not exalt themselves in their exaltation, nor prize themselves in their prized status. Their bestowal upon things is not deliberate toward things. None commands them, yet they are ever naturally so. That they naturally give rise to and naturally rear, that they nurture, mature, nourish, and shelter—all is natural. Because it is natural, they never expect recompense from things; in giving, they do not shirk labor; in bestowing, they do not seek repayment. This is called dark virtue.

The world has a beginning that serves as the world’s mother. Having attained this mother, one thereby knows her child. Having known her child, one then guards her mother—thus, to the end of one’s days, one does not fall into peril. Block its apertures, shut its gates, and one will not toil all one’s life. Open its apertures, aid its affairs, and one will not be saved all one’s life. Seeing the small is called illumination; guarding the soft is called strength. Employing one’s light, one returns to one’s clarity—no calamity will befall one’s person. This is called inheriting the constant.

Su’s Annotation:
The nameless is the beginning of heaven and earth; the named is the mother of the myriad things. When the Dao is still nameless, it is that upon which things rely for their beginning. When it has a name, it is that upon which things rely for their arising. Thus, it is called the beginning and also the mother; its child is the myriad things. The sage embodies the Dao to encompass all things, just as knowing the mother allows one to know her child—all is clear and nothing is left unexamined. Though their wisdom can encompass all things, they never use things to obstruct the Dao, and thus they guard their mother to the end. The world all possesses this Dao, yet they constantly err by obstructing the Dao to pursue things. The eyes delight in forms, the ears in sounds—opening the heart that delights in these and aiding its affairs with them, one sinks into ruin all one’s life and cannot be saved. The sage’s way of never toiling all one’s life lies solely in blocking and shutting, never issuing forth to pursue things. The harm of delight begins small and gradually grows. Knowing the small before it becomes great and blocking it—this is called illumination. Pursuing what delights one without reflection, believing oneself strong when one is not—this is not strength. Only seeing delight and knowing to fear it is called strength. The common people open what delights them and follow things with their persons, going forth and never returning. The sage blocks and shuts, not cutting off from things but responding to them with spirit, employing their light while the self does not engage. The ear’s ability to hear, the eye’s to see, the nose’s to smell, the mouth’s to taste, the body’s to touch, the mind’s to think—all are what is called light. Light engages with things, and though things depart, clarity suffers no loss. Thus, one responds to the myriad changes without exhaustion, and calamity does not reach one’s person. Therefore, one’s constant nature remains serene, inheriting and unbroken.

Lü’s Annotation:
The world has a beginning that serves as the world’s mother—this is what the Classic means when it says, “I do not know its name; I style it the Dao.” The nameless is the beginning of heaven and earth; the named is the mother of the myriad things. The Dao is ever nameless and serves as the world’s mother—why? Because the named is merely the mother of the myriad things and is not sufficient to be the world’s mother. The nameless, the beginning of heaven and earth, gives rise to all below heaven from the nameless. Thus, it is said, “The world has a beginning that serves as the world’s mother.” Hearing the Dao is easy; attaining the Dao is difficult. Attaining the Dao is easy; guarding the Dao is difficult. Now that I have attained this mother and harmonized with it in mind—not merely hearing it but truly knowing it—I know that all things in the world are what I give rise to. Knowing that all things in the world are what I give rise to and constantly guarding this without loss, who in the world could use what I give rise to harm that from which it arises? This is why one does not fall into peril to the end of one’s days. Blocking its apertures and shutting its gates, one does not toil all one’s life—this is what it means to guard one’s mother. The heart moves within, and one indulges it—this is called having apertures. With apertures, the heart issues forth to engage with things. I block its apertures so they do not open; not opening, the heart does not issue forth. Things entice from without, and one admits them—this is called having gates. With gates, things enter and disturb the heart. I shut its gates so they are not admitted; not admitted, things do not enter. Within, nothing issues forth; without, nothing enters. Though the myriad things change endlessly before one, each returns to its root, and one remains unaware—how could there be toil? The ancients had those who could set aside the heart and employ the form—this was their way. If one opens one’s apertures and does not block them, aids one’s affairs and does not diminish them, then one’s heart becomes merely an inn for things. With nothing to rely upon, how can one not perish? This is why one is not saved all one’s life. Only by guarding one’s mother does one, upon seeing the heart’s incipient movement, remain still and unmoving. Still and unmoving, one responds and thoroughly penetrates the world’s patterns. Thus, it is said, “Seeing the small is called illumination; guarding the soft is called strength.” Guarding the utmost softness of the formless, nothing can overcome it—thus, it is said, “Guarding the soft is called strength.” Having employed one’s light to illuminate the incipient movement, one returns to one’s clarity and reverts to stillness. Then, one never opens apertures or aids affairs to the point of being unsaved—how could calamity befall? In this way, one inherits the harmony of knowing and remains profoundly unseen. Thus, it is said, “Employing one’s light, one returns to one’s clarity—no calamity will befall one’s person. This is called inheriting the constant.”

息斋’s Annotation:
The Dao is the mother of things; things are the children of the Dao. The ancients who attained the Dao could keep mother and child undivided, making Dao and things one. Things issue from the Dao; things do not differ from the Dao. Children issue from the mother; children do not differ from the mother. Things do not differ from the Dao, yet the world seeks the Dao by abandoning things. Children do not differ from the mother, yet the world seeks the mother by abandoning children. Fundamentally undivided, they divide themselves—thus, they never attain unity. If one knows that things are the Dao and children are the mother, and knows and guards this, then one meets the source everywhere without difficulty. All things do not employ the Dao themselves; employing things to abandon the Dao—this is because people open their apertures.

The aperture is a protrusion—pierce its protrusion to satisfy desire, and the heart drifts while essence wanes. Then things follow and defenses must be closed. Seal the aperture, bar the gate, and spirit will not stray outward. Returning to the origin, it naturally reverts; mother and child remain undivided, Dao and things become one. The ancients who preserved their persons and thus preserved their lives employed this Way.

The affliction of people lies in their failure to guard the small or nurture their defenses. If from the smallest matters one is vigilant, and from the first defenses one cultivates, then even the small will become clear, even the weak will grow strong. Thus it is said: "To see the small is called clarity; to know softness is called strength." Clarity is the root. Light is what clarity arises from. The original clarity is the root; its branches divide into seeing, hearing, awareness, and touch—all are its light.

The Dao flows from the root to the branches; learning seeks the root from the branches. Thus it is said: "Employ its light to return to its clarity"—this is called "inheriting the constant." The constant is the root; light arises from clarity. Now, by using light to return to clarity, it is said: "inheriting the constant."

Were I to be steadfast in awareness and walk the great Way, I would fear only deviation. The great Way is utterly level, yet the people prefer byways. The court is excessively adorned, the fields are overgrown, the granaries are empty. They wear embroidered robes, gird themselves with sharp swords, sate themselves with food and drink, and hoard surplus wealth—this is called the "bandit’s pipe," far from the Dao!

*[Annotation: "Steadfast in awareness" means to be vigilant in awareness. "Deviation" is the opposite of frugality. "Level" means flat; narrow paths are called byways. "Adorned" means cultivated; it is said in the *Zuo Zhuan*: "To manure and repair the dilapidated huts of one’s ancestors." Blue and red are "pattern"; silk is "embroidery." In the *Zhan Guo Ce*, "embroidery" is the ancient form of "brocade." "Wealth" is also written as "property." "Bandit’s pipe" was mistakenly written as "bandit’s boast"; here following the *Han Feizi* version.]*

**Han Feizi’s Explanation:**
"Excessively adorned" means the court is over-embellished. When litigation is rampant, the fields are neglected; when the fields are neglected, the granaries empty; when the granaries empty, the state grows poor; when the state is poor, the people’s customs turn to extravagance. When customs turn to extravagance, the livelihoods of clothing and food are severed; when livelihoods are severed, the people cannot but adorn themselves with cunning deceit. Adorning themselves with cunning deceit is called "wearing embroidered robes." When litigation is rampant and granaries empty, yet extravagance becomes custom, the state is harmed—like being stabbed with a sharp sword. Thus it is said: "gird themselves with sharp swords." Those who harm the state through such adornment of wisdom must enrich their private households. When private households are enriched, it is said: "hoard surplus wealth." When the state is in such a state, the foolish people cannot but imitate it. Imitating it, petty thieves arise. From this perspective, when great villains act, petty thieves follow; when great villains lead, petty thieves harmonize. The *yu* is the chief of the five notes; when the *yu* leads, bells and lutes follow. When the *yu* plays, all music harmonizes. Now, when great villains act, the vulgar people lead; when the vulgar people lead, petty thieves must harmonize. Thus, those who wear embroidered robes, gird themselves with sharp swords, sate themselves with food and drink, and hoard surplus wealth are called "bandit’s pipes."

**Xisheng’s Annotation:**
When Master Lao says, "Were I clamorously devoid of a heart that knows the Dao," he begins by desiring to align with the world, making whatever he does his sole concern—striving only to conform superficially to the world.

Should I possess even the slightest clarity of the constant Dao, I would walk the great Way, acting only where fitting—yet ever fearful of deviating from the great Way. For the great Way is like a thoroughfare: level and smooth, with no path it does not reach. Its vast straightness brooks no concern for minor detours, yet worldly men, eager for speed, take shortcuts—thus stumbling into confusion, losing their way. The sage laments this, cautious in his teachings, ever wary of guiding the people onto crooked paths that ultimately fail to align with the great Way.

Alas! Enter a state, and its governance may be known. Observe its court and halls, grandly adorned with carved and towering walls, and you will know its lord delights in earthworks and frivolous amusements. Observe its fields lying fallow, and you will know its lord favors corvée labor, seizing the people’s time. Observe its granaries empty, and you will know its lord promotes secondary crafts while neglecting fundamental industries. Observe its robes heavy with embroidery, and you will know its lord indulges in excess and crafty adornments, wasting women’s labor. Observe its girdles hung with sharp swords, and you will know its lord prizes martial valor, breeding state calamities. Observe its feasts ever sated and lavish, and you will know its lord pursues drunkenness and gluttony, forgetting the people’s affairs. Observe its wealth and goods ever in surplus, and you will know its lord delights in amassing riches, draining the people’s resources.

All these instances are the stealthy exploitation of the people’s strength for ostentatious display—thus they are called "bandit’s boasting." Such bandit’s boasting is no governance of the Dao. Yet this "stealthy exploitation" means taking in secret while the people remain unaware. Is this not what is meant by "acting only where fitting—yet ever fearful"?

Su's Annotation:
Those who embody the Dao possess neither knowledge nor action, nor any contrivance—yet things transform of themselves. Now, if one rigidly clings to knowledge and acts upon the great Dao, then contrivance and establishment arise, departing from naturalness. Here lies what is to be feared. The great Dao is level and smooth, without peril or obstruction. Yet the world, failing to comprehend, deems it circuitous and distant, preferring shortcuts in pursuit of swiftness. Thus, all who abandon naturalness to contrive and impose do so in haste. The vulgar are bright and clear, while I alone seem dim; the vulgar are sharp and discerning, while I alone appear dull. How could one further adorn the superficial, neglect the fundamental, and make contrivance their business, boasting to instruct bandits?

Lü's Annotation:
The noble person, in relation to the Dao, cannot but hollow out the heart. When the heart is hollowed to its depths, though ten thousand changes unfold before them, they remain undisturbed in stillness. What contrivance could then inspire fear? If I cannot hollow my heart and instead cling to rigid knowledge, acting upon the great Dao, then I must ever fear contrivance, and the pursuit of a path without peril becomes unattainable. How much more so for those who open their senses and expedite their affairs? The essence of the great Dao lies in knowing without knowledge—this is its utmost levelness. Yet the people seek it through knowledge, preferring shortcuts while ignorant of the proper course.

Man’s life takes food as its root, and food necessarily arises from the fields. When fields are well-tended, granaries fill; when granaries are full, food suffices; when food suffices, wealth abounds; when wealth abounds, the court is well-ordered. This is to know the source from which the root arises. Now, if one acts upon the great Dao with a heart clinging to rigid knowledge, one has already lost the mother. Losing the mother, the child is no longer one’s own. To take what is not one’s own—like sweeping the courtyard while letting the fields grow wild, the granaries empty, yet donning embroidered robes, girding sharp swords, sating oneself with food and drink, and hoarding surplus wealth—is to take what is not one’s own. Without bandit’s boasting, this could not be. How could this be the Dao?

Here follows the translation:

---

Xi Zhai’s Annotation: The Dao does not reside in knowledge—knowledge is but the machinations of the mind. The true Dao must be luminous, yet this luminosity is not knowledge; knowledge cannot attain it. Thus, when applied, it is inexhaustible; when employed, it is boundless. If I proceed along the great Dao with a heart clinging to rigid knowledge, what I know is limited, while the Dao is infinite. Trembling with fear that my application will prove insufficient—this is to dread only the act of application. The great Dao is utterly plain, yet the people prefer shortcuts. Those who prefer shortcuts make knowledge a thief.

If one acts with non-knowing, then all that is seen and heard is nothing but the Dao. How vast and expansive the heart becomes, how at ease and at rest the days! Later ages are not so. When the court is excessively adorned, this is governance; yet when the court is adorned, external embellishments multiply. When fields lie fallow, the heart is not governed; when granaries are empty, conduct is not cultivated. Then one dons embroidered robes to impress others, girds sharp swords to overawe the multitude, and amasses food, drink, and wealth without putting them to use—this is called bandit’s boasting. Thus, one strays far from the Dao.

Those who build well cannot be uprooted; those who embrace well cannot be torn away. Their descendants’ sacrifices never cease. Cultivate it in the self, and one’s virtue becomes true. Cultivate it in the family, and one’s virtue overflows. Cultivate it in the village, and one’s virtue endures. Cultivate it in the state, and one’s virtue abounds. Cultivate it in the world, and one’s virtue becomes universal. Thus, observe the self through the self, observe the family through the family, observe the village through the village, observe the state through the state, observe the world through the world. How do I know the world is thus? By this.

[Note in original: *State* was originally written as *guo* (國), but Han scholars altered it to *bang* (邦) to avoid the taboo on Emperor Gao’s name, disrupting the rhyme. Here, we follow the *Hanfeizi* version.]

Su’s Annotation: Has the world ever seen one who builds yet cannot be uprooted, or embraces yet cannot be torn away? Only the sage knows the truth of human nature and discerns the essence of things, relinquishing externals to cultivate the self. His virtue is fully accumulated, yet nothing is established, yet his building cannot be uprooted; nothing is grasped, yet his embrace cannot be torn away. Thus, even to his descendants, sacrifices never cease. Having cultivated the self, he extends its surplus outward, and even governing the world is possible. What lies beyond heaven and earth is unseen by the vulgar, yet its principle can be inferred by extension.

The ultimate cultivation of the self: observe the self through the self, observe the family through the family, observe the village through the village, observe the state through the state—all these are within my ken. But how do I know the sage observes the world through the world as I observe the self through the self? Is the self alone to be observed through the self, while the world cannot be observed through the world? Thus it is said: How do I know the world is thus? By this—meaning, by observing it through the self.

Lü’s Annotation: All things built will be uprooted; only those who follow the Dao build with constancy and nothingness, thus building well and remaining unshaken. All things embraced will slip away; only those who follow the Dao embrace the spirit with stillness, thus embracing well and remaining inseparable. Only when what is built and embraced is thus will its transmission never end—hence the descendants’ unceasing sacrifices.

The world’s so-called cultivation of virtue sometimes occurs in the state or the world, yet the true root lies in the self. Thus it is said: Cultivate it in the self, and one’s virtue becomes true. Some cultivate it in the self yet cannot extend it to the state or the world, thus: Cultivate it in the family, and one’s virtue overflows; cultivate it in the village, and one’s virtue endures; cultivate it in the state, and one’s virtue abounds; cultivate it in the world, and one’s virtue becomes universal.

Zhuangzi held that the Dao’s primary use is in governing the self, its surplus in governing the state, and its dregs in governing the world—this saying originates here. But how does one observe and cultivate the self? By observing the self through the self. What does it mean to observe the self through the self? When I examine what my self possesses, from whence does it arise? Then I know the origin of my self’s existence. Further, when I examine how my self observes, from whence does this observation arise? Then I know the origin of my observation. Having known the origin of my self’s existence and the origin of my observation, what is needed to cultivate the self is already sufficient, requiring nothing external. Observing the family through the family, the state through the state, the world through the world—all are like this. The ancients’ way of storing the world within the world relied on this Dao.

One whose virtue is profoundly thick is like an infant: poisonous insects do not sting it, fierce beasts do not seize it, birds of prey do not strike it. Its bones are weak, its sinews soft, yet its grasp is firm. It knows not the union of male and female, yet its organ stirs—such is the perfection of essence. It cries all day yet does not grow hoarse—such is the perfection of harmony. To know harmony is called constancy; to know constancy is called clarity.
