To augment life is called auspicious; to force qi with the heart is called strength. When things grow strong, they age—this is called not-Dao. What is not-Dao perishes early.

[Annotations in original: *Poisonous insects*—bees, scorpions, and the like—sting with their tails. *Fierce beasts*—tigers, leopards, and the like—seize with their claws. *Birds of prey*—eagles, ospreys, and the like—strike with their talons. Zhao Zhijian says: "To grasp with four fingers and the thumb is called a firm grasp." *Fang* (防): the male organ. *Hao*: level tone. *Sha* (嗄): hoarse voice. Another version reads *ai* (嗌), meaning "not hoarse." Huang Maocai says: "Ancient texts lacked the character *ai*; it was added later to *Zhuangzi*’s text." *Xiang* (祥): originally meaning "auspicious or inauspicious omens."]

Su’s Annotation: Laozi often compares the Dao to an infant, but this refers only to its essence, not its function.

The infant lies in perfect stillness, free of desire—its essence is thus complete. Yet when things approach, it does not know how to respond, so one cannot yet speak of its function. The Dao has no form or body; nothing can perceive it—how much less can it be harmed? That which causes humans to assume form is the presence of the heart-mind. Thus, where there is heart-mind, there is form; where there is form, there is opposition; and where there is opposition, harm inevitably follows. Beings without heart-mind have no adversary—how then could they be harmed? The reason the infant reaches this state is precisely because it is without heart-mind. It grasps without clinging, acts without desire—thus we know its essence is full, yet this is not due to the heart-mind. When the heart-mind moves, qi is harmed; when qi is harmed, the voice becomes hoarse. Yet the infant cries all day without hoarseness—thus we know its heart-mind does not move and its qi is harmonious. Harmony means that nothing external can harm what is within.

"Returning to the mandate" is called constancy—when encountering things, one knows to return to the root. "Knowing harmony" is called constancy—one attains the root and thereby responds to the myriad things. In truth, these are one and the same Dao. Thus both are called constancy. Life cannot be augmented; to attempt to augment it is to depart from its true nature. Qi detests forced action, and if one further employs the heart-mind to direct it, this is extreme rigidity. To augment life and compel qi to act against its nature is to daily grow more hardened, and old age will follow—thus one loses the infant’s inherent nature.

Lu’s Annotation: At the beginning of human life, one’s virtue and nature are most abundant. As one grows, the ears...

The eyes engage with externals, the heart-mind’s perceptions are received within—thus the daily increase of life’s desires grows ever more abundant, and what was once profound becomes shallow. Those who practice the Dao reduce what life augments; cultivating their nature, they return to virtue, and when virtue reaches its fullness, it becomes one with their beginning. Thus it is said: "Those who contain profound virtue are comparable to the infant."

The infant, as a creature, is distinguished solely by its reception of harmonious qi, accumulated yet undispersed. Poisonous insects do not sting it, fierce beasts do not seize it, and predatory birds do not strike it. How much more so for one who preserves the purity of qi, whose attainment extends to the very source of all things, and whose harmony is in great accord with them—who could harm such a one? This is like the query of Marquis Wen of Wei to Bu Shang.

For only essence can attain harmony. How so? Consider the infant: unaware of what it grasps, it clenches its fist tightly; unaware of what it gives, it stirs in resistance—this is essence. If the infant possessed even the slightest awareness of taking or giving, it would no longer be unified, and its nature would coarsen. Could it then remain as it is? Thus it is said: "Though its bones are weak and its sinews soft, its grip is firm. Unaware of the union of male and female, its organ stirs—such is the perfection of essence." Without care or vexation, though it cries all day, its throat does not grow hoarse—this is harmony. If the infant were troubled or vexed, its qi would turn perverse and lose harmony. Could it then remain as it is? Thus it is said: "Though it cries all day, its throat does not grow hoarse—this is the perfection of harmony."

When form is whole and essence restored, one becomes one with Heaven. Essence refined to the utmost returns to unite with Heaven. When essence is thus united with Heaven, the fullness of harmonious qi and its accord with all things is no wonder. Therefore, to reach the ultimate of the Dao is to return to the mandate; to return to the mandate is called constancy. To contain profound virtue is to attain harmony; to know harmony is also called constancy. Though Dao and virtue may differ, in their convergence with constancy, they are the same.

To know constancy is to follow nature’s spontaneity without augmenting life, to act without knowing one’s actions, to proceed without knowing one’s direction. The body becomes like a withered branch, the heart-mind like dead ashes—how then could one employ the heart-mind to direct qi? Thus, to augment life is to invite omens; without omens, there are no human calamities. Omens are the harbingers of fortune and misfortune, and human calamities follow in their wake. To employ the heart-mind to direct qi is to invite force; those who are forceful belong to the ranks of the dead.

To attain emptiness and preserve softness—this is the Dao. The Dao endures, and one who embodies it does not perish. To attain fullness and forcefulness is to become an object; as objects grow strong, they age—this is not the Dao. Thus it is said: "When things grow strong, they age—this is called not following the Dao. Not following the Dao, they perish early."

Those who know do not speak; those who speak do not know. Block the apertures, close the gates, blunt the sharpness, untangle the knots, soften the glare, merge with the dust—this is called mysterious sameness. It cannot be made close, nor kept distant; it cannot be profited from, nor harmed; it cannot be valued, nor debased. Thus it is honored under Heaven.

Su’s Annotation: The Dao is not a matter of words, nor is it separate from them. Yet those who know may not speak, and those who speak may not know. Only by blocking the apertures and closing the gates to shut out externals, by blunting sharpness, untangling knots, softening glare, and merging with dust to order the internal—only then, in silent non-action, does one become one with the Dao. If it could be made close, it could also be kept distant; if it could be profited from, it could also be harmed; if it could be valued, it could also be debased. One who embodies the Dao covers all things equally—who then is close or distant? Viewing adversity and favor as the same—who then is profited or harmed? Unaware of honor or disgrace—who then is valued or debased? Where human calculations fall short, this is why it is honored under Heaven.

Lu’s Annotation: To know constancy is the perfection of knowledge. When knowledge reaches its perfection, one remains silent and accomplishes all without disorder.

Where then could the heart find lodging? If one cannot remain wordless, one cannot be without self—even if one knows, this is not true knowledge. Thus it is said: "The knower does not speak; the speaker does not know." Seal the senses, close the gates, blunt sharpness, untangle knots, soften glare, and merge with dust—this is called profound union. In such union, silence accomplishes all, and this is the Way.

Those who attain this see the ten thousand things as one storehouse, death and life as the same form. Nothing is excessively close, nothing excessively distant—thus they cannot be made close or kept distant. They neither pursue profit nor oppose harm—thus they cannot be profited from or harmed. They neither glory in success nor shame in adversity—thus they cannot be valued or debased.

For those who *can* be made close or distant, profited from or harmed, valued or debased, honor lies in external things, and things can debase them. For those who *cannot* be made close or distant, profited from or harmed, valued or debased, honor lies within themselves, and external things cannot debase them. Is it not fitting that they are honored under Heaven?

Yet those who know the Dao fundamentally never speak. Thus it is said: "To speak of the Dao with words is to speak of what is not the Dao." And those who know would take speech as knowledge—this is to know knowledge. This is why words cannot but emerge. Yet since these words arise from wordlessness, though spoken, they are as if unspoken.

How could the Dao be mere silence?

The *Bixu* Commentary states:
Master Junping said: The five flavors are on the tongue, the five tones in the ear—what is sweet is not truly sweet, what is bitter is not truly bitter, what is *shang* is not truly *shang*, what is *yu* is not truly *yu*. Yet Yi Ya and Shi Kuang could distinguish them. What enabled them to do so? The mouth cannot speak of it. If even flavors and sounds are thus, how much more so the wondrous Dao? This is why it is said: "Those who know do not speak; those who speak do not know." Blocking the senses and shutting the gates was already discussed in Chapter Fifty-Two. Yet there, the focus was on the purity of the Dao to restrain the beginnings of desire and delight, while here, the emphasis is on the Dao’s wordlessness, giving rise to the discourse on diminishing hearing and discarding sight. The Dao is formless—it cannot be seen with the eyes or transmitted through the mouth. Thus the heart is confounded and cannot know, the mouth opens and cannot discuss. The perfected person, however, does not need to withdraw their gaze or seal their lips to naturally block the senses and shut the gates. Blunting sharpness, resolving entanglements, harmonizing light, and merging with dust were already addressed in Chapter Four. Yet there, the discussion centered on the Dao’s efficacy, while here, it clarifies the conduct of the person. When the perfected person shares the mind of Heaven, they are without knowledge; when they share the body of the Dao, they are without form. Then where could the mind of sharpness and entanglement arise? Where could the intent of light and dust distinctions emerge? Thus, in their movements and dwellings, when manifest, they share the same root as the myriad things; when hidden, they merge their origin with emptiness and nothingness. Speech and silence follow the moment without distinction, and their words, like the *zhi*-vessel, emerge daily to respond to change. This is why they are called "mysteriously identical."

The *Yuanze* Commentary states:
"Not speaking" does not mean secrecy or refusal to speak—it is truly that there is nothing to be spoken of. Why? Because they do not perceive a single dharma. Not perceiving a single dharma, they regard things as things yet are not objectified by things. How then could they be made close or distant, valued or debased? Those who *can* be made close or distant, valued or debased are merely things. But those who regard things as things yet are not objectified by things—though they may hold the rank of Ji, they remain eternally noble.

The *Xizhai* Commentary states:
All that exists in the world is but a dream’s realm. Thus, those who know do not speak, for speech cannot exhaust knowing. The deluded of the world take dreams as reality, clamoring and reciting without ever leaving the dream. Thus, their speech does not know, for their perception has not yet arrived. "Block the senses"—guard what emerges. "Shut the gates"—restrict what enters.

To blunt their sharpness is to govern their inner realm; to untangle their entanglements is to order their outer world. To harmonize their radiance is to restrain what lies within; to blend with their dust is to follow what lies without. Without going out or coming in, without inner or outer, without self or things—this is called *xuantong* (Mysterious Sameness). Having attained this sameness, to call it "close" is yet distant; to call it "distant" is yet near. To call it "benefit" brings no joy; to call it "harm" brings no fear. To call it "noble" does not elevate; to call it "base" does not debase. No mere thing can name it, and thus it becomes the most noble under heaven.

Govern the state with uprightness, employ the army with stratagem, and win the world through non-interference. How do I know this is so with the world? The more taboos in the world, the poorer the people grow. The more sharp instruments the people possess, the more benighted the state becomes. The more cunning and skill the people have, the more strange things arise. The more laws and decrees are promulgated, the more thieves and bandits appear. Therefore the sage declares: I practice non-action, and the people transform themselves. I cherish stillness, and the people rectify themselves. I engage in no affairs, and the people prosper themselves. I am without desire, and the people simplify themselves.

Su’s Commentary:
The sages of antiquity were gentle with the distant and kind to the near, never intending to resort to arms. Only when unavoidable did they undertake punitive expeditions. Thus, governing the state was deemed upright, while employing the army was deemed stratagem. Yet even this was insufficient to win the world, for the world is a sacred vessel—one cannot act upon it. Those who act upon it ruin it; those who grasp it lose it. Only those who embody the Dao remain vast and free from affairs. Though they do not seek to win the world, the world turns to them. When rulers impose many taboos, the sentiments of the people fail to reach above, leaving them impoverished and without recourse. Sharp instruments are but schemes and stratagems; when a wise ruler is above, the people are kept without knowledge or desire. If the people abound in schemes, their superiors grow benighted and confused. When people neglect fundamental work and pursue frivolous skills, strange and useless things proliferate. Fearing the people’s deceit, rulers multiply laws to suppress them, leaving the people with no place to set their hands and feet, driving them daily into banditry.

Lü’s Commentary:
The demeanor of the state does not enter the army; the demeanor of the army does not enter the state—this has been so for a long time. Thus, the means by which one governs the state and employs the army are fundamentally different. Governing the state cannot but be constant and enduring, hence it is done with uprightness, not stratagem. Uprightness is what makes it constant and enduring. Arms are inauspicious tools, not the instruments of the noble person. Therefore, those who embody the Dao do not dwell in them. Yet if one must dwell in them for the sake of constancy and endurance, then it is done with stratagem, not uprightness. Stratagem responds to momentary changes. Because it employs stratagem, it cannot but act; because it employs uprightness, it does not govern the state with cleverness. Governing the state without relying on cleverness is the state’s blessing. That governing the state with affairs cannot win the world is evident. Thus it is said: govern the state with uprightness, employ the army with stratagem, and win the world through non-interference. How do I know that non-interference suffices to win the world? By knowing that interference does not suffice. If one’s actions do not arise from non-action but lead to interference, then the world abounds in taboos to avoid what is detested, causing many to lose their livelihoods and the people to grow poorer. When people possess many sharp instruments to pursue their desires, the lower ranks become inscrutable and the state grows more benighted. As the people grow poorer and more cunning, and the state more benighted, strange things proliferate. This is why laws and decrees multiply while thieves and bandits abound. Laws restrain where they are applied but cannot restrain where they are not. Decrees are obeyed where they are heeded but cannot be obeyed where they are not. When the people are poor and cunning, decrees go unheeded; when the state is benighted and strange things arise, laws fail to apply. This is why laws and decrees multiply while thieves and bandits abound. All this is the fault of seeking to win the world through interference. Therefore the sage declares: I practice non-action, and the people transform themselves. I cherish stillness, and the people rectify themselves. I engage in no affairs, and the people prosper themselves. I am without desire, and the people simplify themselves. This is the proof that winning the world always arises from non-interference. The loss of the world stems from interference, which leads to many taboos and impoverished people, many sharp instruments and benighted states. Only thus do people grow cunning, strange things proliferate, laws and decrees multiply, and thieves and bandits abound. The return to order stems from non-action. Through non-action, the people transform themselves, abandoning cunning and forsaking profit, so that strange things do not arise. Through cherishing stillness, the people rectify themselves, and thieves and bandits vanish. From non-action and stillness come no affairs and no desires. With no affairs, the people prosper themselves and poverty from taboos ceases. With no desires, the people simplify themselves, and the benightedness from sharp instruments fades. Such is the natural order.

Xizhai’s Commentary:
I govern with uprightness, following the people’s inherent rectitude.

Governing through the extraordinary in warfare stems from warfare’s inherent extraordinariness. Taking the world through non-action stems from the world’s inherent non-action. In all my responses to things, is it truly by my doing? It is merely by things themselves. The sages of antiquity, who could regard the world as one body and the central states as one person, governed from their own hearts. If I impose many taboos, prohibitions must be set; once prohibitions are set, how can the people avoid poverty? If I display profit to the people, the people will rush toward profit; once the people rush toward profit, how can the state avoid benightedness? If I display cunning to the people, the people will multiply their skills; once skills prevail, how can strange things fail to proliferate? If I govern the people by laws, the people will also steal the laws for their own convenience; when superiors and inferiors deceive one another, how can thieves and bandits fail to abound?

The sage displays non-action, displays stillness, displays non-affairs, displays non-desire. The world responds to what is displayed to it. Thus it is said: virtue is like the wind, the people are like the grass—the wind over the grass must bend it.

His governance is unhurried, and his people are honest; his governance is exacting, and his people are deficient. Disaster!—fortune leans upon it; fortune!—disaster lurks within it. Who knows its limit? With no constant standard, right reverts to the extraordinary, good reverts to the ominous. The people’s delusion has endured for long indeed.

Thus the sage is square but does not cut, has edges but does not wound, is straight but does not extend, is bright but does not dazzle.

Yet how often does fortune turn to misfortune, right to the extraordinary, good to the ominous—who can say which prevails? All this does is multiply cunning and make governance difficult, leaving nothing whole in its simplicity. Why is this so? For time has its endings and beginnings, the world its transformations; fortune and misfortune, though pure in their coming, may bring what jars or what accords. What jars is called misfortune by the world, yet if it accords, it leans toward fortune. What accords is called fortune by the world, yet if it jars, misfortune lurks within. Who then can know its limit and evade or embrace it?

From the moment one pursues the singular, what is called "right" may deviate, and what is deemed "right" may in truth be unknown. What is right today may become extraordinary tomorrow; what is extraordinary here may be right there. The same holds for good and ominous. Thus fortune and misfortune, right and the extraordinary, good and ominous in the world cannot be fixed. Since the people first gained knowledge, they have clung to delusion—how long it has endured! How can one burden them further with exacting governance and prevent them from returning to simplicity and wholeness?

Therefore, the sage is square but does not cut, so he does not impose a single judgment to benefit the world; he is sharp but does not wound, so he overcomes things without harm; he is straight but does not extend, so he can bend to preserve the crooked and straighten the bent; he is bright but does not dazzle, so he employs his light and returns to his clarity. This is nothing other than embracing the unhurried and rejecting the exacting.

In governing people and serving Heaven, nothing surpasses frugality. Only through frugality can one attain early compliance; early compliance is called the accumulation of virtue. With virtue amassed, nothing is unconquerable; with nothing unconquerable, none can fathom its limit. Fathoming no limit, one may possess a state and its generative power, and thus endure long. This is the Dao of deep roots, firm foundation, long life, and enduring vision.

Thus it is said: in governing people and serving Heaven, nothing surpasses frugality. Only through frugality can one preserve their spirit without expending it, thereby attaining early restoration. If one is not frugal and squanders it, exhausting spirit and fatiguing essence, even if one wishes to return to their spirit, there will be no path to enter. In such restoration, is it not too late? Therefore, it is said: only through frugality can one attain early restoration. The life of a person is fundamentally sufficient in virtue. If one is truly frugal and restores early, virtue will daily increase and fill. Thus it is said: early restoration is called the accumulation of virtue. With virtue amassed, it reaches its utmost. One of utmost virtue cannot be burned by fire, drowned by water, harmed by cold or heat, nor destroyed by birds or beasts—where then can one not prevail? Therefore, it is said: with virtue amassed, nothing is unconquerable. If there is something unconquerable, the Dao has its limit. With nothing unconquerable, who can fathom its limit? Thus it is said: with nothing unconquerable, none can know its limit.

One who possesses land possesses great things. One who possesses great things cannot be objectified by things yet can objectify all things. None knowing its limit, it is not objectified yet can objectify all things. Though possessing land, it is without burden. Thus it is said: none knowing its limit, one may possess a state. Having grasped the mother, one knows the child; knowing the child, one returns to guard the mother, and thus to the end of life does not peril. Therefore, it is said: possessing the mother of the state, one may endure long. Thus frugality as Dao is called the Way of deep roots and firm foundation, of long life and enduring vision.

Spirit is the root of life; frugality preserves it, and thus the root deepens and life endures. Long life is the foundation of vision; guarding and protecting it, the foundation is firm and vision endures.

---

Xi Zhai’s commentary:
In governing people outwardly and serving Heaven inwardly, nothing surpasses frugality. Frugality means nothing is not preserved—cautious within, restrained without, the inner heart not scattered, the outer heart not stirred. Thus one attains early compliance: inwardly, the heart complies; outwardly, the form complies. Still and unmoving, virtue has where to accumulate. Accumulating what does not accumulate, nothing is not overcome; with nothing unconquerable, nothing is ungoverned. Though possessing a state, it is possible. People know one may possess a state but do not know that possessing a state arises from having a root. When the root is deeply amassed, even its dust, chaff, and husks may be molded into Yao and Shun—how much more those below? This is what is called deep roots and firm foundation, the Way of governing people and serving Heaven.

---

Governing a great state is like cooking a small fish: with the Dao, one oversees the world, and its *gui* do not act as spirits. It is not that its *gui* do not act as spirits, but that their spirit does not harm people. It is not that their spirit does not harm people, but that the sage also does not harm them. When the two do not harm one another, virtue and power converge and return.

*(Variant: "the sage also does not harm people" follows "virtue and power converge and return." The meaning refers to the spirit.)*

---

Han Fei’s explanation:
If a craftsman frequently changes his work, he loses his achievement; if a laborer frequently shifts his task, he loses his accomplishment. One person’s work, if half a day is lost daily, in ten days loses five days’ work. Ten thousand people’s work, if half a day is lost daily, in ten days loses fifty thousand days’ work. Thus, those who frequently change their work multiply their losses, and the deficit grows ever greater.

Whenever laws and orders are altered, advantages and harms shift; when advantages and harms shift, the people’s tasks change—this is called changing work. Thus, observing by principle: managing a great multitude and frequently disturbing them yields little success. Storing great vessels and frequently moving them incurs many losses. Cooking a small fish and frequently stirring it spoils its luster. Governing a great state and frequently changing laws wearies the people. Therefore, a ruler of the Dao values stillness and does not esteem changing laws. Thus it is said: governing a great state is like cooking a small fish.

When people suffer illness, they value tranquility; when misfortune strikes, they fear *gui*. When the sage is above, the people have few desires. With few desires, their blood and qi are ordered, and their actions are reasonable. With reasonable actions, they suffer few misfortunes. Free from internal sores, ulcers, and hemorrhoids, and external punishments and executions, their *gui* are light and calm. Thus it is said: with the Dao, one oversees the world, and its *gui* do not act as spirits.

When the people of a well-governed age do not contend with *gui* spirits, it is said: it is not that its *gui* do not harm people.

The spirits do not harm people—this is called *gui* afflictions. When people suffer illness, it is called *gui* harming people; when people drive them away, it is called people harming *gui*. When the people violate laws and decrees, it is called the people harming their ruler; when the ruler executes punishments upon the people, it is called the ruler harming the people. If the people do not violate the law, the ruler likewise does not carry out punishments—this is called the ruler not harming the people. Thus it is said: the sage also does not harm the people. When the ruler does not harm the people and the people do not harm the *gui*, it is said: the two do not harm each other.

When the people do not violate the law, the ruler internally does not employ punishments and externally does not pursue profit from their livelihood. When the ruler internally does not employ punishments and externally does not pursue profit from their livelihood, the people multiply and prosper. When the people multiply and prosper, their stores and accumulations flourish—this is called possessing virtue. All so-called afflictions arise from obstructing and scattering the spirit; when the spirit is scattered, one lacks virtue. When *gui* do not afflict people, the obstructions are not removed; when obstructions are not removed, the spirit is not scattered—this is called possessing virtue. When the ruler’s stores and accumulations flourish and the *gui* do not scatter the people’s spirit, then virtue resides entirely among the people. Thus it is said: when the two do not harm each other, virtue is mutually returned. This means that virtue, flourishing above and below, is wholly returned to the people.

---

**Su’s Annotation:**
One must not stir when cooking a small fish; one must not trouble when governing a great state. If troubled, the people labor; if stirred, the fish falls apart. The sage practices non-action, allowing each to rest in their natural state. Externally, there is nothing to trouble them; internally, there is nothing to fear—thus nothing can invade. Even *gui* have no use for their numinous power. It is not that the *gui* lack numinosity, but that though numinous, they do not harm people. It is not that the numinous does not harm people, but that the sage has never harmed people, so the *gui* have no power to act. The reason people and *gui* do not harm each other lies in the presence of the sage above—thus virtue is mutually returned.

**Lü’s Annotation:**
To govern a state by obtaining the Mother of the state is not difficult, even for a great state. One who cooks fish must not trouble it, and one who cooks a small fish must especially preserve it whole without cutting. Governing a great state is likewise. If one cooks and cuts it, one harms it. Ruling the world with the Dao is called great governance—one likewise does not cut to harm it. When it reaches its utmost, its *gui* do not act as spirits. This is all achieved through non-harm.

Why say so? The nature of *gui* is not without numinosity. When they mingle with people and display their numinosity, they cannot but harm people. That they do not act as spirits is because the sage is able to rule the world with the Dao, preventing people from indulging their natures or altering their virtue, without great joy or anger to disrupt the harmony of yin and yang—this is what is meant by dwelling in the midst of the turbid world and attaining tranquility with the age. Thus the sage also does not harm people. Only the sage can refrain from harming people, so yin and yang are harmonious and still, *gui*-spirits are not disturbed, the myriad things are unharmed, and all living beings do not meet premature death—thus their spirits do not harm people. If spirits do not harm people, they have no way to display their numinous resonance, and so they do not act as spirits.

From this perspective, that the *gui* do not act as spirits is not because they lack numinosity, but because their numinosity does not harm people—thus they do not act as spirits. It is not that their numinosity does not harm people, but that the sage also does not harm people—thus their numinosity does not harm people. If the sage cannot preserve the people’s simplicity and harms them, causing them to lose their nature, so that the four seasons do not arrive and the harmony of cold and heat is not achieved, then the people’s harm to the spirits will be great—how could the spirits not harm people? Only when spirits do not harm people do they return virtue to the people; when spirits do not harm people, the people also return virtue to the spirits. Thus it is said: when the two do not harm each other, virtue is mutually returned.

**Xizhai’s Annotation:**
Governing the state is but a minor task for the sage, no more than cooking a small fish. Though the Dao encompasses the vast, the strange, the cunning, and the marvelous, all are united as one—thus its *gui* do not act as spirits. It is not that the *gui* lack numinosity; if *gui* possessed numinosity, all the vast, strange, cunning, and marvelous would be transformed into the Dao. How could the Dao harm people? The original intent was that we do not harm people, so even in the hidden depths, *gui*-spirits cannot use their numinosity to harm beings. In great governance and hidden spirits, the two do not harm each other—thus the hearts of people and *gui* are mutually returned. Without utmost virtue, how could this be achieved?

**Cheng Ju’s Discourse:**
That the sage does not harm the people is certain, but how can they also cause *gui*-spirits not to harm people? The relation of people within the Dao and the Dao within people is like that of fish in water and water in fish—what distinction is there between life and death? When the Dao rules the world, the people’s lives are tranquil, and so they nurture their lives well; their deaths are serene, and so they nurture their deaths well. Serene and still, how can *gui* act as spirits? Living thus, dying thus—how could there be numinous resonance, afflictions, or calamities?

**Hongfu’s Annotation:**
Stirring a small fish while cooking causes it to fall apart, so the sage governs the world with non-action. Though there may be numinous treachery, it has no use—it is not that the sage can eliminate it to prevent it from acting as spirits. Though numinous, it cannot harm people. Why? Because the sage has never harmed people. If the sage does not harm people, spirits also cannot harm people—this is the two not harming each other. Without harm, virtue is returned. Is there some separate virtue in non-harm? Virtue is already present in harm.

A great state is the lower reaches where all under heaven converge; it is the female of all under heaven. The female always overcomes the male with stillness, always placing itself below. Thus when a great state humbles itself before a small state, it gains the small state; when a small state humbles itself before a great state, it gains the great state. Therefore, some humble themselves to gain, and some humble themselves and gain. A great state desires only to nurture and embrace others; a small state desires only to serve and join others. When both obtain what they desire, the greater should humble itself.

**Su’s Annotation:**
All under heaven turn to a great state as all waters flow to the lower reaches. All movement rushes toward stillness as all heights descend to the low. If a great state is humble, small states will attach to it; if a small state is humble, a great state will receive it. A great state humbles itself to gain people; a small state humbles itself to be gained by others.

**Lü’s Annotation:**
Knowing how to govern one’s state with the Dao, one certainly does not harm its people. But if one does not know how to engage with all under heaven, though one does not harm people, there will still be those who harm its people. A great state is the lower reaches where all under heaven converge; the convergence of all under heaven is the female. The female always overcomes the male with stillness, always placing itself below—this is the way to engage with all under heaven and preserve its people.

For the convergence of all under heaven is the female, and the female always overcomes the male with stillness—this overcoming arises precisely from placing itself below. If a great state truly abides in the lower reaches to attract the convergence of all under heaven, this is the way of the female: by stillness and humility, it overcomes the male. Thus, when a great state humbles itself before a small state, it gains the small state—this is what is meant by "sometimes lowering oneself to gain." "Lowering oneself to gain" means that the great, in relation to the small, ought to humble itself as if it could do otherwise, yet chooses to lower itself to gain. When a small state humbles itself before a great state, it gains the great state—this is what is meant by "sometimes lowering oneself and being gained." "Lowering oneself and being gained" means that the small, in relation to the great, has no choice but to humble itself to be gained.

Now, a great state does not merely desire to annex and nurture others, nor does a small state merely desire to enter into service. If the great lowers itself to the small, and the small in turn lowers itself to the great, each attains what it desires—how could there be any who do not gain? "Gaining" here means winning their hearts without losing them. Yet if both great and small must humble themselves, why does the text say only that "the great ought to humble itself"? If the small cannot humble itself before the great, it not only fails to gain the great state but also invites disaster—thus, there is no question of its being inappropriate. Therefore, the text says only that "the great ought to humble itself."

**Xizhai’s Annotation:**
If a state is great yet can descend to seek others, others will surely converge and submit to it. The female, through stillness and humility, always overcomes the male. Thus, if a great state humbles itself before a small state, it will surely gain the small state; if a small state humbles itself before a great state, it will surely gain the great state. "Sometimes a great state lowers itself to gain a small state, or lowers itself to be gained by a great state"—this means each wins the other’s heart. Therefore, a great state desires only to nurture small states, while a small state desires only to serve a great state. Only thus do both attain what they seek. Mencius said: "He who with a small state serves a large one is one who stands in awe of Heaven; he who with a large state serves a small one is one who rejoices in Heaven. He who rejoices in Heaven will preserve all under heaven; he who stands in awe of Heaven will preserve his state." Thus, a great state must especially humble itself.

**Wu’s Annotation:**
"Convergence" means a gathering place. A great state is the gathering place of all small states, just as the lower reaches of a river are the gathering place of all waters. The female does not move first to seek the male; the male always moves first to seek the female. Those who move to seek invite loss, while those who wait in stillness receive benefit—hence it is said, "with stillness it overcomes the male." Those who move to seek stand above; those who wait in stillness stand below—hence it is said, "with stillness it places itself below." Some say the character "female" may be superfluous. A great state, without relying on its loftiness, humbly lowers itself to small states and thus secures their willing attachment. A small state, willingly abiding in lowliness, humbly submits to a great state and thus gains its acceptance. "Lowering oneself to gain" means a great state humbles itself to win the attachment of small states; "lowering oneself and being gained" means a small state humbles itself to gain the acceptance of a great state. When a great state lowers itself to a small state, it desires only to nurture the small state; when a small state lowers itself to a great state, it desires only to enter into its service. If both can humble themselves, great and small each attain what they desire. Yet the small, being naturally beneath others, need not fear inability to humble itself; the great, not being naturally beneath others, may fear it cannot lower itself. Therefore, the text says, "the great ought to humble itself." The opening metaphor of the lower reaches illustrates that a great state, though not naturally beneath others, can lower itself; the metaphor of the female and male illustrates that a small state, being naturally beneath others, can lower itself.

**Lin Xi-yi’s Annotation:**
A great state desires only to annex and nurture others; a small state desires only to enter into service—neither can do so without humbling itself. Only by humbling itself can each attain its desire. Thus, those who know the Dao must always regard humility as proper. This sentence concludes the chapter. Its meaning is simply that the strong must be able to be weak, and the possessing must be able to be without—this is the consistent intent of the *Dao De Jing*. Many interpreters take its metaphors as literal statements and thus miss the point. Only Huang Maocai said: "This entire chapter borrows things to clarify the Dao"—this is most apt, though he did not extend this insight to other chapters.

The Dao is the innermost recess of the myriad things, the treasure of the good, and the refuge of the not-good. Beautiful words can be traded for esteem; noble conduct can be elevated above others. Why should one abandon those who are not good? Thus, in establishing the Son of Heaven and appointing the Three Dukes, though one may offer large jade disks followed by teams of four horses, it is not as good as presenting this Dao.

**Su’s Annotation:**
All things manifest externally are but their gateways and halls. The Dao within things is like their innermost recess—all things possess it, yet people do not see it. Only the worthy attain and hold it, hence it is called "the treasure of the good." The foolish, though unable to possess it, cannot be at peace without it—hence it is called "the refuge of the not-good." The Dao is not far from people, yet people distance themselves from it. If one truly speaks beautiful words, they can be traded for worldly esteem; if one practices noble conduct, it can be elevated above others. If one who was unjust in the morning hears the great Dao by evening, delusion vanishes and nature is restored—how could one then point to their former ungoodness? And how could one abandon them? In establishing the Son of Heaven and appointing the Three Dukes, the aim is to save people with the Dao. Though one may offer the finest jade and the finest horses, presenting them is not as valuable as presenting this Dao. The Dao is inherently within us—people need only seek it to attain it. The Dao is beyond merit or fault—people need only know it, and no fault can stain them.

**Lü’s Annotation:**
All things embrace yin and enfold yang, blending qi to achieve harmony—there is not a single thing that does not fully possess the Dao. A room has its innermost recess, a deep and tranquil place where the honored reside. All things have such a deep, tranquil, and honored place—this is the Dao. Hence it is said: "The Dao is the innermost recess of the myriad things." Precisely because of this, it is the treasure of the good and the refuge of the not-good. Why? The good know the source of their goodness and thus attain it as an inexhaustible, priceless wealth—is this not their treasure? The not-good know that their ungoodness, when brought to this point, becomes utterly dissolved—even with myriad evils, they vanish without a trace.

Is this not their refuge? Fine words may earn marketable esteem, and noble conduct may impose authority upon others—yet whether good or not, all people inherently recognize what is fine and revere what is noble. The one who embodies the Dao, however, stands to others as heaven and earth: heaven covers all without exception, earth bears all without omission—far beyond the reach of fine words or noble conduct. Then how could the not-good be cast aside? Thus, when establishing the Son of Heaven and appointing the Three Dukes, though they may offer clasped jades before four-horse carriages as tribute to their superiors—reverent in ritual, weighty in gifts—it is not equal to simply presenting this Dao. For the value of the Dao in the world surpasses even such reverence and lavish tribute. The Son of Heaven and the Three Dukes sit in deliberation for no other purpose than this.

Why has this Dao been prized since antiquity? Is it not said: "Seek it and attain; bear guilt and be absolved"? To seek and attain is the treasure of the good; to bear guilt and be absolved is the refuge of the not-good. Precisely for this reason, it is the most precious thing under heaven, and since antiquity, it could not but be held in the highest esteem.

息斋’s Annotation:
The profound (奧) means "to conceal." The Dao is prized by all things, just as the innermost chamber (奧) can conceal them. The worthy treasure the Dao, thus delighting in attaining what they seek; the unworthy have no refuge but the Dao, thus delighting in absolution from guilt. Since it is rooted in the naturalness of the human heart without external imposition, it is said: "Rely on the Dao, and its beauty may be traded; follow the Dao, and its honor may be conferred upon others." If not for the naturalness of the human heart, words would go unheeded, and actions would meet no response.

Since each person is rooted in the Dao, even those who unfortunately fall into unworthiness—how could the sage bear to abandon them? Thus, the establishment of the Son of Heaven and the appointment of the Three Dukes are not merely to indulge in their wealth and nobility. Rather, they do not wish to abandon people in unworthiness but instead use this Dao to guide them toward goodness. Even if one presents clasped jades before four-horse carriages, it is not equal to uttering a single good word or performing a single good deed, so that all under heaven may not lose their goodness. How slight would such a benefit be?

To awaken the people through the Dao is what is meant by "sitting and presenting this Dao." Why has this Dao been prized since antiquity? Because the good do not toil day and night to attain their goodness, and if the unfortunate should fall into unworthiness, it is still sufficient for them to free themselves from evil. How could this not be worthy of the world’s highest esteem?

Wu’s Annotation:
The "profound of all things" (萬物之奧) refers to what is most prized among them. The innermost chamber (奧) is the southwest corner of a hall, and in the design of ancestral temples, there are halls and inner chambers, with the chamber being the more honored. Within the chamber, the southeast corner is called *fang*, the northeast *xun*, the northwest *wulou*, and the southwest *ao*—the most honored place, where the esteemed reside. Thus, the honor of the Dao is like the innermost chamber of a temple hall. "Clasped jades" (拱璧) are jades held in both hands; "four-horse carriages" (驷马) are the horses of a single chariot. Presenting clasped jades before four-horse carriages is like the *Spring and Autumn Annals* saying, "leather first, twelve oxen after." In diplomatic audiences, the four-horse carriages are displayed outside, while the clasped jades are held to convey the message—this is called "presenting first." Though such tribute is the height of reverence, it is not truly prized. It is not equal to kneeling and presenting this Dao, which is the most honored of all.

Ye’s Annotation:
Even the ignorant among husband and wife, if they seek the Dao, will not fail to attain it. Once they hear it, even what they once deemed sinful can be entirely washed away. Thus, the value of the Dao truly lies here, not elsewhere.

Act without acting, attend without attending, savor the tasteless.
Great and small, much and little—repay resentment with virtue.
Resolve difficulties while they are easy; accomplish the great while it is small.
The difficult affairs of the world must arise from the easy;
The great affairs of the world must arise from the small.
Therefore, the sage never strives for greatness and thus achieves greatness.
One who makes light promises must lack trust;
One who treats many things as easy will encounter many difficulties.
Therefore, the sage treats things as difficult and thus never encounters difficulty.

Wu’s Annotation on the Passage:
"Act without acting" means to act in such a way that the act itself cannot be discerned, hence "acting without acting." "Attend without attending" means that when one acts without acting, affairs likewise become "without attending." Not only are affairs without attending, but even when one savors them, they are tasteless. To savor is to reflect and ponder repeatedly. The Dao as a thing cannot be seen, cannot be heard, and cannot be grasped. Though one may reflect and ponder repeatedly, it ultimately returns to nothingness.

Only thus can it be great or small, much or little. Whether much or little depends on me, and what is called resentment has no fixed nature. Thus, even the ungood, I deem good—virtue is goodness; even the untrustworthy, I deem trustworthy—virtue is trust. And I repay all with virtue.

What people find difficult to guard against is resentment, yet repaying it with virtue leaves nothing to burden the heart. Not only this, but one resolves difficulties while they are easy and accomplishes the great while it is small. The difficult affairs of the world must arise from the easy; the great affairs of the world must arise from the small. Yet I am always without action. If there is action, it must be seen in the easy and the small—this is what is meant by "knowing the subtle, it is divine."

If one can grasp this in the heart, then extending it to the world and the state presents no difficulty. Therefore, the sage never strives for greatness and thus achieves greatness—because he accomplishes the great while it is small, not striving for greatness when it is already great.

To make a promise is easy; to retract it is difficult. If one is not cautious when making promises, words become hard to retract. If one plans while things are easy, they remain easy; if one plans when things are difficult, they become difficult. If one does not plan at the outset when things are easy but waits until they are difficult, then they become truly difficult. This is why ordinary people lack trust and encounter many difficulties. Therefore, the sage treats things as difficult from the outset and thus never encounters difficulty—because he treats them as difficult while they are easy, not waiting until they are difficult.

息斋’s Annotation:
Act without acting, and one does not toil; attend without attending, and people are not troubled; savor the tasteless, and things are not wasted. What the world calls great and small, much and little refers to form. The sage roams beyond forms, thus great and small, much and little are equalized as one. If even great and small, much and little are equalized as one, how much more so for favor and resentment, repayment and bestowal?

What I do and bestow is only virtue—how would I know of resentment? How would I know of repayment? With no distinction between favor and resentment, repayment and bestowal, and no difference between great and small, much and little, how could there be any intention in the affairs of the world?

The affair must seek the easy; the great must proceed from the fine. Proceeding from the easy, even the difficult becomes easy; advancing from the fine, even the great becomes fine. Thus, the sage never acts great, yet the great inevitably returns to him—because he is without intention. Precisely because he is without intention in affairs, he never anticipates events or imposes expectations upon things. How could he be like the petty men of the world, who pledge before acting and change their minds when matters arise? Premature pledges必必必必 lead to broken trust; frequent changes必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必必 lead to many difficulties. The sage, when affairs have not yet arisen, always treats them as difficult—not that he truly deems them difficult, but because he does not impose his will upon things, he appears to regard them as such. Precisely because he treats them as difficult, they ultimately present no difficulty.

*Bishu*:
When affairs involve form, there is great and small; when bound to number, there is much and little—this is the origin of resentment. Only the Dao transcends form and number, and the sage unites with it, acting through non-action, engaging in affairs through non-affairs, savoring through tastelessness. Love and aversion vanish; the passions of sage and commoner are exhausted, and he abides serenely in the dwelling of emptiness. Then great and small, much and little are viewed as one—how could resentment or repayment arise? Only virtue encompasses them. Yet this non-action, non-affair, tastelessness cannot be attained by force—it is supremely easy, yet cannot be glimpsed by the eye—supremely fine. Though supremely easy, the supremely difficult is resolved through it; though supremely fine, the supremely great is accomplished through it. How could one neglect it for its easiness or fineness? Some learners of the Dao glimpse this truth yet are distracted by thoughts of greatness, so the easy and fine do not long remain theirs. Only the sage, from beginning to end, embodies non-action, non-affairs, tastelessness, and does not allow the world’s so-called greatness to divide his mind. Thus, when the difficult and great arise, he remains utterly still, without obstruction, and the great Dao is thereby preserved whole. This is what it means to "accomplish the great." Alas! This is not something those with the vision of great men, clear understanding of principles, and resolute minds can merely believe or uphold—how could it be shared with those who lightly pledge and frequently change?

Hold fast to what is tranquil and easy; plan for what has not yet shown signs; sever what is fragile when distinct; disperse what is subtle. Act before things arise; govern before disorder begins. A tree of arm-spanning girth grows from a tender shoot; a nine-tiered terrace rises from a basket of earth; a journey of a thousand li begins beneath one’s feet. Those who act fail; those who grasp lose. Thus, the sage practices non-action and does not fail; holds nothing and does not lose. People in their undertakings often fail when near completion. Be as cautious at the end as at the beginning, and there will be no failed affairs. Therefore, the sage desires the undesired and does not value rare goods; learns the unlearned and reverses the errors of the multitude, relying on the spontaneity of the myriad things without daring to act.

Su’s Annotation:
When things have not yet arisen, holding fast and planning is sufficient. When they are about to manifest, one must dissolve and disperse them before they depart.

Yet this is still preferable to what has already come to pass. Thus, to act before things arise is supreme; to govern before disorder emerges is secondary. The tree, the terrace, the journey—all accumulate from the small to become great. The coming of order and chaos, fortune and misfortune, follows these same three patterns. The sage awaits them with non-action and guards them with non-grasping, thereby allowing fortune to arise of itself and misfortune to vanish of itself. It is like planting seedlings: deep plowing and careful tending ensure a bountiful autumn harvest. It is like facing a thief: sitting in vigilant stillness and gazing intently will make the thief retreat of his own accord. The world does not understand the spontaneity of things, believing that without action nothing is accomplished, without grasping nothing remains. Thus, they constantly contend with misfortune for victory and struggle to produce fortune, only to find misfortune beyond remedy and fortune beyond attainment. Such is the principle at work.

The sage knows the harm of forced action and does not use human effort to assist Heaven. From beginning to end, all follows its natural course, and so nothing fails to reach completion. The worldly, their minds set on gain, guard against even the slightest signs of trouble, yet may still fail to recognize them, allowing things to unfold naturally. But when they see success nearly within grasp, fearing its loss, none do not then attribute failure to this very neglect. Therefore, it is said: be as cautious at the end as at the beginning, and there will be no failed affairs.

People all pursue their desires to the detriment of things and trust their learning to the harm of principle. The sage is not without desire, yet desires the undesired—thus, though desiring, does not harm things. The sage is not without learning, yet learns the unlearned—thus, though learning, does not harm principle. Only then, with inner and outer clarity and vast emptiness, can one practice non-action, assist the spontaneity of the myriad things, and await their self-completion.

Lü’s Annotation:
What is at peace is easy to maintain; what is perilous and then maintained is difficult.

Those without signs are easy to plan for; once set in motion and then planned for, it becomes difficult. What is fragile is easy to break—one must not let it grow firm. What is subtle is easy to disperse—one must not let it become manifest. All things are thus, but the mind is most so. Mastering the mind enables one to manage the world and the state without difficulty.

To be at peace is to be without signs; thus, one acts before things arise. What is fragile and subtle is treated before disorder emerges. A tree of arm-span girth grows from a tiny sprout; greatness arises from the small. A nine-tiered terrace begins with heaped earth; height starts from below. A journey of a thousand *li* begins with a single step; distance starts from what is near. Thus, acting before things arise and governing before disorder emerges—this is the constant principle of root and branch.

To act before things arise is to act through non-action. To govern before disorder emerges is to govern through non-governance. Once things have arisen and one acts, the act seeks completion but instead brings failure. Once disorder has set in and one governs, the governance seeks stability but instead incurs loss. Therefore, the sage acts before things arise, thus inherently practicing non-action—hence no failure. Governs before disorder emerges, thus inherently practicing non-attachment—hence no loss. The people, however, always act after things have arisen, and so nearly succeed only to fail, for they do not know the root. If they understood that greatness arises from the small, height from below, and distance from what is near, and were as cautious at the end as at the beginning, there would be no failed affairs.

Thus, the sage desires the undesired and does not prize rare, hard-to-obtain goods, thereby purifying desire in the俗 and striving to attain clarity—those who do not know this are not so. Learns the unlearned to redress the transgressions of the multitude, assisting the spontaneity of the myriad things without daring to act—those who seek to refine their nature through俗 learning to return to their origin do not know this.

Yuanze’s Annotation:
The desire for the undesired is not the absence of desire; the desire lies precisely in the undesired. Thus, the sage does not prize rare goods. What the sage calls non-action and non-attachment is not to the point of complete detachment but to refrain from generating anything beyond one’s inherent nature. Moreover, people must eat their fill and wear warm clothes—these are unavoidable desires. To desire these alone is not to depart from one’s nature; to further prize rare, hard-to-obtain goods is the folly of the deluded, who have long since lost their root. Therefore, the sage constantly desires the undesired to rescue the deluded and return them to their nature.

The learning of the unlearned is not the absence of learning; the learning lies precisely in the unlearned, to redress the transgressions of the multitude. The multitude pursues the trivial and busies itself with many things; the sage, through the learning of the unlearned, rescues their transgressions and returns them to the Dao, assisting spontaneity—what Zhuangzi calls "returning to assist Heaven." To act is to use the human to destroy the Heavenly. Therefore, one does not dare to act. Then the myriad things rest in the constancy of their nature and destiny, and things show no signs of arising. How could there be anything fragile to dissolve or subtle to disperse?

Xizhai’s Annotation:
The sage constantly abides in what is at peace, thus his peace is ever-peaceful. He holds without holding, hence it is called "easy to maintain." By not holding, he maintains his peace. If the slightest stirring of the mind arises, he plans for it before signs appear; if it is fragile, he breaks it before it grows firm; if it is subtle, he disperses it before it becomes manifest. Thus, nothing disturbs his peace. He maintains his peace before things arise and governs his mind before disorder emerges. It is like a tree of arm-span girth growing from a tiny sprout, a nine-tiered terrace rising from heaped earth, a journey of a thousand *li* beginning with a single step—within non-action, action arises naturally; within non-accumulation, accumulation occurs naturally. Day by day, goodness departs, and day by day, virtue accumulates, yet the sage remains unaware.

If one acts with intention, one brings failure; if one governs with attachment, one incurs loss. Because he practices non-action, there is no failure; because he practices non-attachment, there is no loss. The reason the world finds the Dao difficult to achieve and easy to ruin lies in its intentional action and attachment. At the outset, it seems there is no room for diligence, yet by the end, one cannot avoid slackening—all due to the fault of acting and attachment. If one does not act at the outset and does not attach, one cannot even find diligence, let alone slackening. This is why the world nearly succeeds only to fail.

Only the sage takes the undesired as desire, thus never prizing rare, hard-to-obtain goods. What is called "rare" need not be gold or jade; all things beyond the body are hard to obtain. The multitude transgresses with their eyes in color and their ears in sound. I take the unlearned as learning, reversing sight and hearing to redress the transgressions of the multitude, assisting the spontaneity of the Dao without daring to act. How could there be the affliction of acting and attachment?

Wu’s Annotation:
"Be as cautious at the end as at the beginning" further develops the two instances of "end" in the preceding text: "ultimately without difficulty" and "ultimately not acting greatly." Though one may initially deem something difficult, by the end one no longer finds it so; though one may initially dare not consider something great, by the end one deems it so. Thus, affairs nearly completed may yet fail in the end. Therefore, one must be as cautious at the end as at the beginning. If one deems something difficult at the outset and continues to do so at the end, and does not deem something great at the outset nor at the end, then there will ultimately be no failed affairs.

Liu’s Annotation:
Desiring what the multitude does not desire is called "desiring the undesired." Not desiring what the multitude desires is called "not prizing rare, hard-to-obtain goods." Learning what the multitude does not learn is called "learning the unlearned." Not learning what the multitude learns is called "redressing the transgressions of the multitude."

Those of old who were adept in the Dao did not seek to enlighten the people but to make them simple. The people are difficult to govern because of their excessive knowledge. Thus, governing the state with knowledge is the state’s bane; governing the state without knowledge is the state’s blessing. Knowing these two is also a model. To know the model is called "mysterious virtue." Mysterious virtue is profound, far-reaching, and contrary to things, yet ultimately attains great compliance.

Annotation:
"Model" and "standard" are terms of method. Below, "profound" means deep, "far-reaching" means encompassing, and "contrary" means opposed. "Ultimately attains compliance" means, as Sima explains, "Though the feelings of things all prize knowledge, those with mysterious virtue alone devalue it. Though contrary to things, they are compliant with the Dao."

Lü’s commentary: The multitude are bright and clear, while I alone seem dim; the common folk are sharp and discerning, while I alone am dull and muddled. My heart is that of a fool—ah! Those of old who were adept in the Dao were thus within themselves, and thus they extended it to the people: it was certainly not to make them sharp but to make them simple. "Sharp and discerning, bright and clear"—this is what is meant by making them sharp. "Dim and muddled"—this is what is meant by making them simple. The people lose their nature by dwelling in ornament and forsaking substance, so their knowledge multiplies and they become difficult to govern. If one truly wishes to govern them, one must dispel knowledge and habit, steadying them with the nameless uncarved block, so that they transform themselves. To govern them with knowledge is precisely what brings disorder. For to destroy the principle of "not knowing, not perceiving, and following the emperor’s rule" is to become a thief. Governing the state with knowledge destroys this principle.

Thus it is said: "Governing the state with knowledge is the state’s bane; governing the state without knowledge is the state’s blessing." Knowing these two is not only a model for governing the state but also a standard for the self, one that is never transgressed. For to know the child and guard the mother, so that one’s body does not perish—this is the blessing of not governing with knowledge. To open its apertures and assist its affairs, so that one is never saved—this is the bane of governing with knowledge. Then is not "not governing with knowledge" as opposed to "governing with knowledge" also my standard? Truly knowing the standard and never transgressing its virtue—this may be called "mysterious."

When virtue reaches the mysterious, it is deep and unfathomable, far-reaching and immeasurable. Viewed through the lens of emotion, it cannot be grasped by the heart, yet when it reaches its ultimate, it returns to the root with nothing opposed. Thus it is said: "To know the standard is called 'mysterious virtue.' Mysterious virtue is profound, far-reaching, and contrary to things."

Then only does one attain great compliance.

Master Xizhai annotates: What is called the Dao does not reside in the realm of ears, eyes, sounds, or colors. Yet the world’s people regard what the eye sees as color and what the ear hears as sound, being obstructed by sounds and colors, unaware that seeing, hearing, perceiving, and knowing extend beyond sounds and colors. If one were to abruptly tell them, “Your seeing, hearing, perceiving, and knowing are not real,” who would not find it strange and laughable? Thus, the Dao cannot be made evident to the people, for they would deem it foolish and pity it. The people’s foolishness lies in being obstructed by sounds and colors, unable to free themselves. Even if I were to show them non-action and simplicity, they would still not awaken—how much less if I were to employ knowledge to instruct them?

The affliction of the people lies in excessive knowledge; when knowledge is not used rightly, it becomes perverse. If I cannot awaken them to their perversity but instead employ knowledge to instruct them, this only further opens the door to their falsity. Thus, governing the state with knowledge is the state’s bane. If one were to act without contrivance and not awaken the people’s cunning minds, so that cunning minds do not arise, then purity and whiteness would be complete—could this not be called the state’s blessing?

What those above practice, those below emulate. There has never been a case where I remain tranquil while they are restless, where I remain plain and unadorned while the people are full of desires. Thus it is said: “To know these two is also my standard. To truly know the standard is called ‘mysterious virtue.’ Mysterious virtue is profound, far-reaching, and contrary to things.” Only by aligning with the Dao can this be called great compliance.

Liu Zhongping annotates:
To govern the state without knowledge is to open the celestial; to govern the state with knowledge is to open the human. Opening the celestial brings compliance—compliance allows one to act without contrivance, and thus governance is unoppressive yet ordered. Opening the human brings contrivance—contrivance leads to excessive scrutiny, and thus the people are no longer pure and become deficient. Hence it is said: “To govern the state with knowledge is the state’s bane; to govern the state without knowledge is the state’s blessing.”

The reason rivers and seas can be kings of the hundred valleys is that they are good at lowering themselves—thus they can be kings of the hundred valleys. Therefore, when the sage desires to be above the people, he places his words below them; when he desires to lead the people, he places his person behind them. Thus, though he dwells above, the people do not find him burdensome; though he stands in front, the people do not find him harmful. Therefore, the world delights in exalting him without weariness. Because he does not contend, none in the world can contend with him.

*Wang’s note: “Wang” signifies that to which the world turns and flows. To dwell above is to lower one’s voice; to share joy is to lower one’s tone.*

Su annotates:
The sage does not desire to be above or lead the people. Yet when he lowers himself and places his person behind, his Dao inevitably elevates and advances him.

Lü’s annotation:
That the rivers and seas can be kings of the hundred valleys is because they excel at lowering themselves—thus they can be kings of the hundred valleys. Likewise, those who can be kings of the world excel at lowering themselves. The profound virtue (xuande) is precisely the Dao of lowering oneself to the utmost. Zhuang Zhou held that by dwelling in this lowliness, one embodies the Dao of the mysterious sage and unadorned king. When Shun remained below, it was said that his "profound virtue ascended to be heard"—thus, profound virtue is inherently the Dao by which the sage dwells in lowliness. To occupy a position above others while following the Dao of lowliness is to truly lower oneself.

When the sage possesses the world, in terms of his position, he indeed desires to be above the people. Yet he styles himself "orphaned, widowed, and unworthy" and accepts the world’s disgrace and misfortune—this is to place his words below them. In terms of precedence, he indeed desires to lead the people. Yet he moves only under compulsion, responds only to stimulus, and rises only when there is no alternative—this is to place his person behind them. Precisely because he places his words below, though he dwells above, the people do not find him burdensome. Not burdensome, they thus find bearing him light. Because he places his person behind, though he stands in front, the people do not find him harmful. Not harmful, they thus find following him beneficial. Not burdensome and not harmful—this is why the world delights in exalting him without weariness. Because he places his words below and his person behind, he does not contend. Delighting in exalting him without weariness, none in the world can contend with him. Could one who does not embody profound virtue achieve this? Therefore, it is said: "Only because he does not contend can none in the world contend with him."

Xizhai’s annotation:
When the sage is compelled to oversee the world, how does he regard towering above all things or standing above the people? Thus, though he possesses the world, he can place his words below the people and his person behind them—none but one who guards the world can do this. Therefore, though he dwells above the people, they do not find him burdensome, unaware he is their ruler. Though he stands before them, they do not find him harmful, unaware they are his people. Only when ruler and ruled guard one another does the world delight in exalting him without weariness. For what I take is not what the people contend for. The world is governed by non-action—how could it show its traces, let alone be contended with?

The world all says my Dao is great yet seems unworthy. Precisely because it is great, it seems unworthy. If it were worthy, it would have long been petty. I possess three treasures, which I treasure and hold fast: the first is compassion, the second is frugality, the third is not daring to be first in the world. Through compassion, one can be courageous; through frugality, one can be expansive; through not daring to be first, one can become the chief of vessels. Now, if one abandons compassion yet seeks courage, abandons frugality yet seeks expansiveness, abandons the last yet seeks the first—this is death. With compassion, one prevails in battle and stands firm in defense. Heaven will save and protect him with compassion.

Su’s annotation:
The Dao is vast and formless, declining and nameless, pervading all things yet resembling none—this is why it is great. If it resembled things, it would be but one thing among them—how could it be great?

The Dao is great precisely because it does not resemble things. Thus, when it operates as virtue, it moves dimly, finding advantage in dullness and advance in retreat, never conforming to worldly customs. Now, the world values courage, esteems vastness, and praises aggressive advancement, while what I treasure are compassion, forbearance, frugality, restraint, and humility—these three are all deemed unworthy by the world.

The world considers bold decisiveness worthy and compassionate forbearance inadequate for affairs, not knowing that bold decisiveness is easily thwarted, while compassionate forbearance cannot be overcome in the end—it must culminate in courage. The world values vastness to overshadow things and deems frugality and restraint base, not knowing that vastness is easily exhausted, while frugality and restraint are easily sufficient—they must culminate in expansiveness. The world esteems aggressive advancement as ability and regards not daring to be first as shameful, not knowing that aggressive advancement incurs much resentment from others, while not daring to be first brings joy in being pushed forward by the world—it must ultimately become the chief of vessels.

For when uncarved wood scatters to become vessels, the sage employs them as officials and leaders. Only after uncarved wood becomes vessels do they have subordinates and superiors. Courage, expansiveness, and being first—these three are what people universally resent. Resented by the multitude, they thus constantly verge on death. With compassion, one protects things, and things cherish him as parents; though they would die for him without hesitation, he can thus engage in battle and defense. When Heaven is about to save such a person, it opens his heart and will, making him compassionate without exception. If he is compassionate without exception, all things will protect him.

---

Lü’s annotation:
The world merely sees the greatness of my Dao and calls it unworthy, not realizing that its very greatness lies in appearing unworthy. How can this be explained? The Great Dao flows everywhere, left and right—there is nowhere it is not present. Those who see its absence in some places, its lack of resemblance to anything, call it unworthy, not knowing that its absence in no place and resemblance to nothing are precisely why the Dao is great.

For all things are none other than the Dao; thus, there is nothing outside the Dao. If there is nothing outside the Dao, there is nothing it resembles—this is why it is great. If it resembled something, there would be things outside the Dao. If there were things outside the Dao, the Dao would not be present in all places—how could it still be great? Therefore, it is said: "The world all says my Dao is great, seeming unworthy." Only because it is great does it seem unworthy. If it resembled things, it would long since have been insignificant.

Now, the reason my Dao is so great is that I have no self and do not contend. Only by having no self and not contending can one hold fast to what others find difficult to hold. I possess three treasures, which I treasure and hold fast: the first is compassion, the second is frugality, the third is not daring to be first in the world—these are all things others find difficult to hold. Why? People cannot be without self and cannot cease contending. Thus, they are courageous but cannot be compassionate, expansive but cannot be frugal, forward but cannot be last. Only having no self and not contending enables one to preserve these three treasures and hold them fast.

The world has a beginning, which is taken as the mother of all things, and I guard it, ever tolerant toward things, never cutting them down—is this not compassion? In conducting oneself, one moves slowly and does not waste, taking restraint as the principle—is this not frugality? Never putting oneself before others, always following them, while others all seek to be first, I alone take the last—is this not "not daring to be first in the world"?

Compassion appears soft and weak yet can overcome the hard and strong—this is courage. Frugality appears wasteful yet its use cannot be exhausted—this is expansiveness. Not daring to be first in the world appears to put one last, yet the sage employs it to become an official and leader, for all follow me—this is becoming the chief of vessels.

Now, if one abandons compassion yet seeks courage, abandons frugality yet seeks expansiveness, abandons the last yet seeks the first, one is merely a brute of hardness and strength—is death not fitting? From a practical perspective, in military affairs, love establishes authority, and without deceit, there is no achievement—compassion seems as if it cannot be applied. Yet from a fundamental perspective, the very ability to establish authority lies in compassion. Therefore, it is said: "With compassion, one prevails in battle and stands firm in defense." Only through compassion does one attain frugality; through frugality, one does not dare to be first in the world. Thus, compassion is the origin of the three treasures. Abandoning compassion leads to death; preserving compassion leads to life. Compassion is thus what protects one’s life. Therefore, it is said: "Heaven will save and protect him with compassion." When compassion is established, the three treasures are complete.

---

Yuanze’s annotation:
"Resemblance" means having something to liken oneself to. The Dao is the ancestor of all things, so those who embody the Dao should resemble me—how could I resemble things? For if one resembles something, it is like imitating that thing, and that thing must be great while I am small.

These three treasures are all deemed insignificant by worldly sentiments, yet they are what the perfected person uses to become great. Compassion is rooted in love—loving things is benevolence. Yet it is uniquely called "compassion" because benevolence is a broad virtue that covers all below in its fullest expression. Laozi speaks of its root, so he does not say "benevolence" but "compassion." Compassion is the fatherly way, the root of benevolence, requiring no artificial effort.

Frugality as a virtue means few desires, valuing the root, and cherishing things. One word encompasses three excellences—what is more perfect than frugality? Confucius said: "In ritual, it is better to be frugal than extravagant." For the sage establishes ritual to encourage frugality, yet when practicing ritual, one cannot be frugal alone. Without ritual, there is no refinement; without Laozi, one does not know the root. Is the root not the ultimate? In the *Book of Changes*, this is modesty. Heaven, Earth, humans, and spirits all value modesty, so the sage treasures it.

Compassion does not contend yet is victorious—no courage is greater. Those who vainly expand themselves through extravagance are extremely narrow. Only those who do not harm the root with the branches, do not extinguish the internal with the external, attain the utmost expansiveness.
