This is the sage’s practice, so he merely becomes the chief of vessels. As for the Dao’s leadership, it has never lagged behind things.

To use humans to extinguish Heaven, to let affairs overcome the Dao—even if one escapes by luck, one has lost what sustains life. The three treasures are all Heavenly virtues and establish the root. This is like the "orthodox troops" in warfare—orthodox troops mean an army that is ordered and unmoving. Loving the people as children, the people love him as a father; without commands, they are united. Those who turn their weapons to attack from behind and are defeated do so because their superiors are not compassionate.

One version reads: "With compassion, one prevails in battle." Battle and defense are rooted in killing and attack, yet they esteem compassion as virtue—other matters can be inferred. Han Fei’s explanation:
One who loves a child is compassionate toward the child; one who values life is compassionate toward oneself; one who prizes achievement is compassionate toward affairs. A compassionate mother’s love for her weak child...

Pursue the attainment of fortune; by pursuing the attainment of fortune, affairs are freed from calamity. When affairs are freed from calamity, thoughts and plans mature. When thoughts and plans mature, one grasps the principles of affairs. Grasping the principles of affairs ensures certain success. Certain success means acting without doubt—acting without doubt is called courage.

The sage’s approach to all matters is like a compassionate mother’s care for her weak child. Thus, when one sees the Way that must be followed, one acts with clarity, and in undertaking affairs, there is no doubt—this, too, is called courage. Doubtlessness arises from compassion; hence it is said, "Compassion gives rise to courage."

The Duke of Zhou said: "In winter’s freezing closure, if not firm, the growth of grasses and trees in spring and summer will not flourish. Heaven and Earth cannot constantly indulge in excess or waste—how much less can man? Thus, all things must have flourishing and decline, all affairs must have tension and relaxation, states must have civil and martial governance, and official administration must have rewards and punishments. Therefore, the wise man conserves his resources to enrich his household; the sage cherishes his spirit to refine his essence. The ruler values his soldiers in war, and the people multiply; a numerous people expands the state. Hence it is said: 'Through frugality, one attains expansiveness.'"

Dong’s commentary: "This uses warfare to illustrate that the Dao’s excellence lies in upholding compassion. Some say: If we speak of the human body, then wayward thoughts are like enemy states, and desires are sharper than blades. Should we not then reduce desires to the point of none, to preserve what is truly treasured?"

Mengde’s commentary: "This concerns how I conduct myself. The *Yi* says: 'Nothing is not aided; difficulties are resolved.' Thus, among the Three Treasures, compassion is placed first, and the text ultimately clarifies that with it, one prevails in battle and stands firm in defense—this is the urgent need of the present age. If Heaven wishes to save the people, it must bring forth one who acts with compassion to protect them. This is what Laozi deeply laments, hoping for the world."

One who excels as a warrior is not martial; one who excels in battle is not angry; one who excels in defeating the enemy does not contend; one who excels in employing others humbles himself. This is called the virtue of non-contention; this is called employing the strength of others; this is called matching Heaven, the ultimate of antiquity.

In ancient times, chariot warfare involved three armored warriors on the chariot: the left wielded a bow, the right held a spear, and the center drove and managed the flags and drums—all sought to be strong and martial. Seventy-two infantrymen stood below the chariot. Since contention arises only in warfare, it is used here to illustrate the virtue of non-contention.

Luo Zhu said: "When mind and form are empty, who can contend with one who is without thing?"

Su’s commentary: "A warrior should take martial skill as the foundation but act with caution. If one acts with martiality in a martial manner, one will die. The sage resorts to battle only when unavoidable; if one acts out of anger, it is for one’s own sake that one kills. If one kills for one’s own sake, Heaven will surely bring calamity. Because I do not contend, I can overcome the contender. If both sides contend, victory is not assured. All people have the desire to surpass others, so none can be employed by another. If one truly humbles oneself, then all under Heaven become one’s resource."

Lü’s commentary: "The term *shi* (warrior) refers to one who serves the Dao and takes leading others as their duty. Serving the Dao means overcoming through non-contention; leading others means employing their strength. If so, what need is there for martiality? Hence it is said: 'One who excels as a warrior is not martial.' If a warrior has no need for martiality, then one who excels in battle is not angry, one who excels in defeating the enemy does not contend, and one who excels in employing others humbles himself—this is entirely fitting.

Why is this so? One who embodies the Dao does not contend; if one does not contend, none in the world can contend with them. Thus, one who excels as a warrior is not martial, one who excels in battle is not angry, and one who excels in defeating the enemy does not contend—this is called the virtue of non-contention. One who embodies the Dao can humble themselves; one who can humble themselves is gladly employed by others and does not act for themselves. Thus, one who excels in employing others humbles themselves—this is called employing the strength of others. With virtue, one does not contend; with strength, one employs others. Even in the peril of warfare, I remain effortless—how much more so in other matters? Acting through non-action is called the virtue of Heaven. When one reaches non-action, one aligns with Heaven and cannot be surpassed. Hence it is said: 'This is called matching Heaven, the ultimate of antiquity.'"

Xizhai’s commentary: "The sage does not even claim possession of their own body—how much less would they contend? Because they do not contend, all their responses to things arise from necessity. Thus, as a warrior, they are not martial; when fighting, they are not angry; when defeating the enemy, they do not necessarily contend with them; when employing others, they do not place themselves above them. Therefore, they can subdue others through virtue, and others are glad to die for them. This is called matching Heaven, the ultimate of antiquity."

There is a saying in warfare: "I dare not act as the host but as the guest; I dare not advance an inch but retreat a foot." This is called advancing without formation, seizing without arms, resisting without an enemy, and wielding without weapons. No disaster is greater than underestimating the enemy; underestimating the enemy nearly forfeits what I treasure. Thus, when evenly matched armies meet, the one who grieves prevails.

Truly knowing that the constant of the Dao emerges from non-action, then in my conduct there is always non-conduct; in my repelling, always no arms; in my resistance, always no enemy; in my wielding, always no weapons. Where could one not prevail?

If one cannot emerge from non-action, knowing only the host and not the guest, knowing only advance and not retreat—this is called underestimating the enemy. Underestimating the enemy nearly forfeits what I treasure as the Three Treasures. Thus it is said: "No disaster is greater than underestimating the enemy; underestimating the enemy nearly forfeits my treasure." Only one who takes non-contention as victory cannot be overcome. Thus it is said: "When evenly matched armies meet, the one who grieves prevails."

My words are exceedingly easy to know, exceedingly easy to practice—yet none under Heaven can know them, none can practice them. Words have their source; affairs have their lord. It is only because of ignorance that they do not know me. Those who know me are rare; thus I am esteemed. Therefore the sage wears coarse cloth while holding jade within.

To know yet seem not to know is supreme; not to know yet seem to know is a flaw. Only by regarding this flaw as a flaw can one be without flaw. The sage is without flaw because he regards the flaw as a flaw—thus he is without flaw.

The Xizhai Annotation states: "The knowing chase after things, thus they are full of artifice; the unknowing return to the root, thus they are close to the real. One who can know the unknowing is close to the real; knowing the knowing as full of artifice is supreme. If one does not recognize that knowing is artifice and merely increases their knowing, this is like taking illness as medicine—such illness cannot be fully described. Only by knowing that knowing is illness can one be without illness. The sage is without illness because he knows this."

The Bishu states: "The Dao enters through knowing and transforms through unknowing. Knowing is the Buddha’s 'knowing nothing'; unknowing is the Buddha’s 'nothing knowing.' First, one uses 'knowing nothing' to dispel existence, then uses 'nothing knowing' to dispel knowing. All dharmas return to nothingness, yet nothingness does not stand alone—how could this not be supreme? Yet when they impose views upon emptiness or conjure mountains and rivers within purity, plucking flowers from their eyes or mistaking shadows for heads, this is illness. All knowing is delusion; all delusion is illness. Scholars, accustomed to grasping at what they deem profound and treasuring it, how can their illness be cured? The sage cannot abandon knowing, just as others cannot, yet knowing does not become illness for him because he knows that knowing is illness. Knowing it as illness, the illness is cured without medicine. 'Knowing the unknowing' is supreme—what is born yet without birth, the true nature serene. 'Not knowing the knowing' is illness—what is unborn yet born, karma and fruit manifest. Only by regarding illness as illness can one be without illness—this is 'knowing illusion and immediately departing, not employing expedient means.'"

When the people do not fear authority, great authority arrives. Do not constrict their dwelling; do not weary of their life. Only by not wearying can one be unwearying. Thus the sage knows himself but does not display himself; loves himself but does not esteem himself. Therefore, he discards that and adopts this.

Su’s Annotation states: "Human nature inherently possesses authority—lofty, luminous, vast, and resplendent—nothing can surpass it. This is what is called great authority. People are constantly troubled by drowning in delusions, fearing life and death, and dreading the myriad threats of things that assail them, leaving no respite for lifelong anxiety. Though great authority exists, they do not recognize it. If one truly knows, life and death are equal, gains and losses are level—fearlessly, great authority appears before them. The greatness of nature can envelop heaven and earth, yet the unknowing take their four limbs and nine orifices as themselves, guarding them without weariness. Thus, their vision does not extend beyond seeing, their hearing does not extend beyond listening—they are utterly confined. Therefore, the teaching says: 'Do not constrict their dwelling.' Those who know this recognize the vastness of nature and the narrowness of their own lives, thus they lament, desiring escape yet unable, unaware of the path of weariness and longing, bound by things. Therefore, the teaching says: 'Do not weary of their life.' Only the sage is neither constricted nor wearied. Born like others, he dwells with the Dao, without distinction between vastness and narrowness, purity and filth. Only after not wearying of life does one know that life is not wearying. Though the sage knows himself, he does not display himself; though he loves himself, he does not esteem himself, fearing that others will develop weariness and longing. Until the heart of weariness and longing is dispelled, fear remains. Only when fear departs does great authority arrive."

Lü’s Annotation states: "When the people are not attuned to the Dao but rely solely on knowledge, their lives become excessive, and with excessive life comes disregard for death. Disregarding death, they do not fear authority. When the people reach the point of not fearing authority, there is nothing they will not do—this is how Heaven manifests its authority and metes out great punishment. Thus it is said: 'When the people do not fear authority, great authority arrives.' Only one who governs the world with the Dao knows that all under Heaven, born of Heaven, share the same dwelling—let it not be constricted, and the spirit will flow unimpeded in all directions. All under Heaven, born of Heaven, share the same life—let it not be wearied, and the people will have no constant attachment to the self. Only by not wearying can the people also not weary—how then could they not fear authority? Therefore, the sage knows himself through constancy yet does not display himself, placing himself externally. Knowing constancy and placing himself externally, he does not constrict his dwelling. Loving himself through frugality yet not esteeming himself, relinquishing his life, he does not weary of life. Thus, he discards the illness of knowledge and adopts the non-illness of not knowing."

Yuanzhe’s Annotation states: "When the people are simple and the ruler is generous, they fear authority. When the ruler loses the Dao and engages in excessive action, diminishing the Dao and obstructing life, the people become cunning and insincere, and authority cannot subdue them. In this case, Heaven’s punishment will be applied, and calamities will arise. Thus it is said: 'Great authority arrives.' Human nature is inherently vast and flowing, yet as worldly teachings decline, it cannot return to simplicity. Instead, its dwelling is constricted, making it narrow, and its life is wearied, making it obstructed. Only by constricting their dwelling do the people become impure and artificial; only by wearying their life do they become thin and insincere. The sage is not like this—he allows the people to roam freely in the vast dwelling of the world, each fulfilling their expansive nature. Then, how could they offend authority? Zhuangzi said: 'The lands of the Xirong and Xunwei, the parks of the Yellow Emperor, the palaces of Yao and Shun, the halls of Tang and Wu'—this illustrates how each generation declines, gradually constricting their dwelling. Were those sages of varying excellence? No, the circumstances of their times made it so. The ruler does not weary of his own life, fully realizing his nature, so the people also realize their nature. Zhuangzi said: 'Not wearying of Heaven'—this is what it means. Weariness is Heaven’s weariness. Displaying oneself leads to pride in achievement; esteeming oneself leads to belittling others. This is how one constricts their own dwelling and wearies their own life, thereby constricting the people’s dwelling and wearying their life. Knowing oneself is clarity of nature, not engaging in delusion; loving oneself is preserving the body, not committing wrongs. How then could one offend Heaven’s authority?"

Dong’s Annotation states: "Dwelling is the ground of nature. Dwelling in the vast dwelling of the world, one’s body becomes one with the great void. Life is the gathering of qi, containing the utmost essence of the Great One, its function the same as creation."

Only the narrow-minded scholar cannot be spoken to of the Dao. If one is not submerged in petty techniques yet truly has no means to constrict their own dwelling, they will surely abandon the existent for the non-existent and be deluded by annihilation, thereby wearying what gives them life. Thus, one is instructed in non-constriction and non-weariness, so that learners may know that nature is the commander of qi, its greatness boundless—it should be expanded and filled to preserve its vastness. Life is the lodging of the Dao, its function inexhaustible—it should be nurtured and cultivated to fulfill its use. Then, in nurturing qi, preserving life, fully realizing nature, and fulfilling destiny, no weariness or longing arises, for one embodies nothing that is not strength, nothing that is not vitality.

Long life is not clinging to life, nor is life an obstacle. In this way, life dwells with the Dao, and the Dao never wearies of or abandons humanity. To "see worthiness" is to make it universally manifest. Through self-knowledge, one reflects inwardly without fear and does not constrict their dwelling; through self-love, one severs external cravings and does not weary their life. Only self-knowledge and self-love bring tranquility to one’s dwelling. Yet by not displaying oneself to boast of ability, and not esteeming oneself to belittle others, one forgets the perception of being known and the heart of being loved.

**Xizhai’s Annotation:**
Those who seek others’ awe and affection are all inwardly deficient and outwardly longing. The sage is inwardly sufficient, viewing others’ joy without adding to it, their lack of fear without diminishing it—thus, all things are at ease with them without effort. To place oneself above things, making others fear oneself, is easy; to make others unafraid of oneself is difficult. To make me guard against others is easy; to make the world guard against me is hard. If the people do not fear authority, it is because the world guards against me—this is none other than the authority of virtue and the Dao. The sage encounters things and rests in whatever they meet, never constricting their dwelling nor wearying their life, for they know that their dwelling and life are not their true reality. Only because I do not weary myself does life not weary me. From self-knowledge comes the absence of self-display, though knowledge exists; from self-love comes the absence of self-esteem, though love exists. If one harbors the heart of self-display and self-esteem, they constrict their dwelling and weary their life, unable to find peace even for a day.

**Wu’s Annotation:**
What is fearful harms life and injures the body; the greatest fear is death. Those who do not fear what should be feared will injure their bodies, harm their lives, and hasten their death—then the greatest fear arrives. Zhuangzi said: "What people take as fearful lies in their bed and at their meals, yet they do not guard against it—this is their error." *Xia* (狎) means familiarity and habit. One’s dwelling is where the body resides; *yan* (厌) means to detest and abandon. In daily life, one fearlessly indulges in things that harm life and injure the body, becoming habituated and complacent—this is not fearing authority. To weary one’s life is to harm life and hasten death, detesting one’s existence and abandoning one’s destiny—then the greatest fear arrives. Only by not becoming familiar with one’s dwelling and fearing what should be feared can one avoid wearying their life, and the greatest fear will not come. *Self-knowledge* is knowing the way of loving one’s body; *self-display* is flaunting one’s knowledge to others; *self-esteem* (as in a later chapter) refers to the excessive desire for life. The sage, though inwardly knowing the way of self-love, embodies virtue and conceals illumination, knowing as if not knowing, not outwardly displaying it. Though deeply loving life, they embody the Dao naturally, not clinging to life or committing the error of excessive desire. The former refers to self-display and self-esteem; the latter refers to self-knowledge and self-love. The above speaks of not fearing leading to the calamity of great fear, and not becoming familiar leading to the blessing of non-weariness—this is addressed to the common people. As for the sage, they do not wait for fear to be without what is fearful, nor do they wait for non-familiarity to be without what is familiar. Inwardly, they possess the reality of self-knowledge and self-love; outwardly, they leave no trace of self-display or self-esteem. What is absent is what is discarded; what is present is what is embraced.

Those bold in daring meet death; those bold in restraint live. These two may bring either benefit or harm—who knows the reason for Heaven’s aversion? Thus, even the sage finds this difficult.

The Dao of Heaven does not contend yet excels in victory, does not speak yet excels in response, is not summoned yet comes of itself, and is unhurried yet excels in planning. Heaven’s net is vast and sparse, yet nothing escapes it.

*(Annotations in the original: "殺" means death; "利" refers to life, "害" to death; "惡" is read in the departing tone; "繟" is pronounced "chǎn," meaning leisurely and unhurried. Wang Bi’s version uses "坦," while Yan Zun’s uses "默," but "繟" is superior, for "默" implies excessive weight in silence, and "坦" approaches non-contention.)*

Su Zhe’s commentary:
To be bold in daring is to die; to be bold in restraint is to live—this is the constant principle of things. Yet the daring may sometimes survive, and the restrained may sometimes perish, leading the world to chance upon such exceptions while ignoring the regular pattern. How vast is the Dao of Heaven! Even if there is an exception, who knows whence its preferences and aversions arise? Thus, even the sage takes the constant as the norm and ever finds the matter of bold daring difficult.

Liezi says: "To anticipate Heaven’s intent and weigh profit and harm is not as good as refraining." How difficult it is to fathom the Dao of Heaven! Therefore, it is expounded in detail: one does not contend with things in the moment but seeks ultimate victory. What does Heaven say? The four seasons proceed, and the myriad things are born.

No seeker goes unanswered—
the advent of the numinous cannot be measured,
how much less can it be aimed at?
Yet who summons it?

It unfolds with ease, as if aimless,
yet its plans and measures surpass human reach.
The world observes Heaven through the senses,
seeing but a fragment and missing the great whole.
When good meets misfortune and evil meets fortune,
none doubts the looseness of Heaven’s net and its many lapses.

Only by grasping the beginning and end,
exhausting all transformations,
does one know its vastness—
though seemingly loose, it never fails.

---

**Lü’s Commentary:**
To impose one’s strength and insist on things is to be "bold in daring"—such are the ranks of the dead. Hence it is said: "Bold in daring is to die."
To yield one’s rigidity and commit to nothing is to be "bold in restraint"—such are the ranks of the living. Hence it is said: "Bold in restraint is to live."
What men deem gain in bold daring may harbor harm;
what men deem harm in bold restraint may harbor gain.
Yet Heaven’s aversions—how can they be known by knowledge or recognized by perception?
Thus the sage’s every move is
as cautious as wading winter streams,
as wary as fearing four neighbors—
so difficult is it, for Heaven’s aversions are unknowable.
Only by remaining unknowing and unperceiving
can one accord with the Emperor’s principles.

Heaven gives life to things according to their nature and enriches them:
what is planted, it nurtures;
what leans, it topples.
It never contends with things,
yet nothing can defy it.
Thus it is said: "Not contending, yet excelling in victory."
What does Heaven say?
The four seasons proceed,
the myriad things are born—
their procession and birth never err.
Thus it is said: "Not speaking, yet excelling in response."
What acts without acting is Heaven;
what arrives without being summoned is fate.
Thus it is said: "Uncalled, it comes of itself."
The *Yi* is easy to know,
its Dao waxes and wanes with time,
never deviating.
Thus it is said: "At ease, yet excelling in planning."
Heaven’s keen hearing comes from the people’s keen hearing;
Heaven’s bright majesty comes from the people’s bright majesty.
Its hearing and majesty are never self-serving—
blessing follows compliance,
misfortune follows defiance,
swift as echo follows sound.
Thus it is said: "Heaven’s net is vast, seemingly loose, yet never failing."
Only because Heaven’s Dao is unknowable in this way
does the sage practice bold restraint,
remaining unknowing and unperceiving—
this is how one accords with it.

---

**Xizhai’s Commentary:**
People know "bold in daring" but not "bold in restraint."
"Bold in daring" is treading on naked blades;
"bold in restraint" is treading the Middle Way.
Thus it is said: "Blades may be trodden, but the Middle Way cannot."
Comparing the two:
treading blades brings harm, yet people dismiss the harm;
treading the Middle Way brings benefit, yet people ignore the benefit.
Is this not why the Middle Way is so difficult?
To act with reckless boldness is what men resent and Heaven abhors—
who knows the reason?
Even the sage finds it hard,
how much more the common people!

For Heaven’s Dao:
not contending, yet excelling in victory;
not speaking, yet excelling in response;
uncalled, yet coming of itself;
at ease, yet excelling in planning.
Knowing its aversion to reckless boldness—
before Heaven’s victory, before its response,
before its arrival, before its plans are set,
one might say the strong sometimes prevail,
the bold sometimes win—
such cases exist.
But observe the outcome in time,
and you will see:
Heaven’s net is vast, seemingly loose, yet never failing.

---

**Wu’s Commentary:**
The recklessly bold are those Heaven abhors,
yet Heaven’s aversions are profound and obscure—
how can one know who is truly abhorred?
Even when such a person deserves death,
the sage still hesitates and does not lightly kill.
If the sage does not lightly kill,
then wrongdoers all slip through the net—
does this mean Heaven’s net has gaps?
Heaven’s dealings with the wicked are not like man’s forceful contention;
Heaven’s victory is certain without effort.
They are not like man’s verbal pronouncements;
its responses are as swift as echo follows sound.
Its retribution comes quickly,
arriving unsummoned.
The wicked meet their due,
and no cunning can escape.
Though Heaven is without intent,
calm and unhurried,
its retribution is more artful than human schemes.
This is Heaven’s vast net—
seemingly loose, yet never failing.
Not a single wrongdoer escapes;
none slip through.
Though the sage does not kill,
Heaven kills of itself.

---

**Hongfu’s Commentary:**
Victory through non-contention,
response through non-speaking,
arrival through non-summoning—
this is Heaven’s seeming looseness that never fails.
If one contends to win,
though not loose, there will be failure.

---

The people do not fear death—
why threaten them with it?
If one could make them always fear death
and execute those who act strangely,
who would dare?
Yet there is always an executioner—
to kill in place of the executioner
is like hewing in place of the master carpenter.
Few who hew in place of the master carpenter
do not injure their hands.

---

**Su’s Commentary:**
When governance is oppressive and punishments severe,
the people have nowhere to turn—
they cease to fear death.
Threatening them with death is futile.
When the people are at ease with good governance,
they cherish life and fear death.
Then, seizing those who act strangely and disrupt order to execute them—
who would dare disobey?
The executioner is Heaven.
In a well-ordered age,
if strange and disruptive people run rampant,
they are Heaven’s rejects.
To kill them is to act as Heaven’s agent—
not my own doing.
To kill those not rejected by Heaven
is to kill in place of the executioner.
To hew in place of the master carpenter is to injure one’s hands;
to kill in place of the executioner is to bring harm upon oneself.

---

**Lü’s Commentary:**
The people’s nature is such that they do not fear death.
Only by not constricting their dwelling,
not sating their desires,
can one make them respect authority and value life.
Why threaten them with death to make them fear it?
How do we know this?
If the people always feared death
and one could seize and execute those who act strangely,
who would dare?
Yet seizing them does not stop them,
killing them does not end it—
this proves the people do not fear death
and cannot be threatened with it.

Then how should one govern the world through the Dao?

Let the executioners kill—do not usurp their role.

What does it mean to let the executioners kill?
The Heavenly net is vast and wide, its mesh sparse yet nothing escapes.
When Heaven punishes crime, the five punishments and five penalties are applied—
this is the executioner’s role, Heaven’s own work.

If punishments and executions arise from personal preference
rather than Heaven’s judgment,
then one is usurping the executioner’s role.

To usurp the executioner’s role is to usurp the master carpenter’s axe—
few who do so escape harm to their hands.
Thus, the harm of usurping the executioner’s role is clear.

---
**Zhang Lin’s Commentary:**
Human life is not taken at the hearth or in meals;
disease, theft, and punishments claim more than half.
Thus, people do not truly know fear of death.
Yet those who govern things wish them to fear—
only by threatening death can they hope to instill fear.
Hence it is said: *The people do not fear death—
why threaten them with death?*
If they truly feared death,
I could seize and execute those who act strangely—
who would dare?
Yet seizing them does not stop them,
killing them does not end it—
this proves death cannot incite fear.
Thus it is said:
*If the people feared death and acted strangely,
I could seize and execute them—who would dare?*
Things do not fear those who wield death,
for all things fade and return to extinction—
only then does the executioner’s role persist.
To seize the executioner’s authority and use it to instill fear
is not only insufficient to deter things
but also fails to reach its mark.
Thus it is said:
*Let the executioners kill—
to usurp their role is to usurp the master carpenter’s axe,*
and few escape harm to their hands.
Then how can one who holds the reins of governance,
driving all under Heaven’s people to obey,
do so rightly?

---
**Xīzhāi’s Annotation:**
This shows that the world’s laws are unreliable for governance.
The people do not fear death—
why threaten them with death?
If the people truly feared death,
seizing and executing those who act strangely
would suffice to govern.
Yet the more one kills, the less it deters—
punishment is insufficient.
The Qin used strict laws with a dense net,
yet crimes did not cease.
The Han loosened the net, letting even great offenders slip through,
yet all under Heaven turned to them.
This alone proves it.
Heaven’s way rewards virtue and punishes licentiousness—
the executioner’s role is fixed within its bounds, inescapable.
If one must usurp the executioner’s role,
like usurping the master carpenter’s axe,
there are none who do not harm their hands.

---
**Xuē’s Annotation:**
Our dynasty’s Emperor Taizu, in his preface to the *Dao De Jing*, said:
*"Since my ascension, I knew not the Way of ancient sage-kings.
I asked others, but all spoke from their own perspectives.
One day, I read the *Dao De Jing*—
its words were simple, its meaning profound.
Later, I saw in the text: ‘The people do not fear death—
why threaten them with death?’
At that time, the world had just been unified,
the people were stubborn, officials corrupt.
Though ten were executed in the morning,
a hundred more arose in the evening.
Was this not as the text foretold?
I abolished extreme punishments and confined them to labor—
within a year, my heart grew lighter.
I knew this text was the root of all things,
the supreme teacher of kings,
the utmost treasure of subjects and ministers—
not mere alchemical arts.
Ah! The Emperor was indeed a sage of Heaven’s endowment,
his wisdom and insight penetrating the text’s depths.
Thus, the sage-king of the Yuan Dynasty, Taizu, was born.
The words of the virtuous spread far and wide—
this is the *Dao De Jing*."

---
The people hunger because their rulers take too much in taxes—
thus hunger arises.
Governance is difficult because rulers act—
thus difficulty arises.
The people treat death lightly because life is made too plentiful—
thus they treat death lightly.
Only those who do not cling to life
are wiser than those who prize it.

---
**Sū’s Annotation:**
When rulers act excessively, the people respond in kind—
thus governance becomes complex.
When rulers prioritize profit and desire,
the people compete for abundance in life,
so even death cannot sate their greed.
Thus, those who do not cling to life
preserve themselves naturally.

---
**Lǚ’s Commentary:**
If one man’s labor feeds several households,
why should there be hunger?
Yet hunger exists—
is it not because rulers take too much in taxes?
Weave and you are clothed; till and you are fed—
this is harmony in virtue.
Why is governance difficult?
Is it not because rulers act excessively?
If their food is sweet, their garments fine,
their customs settled, their dwellings joyous—
why should they treat death lightly?
Yet they do—
is it not because life is made too plentiful?
Thus the sage acts without action, and the people prosper naturally.
The sage desires nothing, and the people return to simplicity.
Then there is no hunger from excessive taxation,
no difficulty from governance through action,
no lightness toward death from abundance in life.
It is because they struggle for gain,
chase knowledge, and vie for profit—
this cannot be stopped.
Thus, those who do not cling to life
are wiser than those who prize it is clear.
Therefore, the sage does not exalt himself,
does not value his body,
does not cherish his life—
he knows only to act without clinging to life.

---
**Xīzhāi’s Annotation:**
If rulers take much, the people grow poor.
If rulers act, the people rebel—
this is inevitable.
I seek to enrich their lives,
yet neglect others’—
how can they not treat death lightly?
The sage’s regard for life is not indulgent;
he sees others as not clinging to life.
Would he enrich himself while depriving others?
This is what it means to be wiser than those who prize life.

---
A living thing is soft and weak;
when it dies, it becomes hard and strong.
Grass and trees, when living, are soft and brittle;
when dead, they wither and decay.
Thus, hardness and strength belong to death,
softness and weakness to life.
Therefore, the strong in arms are not victorious,
the strong in wood are easily broken—
the great place the low, the weak the high.

---
**Annotations:**
*Firm and strong belong to death’s domain;
soft and weak to life’s.
When hands join, they are called *gòng* (共).
*Shang* (商) and *Sāng* (桑) are trees;
*Gǔ* (谷) grows in the morning and reaches full size in seven days.
*Qín Bó* (秦伯) was angry, and *Shèng Shū* (蹇叔) said:
*"The tomb trees have grown to encircle the grave"*—
all have bent to embrace it.
*Dòng* (董) sounds as written;
*chù* (处) means "place."
*Gòng* (共) here means "to act together, to join in felling."
*Shàng* (上) is "above," *xià* (下) is "below."

The **Dao** abides within—
when **qi** surges forth, the body knows no ailment of hardness and strength;
when the utmost principle resides, affairs bear no burden of rigidity and force.
Army that triumphs through righteousness is not strong;
strength without righteousness—its defeat is swift.
Wood that bends itself to encircle must be felled—
this is the nature of things:
the refined rests above, the coarse below;
the refined must be soft and weak, the coarse strong and great.

---

**Commentary of Lü:**

The **Dao**, as a thing, is formless yet does not contend—
thus, the utmost **gù** (孤, "lonely") and weak, yet none comprehend it.
Therefore, I liken it to form:
*"Man’s life is soft and weak, his death firm and strong;
wood’s life is soft and brittle, its death withered and decayed.
Though form exists, it dies in rigidity and lives in softness—
how much more so the **Dao**, formless, that does not attain softness?
Thus, armies strong in arms grow arrogant, inviting enemy schemes—
my arrogance invites their plots, so I do not prevail.
Wood strong in trunk must be felled—
this is not mere chance.
But in terms of station:
Heaven’s **qi** is above, Earth’s form below—
**qi** is soft and weak, form firm and strong.
Ministers act; the sovereign nurtures.
Action is firm and strong, inaction soft and weak.
Firm and strong dwell below, soft and weak above—
this is the nature of things.
Thus, it is clear that softness and weakness can overcome rigidity and strength.

---

**Commentary of Xī Zhāi:**

This chapter broadly speaks of softness and weakness bringing life,
rigidity and strength bringing death.
Though softness and weakness are not the **Dao** itself,
they approach *wuwei* (無為, non-action);
rigidity and strength, though distant from the **Dao**,
lean toward *youwei* (有為, action).
*Wuwei* does not stray far from the **Dao**,
but *youwei* brings fortune and misfortune, regret and blame—
ever more distant from the **Dao**.

---

**Commentary of Wú:**

When wielding arms, feigning weakness conceals deep strategy,
while the enemy, lighthearted, underestimates—thus victory.
Relying on strength invites shallow calculation and arrogance,
while the enemy, fearful, prepares—thus defeat.
The art of war begins like a maiden’s modesty,
then bursts forth like a fleeing hare—
if the enemy cannot intercept, victory is assured.

The armies of Qin, crossing Zhou’s border with three hundred chariots,
were defeated at Zhaoyao.
The armies of Qi, invading Jin, were ambushed by Jie Shi’s stones—
both perished by their own overconfidence.

*Gòng* (共) means the two hands encircling.
The weak, swaying reeds are the slender branches near the end;
the strong, unyielding trunks are the sturdy roots.
Thus, armies strong in arms are felled by others—
this is dwelling below.
Wood strong in trunk, near the roots, is also below—
yet it cannot rise like the slender branches.

Pushing this principle further:
In human virtue, those rigid and strong, proud and domineering,
shall stumble—
their lofty station shall bring them low.
The soft and weak, revered by all,
shall ascend in esteem.

---

**Heaven’s Way—like drawing a bow:**
The high are pressed down, the low raised;
the overabundant are diminished, the deficient are supplemented.
Heaven’s Way: to diminish the surplus and supplement the lacking.
But man’s Way is not so:
he diminishes the lacking to serve the surplus.
Who can, with their surplus, nourish all under Heaven?
Only the **Dao**-bearing Sage.
Thus, the Sage acts yet does not rely on results,
achieves yet does not claim them—
his virtue is unseen.

---

**Commentary of Xī Shēng:**

*The Book of Changes* says:
*"Establish Heaven’s Way—it is yin and yang.
Yang ascends, yin descends.
When yang reaches its peak, it descends;
when yin reaches its peak, it ascends.
This is pressing down the high and raising the low—
the image of a drawn bow."*

Heaven’s Way of surplus and deficiency:
seven *rùn* (闰, intercalary) days to compensate for the sun’s excess,
supplementing the moon’s lack.
But man’s Way is otherwise:
he diminishes the lacking people to serve the surplus ruler.
Who can model Heaven’s Way for man’s?
Only the Sage, who diminishes his surplus to nourish all under Heaven.

In *Changes*, *"Punishing the high, benefiting the low"* is *Sǔn* (损),
*"Benefiting the high, punishing the low"* is *Yì* (益)—
the low is the foundation.
Thus, the Sage acts yet does not rely on deeds,
achieves yet does not dwell on them—
his virtue is unseen by men.
Therefore, the world calls him *"seemingly unfilial"*—
all to diminish the surplus and serve the lacking.

---

**Commentary of Sū:**

Drawing a bow:
when slack, the upper sinew droops, the lower angle rises;
when taut, the upper angle rises, the lower sinew droops.
Thus, pressing down the high and raising the low—
Heaven has no bias, so it balances.
Men are biased, so they do not balance.
The **Dao**-bearing Sage nourishes all things without complaint—
giving to others only increases his own abundance,
bestowing upon others only multiplies his own wealth.
None but the **Dao**-bearing can endure this.
If one acts yet relies on results,
dwells on achievements—
then virtue is seen in the world,
and virtue seen in the world is but self-aggrandizement.

---

**Commentary of Lü:**

Heaven’s Way is simply *wuwei*.
*Wuwei* means no partiality, no partiality means balance—
just as drawing a bow:
the high are pressed down, the low raised;
the surplus diminished, the lacking supplemented—
this is balance alone.

Heaven’s Way does not deliberately press down the high or raise the low;
*wuwei* lets things follow their nature—
the high become surplus and must be pressed down,
the low become lacking and must be raised.
*"Fullness invites diminishment, humility invites benefit"*—
this is Heaven’s Way.

But man’s Way cannot be *wuwei*;
cannot be *wuwei*, so cannot be without partiality;
cannot be without partiality, so diminishes the lacking to serve the surplus—
the difference is clear.

Only the **Dao**-bearing knows:
before things exist, there is no action or achievement—
wealth and fame are my surplus, the world’s lack.
Thus, he diminishes his surplus to nourish all under Heaven,
yet does not possess it—
hence, *"Who can diminish his surplus to nourish all under Heaven?
Only the **Dao**-bearing Sage."*

The Sage acts yet does not rely on results,
achieves yet does not dwell on them—
his virtue is unseen by men.
This is no other than following Heaven’s Way.

Now, the **Dao**-bearing Sage understands:

*"To gather much and benefit the few is the Way of Heaven;
to gather the few and benefit the many is the Way of man.*
Only the Sage can diminish his own surplus to nourish others’ lack,
thus his power reaches all things without relying on it—
his achievements overshadow all under Heaven yet he does not claim them,
his blessings extend to all under Heaven yet he does not seek recognition as virtuous.
Only the **Dao**-bearing can act thus.*

Nothing under Heaven is softer or weaker than water,
yet in overcoming the hard and strong, nothing can surpass it—
for there is nothing to replace it.
Thus, the soft overcomes the hard, the weak overcomes the strong.

Nothing under Heaven does not know this,
yet few can practice it.
Therefore, the Sage says:
*"To bear the defilement of the state is called the sovereign of the land;
to bear the misfortunes of the state is called the king of all under Heaven.*
This is **Dao**-conforming speech turned upside-down."

**Xu Zhu’s annotation:**
*"**Dao**-conforming speech aligns with the Way yet opposes common conventions.
Common men regard bearing defilement as shame and misfortunes as calamity—
thus the Sage’s words."*

**Lü Buwei’s annotation:**
*"Among all things under Heaven, only water follows the contours of the curved and straight, the round and square—
this is its softness and weakness, unmatched by any.
Yet it flows over vast lands, turns great stones, pierces steep cliffs, and carries all things across the Earth—
only water can do this, making it unsurpassed in overcoming the hard and strong.
The reason lies in its nature:
though it twists and turns in countless forms, it never loses its essence as water—
this is why nothing can replace it.
Water’s softness and weakness conquer hardness and strength,
though all know this, the Master repeatedly emphasizes it—
for all know it yet few can embody it.
Though one may be clever and wise enough to rule,
his woe lies not in lacking ability or strength,
but in failing to diminish his surplus to nourish the world,
to govern with softness and weakness yet always be burdened by empty titles,
a mere corpse of wisdom, weighed down by the schemes of ministers.
Thus, the Master speaks of the end of **Dao** and virtue,
and repeatedly returns to this principle,
also citing the Sage’s words to confirm it:
*'To bear the defilement of the state is called the sovereign of the land;
to bear the misfortunes of the state is called the king of all under Heaven.'*
He makes clear that the true means of subduing the world lies here,
not in the other empty words.
The kings of the Three Dynasties must first be known for their good reputation,
yet they say: *'To bear the defilement of the state is called the sovereign of the land;
to bear the misfortunes of the state is called the king of all under Heaven.'*
Why so?
It is not merely to avoid fame’s empty words—
those are insufficient.
To bear the defilement and misfortunes of the state is to transcend fame’s words,
for fame cannot describe it.
Fame’s words do not reach the truth—
they are merely responses to circumstances.
The Sage’s words embody the **Dao**—
they are the true speech.
Yet he says: *'To bear the defilement of the state is called the sovereign of the land;
to bear the misfortunes of the state is called the king of all under Heaven.'*
Thus, it is called **Dao**-conforming speech turned upside-down."*

**Shen Ye’s annotation:**
*"Though hardness and strength may conquer hardness and strength,
they will always end in flaws and exhaustion.
Thus, nothing can surpass softness and weakness in overcoming hardness and strength—
the soft and weak conquer without intent yet naturally prevail.
Hence, he warns: *'Do not treat softness and weakness lightly!'
In the end, softness and weakness conquer hardness and strength,
while hardness and strength cannot contend with them.
Mountains and forests harbor diseases—
this is the utmost softness.
Rivers and marshes collect filth—
this is the utmost weakness.
If one claims to be the sovereign of the land yet cannot bear the defilement of all directions,
or to be the king of all under Heaven yet demands the state be free from calamity and the seas from evil,
can such a man exist?
Even if he seeks to eliminate and extinguish them,
he will only exhaust himself in vain.
This is indeed **Dao**-conforming speech turned upside-down,
yet in truth, it is the most **Dao**-conforming speech of all—
one must carefully observe it."*

「To reconcile great grudges, there must inevitably be lingering grudges—
how can this be considered good?
Hence, the Sage holds the left tally without demanding recompense from others,
so that those with **virtue** govern the tally and those without govern the passing through.
The **Dao** has no partiality, always favoring the virtuous.

**【Tally (契):** *a wooden token split in two, each half held by different parties to confirm agreements or transactions. **Left tally (左契):*** the half kept by the giver, symbolizing trust without coercion. **Right tally (右契):*** the half held by the receiver, used for verification. **Passing through (彻):*** forcing a transaction or demanding payment. **Sympathy (讣):*** a variant of "tally," here implying a forced or partial reconciliation. **】*

**【Sū’s Annotation:】**
Grudges arise from delusion, and delusion stems from human nature.
Those who understand human nature do not see delusion manifest,
so how could there be grudges?
Now, if one does not remove the root yet seeks to reconcile the branches,
then even if the surface appears reconciled, the inner resentment remains unguarded.
The left and right tallies exist to establish trust and prevent disputes—
the Sage treats others as he would the tally, demonstrating **Dao**-conforming conduct
without partiality or calculation.
He shows people their true nature,
teaching them to eliminate delusion and restore their original state,
so that when delusion is exhausted and nature returns,
they naturally find fulfillment without needing the left tally’s restraint
or the right tally’s demands.
Thus, though great grudges persist,
they will dissolve like melting ice—
knowing their root is nonexistent,
why bother reconciling?
Those without **virtue** who seek to force universal harmony
will only labor in vain.
The **Dao** has no partiality, always aligning with the virtuous—
this is the governance of the tally.
The **Dao** has no favoritism, only the virtuous receive its blessing—
this too is the governance of the tally.

**【Lǚ’s Annotation:】**
When retaliating, one does not break the tally;
though there may be resentment, no grudge is held.
Roof tiles may fall, but the **Dao** of universal balance does not arise from this.
Thus, grudges spring from those who seek to reconcile them—
if one insists on reconciling great grudges, the lingering resentment will never end.
Hence, it is said:
*"To reconcile great grudges, there must be lingering grudges—
how can this be considered good?"*
Therefore, the Sage holds the left tally without demanding recompense,
allowing those who come to take to do so naturally,
without calculating their virtue or malice.
Reconciling grudges involves a desire to benefit others,
but it is not as pure as the **Dao**-conforming conduct of holding the left tally,
observing things in stillness,
and letting nature take its course.
Those with **virtue** act without calculation;
those without **virtue** act with scheming intent.
**Passing through** is the scheming way.
In ancient times, the **Eight Families (八家)** assisted in farming,
but the Duke of Zhōu feared their private fields would yield uneven harvests,
so he ordered them to work together and share equally.
The **Eight Families** received equal rewards,
with no surplus or deficiency—
this was the governance of the left tally,
allowing others to take without concern for their virtue or malice.
Thus, those who govern the **Dao** of the tally
do not demand recompense from others,
while those who govern the **Dao** of passing through
scheme to force transactions.
Reconciling grudges fears the virtuous may suffer,
but even with good intentions,
it resembles the governance of passing through—
those who assist the weak fear their rewards will be meager.

**【Wú’s Annotation:】**
To hold the left tally without demanding recompense
is to act without scheming intent.
The tally is a split wooden token used as proof;
the giver holds the left half, the receiver the right.
When one demands payment from another, it is called *"demanding the tally (责契)."*
The left tally belongs to the giver,
while the right is given to the receiver.
The *Shǐjì* records that one *"holds the right tally to demand service (操右契以责事),"*
and the *Lǐjì* states that those *"offering land or houses hold the right tally (献田宅者操右契)."*
The left tally is held by the receiver,
as explained by Zhèng.
Holding the left tally means
one does not demand recompense from others,
but waits for them to come and take from oneself—
if they bring the right tally to match, then the transaction is given freely,
without calculation or partiality.
Reconciling grudges involves scheming to benefit others,
but it is not as pure as the **Dao**-conforming conduct of holding the left tally,
observing things in stillness,
and letting nature take its course.
Those with **virtue** act without scheming intent;
those without **virtue** act with calculation.
**Passing through** is the scheming way.

In ancient times, the **Eight Families (八家)** assisted in farming,
but the Duke of Zhōu feared their private fields would yield uneven harvests,
so he ordered them to work together and share equally.
The **Eight Families** received equal rewards,
with no surplus or deficiency—
this was the governance of the left tally,
allowing others to take without concern for their virtue or malice.

Thus, those who govern the **Dao** of the tally
do not calculate others’ virtue or malice,
while those who govern the **Dao** of passing through
scheme to force transactions.
Reconciling grudges fears the virtuous may suffer,
but even with good intentions,
it resembles the governance of passing through—
those who assist the weak fear their rewards will be meager.

---

**A small state with few people:**
Though they have war chariots and weapons of a hundred kinds,
they do not use them.
Though they have ships and carriages,
they have no place to ride in them.
Though they have armor and arms,
they have no place to display them.
Let the people return to using knotted cords for record-keeping,
enjoy simple food, wear beautiful clothes,
live in comfortable homes,
and delight in their customs.
Though neighboring states are close enough to hear the cocks crow and dogs bark,
the people grow old and die without ever visiting each other.

**【Han Book Annotation:** *The emperor’s decree orders officials’ residences not to place household goods (什器). Yán Shīgū’s note explains that five people form a *wǔ* (伍), ten a *shí* (什), and they share tools, hence "什物" refers to household goods. "Not using them" means avoiding unnecessary travel and labor. Though they have ships and carriages, they do not ride in them—avoiding transport and commerce. Though they have armor and arms, they do not display them—avoiding military conflict. "The people grow old and die without ever visiting each other" implies self-sufficiency, so that there is no need for mutual aid or borrowing. When the **Dao** is complete, there are no affairs to attend to. When resources are abundant, there is no need for mutual aid or borrowing.* **】**

**【Sū’s Annotation:】**
Lǎozǐ was born in the declining years of the Western Zhōu,
when cultural refinement was at its peak and corruption was imminent.
He wrote this to advocate **non-action (无为)** as a means of salvation.
At the end of the text, he expresses his ideal:
*"If only one could attain a small state with few people!"*
This was his earnest hope—
though unattainable, it serves as a model to emulate.
When the people each find contentment in their allotted roles,
even those with modest talents do not seek worldly use,
and those who can manage a hundred *shí* or *bǎi* (什伯) people
are capable of leading a hundred or a thousand households.
**【什伯 (shí-bǎi):** *a hundred or a thousand, emphasizing modest scale.* **】**」

When governance is simple and the people are unspoiled,
even knotted cords suffice for record-keeping.
Content within, they covet nothing beyond—
thus they deem their possessions as beauty,
their abodes as joy, and seek no more.
When the people and their needs are abundant yet unclaimed,
each finds sufficiency in the other—
this is why they lack want.

**Lü’s Annotation:**
From the Three Dynasties onward, until the decline of the Zhou,
refinement and ornamentation grew extreme.
The people lost their natural way of life,
so Laozi’s words seek to restore simplicity,
to return to the governance of ancient times.
In small states with few people,
there are implements for a hundred or a thousand,
yet they remain unused.
The people hold death dear and do not wander far—
though chariots and arms exist, they have no use for them.
This is the restoration of simplicity,
a return to the **Dao of the most ancient age**.

Zhuang Zhou spoke of the age of **ultimate virtue** thus:
*"In the time of Róngchéng, Dàtíng, Bóhuáng, Zhōngyāng,
Lùlù, Líxù, Xuānyuán, Hèxiū, Zūnlú, Zhùróng, Fúxī,
and Shénnóng—
when the people used knotted cords for record-keeping,
they found their food sweet, their garments fine,
their customs joyful, their dwellings secure,
and neighboring lands so near that roosters and dogs’ barks could be heard.
The people lived to old age without traveling far—
such was the state of affairs.
This was not merely Laozi’s vision alone;
ancient times truly knew this **Dao** as well.
Yet the *Shī* and *Shū* speak only of Yao, Shun, and the Three Dynasties,
while Laozi sought to restore the governance of the most ancient age—
why so?
For the **Dao** and the world have long obstructed each other.
Only the **Great Dao** can return people to their natural way.
To speak of the **Dao** without reference to its age
is insufficient to grasp the **Dao’s** proven worth—
this is why one must return to the governance of the most ancient age.

Yet the world has long since departed from the most ancient age.

Can the **Dao** thus be fully restored?
*"No,"* it replies. *"Then what is its purpose?"*
*"Ritual reaches its fullness in the combined rites of the Three Kings,
music in the complete traditions of the Six Dynasties—
their forms are perfected.
Yet ritual does not place the *shāngshuǐ* [sacrificial water] and great broths
above the *lǐjiǔ* [sacrificial wine] and harmonized soups,
nor does music layer the *huìguǎn* [reed pipes]’ clear tones
over the *zhūfú* [red silk] and sparse *shūyuè* [slow dances].
This is to make people grasp the essential intent of ritual and music—
what they must abandon is as clear as what they seek to restore.
In such times,
Confucius sought to revive the lost traditions of Wen and Wu, and the Duke of Zhou,
while Laozi’s teachings on the **Dao** and the world shared this same intent.

It is like the moon waxing while fire begins to wane,
and the *jīn* [metal] *qi* already stirring between them—
this is how the Five Phases succeed one another without interruption.
Thus, though ancient governance had myriad implements,
they were not employed;
though chariots and armor existed,
they were not used in battle.
Only when great undertakings required great assemblies
was it necessary.
Hearing of the people using knotted cords for record-keeping,
neighboring lands within earshot of roosters and dogs,
living to old age without travel—
this was the simplicity before elaborate rites, exhausting decrees,
and the relentless scrutiny of officials.
Footprints pressed the fields, and cart-ruts marked the roads,
while distant lands were reached only when unavoidable—
what was unavoidable was always here,
yet what was sought to restore was always there.

Would they then weary themselves with excessive governance,
deepening the people’s faults?
Yet if the world cannot achieve lasting peace and ease,
then even in death, they speak no more—
as if they had discarded the *shāngshuǐ* and abandoned the *shūyuè*,
who would know that ritual’s frugality and music’s restraint
were the very essence of returning to origins and beginning anew?

The words of the sages—
are they not profound in their intent?"*

The Way of the small state with few people—
then the people are pure and simple.
Indeed, the larger the state, the more the people,
and the more their interests clash and cunning deceits arise.
The customs of those in cities and villages differ greatly—
this is the proof of it.

In a world without the Dao/Way,
the people are poor, the land barren,
and desires for profit prevail.
The love of life grows weak while its roots decay,
and greed for gain is ever-present.
They admire distant lands yet fear the law,
treading perilous paths as if on level ground,
while cart-ruts and footprints crisscross everywhere—
the four quarters are traversed.
If they delight in life and follow their nature,
they will fear death and cling to their land;
if they seek no gain, they will not wander far—
this is the very governance of fulfilling their nature,
and the people fulfill their own nature as well.

I have pondered the *Lunyu* and the *Mengzi*—
both conclude with praise of Yao, Shun, Yu, and Tang,
the deeds of the sages.
If one takes this book and applies its governance,
its effects may be as such.

Laozi was a great sage,
yet the changes he faced aligned with the time
when returning to origins and fulfilling nature was paramount.
Thus, he alone illuminated the intent of *Dao* and *virtue/power*,
to gather and unify the scattered,
to restore simplicity and sincerity.
If one takes his book and applies its governance,
the people will be transformed and customs refined—
this is the effect of this chapter.
Hence, the meaning of the *Classic* is fulfilled in it.

The governance of ten as a *shí* and a hundred as a *bǎi*—
these are heavy instruments, great tools shared by the multitude.
If unused, they are not cultivated—
this is the absence of greed for heavy instruments without purpose.
To hold death in reverence is to treat death as weighty,
while cherishing life in its duties—
to not wander far is to be rooted here, to die here,
and not seek elsewhere.
Laozi sought to reverse the decline of Zhou,
restoring the simplicity of ancient times.
A great state means many people, difficult to govern;
a small state with few people is easier to rule,
so that its people do not covet the outside
but find sufficiency within.
Thus, they do not stray.

Boats, chariots, armor, and weapons—
no single person can wield them alone;
these are the *shí* and *bǎi* instruments.
If there is nothing to ride upon, nothing to array,
then they are unused.
If there is nowhere to go, then they serve no purpose.
If there is no contention over boats and carriages,
then they are of no use.
If the people are pure and affairs are simple,
the governance of ancient knotted cords may be restored—
even if writing and contracts exist, they may be dispensed with.
Not only are the *shí* and *bǎi* instruments unused,
but the food they eat is sweet,
the clothes they wear are beautiful,
and they are naturally content,
cherishing life—
this is the meaning of holding death in reverence.
They take this place as their home and find comfort in it,
this land’s customs as their joy—
who would lightly abandon it to wander far?
The people cling to their soil;
even neighboring states are visible to the eye,
their roosters and dogs audible to the ear—
yet they do not visit one another,
let alone wander far.

Truthful words are not beautiful;
beautiful words are not truthful.
Good words are not persuasive;
persuasive words are not good.
The wise are not erudite;
the erudite are not wise.
The sage does not accumulate—
the more he gives to others, the more he possesses;
the more he bestows, the more he has.
The Way of Heaven benefits without harming;
the sage’s Way acts without contending.

---
**Su’s Commentary:**
Truth is substance alone—
beauty is mere appearance.
Goodness as the foundation does not seek eloquence;
eloquence as the foundation is not necessarily good.
One principle unifies all—
the erudite accumulate knowledge daily but may not grasp the Dao.
The sage holds to unity alone;
what need has he of accumulation?
Only because he lacks accumulation
does he become one with all things—
thus, he is supremely rich.
If he were to accumulate,
his resources would be finite,
and how could he ever possess more?
Laozi’s words:
Within, he observes the heart;
without, he observes things;
above, he observes Heaven;
below, he observes Earth—
all in harmony.
Yet the vulgar hear and scoff,
the world deems him foolish—
this is not beauty.
His words are closest yet point farthest—
this is goodness,
yet he does not speak for pleasure—
this is not eloquence.
His wisdom reaches the realm of non-wisdom,
yet remains unbroken—
this is knowledge,
yet does not stray from the heart—
this is not erudition.
But scholars seek beauty, eloquence, and erudition—
they stray from the Dao.
Laozi’s Way rejects accumulation;
even the sage abandons it.
Thus, in utter emptiness,
he fulfills all demands—
the more he possesses, the more he gives.
Yet scholars, unable to carve their hearts,
cannot attain this.

All things have benefits and harms;
where there is harm, there is injury.
Only the Way of Heaven benefits without harming—
where there is no benefit, there is no harm.
All things that act do so with self-interest;
self-interest breeds contention.
The sage’s Way acts yet does not act—
without action, there is no self;
without self, there is no contention.
This is the Way of Heaven.

---
**Lü’s Commentary:**
The Dao is a thing unseen when gazed upon,
unheard when listened to,
untouched when grasped—
it can be silently understood but not sought through reasoning.
Thus, truthful words are true to this alone—
what need for beauty?
Good words are good to this alone—
what need for eloquence?
Persuasive words are persuasive to this alone—
what need for cleverness?
Erudite words are erudite to this alone—
what need for breadth?
From this, it is clear:
beauty is not truth,
eloquence is not goodness,
erudition is not wisdom.
The Dao was never a thing in the first place.
The sage merges with the Dao—
what accumulation could he have?
Only because he lacks accumulation
does he become one with all things—
thus, he is supremely rich.
If he were to accumulate,
his resources would be exhausted,
and how could he ever possess more?
Laozi’s words:
Within, he observes the heart;
without, he observes things;
above, he observes Heaven;
below, he observes Earth—
all in harmony.
Yet the vulgar hear and scoff,
the world deems him foolish—
this is not beauty.
His words are closest yet point farthest—
this is goodness,
yet he does not speak for pleasure—
this is not eloquence.
His wisdom reaches the realm of non-wisdom,
yet remains unbroken—
this is knowledge,
yet does not stray from the heart—
this is not erudition.
But scholars seek beauty, eloquence, and erudition—
they stray from the Dao.
Laozi’s Way rejects accumulation;
even the sage abandons it.
Thus, in utter emptiness,
he fulfills all demands—
the more he possesses, the more he gives.
Yet scholars, unable to carve their hearts,
cannot attain this.

All things have benefits and harms;
where there is benefit, there is harm.
Only the Way of Heaven benefits without harming—
where there is no benefit, there is no harm.
All things that act do so with self-interest;
self-interest breeds contention.
The sage’s Way acts yet does not act—
without action, there is no self;
without self, there is no contention.
This is the Way of Heaven.

---
**Bǐ Shèng’s Note:**
Some say that Laozi’s book
allows one to receive and savor it—
this is near to beauty.
It exhausts the principles of all things without omission—
this is near to goodness.

The discerning observer marks all things’ transformations without omission—
this is near to mastery.

Yet those who are vast yet lack sincerity,
who are clever yet not virtuous,
who are learned yet not profound,
who are erudite yet not wise—
what use are they?

For the Five Thousand Words speak only of the Way of non-accumulation.
Non-accumulation means the heart dwells nowhere.

To accumulate yet not accumulate—
this is to speak yet not speak.
To speak yet not speak is not to withhold from others,
yet never to claim possession for oneself.
To never diminish one’s abundance—
how could this be faulted for being argumentative or overreaching?

If one does not follow the Way of non-accumulation
but clings to rigid opinions and contends with the world,
then many words will lead to exhaustion—
this is not the Heavenly Way.

The Heavenly Way benefits without harming;
the sage’s Way acts without contention.
Scholars who carve their hearts here
have already grasped half of Laozi’s teaching.

Thus it is said:
To teach without teaching—
why seal the mouth at *Pīyé*?
To guard speech is already to clarify the heart.
To those reading beneath the pillars—
strive diligently!

The *Records of the Historian*’s *Biography of Laozi* states:

Laozi was a man of **Qū County**, **Lì Xiàng**, in **Kǔ Prefecture**—his clan name was **Lǐ**, given name **Ēr**, with the courtesy name **Bóyáng**, posthumously titled **Dān**. He served as the **Keeper of the Royal Archives** in **Zhōu**. When **Kǒngzǐ** traveled to **Zhōu** to inquire about rites, Laozi said:

*“What you speak of—both the man and his bones—are long since decayed. Only his words remain. Moreover, a gentleman who finds his time may ride in a carriage; if he does not, he walks with a staff. I have heard that a skilled merchant conceals his goods as if empty, while a gentleman of virtue appears humble yet is profound. Abandon your arrogance and excessive desires, your lustful expressions and licentious thoughts—these bring no benefit to your body. This is all I have to tell you.”*

Kǒngzǐ departed and told his disciples:
*“I know how birds fly and fish swim, how beasts run. Those that run can be trapped, those that swim can be snared, those that fly can be caught in nets. But as for the dragon—I cannot fathom how it rides the winds and clouds to ascend to heaven. Today, I have seen Laozi—he is like the dragon!”*

Laozi cultivated **virtue and the Way**, making **self-effacement and namelessness** his pursuit. After long residence in **Zhōu**, seeing its decline, he departed. At the **Guān Pass**, the **Prime Minister of Guān**, **Xǐ**, said:
*“Master, you are about to withdraw. Please write a book for me.”*
Thus Laozi composed the *Book of the Way and Virtue* in two sections, totaling over five thousand words, before departing. No one knows his ultimate fate. Some say **Lǎolǎizǐ** was also from **Chǔ**, having written fifteen treatises on **Daoist practice**, contemporary with **Kǒngzǐ**. It is said Laozi lived over a hundred and sixty years—or even over two hundred—due to his cultivation of the **Way** and longevity.

After **Kǒngzǐ’s** death, one hundred and twenty-nine years later, the **Grand Historian of Zhōu**, **Dàn**, reported to **Qín Xiàn Gōng**:
*“When **Qín** first separated from **Zhōu**, it was a union of five hundred years. After seven decades, a ruler will arise to dominate.”*
Some say **Dàn** was Laozi; others deny it. The world knows not the truth.

Laozi was a **hidden sage**. His son, **Zōng**, served as a general for **Wèi** and was ennobled at **Duàn Gàn**. **Zōng’s** descendant, **Zhù**, had a son, **Jǐa**, who served under **Hàn Xiào Wén Dì**. **Jǐa’s** son, **Jiě**, became the **Tutor of Jiāoxī Wáng Àn**, and his family settled in **Qí**. Those who studied Laozi disparaged **Confucianism**, while Confucians likewise disparaged Laozi. Their **Dao** differed, and they did not seek mutual understanding—was this not so?

**Lǐ Ēr**—**self-so without action, pure and self-regulating**.

The **Stele of Laozi’s Temple** by **Xuē Dàohéng** of the **Sui** reads:

From the **Great Ultimate’s** transformation, the **Primordial Void** emerged. At the **Opening of the Myriad Things**, the **Celestial Pivot** was suspended, and the sun and moon were arrayed. The **Earthly Axis** was established, bearing mountains and rivers. The **breath of yin and yang**, in its **expansion and contraction**, gave rise to all things—**spirits above, life below**. The **climates and seasons** were thus ordained, nurturing the **ten thousand beings**, granting **people and rulers** their place.

The **Ancient Sovereign** observed the **ancient ways**: in summer, he dwelled in high halls; in winter, in deep caves. He quieted his mind, refined his wisdom, and dwelled in simplicity. His **great rites** harmonized with **Heaven and Earth**; his **music** resonated with the **cosmic order**. How could he be constrained by **sacrificial vessels** or **musical instruments**?

When the **Way declined**, **virtue waned**; when **virtue declined**, **benevolence faltered**. Rulers and peoples differed in their **steps and customs**, their **manners pure or corrupt**. Thus, **Confucians and Mohists** vied for fame, **Legalists** raced in their schemes, and though the **Three Hundred Rites** could not restrain human nature, nor the **Three Thousand Penal Codes** suppress their deceit—
how then could one **clarify the currents** without **purifying the source**, or **straighten the branches** without **rectifying the root**? Only the **Great Dao** suffices as the **source and root**.

Laozi, sensing the **celestial mandate**, was born of **mysterious origin**, his lineage traced to a **tree**—his surname unclear. His **birth was marked by the **Lü harmonics**, his **pregnancy held divine essence**. At over seventy years, he was born with **white hair**, thus taking the name **Laozi**. His form was strange:
—**Three gates**, **double pillars**, **ears like a dragon’s**,
—**eyes like a **qi**-creature’s**, **limbs like a **five-clawed beast’s**,
—**hands and feet of ten forms**, **unlike ordinary men**.
From **Fúxī** to the **Zhōu Dynasty**, through successive ages, his **substance remained unchanged**, his name recorded in the **Book of Changes**. In the time of **Wén Wáng** and **Wǔ Wáng**, he held the office of **Keeper of the Archives** and **Pillar Historian**, often altering his appearance without revealing his true form. When **Kǒngzǐ** glimpsed him, he sighed:
*“The **Dragon Virtue** is hard to know!”*
When **Guān Yīn Xǐ** gazed upon him from afar, he recognized the **True Person** about to withdraw, and thus Laozi expounded his **profound mysteries**, writing two treatises. He returned to his **natural Way**, using **non-action** as his method. His words were **concise yet profound**, his **defenses deep and far-reaching**. Even the **Flying Dragon’s** transformations could not compare to his **refinement**; the **Lynx’s** pen could not match his **luminous insight**. Whether **bright or hidden**, his **application**—
when used for **self-cultivation**, brought **clarity of spirit and tranquility of mind**;
when applied to **governance**, restored **simplicity and purity**.

Later, he **transcended form**, his **traces vanished**, his **outer shell shed**. In **recluses’ robes**, he rode a **crane**, coming and going between the **Purple Palace** and **Jīn Jiāng**, partaking of **jade nectar and immortal wine**. His **luster rivaled the sun and moon**, his **beginning and end** one with **Heaven and Earth**. Those who followed his **teachings** cast off **worldly dust**; those who entered his **gates** soared on **clouds and mists**. The **Great Chūn** withered yet revived—how could the **mayfly** comprehend it? The **boundless depths**—how could **Yún Yí** fathom them?

**Grand indeed!** Yet without **virtue**, how could he be thus extolled?

**Zhuāngzǐ** said:
*“When **Lǎodān** died, **Qín Yì** wept for him, thrice wailing before departing—this was called the **Form of the Heavenly Retreat**. Though it was but a **mockery of officials**, it was also the **subtle transformation of a cicada’s molt**.”

The **Emperor’s** sacred birth—
his **august virtue** shone like the **red comet** at **Chōnglíng**, the **purple gas** of **Mángshān**. His **jewel-like horns** and **moon-angled brow** surpassed all **ancient kings**. His **mirror-like clarity** and **vessel-like purity** endured for **thousands of years**. The **Zhōu Way** declined in its **late age**, its **nine tripods** submerged, its **waters mingling with the sea**. Two suns rose, and **Lòchūan** battled in the **Lò River**. The **Heavenly Pivot** tilted; the **Earthly Axis** wavered. **Snakes devoured copper**, **whales swallowed jade**—in the lands of **Tónglíng** and **Yùlěi**, **wolves howled**, **owls perched**, **delusion spread**, and **poisonous calumny** festered. **Divine schemes** arose within, **military expeditions** without. **Opposing armies clashed**; **weapons clashed**; **Jiāng Yóu** was slain, **Chīyóu** executed. The **Heavenly Pivot** was restored, the **Earthly Axis** righted—**Gōnggōng** was cut down, **Yóuzhōu** destroyed. The **winds and rains** were regulated; **creation and transformation** were governed. The **myriad creatures** rejoiced, their **souls and spirits** blessed. The **nine regions** sang in praise, and the **five directions** responded in harmony.

The **Heavenly Son** ascended the **Tái Platform** to worship **Heaven**, seated in the **Míng Hall** to receive the **clan leaders**. In ancient times, **Xuānyuán** and **Zhuānxū** established their **kingdoms differently**; **Dàhào** and **Shàohào** marked their **boundaries distinct**. **Shùn** altered **Yáo’s** capital; **Yù** moved the **Yú domain**. Selecting the **former sages**, he **preserved their institutions**, cherishing the **ten thousand families**, loving the **myriad peoples**, and **initiating the Imperial Residence**. The **Celestial Treasury’s** layout followed the **Purple Extremity**, adhering to the **Sovereign’s Plan**. The **upper eaves** were low, the **foundations** firm—**left and right** aligned with **equality and rectitude**. The **morning and evening halls** were unified; the **Yuānluán Tower** rose without **crane-like scaffolding**. **Laborious governance** and **benevolent care** nurtured the people. In **sleep and waking**, he pondered their **suffering**; his **heart ached like thorny forests**, his **stones wept**. He **bestowed grace**, **pardoned crimes**, and **simplified rituals**. His **garments bore no shame**; his **deeds knew no guilt**. **Heaven’s** **unenterable star** did not descend; **Earth’s** **city-destroying ox** did not rise. The **Grand Hall** was expanded, the **inner precincts** examined. **Classics were compiled**; the **Shíqú Jade Water** was unearthed. **Confucian learning** was revived; the **Five Rites**, on the verge of collapse, were restored. The **Six Musics**, already lost, were rectified. The **songs of drowned ambition** and **celebrations of merit** were composed. The **Double Suns** shone brightly; the **eternal light** endured. The **foundations were firm**; the **lineage was exalted**. The **vessels of state** were secure as **mountains of jade**. **Talented ministers** flourished; **treasured officials** were **cultivated in virtue and martial skill**, serving as the **kingdom’s bulwark** and the **transcendent’s brushstroke**.

The **Xióngnú** and **Dí** had long been a **menace**, their **claws and fangs** sharp.

The **unexcelled stratagems** were employed to govern it,
Yet the **lowest tactics** were the most difficult to subdue.
Self-generated fortune shone with **virtue**,
Arms were restrained, **merit** was felt, **benevolence** was embraced.
**Vassal states** acknowledged their **tribute obligations**,
**Kneeling in submission**, bowing in homage—
**Night郎** and **Mò** were swept away,
**Sāngyú** and **Lǐ** lands all came under **Dharma’s influence**.
The **Fúyú** and **Mǐnyuè**, **Gōuwú** alike,
Never failed in their **tribute duties**—
**My** own **Xióngnú** and **Dí** were tamed,
**Barbarian courts** stood without interruption, near and far.
**Sacrificial rites** were refined,
**The Great Harmony** was cultivated—
Since the **Three Dynasties** and the **Six Warlords**,
Who vied in **strife and conquest**:
**Qín** held the **intercalary throne**,
**Hàn** mingled **hegemony** with **wayward paths**,
**Wéi** brought **tyranny** deep,
**Jìn** restored **civilization**—
Thus, **barbarians and Chinese** became indistinct,
**Imperial traces** and **royal winds** faded into silence.
A thousand years passed in **celestial mandate’s** stillness,
**Sacred virtue** stood firm, **prosperity** and **auspice** endured.
**Divine Way** transformed the **people’s** perception,
Reestablishing the **Three Five** order.

**Yīng** and **Míng** were harmonized in **auspicious resonance**,
**Celestial omens** shone forth in **divine brilliance**—
Like the **three-legged crow** taking flight,
Or the **solar essence** manifesting in form.
**Spring waters** flowed as **sacred wine** from the **capital’s** heart,
**Autumn dews** condensed sweet, spreading through **bamboo and reeds**.
**Stellar light** like the moon,
**Cloud-qi** rising as **flying mist**—
The **three harvests** answered the **bronze bell’s** call,
The **five stars** followed the **pearl pouch’s** measure.

**Harmony** could be **proclaimed**,
**Processions** could move—
**Southern sacrifices** to **Fire**, **Fire’s** rites to **God**,
Yet still the **rituals** feared disorder,
**Ancestral altars** were left unrefined.
**Eternal benevolence** was spoken of,
**Longevity** was sought in the **sacred precincts**—
**Deer and antelope** shifted in reverence,
**The Azure Pavilion** stood in **serene majesty**.
**Pillars and beams** were carved with **divine precision**,
**Roofs and eaves** painted in **celestial splendor**,
**Square wells and circular ponds** aligned in **geometric grace**,
**Statues and halls** rose in **mutual support**.
