**The sovereign’s likeness** was **revered** in **solemn stillness**,
**Divine chambers** remained **unoccupied**, yet **auspicious**—
**Dripping blessings** fell as **echoing response**,
**Pure-hearted, clean-minded** scholars preserved **unity’s** companions.
From **four directions**, **thousands gathered** in **respectful homage**,
**Sacrificial rites** were solemnly offered,
**Ren’s** aid was **displayed** to the **king**,
**Ming’s** fortune was **bestowed** upon the **people**.

This is the **Great Dao**—
Great in **name**, great in **stratagem**,
Yet **difficult to guard** against its **transcendence**.

If **eloquent words** were abruptly silenced,
**Hidden springs** left unopened—
**Forms and instruments** unexhibited,
**Divine marvels** left unmanifest—
**Heroic deeds** would not ascend to **Danqing’s** brush,
**Bright reputations** would not be **engraved** on **gold and stone**,
And yet—what **harmony** could this be?

In the **year of Dūncháng**,
**Lǜzhōng** was **purified**,
**Great Sui** ruled the **land** for **six years**—

The decree was issued to the subject to erect a stele and compose an ode in praise thereof:

*"Ah, vast and ancient beyond reckoning!*
The Emperor’s forebears, four generations past,
Sustained the earth’s eight pillars, upheld the heavens’ clusters,
Gathered the myriad streams, shaped the great valleys and rivers.
The **Dao’s** numinous workings, the **Way’s** spontaneous virtue—
The Five Agents responded in kind, the Three **Precepts** succeeded in turn,
Establishing **guardians** to tend the people, wielding their **symbols** in accord.
The **Emperor’s** traces surpass the **August** and **Sovereign**—
His **virtue** transcends the distant, yet pure customs fade,
The times grow askew, the **Dao** of simplicity lost,
The people’s ways diverge—**harmony** and **virtue** now rare.
**Cunning** and **craft** are prized, **law** and **net** are set,
The fish are startled, the birds take flight—
**Weapons** multiply, the **Way** declines.

Then came the **True Person**, descending from the stars,
His **virtue** like the **Dragon’s**—blessed in person,
His **qi** as the **Crane’s**—bowed in humility.
He dispelled strife, blunted sharpness,
Rejected the thin, embraced the substantial,
His **virtue** like the **horns of the sun and moon**—
Enduring as heaven and earth.

Now, though this be but one of the Five **Sacred Peaks**,
A mere corner of the Nine **Provinces**—
Yet he would soar aloft, transcend the mundane,
And ride the **Blue Ox** into the distance.
**Purple vapors** rise, the **Way’s** gate opens wide,
Its **transformations** flow unseen—
Yet rooted in the old homeland, the **Luo River’s** legacy,
Ancient traces from past to present,
As time shifts and the world changes—
The **spirit-temples** decay, the **shrine-altars** lie empty,
The Nine **Wells** sprout **paulownia**, the twin steles crumble to stone.

Now the **August One** receives the **Mandate**—
**Divine** and **Sage** alike!
The earth resounds, the **celestial bell** tolls,
The **heavenly mirror** hangs suspended—
The cosmos clears, the **demonic mists** disperse,
The **luminous essence** aligns in reverence,
The **sacred** and **profound** take their rightful place.
The **flowing sands** coil, the **phoenix’s** nest is sealed,
The **strange trees** return, the **clusters** converge—
Ten thousand lands find their home,
Their hearts united in **harmony**.
The **Royal Master of Ceremonies** receives the **tribute**,
The **King’s** **precepts** are inscribed, the **treasures** laid out.
The **fragrant winds** waft through the **chamber**, the **music** resounds—
The **cauldron** is balanced, the **house** prospers,
**Rites** and **deference** endure eternally.

Even beneath the **pillars**, I am ashamed to compare myself to the **August Ancestors**.
Thus, a **pure shrine** is erected, its **diagrams** drawn,
Its **spirit-forms** mapped—
Across the **level fields** and **lofty mounds**,
The **meadows** stretch far, the **plum and cassia** beams rise,
The **curved eaves** and **clustered pillars** stand,
As **mist and cloud** drift, **wind and rain** weep—
**Celestial officials** take their stations,
**Feathered guests** arrive in procession.
Abundant blessings descend, the **bright and numinous** manifest—
Their **logic** is unfathomable, yet their **symbols** endure,
Their **great sound** is rare, their **high resonance** echoes far.
Near and distant alike offer praise,
To the **profound** and **luminous**—
That all may revere and uplift,
Their names **engraved on gold and stone**,
To be spread through heaven and earth.

With reverence, I, **Respectful Chú**, the descendant of **Companion Zhòng**,
**Companion Zhòng** having said to **Rú’s lord**:
*"Confucius, the **Sage’s** heir,
One who would attain the **Way**—
I have heard that **Lǎo Dān** was learned in antiquity,
Mastered the present, fathomed the **origins of rites and music**,
Clarified the **return of virtue and the Dao**—
He is my **Teacher**."*

**Respectful Chú** spoke to **Rú’s lord**:
*"Kǒng Qiū, the **Sage’s** descendant,
One who would attain the **Way**—"*

「To fulfill the command of my predecessor, I must necessarily follow his teachings. Now, Confucius is about to journey to the Zhou, to observe the institutions of the ancient kings and examine the ultimate refinements of rites and music—this is a grand undertaking. My lord, why not grant him a carriage and attendants to aid his way? I beg to accompany him.»

**Rú’s lord** granted him a carriage with one pair of horses and two attendants, and **Respectful Chú** went with him to the Zhou. There, they inquired about **rites** from **Lǎo Dān** and about **music** from **Cháng Yǐng**. They surveyed the **sacrificial grounds** and **ancestral temples**, examined the **Hall of Clear Clarity** and its **four gates**, and observed the **walls of the hall**—where were the **symbols** of **Yáo**, **Shùn**, **Jié**, and **Zhòu**—each bearing marks of **good and evil**, **rise and ruin**, as warnings.

Moreover, there was the **image** of **Zhōu Gōng** assisting **Chéng Wáng**, holding him and bearing the **axe-hilt banner**, facing south to receive the **tribute of the feudal lords**. **Respectful Chú** sighed, *"Now I understand **Zhōu Gōng’s** sagehood and the reason **Zhōu** came to rule!"*

As they departed the Zhou, **Lǎo Dān** sent them off, saying, *"The wealthy give gifts with wealth, the virtuous give gifts with words. I claim the name of the virtuous and give you words: Those who live in this age, though clever and profound, are near to death. They delight in criticizing others’ faults. Those who are broad in debate and vast in knowledge risk endangering themselves by exposing others’ flaws. As ministers, they cannot bear to have their own opinions; as sons, they cannot bear to have their own desires. When I return from the Zhou, the **Way** will grow ever more revered. The distant disciples who come to me number three thousand!"*

Confucius sighed, *"Since **Respectful Chú** rode in my carriage, my **Way** has gained momentum. Otherwise, my **Way** would have been lost!"*

The *Records of Rites* that are cited today—what I heard from **Lǎo Dān**—are all from Confucius’ inquiries with **Lǎo Dān** about the essentials of **rites**.

**Wáng Zǐ Nián**, in his *Lost Records*, wrote that **Lǎo Jūn** dwelled on **Jǐng Shì** Mountain, cutting himself off from the world. Only five old men visited him: sometimes riding on **crying cranes**, sometimes clad in **feathered robes**. Together, they discussed the **numbers of heaven and earth**. The **scriptures** he composed amounted to over **ten thousand words**. In the **Fú Tí** Kingdom, two men offered **books of good deeds**—now old, now young, their forms shifting. When unseen, their shadows appeared; when their voices were heard, their forms vanished. At times, they emerged from a **golden vessel** containing a black substance like **primeval lacquer**, which they sprinkled on trees and stones, transforming them into **inscribed characters** and **divine diagrams**, recording the **beginning of creation and human relations**.

All the **scriptures** of **Lǎo Jūn** were written on **jade tablets**, bound with **gold cords**, and stored in **jade cases** and **golden vessels**. When the black substance was exhausted, the two men wished to **carve their hearts and drain their blood** to replace it. This is what was spoken of in the **Lǎo** and **Shào** Mountains of **Luó Zhōu**—the **Nine Transformations** and **Longevity** **scriptures**, numbering over **a hundred thousand** **volumes**, hidden in mountain caves and stone chambers, unrevealed until now. About **six thousand volumes** have emerged.

**Guān Yǐn Zǐ**, **Yǐn Xǐ**, served under **Zhāo Wáng** of the Zhou as a **great official**, skilled in **celestial omens** and **divination**. He foresaw the **True Qi** of the east and south, appearing like **dragon and snake**, and the **fusion of wind and moon**, the **threefold unfolding of heaven’s principles**. When he journeyed westward, he knew there was a **sage** who would **cross the Pass**. He begged to be allowed to leave his post as **Kuan Pass Keeper** and **Zhāo Wáng** granted it. In the **fifth month of the year Gǔ Chǒu**, on a **day Rén Wǔ**, a **purple vapor** rose in the sky. An **old man** drove a **green ox**, pulling a **white carriage**, with disciples following behind. **Xǐ** welcomed him and bowed in reverence. The **wind of respect** was **Lǎo Zǐ**.

**Xǐ** said, *"The **Great Way** is about to vanish. Forcefully, he has written a book for me."* He lodged at the **Southern Pavilion** of **Zhōng Nán** Mountain and studied under him. The next year, in **Jiā Yín**, he received the **Five Thousand Words** of **Virtue and the Dao**, encompassing **heaven and earth**, **creation and transformation**, **sovereigns and subjects**, **people and things**—none were left out. He exalted the **Virtue and the Dao**, placing **rites and rightness** below them, thus honoring the **Emperor of Heaven** and placing lesser **feudal lords** beneath him. In essence, it was about **clarifying the Way of Heaven**, **clarifying the Way of humanity**, **rectifying the heart of the ruler**, and **rectifying the hearts of the people**. He said, *"To govern the state with **rectitude**, to employ **strategy** in warfare."* He also spoke of the **"Left General"** and **"Right General"**—in all, several chapters with **praise and blame** of the **Five Lords**. Scholars doubt whether **Zhāo Wáng** had yet achieved lordship or if this was during the reign of **Jìng Wáng**. But the decline of **Xià** and **Shāng** had already begun, and **Kūn Wú** and **Dà Píng** had even proclaimed themselves **lords**. **Zhāo Wáng’s** refusal to return to the **Jiāng** River was no longer awaiting the rise of **Qí**, **Chǔ**, **Xīn**, and **Jìn**. The **lords’** influence had already appeared.

This is how **Lǎo Zǐ** and the **sage** composed the **scriptures** on **Virtue and the Dao**, integrating their **efforts and powers**.

According to the *Records*, the **Three Stars** of **Xiàng** (the **North Star**) and **Hé** (the **Milky Way**) govern the **son of heaven** and the **three armies**. The **central star** is the **great general**; the **left star** is the **left general**; the **right star** is the **right general**. The **Summer Office** and the **upper great marshal** are also mentioned in the *Institutes of Zhou* and the *Inner Teachings of King Mù*. It is clear that the transmission of the **scriptures** occurred during **Zhāo Wáng’s** time when he ascended to **Zhōng Nán** and built the **grass pavilion**.

**Guān Yǐn Zǐ** also wrote **nine chapters**, titled *Guān Yǐn Zǐ*. His disciple, **Jìn Gōng Sūn Xīn**, with the courtesy name **Jì Rán**, studied under **Lǎo Zǐ**. In the **second year of Jìng Wáng** (on a day **Rén Wǔ**), he traveled south to **Chǔ**, where **Píng Wáng** honored and invited him, seeking his teachings. Later, he went to **Yuè**, where **Fàn Lì** became his disciple. **Jì Rán** taught him, and **Lì** wrote **twelve chapters**, titled *Wén Zǐ*. Among them was **Píng Wáng’s** inquiry, recorded in **sentences and phrases**. When **Lì** ascended the throne as a **duke**, he appointed **Jì Rán** as a **great official**. Later, when **Yuè** attacked **Wú**, **Lì** advised, *"War is a cruel instrument; battle goes against **virtue**. Secret plots go against **virtue**. Those who delight in cruel instruments and secret plots—**Heaven’s Highest** forbids them. This will not succeed."* He quoted the words of *Wén Zǐ*.

**Lì** did not listen and was defeated at **Fú Jiāo**, imprisoned in the **Stone Chamber**. He bribed **Tài Shǐ Pí** and returned home. He tasted **gall** to steel his resolve and plotted revenge against **Wú**. Later, he consulted **Jì Rán**, who said, *"**Gōu Jiàn** has a long neck and raven’s beak. He can share calamity but not prosperity."* **Lì** followed his strategy, fleeing to the **Five Lakes**. **Jì Rán** also pretended madness and withdrew to **Fēng Yú**, where he once climbed a mountain to plan his escape. Now, the **Jiàn Cǎo** Mountain in **Wú Xìng** is where he did so.

**Liè Yù**, dwelling in **Jìng** for **forty years**, was unknown to the people. In the **fourth year of Ān Wáng**, he wrote **eight chapters**, expounding **Lǎo Zǐ’s Way**. **Zhuāng Zhōu**, with the courtesy name **Nán Huá Zǐ**, served in the **thirtieth year of Xiǎn Wáng** of **Chǔ**, but declined the appointment as **prime minister** and hid at the **Háo** River’s **Lacquer Garden**. He wrote **fifty-three chapters**, titled *Zhuāng Zǐ*. Now, **thirty-three chapters** remain.

As for **Gēng Sāng**, **Chǔ Nán Hóng**, **Zhuī**, **Zhuì**, **Bó Jǔ**, **Shì Chéng**, **Qǐ Yǐn**, and **Wén Zǐ’s** disciples, all were contemporaries who studied under **Lǎo Zǐ** and transmitted his teachings, each leaving behind their own writings recorded in the **classics**. Yet **Lǎo Zǐ** had already traveled extensively through **Shāng** and **Zhōu** for a long time. Those who personally saw him were like **Yóu Lóng**—as for **Confucius**, he alone could be said to have done so.

Since **Sī Mǎ Qiān**, **Lǎo** and **Hàn** have been conflated in their transmission, and **Lǎo Zǐ’s** disciples have been lost to history. Thus, the **shared teachings** of **Confucius** and **Lǎo Zǐ** have not been passed down to later generations.

Alas! The title of **"scriptures"** for **Lǎo Zǐ’s** teachings began with **Hàn Jǐng Dì**. **Wú Qián Zé** addressed the **Emperor**, saying, *"Xǔ Chéng Zǐ, **Yuán Yáng Zǐ**, **Lǎo Zǐ**, and **Zhuāng Zǐ** all cultivated their **virtue**, indulged in **self-pleasure**, and roamed freely in mountains and valleys, letting their **minds** wander. Their learning returned to **simplicity and tranquility**. When **Hàn Jǐng Dì** ascended the throne, he found that **Yellow Emperor’s** and **Lǎo Zǐ’s** teachings were particularly profound. He changed the name from **"master"** to **"scripture"** and established the **Daoist** **teaching system**, commanding all under **heaven** to recite and study them."*

The **Han** scholar **Bān Gòng** recorded that **Lǎo Zǐ’s** teachings were transmitted by the **Fù** lineage (with **37 chapters**), the **Lín** lineage (with **4 chapters**), and the **Xú** lineage (with **6 chapters**), while **Liú Xiàng** also offered his interpretation (with **4 chapters**). Alas! Their books have long since been lost.

Today, the transmitted **Daoist** *Dào Dé Jīng* either comprises **two volumes** (upper and lower) or is divided into **81 chapters** or **72 chapters**. The **Hé Shàng Gōng** version splits it into **81 chapters**: the upper section follows the **Dao of Heaven** (with odd numbers), hence **37 chapters**; the lower section follows the **Dao of Earth** (with even numbers), hence **44 chapters**. **Yán Zhēn** then adjusted it to **8 for the Yin Dao** and **9 for the Yang Dao**, resulting in **72 chapters**: **40 in the upper** and **32 in the lower**—all conflicting with **Hé Shàng Gōng’s** arrangement. Their texts differ, and their commentaries vary; none can be verified.

The **recluse** **Xié Shǒu Hào** (of the **Sòng** dynasty) noted that the *Dào Dé Jīng*, as studied by **Fù Yì** of the **Táng** dynasty, was meticulously examined across multiple versions. He recorded:

- The **Xiàng** lineage’s version (from **Ān Qiú’s** tomb, discovered by **Xiàng Yǔ’s** descendant in the **5th year of Qīng Tíng** (569 CE) under **Emperor Qí Jìng** of **Qí**),
- The **Wèi** lineage’s version (obtained by **Dàojiàn Zhī** in the **Tàihé** era of **Wèi**),
- The **Hé Shàng Zhàngrén** version (transmitted by the **Chǔshì** **Qiú Yùe**),
- The **Shān Yáng** **Wáng Bì** version (with **5,683** or **5,610** characters),
- The **Hé Shàng Gōng** version (with **5,355** or **5,590** characters),
- The **Lùoyáng** official version (with **5,635** characters),
- The **Yán** lineage’s version (with **5,722** characters, matching **Hàn Fēi’s** *Yù Lǎo* in content),
- The **Lǐng** lineage’s version (with **5,722** characters, also aligning with **Hàn Fēi’s** *Yù Lǎo*).

Over the years, these versions diverged as scholars relied on their own transmissions or cross-referenced others, leading to inconsistencies. **Sīmǎ Qiān** stated that **Lǎo Zǐ’s** book, discussing **virtue** and **Dao**, contained **over 5,000 words**, but not exceeding **6,000**, hence **5,000+**. The **Daoist** tradition later generalized this as **5,000 zhang** (a **zhang** being a measure of length, here likely metaphorical for the entire text). **Dù Guāngtíng** remarked that **5,000+ words** was indeterminate, with **half in prose** and **half in verse**, blending **principles** and **practical teachings**. Any attempt to **trim the text** or **force the count** distorts its original intent. **Sīmǎ Qiān’s** claim of **5,000+ words** likely refers to **5,000 characters**, but his assertion that **30 extra characters** were added to reach **5,000** is rigid and unpersuasive—like **binding oneself to a pillar** or **carving a boat** after the fact.

Among the **60+** commentaries, some focus on **sequential explanations** (e.g., **Yín Xǐ’s** *Lǎo Jūn Shí Lù* and his *Internal Modifications*), while others provide **concise interpretations** (e.g., the **Sān Tiān Fǎshī** **Zhāng Dàolíng’s** *Two Volumes*). Notable works include:

- **Hé Shàng Gōng’s** *Chapter-by-Chapter Commentary* (attributed to **Hàn Wén Dì’s** descendant who settled near the **Hé River** in **Shǎnzhōu**; his temple still stands),
- **Yán Jūn Píng’s** *Ten-Volume Guide* (a **Shǔ** man named **Zhēn** during **Hàn Chéng Dì’s** reign),
- **Shānyáng** **Wáng Bì’s** *Commentary* (a **Wèi** scholar, **Shàngshū Láng**),
- **Nányáng** **Hé Yàn’s** *Commentary* (a **Wèi** official, **Bìmǎ Dūwèi**),
- **Hénán** **Guō Xiàng’s** *Commentary* (a **Wèi-Jìn** recluse, **Zǐshì**),
- **Yǐngbuàn** **Zhōng Fáng’s** *Commentary* (a **Wèi Míng Dì** scholar, **Shìjì**),
- **Yǐnshì** **Sūn Dēng’s** *Commentary* (a **Wén Míng** recluse, **Gōnghé**),
- **Tàishǐ** **Yáng Shǒuxiān’s** *True Sayings and Collected Commentaries* (a **Gāozōng** scholar, **20 volumes**),
- **Lǐngshì** **Zhāng Píng’s** *Four-Volume Commentary* (a **Míng Dì** **Tàicháng** scholar),
- **Wèi** **Zhào Zhēng’s** *Five-Volume Explanations* (a **Táng** official, **Tàizōng’s** chancellor),
- **Zōng Wénmíng’s** *Five-Volume Explanations*,
- **Hú Chāo’s** *Fifteen-Volume Commentary and Interpretations*,
- **Yǐn Zhēng Guānyì** **Yǐn Yǐng’s** *New Explanations* (**15 volumes**, a **Yǐn** recluse),
- **Chéngshì** **Xú Miǎo’s** *Four-Volume Commentary*,
- **Shíhànlín Dàoshì** **Hé Sīyuǎn’s** *Two-Volume Guide* and *Eight-Volume Demonstrations*,
- **Héngyuè Dàoshì** **Xuè Jìchāng’s** *Ten-Volume Golden Thread* and *One-Volume Numerical Treatise*,
- **Hóngyuán Xiānshēng** **Wáng Dì’s** *Two-Volume Commentary*, *Three-Volume Pearls*, and *Two-Volume Oral Precepts*,
- **Fǎshī Zhào Jiān’s** *Six-Volume Lecture Commentary*,
- **Chéngdū Dàoshì** **Lí Yuānxīng’s** *Four-Volume Commentary*,
- **Tàiyuān Shàoyǐn** **Wáng Guāngtíng’s** *Two-Volume Source Concordance*,
- **Dàoshì Zhāng Huìchāo’s** *Two-Volume Covenantal Commentary*,
- **Gōng Fǎshī’s** *Four-Volume Compiled Explanations*,
- **Tōngyì Gōng Dàoshì** **Rèn Tàiyǐn’s** *Two-Volume Commentary*,
- **Dàoshì Chōng Xū Xiānshēng** **Shēn Fǔ’s** *Five-Volume Commentary* (a **Dàoshì** scholar, **Shìzhōng Jiàn**),
- **Mínshān Dàoshì** **Zhāng Jūnxiàng’s** *Four-Volume Compiled Explanations*,
- **Dàoshì Chéng Yǐng’s** *Six-Volume Lecture Commentary*,
- **Hànzhōu Shìyì** **Wáng Zhēn’s** *Two-Volume Military Stratagems and Commentaries*,
- **Dàoshì Fú Shǎomíng’s** *Two-Volume Daoist Genealogy and Strategies*,
- **Lǐngzōng Huángdì’s** *Commentary* on the *Dào Dé Jīng* (**two volumes**; with **six volumes** of lecture notes).

「**Héshàng Gōng** and **Yán Jūn Píng** both expounded the **Dao** of governing the state and nurturing the people. **Sōng Líng Xiānshēng** (Wei **Dài**), **Sūn Dēng**, **Táo Yǐn Jū** of the Liang, **Gù Huān** of the Southern Qi, and all elucidated the **Dao** of cultivating the self.

**Dí Jiān**’s time, **Luó Shí** and **Tú Chéng** of the Later Zhao; **Liáng Wǔ Dì**, the **Daoist** **Dòu Luè**, and all clarified the **Dao** of cause and effect, reason and principle.

The **Daoists** of the Liang—**Mèng Zhì Zhōu**, **Zāng Yǐng Jìng**—and of the Chen—**Zhū Jiāo**—as well as **Sòng Zhōu** **Dàoshì Liú Jìn Xǐ**, **Chéng Zǐ Huǎng** of the Tang, **Cài Zǐ Huǎng**, **Huáng Yǐn Zé**, **Lǐ Róng**, **Chē Huì Bì**, **Zhāng Huì Chāo**, and **Lǐ Yuán Xīng** all expounded the **Dao** of **zhòng** (重, *weight/importance*).

**Hé Yàn**, **Zhōng Fáng**, **Dú Yuán Kǎi**, **Wáng Fǔ Sì**, **Zhāng Sì**, **Yáng Hù**, **Lú Shì**, and **Liú Rén Fáng** all mastered the **Dao** of **xūjí wúwéi** (虚极無為, *emptiness and non-action*) in governing the family and the state. These are the scholars whose interpretations diverge in meaning.

Moreover, each school’s learning and doctrinal foundations differ:
- **Yán Jūn Píng** took **xū** (虚, *emptiness*) as his core.
- **Gù Huān** emphasized **wúwéi** (無為, *non-action*).
- **Mèng Zhì Zhōu** and **Zāng Yǐng Jìng** centered on **Dào Dé** (道德, *Virtue and Way*).
- **Liáng Wǔ Dì** held **fēiyǒu fēiwú** (非有非無, *neither being nor non-being*) as his principle.
- **Sūn Dēng** expounded the **Dao** of **zhòng** (重).
- **Zhōng Fáng**’s middle path, as praised by **Sūn Shì**, was considered profound.

The *Guāngchuān Dòngshū Zhì* (廣川董逌藏書志) states that under **Táng Zhōng** (唐宗), after **Lǐ Yào** (李約) annotated the *Lǎozǐ* (老子), the text was definitively revised, with the title changed to the *Dào Dé Jīng* (道德經). Wherever the term **Dao** (道) appears, it belongs to the upper volume; wherever **Dé** (德) appears, it belongs to the lower volume. The stone inscriptions were carved at **Wōkǒu** (涡口) in the **Lǎozǐ** Temple. It further states that the Tang **Daoist** **Zhāng Dàoxiàng** (張道相) compiled annotations of the *Dào Dé Jīng* in seven volumes, encompassing thirty families. Among those whose names remain recorded are:
**Héshàng Gōng**, **Jié Jiě** (節解); **Yán Zūn** (嚴遵), **Wáng Guāngtíng** (王光庭); **Hé Yàn** (何晏), **Guō Xiàng** (郭象); **Zhōng Fáng** (鍾防), **Sūn Dēng** (孫登); **Yáng Hù** (羊祜), **Jiū Mó Luóshí** (鳩摩羅什); **Lú Jǐng Yù** (盧景裕), **Liú Rén Fáng** (劉仁防); **Gù Huān** (顧歡), **Táo Yǐn Jū** (陶隱居); **Sōng Líng** (松靈), **Péi Chǔ Sī** (裴處思); **Dú Bì** (杜弼), **Zhāng Píng** (張凭); **Zhāng Sì** (張嗣), **Zāng Yǐng Jìng** (臧靜); **Mèng Ān Qī** (孟安期), **Mèng Zhì Zhōu** (孟智周); **Dòu Luè** (窦略), **Sòng Wén Míng** (宋文明); **Qú Rǒu** (虞柔), **Liú Jìn Xǐ** (劉進喜); **Cài Zǐ Huǎng** (蔡子晃), **Chéng Zǐ Huǎng** (成子晃); **Chē Huì Bì** (車恵弼).

Upon reviewing the *Yìwén Zhì* (藝文志) of the *New* and *Old Tangshū* (新舊唐書), additional names appear: **Mǔ Qiū Wàng** (母丘望, *humble and obscure surname omitted*); **Chéng Shào** (程韶), **Wáng Shàng Shù Cái** (王尚蜀才); **Yuán Zhēn** (袁真), **Shì Huì** (釋惠); **Yán Zūn** (嚴遵), **Yán Líng** (嚴靈); **Yì Yíng** (義盈), **Liáng Kuàng** (梁旷); **Zhōng Shān Chuán** (鍾山傳), **Yú** (奕); **Yáng Shàng Shàn** (楊上善), **Lǐ Yǔn Yuàn** (李允願); **Chén Sì Gǔ** (陳嗣古), **Rèn Zhēn Zǐ** (任真子); **Féng Guō** (馮郭), **Yǐng Shàng Qì** (景上器); **Hán Dú** (韓杜), **Liáng Wǔ Dì** (梁武帝); **Liáng Jiǎn Wén Dì** (梁簡文帝); **Jiǎ Dà Yǐn Jū** (賈大隱居), **Bì Lǘ** (辟閭); **Rén Dàn** (仁谞), **Liú Zhòng Róng** (劉仲融); **Wáng Sù** (王肅), **Dài Jìng** (戴逵); **Lǘ Zōng** (陸宗), **Lú Cáng Yòng** (盧藏用); **Xíng Nán Hé** (邢南和), **Féng Cháo Yǐn** (馮朝隱); **Bái Lǚ Zhōng** (白履忠), **Lǐ Bō** (李播); **Yǐn Zhī Zhāng** (尹知章), **Lù Dé Míng** (陸德明); **Chén Tíng Yù** (陳庭玉), **Lù Xī Shēng** (陸希聲); **Wú Shàn Jīng** (吳善經), **Sūn Sī Miào** (孫思邈); **Lǐ Hán Guāng** (李含光)—a total of forty families. Among those collected by **Zhāng Dàoxiàng** (張道相), **Guō Xiàng** (郭象), **Liú Rén Fáng** (劉仁防), **Sōng Líng** (松靈), **Péi Chǔ Sī** (裴處思), **Dú Bì** (杜弼), **Zhāng Sì** (張嗣), **Zāng Yǐng Jìng** (臧靜), **Dòu Luè** (窦略), **Sòng Wén Míng** (宋文明), **Qú Rǒu** (虞柔), **Liú Jìn Xǐ** (劉進喜), **Cài Zǐ Huǎng** (蔡子晃), and **Chē Huì Bì** (車恵弼)—these fourteen families are not listed in the *Zhì*. The *Zhì* records that **Zhāng Dàoxiàng** compiled four volumes, yet the texts collected by **Dòngshū** (董逌) number seven. Perhaps later scholars added to them.

In the **Chóngníng** (崇寧) era of our dynasty, the **Dàozàng** (道藏) was re-examined and standardized. This book is no longer found in the **Dàozàng**; only the four commentaries of **Lǐ Yào** (李約), **Héshàng Gōng** (河上公), **Yán Zūn** (嚴遵), and **Lù Xī Shēng** (陸希聲), along with the ancient edition of the *Dào Dé Jīng* transmitted by **Yú** (奕), remain.

Outside the **Dàozàng**, **Lǐ Yuè** (李約), **Lǐ Róng** (李榮), **Jiǎ Qīng Yì** (賈淸夷), and others each have their own commentaries. **Wáng** (王) and **Gù** (顧) and others, at the **Dàozàng**’s behest, compiled their annotated scriptures, and **Dú Guāngtíng** (杜光庭) further expounded the *Guǎngshèng Yì* (廣聖義)—all Tang-era scholars, now preserved in the **Dàozàng**. Only then did we realize that the *Zhì*’s records were incomplete. Alas, of those whose names remain, nine out of ten have lost their works.

**Lè Chén** (樂臣) studied under **Huángdì** (黃帝) and **Lǎozǐ** (老子), whose master was called **Héshàng Zhàngrén** (河上丈人). His origins are unknown. **Héshàng Zhàngrén** taught **Ān Qī Shēng** (安期生), who taught **Máo Xī** (毛翕); **Máo Xī** taught **Lè Huǎng** (樂瑕); **Lè Huǎng** taught **Lè Chén** (樂臣). **Lè Chén** then taught **Gài** (盖), who instructed in **Qí Gāomì Jiāoxī** (齊高密胶西) as **Cáo Xiàngguó**’s (曹相國) master—recorded in the *Shǐjì* (史记) *Lè Yì* (樂毅) transmission.

**Hàn Huán Tán** (桓谭) said: **Zhōng Fáng** (鍾防) expounded **Lǎo Dān**’s (老聃) teachings on **xūwú** (虚無, *emptiness and nothingness*) in two works. Later generations who favored them considered them excessive compared to the Five Classics. From the reigns of **Hàn Wén** (漢文) and **Hàn Jǐng** (漢景) onward, as well as **Sīmǎ Qiān** (司馬遷), all spoke of them—recorded in the *Yáng Xiáo* (扬雄) transmission.

**Yán Jūn Píng** (嚴君平) practiced divination with **shī** (蓍, *milfoil stalks*) and **shǎo** (筮, *yarrow stalks*). A native of **Chéngdū** (成都市), when faced with malevolent or improper questions, he relied on the **shī** (蓍) for guidance, speaking of profit and harm. When advising sons, he followed **xiào** (孝, *filial piety*); when addressing brothers, he adhered to **shùn** (順, *harmony/compliance*); when counseling ministers, he emphasized **zhōng** (忠, *loyalty*). He skillfully directed each according to their circumstances, and daily, teaching several students, he earned enough to sustain himself. Then he closed his shop and secretly transmitted the *Lǎozǐ* (老子), mastering all its teachings. His annotations, based on **Yán Zūn** (嚴遵) and **Lǎo** (老), amounted to over ten thousand words.

**Ruǎn Jí** (阮籍) wrote the *Tōng Lǎo Lùn* (通老論), stating: The **Dao** (道) is the principle that follows **zìrán** (自然, *nature/spontaneity*) and thus transforms. Sovereigns and kings who guard it will see all things naturally transformed. **Yì** (易, *Change*) calls it **Tàijí** (太極, *Supreme Polarity*); the *Chūnqiū* (春秋, *Spring and Autumn Annals*) term it **Yuán** (元, *Origin*); and **Lǎozǐ** calls it **Dao** (道)—recorded in the *Tàipíng Yùlǎn* (太平御览).

**Wáng Fǔ Sì** (王辅嗣), a native of **Shānyáng Gāopíng** (山陽高平), was precociously insightful. By his teens, he already studied the **Zhuāng** (莊) and **Lǎo** (老) texts, mastering their distinctions. **Hé Píng Shù** (何平叔) annotated the *Lǎozǐ* (老子), and when **Wáng** (王) saw his work, he marveled at its profundity, declaring: "Such a man can discuss the interplay between **Tiān** (天, *Heaven*) and **Rén** (人, *Man)!"** Thus, he composed the *Dào Dé Èrlùn* (道德二論, *Two Treatises on Virtue and Way*). The *Wèi Shì Chūnqiū* (魏氏春秋) states: "His annotations on the **Yì** (易) surpass **Hé Yàn**’s (何晏) in elegance, yet **Zhōng Fáng**’s (鍾防) natural exposition is even more refined."

**Yīn Zhòng Kān** (殷仲堪) possessed keen reasoning and could speak clearly. He once said: "If I do not read the *Dào Dé Jīng* (道德經) for three days, my tongue feels stiff and unyielding"—recorded in the *Shìjiè* (世說).

**Yǔ Zǐ Sōng** (庾子嵩) was broad-minded and measured. He claimed to be a disciple of **Lǎozǐ** and **Zhuāngzǐ**, saying: "Before reading their works, I thought their principles were such-and-such. Now that I’ve seen them, they perfectly align with my own darkly intuitive understanding"—recorded in the *Jìn Yángqiū* (晉陽秋).

**Ruǎn Xuān Zǐ** (阮宣子), who favored the **Lǎozǐ** and **Yì** (易), could articulate their principles. When he encountered common people, he would leave them with disdain, never engaging. Though his family had no wealth, he lived in poverty like **Hé Yàn**—recorded in the *Míngshì Zhì* (名士傳).

**Zhōu Yàn Lún** (周彦倫) mastered the principles of names and logic. When guests gathered, he would expound on **xū** (虚, *emptiness*) without hesitation. His words flowed like a river, and listeners forgot weariness. He was particularly skilled in the **Lǎozǐ** and **Yì**, often engaging in prolonged discussions with **Zhāng Sīguāng** (張思光), leaving them unresolved for days.

**Ruǎn Zhàn** (阮瞻), son of **Ruǎn Xuān** (阮宣), was seen by **Sītú Wáng Róng** (司徒王戎). When **Wáng Róng** (王戎) asked him about **Lǎozǐ** and **Zhuāngzǐ**, he replied: "The **Dao** (道) of **Lǎozǐ** and **Zhuāngzǐ** is the same." **Wáng Róng** (王戎) sighed in admiration for a long time, then appointed him as **Bì** (辟) and called him the **"Sān Yǔ Dūn"** (三語掾, *Three-Word Magistrate*)—recorded in the *Jìnshū Běn Zhuàn* (晉書本傳) and *Shìjiè* (世說), with some accounts naming him **Ruǎn Xiū** (

---

**Zōng Cè** (宗测) in his youth was calm and serene, finding no joy in worldly affairs. When **Yùzhāng Wáng Yì** (豫章王嶷) conscripted him as a **cāngūnjūn** (參軍, *paramount military officer*), he replied: *"What is the point of this?"*—thus injuring the sea birds by felling trees and the mountain woods by cutting them down. To avoid such pursuits, he hung his ancestor’s portrait of **Xiàngzǐ Píng** (向子平) upon his wall and took along the *Lǎozǐ* and *Zhuāngzǐ* texts to bid farewell to his family in sorrowful tears. **Zōng Cè** (宗测) let out a long sigh and turned away without looking back.

**Zhòng Zhāng** (仲長), whose courtesy name was **Sīguāng** (子光) and whose personal name was **Zhāng Xuān** (張宣), traveled between **Hé Dōng** (河東) and worked to sustain himself, owning no home or dwelling and severing ties with his wife and children. By the end of the **Kāihuáng** (開皇) era, he retired to the banks of the **Fén** (汾) River, where he sold medicine as his livelihood. None knew him there. **Yóu Yóu** (游河) of **Fényīn** (汾陰) once visited him at the riverbank and, upon seeing him, exclaimed: *"This man surpasses even **Dōngfāng Shuò** (東方朔) and **Guǎn Shè** (管輅)!"* From that point onward, he gained renown and respect. When officials came to pay their respects, he would remain silent due to illness, never engaging in conversation. He preferred solitary wandering and composed a poem in praise of the **Hé Dōng** (河東) recluse, using it as his own epitaph. Those who knew him recognized him as a man of profound understanding. Some who sought the **Dao** (道) would write the characters **"Lǎozǐ Yì"** (老子易) and show them to him. He would then play the **qín** (琴) and dispense medicines, thus ending his days.

**Wáng Jì** (王績) was fond of wine and unable to bear official duties. He had several slaves who cultivated millet, and in spring and autumn, they brewed wine and raised ducks and geese. He cultivated medicinal herbs to sustain himself, reading the *Zhōu Yì* (周易), *Lǎozǐ* (老子), and *Zhuāngzǐ* (莊子) while keeping other books aside. He numbered his travels to the **Běishān** (北山) and **Dōnggāo** (東皋), and from then on, he called himself **"Dōnggāo Zǐ"** (東皋子).

**Wáng Xīyí** (王希夷) hid himself in the **Sōngshān** (嵩山) and studied under **Huáng Dǎn** (黃赜), learning the arts of **yǎngshēng** (養生, *nourishing life*). He delighted in reading the *Zhōu Yì* (周易), *Lǎozǐ* (老子), and consuming pine and cypress leaves mixed with flowers. At over seventy years of age, his vitality remained unwaned. When **Lǚ Zhēnyí Qīng** (盧齐卿), the **shìshì** (刺史, *prefect*), visited to inquire about governance, **Wáng Xīyí** (王希夷) replied: *"Do not impose upon others what you yourself do not desire."* The prefect deemed this answer sufficient.

**Zhēnyī Xiānshēng** (貞一先生), **Sīmǎ Chéngzhuān** (司馬承祯) by courtesy name, was called **Zǐwēi** (子微). He dwelled at **Tiāntái** (天台) and did not leave. When the **Ruìzōng** (睿宗) Emperor sent his brother **Sīmǎ Chéngyí** (司馬承祎) to summon him, he was already there. The Emperor led him into the **zhōngyuè** (中掖) hall and inquired about his teachings. **Sīmǎ Chéngzhuān** (司馬承祯) answered: *"The **Dao** (道) is daily diminished—diminished again and again, until it reaches **wúwéi** (無為, *non-action*). For what my mind and eyes perceive, I must already diminish it, yet still cannot cease. How much more so when confronting heterodox doctrines and straining my intellect!"*

The Emperor asked: *"How do you cultivate yourself?"* He replied: *"The state is like the self."* Thus, he sought harmony with simplicity, blending his **qi** (氣) with tranquility, and acted naturally without personal bias.

「Yet the realm was in harmony, and the Emperor sighed in admiration, saying, *"This is the speech of Guǎngchéng!"*

During the **Kāiyuán** (開元) era, he was summoned again and brought to the capital. **Zōng** (宗) issued an edict at **Wànghú Shān** (王屋山) to establish a hall and chamber for his residence. He was commanded to transcribe the *Lǎozǐ* (老子) in **sān tǐ** (三體, *three scripts*), and to settle the text and words. **[As recorded in the *Xīn Tángshū* (新唐書) *Yǐnshì* (隱士) biography and the *Jiùshū* (舊書) *Běnzhuan* (本傳), Zōng ordered him to transcribe the *Lǎozǐ Jīng* (老子經) in three scripts, and to correct and fix the text, establishing five thousand three hundred and eighty words as the authentic version, which he then presented to the Emperor.]**

**Lú Hóngyī** (盧鴻一), with the courtesy name **Hàorán** (颢然), secluded himself in **Sōngshān** (嵩山). In the sixth year of **Kāiyuán** (開元六年), he was conscripted to the Eastern Capital (洛陽) where he met with the Emperor but refused to bow. The Chancellor sent a **tōngshì** (通事) to inquire about his reason, and he replied: *"I have heard the Master Lǎo’s words: ‘The observance of rituals is but the thin veneer of loyalty and sincerity, insufficient to rely upon.’ Since I, Lú Hóngyī, hold loyalty and sincerity dear, I dare not prostrate myself before you."* **[As recorded in the *Jiù Tángshū* (舊唐書) *Yǐnshì* (隱士) biography.]**

**Zōngyuán Xiānsheng** (宗元先生), **Wú Jūn** (吳筠), was a scholar from **Lǔzhōu** (魯中). He entered **Sōngshān** (嵩山) and became a **Dàoshì** (道士, *Daoist). For many years, he wandered at **Tiāntái** (天台). When Zōng summoned him, the envoy conscripted him to the capital. Upon arrival, he was asked about the **Dao** (道) and its methods. He answered: *"The essence of the Dao/Way’s teachings is nothing like the Five Thousand Words. Its various branches and sprawling explanations merely waste ink and parchment."* **[As recorded in the *Jiù Tángshū* (舊唐書) *Yǐnshì* (隱士) biography and the *Xīnshū* (新書) *Běnzhuan* (本傳), the Emperor once asked him about the Dao, and he replied: ‘None surpass the profundity of the *Lǎozǐ*’s Five Thousand Words. The rest are but idle scribblings that waste paper.’ When further asked about immortality and alchemy, he said: ‘These are matters for rustics. Accumulating years and months to seek them is not a concern for the ruler.’ [The *Jiù Tángshū* (舊唐書) *Shàoshǐng* (少昇) biography has slight variations, hence both are included.]**

**Qín Xì** (秦系), a native of **Fáng** (防稽), fled to **Jiǎnxī** (剡溪) during the late **Tiānbǎo** (天寶) era to avoid the chaos. He dwelled in **Nán’ān** (南安) as a guest in **Quánzhōu** (泉州). There was a great pine tree over a hundred years old, whose roots he used to build his hut. Beneath it, he carved a stone for his ink slab and inscribed the *Lǎozǐ* (老子), never leaving. People called his dwelling **Gāoshì Fēng** (高士峰, *Peak of the High Virtue/Power Man*).

Emperor **Huìzōng** (憲宗) of the **Táng** (唐) consulted his Chancellor, saying: *"Is the matter of immortals and transcendent beings to be believed?"* **Lǐ Fān** (李藩) answered: *"The teachings of immortals originate from the **Dàojiā** (道家, *Daoist school). The Daoists revere the *Lǎozǐ*’s Five Thousand Words as their foundation. The *Lǎozǐ*’s principles align with the **Tàiyì** (太易, *Great Transformation*)—those who came before him, like **Xiānhuáng** (轩皇) and **Huángdì** (黃帝), sought the Dao/Way’s harmony. Later, the **Xiānhuáng** (轩皇) and **Huángdì** (黃帝) were deceived by strange and extravagant claims, falsely attributing immortality and transcendence to the *Lǎozǐ*. Thus, **Qín Shǐhuáng** (秦始皇) and **Hàn Wǔdì** (漢武帝) were misled by these teachings and ultimately gained nothing."* **[As recorded in the *Jiù Tángshū* (舊唐書) *Huìzōngjì* (憲宗紀).]**

**Táng** (唐) Chancellor **Lǔ Xīshēng** (陸希聲) annotated the *Dàodé Jīng* (道德經) in four volumes. His preface briefly states: *"The Master Lǎo’s teachings take the **Dao** (道) as their essence and use its name as their practice. They embody **wúwéi** (無為, *non-action*) yet achieve **wúbùwéi** (無不為, *nothing left undone*), aligning with the **Huángjí** (皇極, *Supreme Polarity*). The six schools that followed him—**Yángzhū** (楊朱), **Zhuāngzi** (莊周), **Wángshī** (王何), and others—all erred in their interpretations. **Yángzhū** (楊朱) lost sight of the Master Lǎo’s essence by overemphasizing self-preservation and undervaluing external things. **Zhuāngzi** (莊周) lost the balance by exaggerating the application of the Dao. **Shēnxiān** (申韓) distorted the Master Lǎo’s name by adopting harsh and extreme methods. **Wángshī** (王何) strayed from the Dao’s core by embracing empty, unrestrained doctrines. These six schools are all the Master Lǎo’s ‘sinners.’*

Now, I elucidate and annotate to clarify his teachings and to harmonize with **Fáng** (防). Again, I say: *"In ancient times, **Fǔxī** (伏羲) drew the **Bāguà** (八卦, *Eight Trigrams), symbolizing the principles of all things, exhausting the essence of life and nature. He followed the **Dàodé** (道德) in harmony. The *Lǎozǐ* (老子) expounded upon **xiāntiāndì** (先天地, *before Heaven and Earth*), originating from **yīnyáng** (陰陽, *yin-yang) and reaching the extremes of life and nature. This aligns with **Fǔxī** (伏羲) in its source. **Wénwáng** (文王) observed the **Tàiyì** (太易, *Great Transformation*) through the **Jiǔliù** (九六, *Nine and Six*) movements, valuing the **jiāng** (剛, *strong/rigid) and upholding change, yet balancing it with moderation. The *Lǎozǐ* (老子) discerned the **Tàiyì** (太易) in its **qīng** (清, *seven*) and **zhèng** (正, *eight*) aspects, seeking the **róu** (柔, *soft/compliant) and guarding stillness, yet unifying it with the **Dà** (大, *Great). This aligns with **Wénwáng** (文王) in its core principles. **Kǒngzǐ** (孔子) inherited the traditions of **Yáo** (堯) and **Shùn** (舜), following the constitutional models of **Wénwáng** (文王) and **Wǔwáng** (武王), guiding the people with the teachings of **rén** (仁, *benevolence*) and **yì** (義, *righteousness). The *Lǎozǐ* (老子) contemplated the **Fǔxī** (伏羲) and synthesized **Huángdì** (黃帝), embracing the world with the **Dàodé** (道德) transformation. This aligns with **Kǒngzǐ** (孔子) in its authority. These three sages—**Fǔxī** (伏羲), **Wénwáng** (文王), and **Kǒngzǐ** (孔子)—are the pinnacles of sainthood. The *Lǎozǐ* (老子) transformed and harmonized their teachings, reversing and merging them to explore the mechanisms of change and the subtleties of the Dao’s essence. This can be called the most profound of all."*

**Lǐ Wèigōng** (李衛公), **Déyù** (德裕), advised Emperor **Jìngzōng** (敬宗) against searching for Daoists in his memorial, stating: *"I have heard that the highest **Dao** (道) is none other than **Guǎngchéng** (廣成). Among the sages of antiquity, none surpasses **Xiānhuáng** (轩皇). **Kǒngzǐ** (孔子) questioned **Xiānhuáng** (轩皇) about the principles of self-cultivation, and **Guǎngchéng** (廣成) replied: ‘Do not look, do not listen; embrace the **shén** (神, *spirit) and maintain stillness. Your form will naturally correct itself; your spirit will naturally purify. Do not exert your body, do not shake your **jīng** (精, *essence). Thus, you may attain longevity.’ Again, he said: ‘Those who attain my Dao will become **Huáng** (皇, *Emperor*) above and **Wáng** (王, *King*) below.’"* **[As recorded in *Lǐ Wénráo Jí* (李文饒集).]** *"**Kǒngzǐ** (孔子) said: ‘Remove your arrogant **qì** (氣), your excessive desires, your indulgent expressions, and your licentious thoughts—these are all harmful to your body. What I have told you, if you follow, will suffice.’ The sages of old, in their profound understanding of the Dao, did not err in their teachings. If **Guǎngchéng** (廣成) and **Xiānhuáng** (轩皇) were to appear and speak to Your Majesty, I believe their words would not surpass these."*

**Xiāngshān Báifēngōng** (香山白文公) resided in **Yì** (易) and said: *"To make the people frugal and simple, and to bring peace and harmony to the times, one must first embody the **Dàodé** (道) of **Huáng-Lǎo** (黃老). Its **Dao** (道) lies in upholding **kuānjiǎn** (寬簡, *leniency and simplicity), practicing frugality, and maintaining naturalness. It does not require **cōngchá** (聰察, *sharp discernment) nor does it force the intellect. The skill lies in its application—even in a single village, a single prefecture, a single state, or reaching the entire world, one may achieve the principle of **qīngjìng** (清靜, *clarity and stillness).* **Fáng** (防) and **Mì** (宓) were humble yet attained it, so they did not descend from their chambers, yet the people of **Dānfǔ** (單父) were transformed. **Jì** (汲) **Pǐn** (黯) attained it, so he did not leave his study, yet the governance of **Dōnghǎi** (東海) was accomplished. **Cáo Shēng** (曹參) attained it, so he did not interfere with the markets or prisons, yet **Qíguó** (齊國) was in great harmony. **Hàn Wéndì** (漢文帝) attained it, so he did not use punishments, yet the realm was greatly governed. The reason is this: where **qīngjìng** (清靜) prevails, **qīngjìng** (清靜) is its outcome."* **[As recorded in *Bái Shì Chángqìng Jí* (白氏長慶集).]**

「Zhang Xuanming (張荐明) in his youth was renowned for his Confucian learning and traveled through the **Hé Shuò** (河朔) region. Later, he became a **Dàoshì** (道士, *Daoist adept*), mastering the **Dào** (道, *Dao/Way*) of **Lǎo Zǐ** (老子) and **Zhuāng Zhōu** (莊周). When summoned by **Jìn Gāozǔ** (晉高祖, *Emperor Gaozu of Jin*), he was asked whether the **Dàojiā** (道家, *Daoist School*) could govern a state. He replied, *“The **Dào** (道, *Dao/Way*) is that which subtly penetrates all things; those who grasp its utmost may govern the world even while seated at home.”* The emperor was delighted by his words, invited him into the inner palace, and lectured him on the *Dàodéjīng* (道德經, *Classic of Virtue and the Dao*). He then bowed to Zhang as his teacher, bestowed upon him the title **Tōng Wǔ** (通微, *Master of Profound Insight*), and afterward he vanished without trace. **[As recorded in *Wǔdài Shǐ* (五代史, *History of the Five Dynasties*)—*Yīxíng Zhuàn* (一行傳).]**

**Tàizǔ** (太祖, *Emperor Taizu of Song*) was campaigning against **Tàiyuán** (太原) and stationed at **Zhèn Yáng** (鎮陽, *Zhenyang*). He heard that the **Dàoshì** (道士, *Daoist adept*) **Sū Chéng** (蘇澄) had secluded himself since the **Wǔdài** (五代) era, repeatedly declining invitations to court. When summoned to the imperial residence, Sū was already eighty years old. The emperor asked him about **yǎngshēng** (養生, *nurturing life*). Sū replied, *“My method of nurturing life is nothing more than refining thought and harmonizing **qì** (氣, *qi*). For rulers, however, it is different. **Lǎo Zǐ** (老子) said, ‘If I act with non-action, the people transform themselves; if I have no desires, the people become simple.’ Non-action and non-desire mean focusing the mind and achieving serenity. In ancient times, **Huángdì** (黃帝) and **Táng Yáo** (唐堯) ruled for long years by this **Dào** (道, *Dao/Way*).”* The emperor marveled at his words. **[As recorded in *Dōngdū Shìlüè* (東都事略) in *Yǐn Mì Zhuàn* (隐逸传) and *Gāo Dào Zhuàn* (高道传).]**

**Sòng Tàizōng** (宋太宗, *Emperor Taizong of Song*) spoke to his close ministers, *“When I read **Lǎo Zǐ** (老子) and reach the passage ‘**Jiābīng** (佳兵, *fine weaponry*)—that which is not auspicious, the **shèngrén** (聖人, *sage*) uses only when forced,’ I cannot help but reread it three times as a warning.”*

**Hóngméng Zǐ** (鸿蒙子) **Zhāng Wúmèng** (張無夢), styled **Língyǐn** (靈隱), loved **qīngxū** (清虚, *clarity and emptiness*). He delved deeply into **Lǎo Zǐ** (老子) and **Yì** (易, *Classic of Changes*), entering **Huáshān** (華山) where he befriended **Liú Hǎichán** (劉海蟾) and **Fàng** (放, *the recluse*). He served **Chén Xīyí** (陳希夷) as a **fāngwài yǒu** (方外友, *lay friend of the Dao*). Wúmèng often grasped subtle principles of **Mìfāng** (微防, *gentle restraint*), and after a long time, he retreated to **Tiāntái Shān** (天台山). **Zhēnzōng** (真宗, *Emperor Zhenzong of Song*) summoned him and asked about strategies for longevity. Wúmèng replied, *“I am a rustic. In the mountains, I have only recited **Lǎo Zǐ** (老子) and **Zhōuyì** (周易, *Classic of Changes*). I know nothing else.”* When offered the position of **zuǒláng** (作佐郎, *assistant official*), he declined and returned to the mountains. The emperor bestowed upon him gold and silk, along with the title **chǔshì** (處士, *recluse*), but he did not accept. **[As recorded in *Gāo Dào Zhuàn* (高道传).]**

**Lěizhāi** (了斋) **Chén Zhōngsù** (陳忠肅) once wrote twenty-plus essays titled *Zhāoyǔ* (昭语, *Illuminating Words*), whose preface briefly states: *“The **Yùqīng Zhāoyìng Gōng** (玉清昭应宫) instructed me to request **Wáng Zēng** (王曾) to review the **Dàojiā** (道家) scriptures in the **Sānguǎn** (三馆, *Three Scriptural Halls*). I spoke of my work, which, though not as refined as **Lǎozǐ**’s (老氏) *Wǔqiān Zhēn* (五千言, *Five Thousand Words*), still embodies **qīngjìng** (清静) and **jiǎn’āo** (简约, *simplicity and brevity*).”*
**Zhāng Zhībái** (張知白) said, *“Your Majesty’s attention to this is the art of **wúwéi** (無為, *non-action*) in governing the state.”* **[As recorded in *Lěizhāi Jí* (了斋集).]**

**Tián Jiànyì** (田谏议) **Xīchǐ** (锡尺) wrote in the preface to his *Shùfù* (述赞): *“When the **lóng** (龍, *dragon*) rises, it ascends from wood; when a ruler emerges, humanity is his foundation. Yet there are **sèngshèng** (聖聖, *sages without rank*) and **táocáng zhān** (韜光之賢, *virtuous scholars who conceal their brilliance*), whose legacies and abilities intersect like **Qiū Míng** (丘明) and **Zhòngní** (仲尼). Thus, **Qiū Míng** (丘明) could elucidate the *Chūnqiū* (春秋, *Spring and Autumn Annals*) to establish **shēngjiào** (聲教, *moral teachings).*
**Yǐn Xǐ** (尹喜) was such a **zhān** (賢, *virtuous scholar), and **Lǎo Dān** (老聃, *Lǎo Zǐ*) was his **chǐchǔ** (尺木, *foundation*). Thus, he could consult on **Dàodé** (道德, *Virtue and the Dao*) to leave guidance for posterity.”** **[As recorded in *Xián Píng Jí* (咸平集).]**

**Chén Zhōngsù** (陳忠肅) said, *“**Lǎo Zǐ** (老子) states that the world’s sacred instrument—the **Dào** (道, *Dao/Way*)—cannot be wielded by force; those who attempt to seize it ruin it. He also says, ‘To govern a great state is like cooking a small fish.’ The cook of fish need not fuss over the flames; he merely balances **shuǐhuǒ** (水火, *water and fire*) to achieve the proper degree of done-ness. **Shùn** (舜) ruled with non-action—was it not by this principle?*
*Furthermore, **Wǔdì** (武帝, *Emperor Wudi of Han*) abandoned **Huánglǎo** (黃老, *Yellow Emperor and Laozi’s teachings*) for Confucianism, yet he never strayed from **rényì** (仁義, *benevolence and righteousness*). In practice, however, his effects were no different from **Qín Shǐhuáng** (秦始皇). When **Fáng Xī** (防希, *Mifang*) was in office, the world was not without chaos for a single day. People longed for the governance of **Wénjǐng** (文景, *Emperors Wen and Jing of Han*), yet that could never be recovered. Then again, what harm did **Huánglǎo** (黃老) bring to the world?*
*Again, **Shū Guǎng** (疏廣) told his son **Shū Shòu** (疏受): ‘Knowing contentment brings no disgrace; knowing restraint brings no peril. Once fame and office are achieved, to depart in fear of regret is wise.’ Thus, father and son retired together, feigning illness, and returned to their homeland. Later generations and scholars of virtue have praised this, yet their understanding of **zhǐ** (止, *restraint*) stems from **Lǎozǐ**’s (老氏) teachings.”* **[As recorded in *Lěizhāi Jí* (了斋集).]**

**Ōuyáng Wénzhōng** (歐陽文忠) **Gōng Xiū** (公修) wrote in his *Qiánhòu Jì* (前后记): *“The **Qián** (前, *before*) and **Hòu** (後, *after*) follow one another; the **cháng** (長, *long*) and **duǎn** (短, *short*) contrast each other. Extending this analogy, the principles of all things follow this pattern. Yet **Lǎozǐ** (老子) composed his book with words that seem to advocate **xūwú** (虛無, *emptiness and nothingness**), yet his methods for governing humanity are profound. He said, ‘If I act with non-action, the people transform themselves; if I love stillness, the people naturally align.’ Even the **sage’s** governance from the southern face of his throne cannot surpass this.”* **[As recorded in *Běn Jí* (本集).]**

**Yánpíng Xiānshēng** (延平先生) **Luó Cóngyàn Zhōng** (罗从彦仲) once remarked: *“**Kǒngzǐ** (孔子) never praised nor criticized **Lǎozǐ**’s (老子) writings. If he had praised them, later scholars would have been drowned in his **hétóng tóngchén** (和光同塵, *harmonizing with the world and blending with dust*) teachings, descending into **bùjī** (不羁, *unbridled behavior*). If he had criticized them, the principle of **qīngjìng** (清静) would have been dismissed as mere theory. Yet why should it be criticized? Since he neither praised nor criticized, it is only proper that he did not speak of them at all. Thus, he merely said, ‘I compare myself to **Lǎo Dān** (老聃) and **Péng** (彭).’”*

Someone asked **Yáng Wénjìng** (杨文靖) of **Yǐngshān** (荥山) about the term **Lǎo Péng** (老彭). He replied: *“‘**Lǎo Péng**’ refers not to **Péng**’s (彭) longevity but to **Lǎozǐ** (老子) and **Péng** (彭籛, *Péng Zhèn*). Yet **Lǎozǐ**’s (老氏) writings truly embody **shù ér bù zuò** (述而不作, *reciting without composing*) and **xìn ér hǎogu** (信而好古, *believing in and loving antiquity*).”* **[As recorded in *Yǐngshān Jí* (荥山集).]**

**Yǐngshān** (荥山) said: *“Only when all private intentions are exhausted can one respond to the world. **Lǎozǐ** (老子) said, ‘You are now the **wáng** (王, *king*).’”* **[As recorded in *Yǔlù* (语录).]**

**Yíngyáng Lǚ Gōng Xīzhé** (荥阳吕公希哲) once inscribed on a wall: *“In governing people and harmonizing with heaven, nothing surpasses **sè** (啬, *frugality) at the outset.”* He considered this the essential method of **xiūyǎng** (修養, *cultivation and nurturing*). Though it is a technique for **yǎngshēng** (養生, *nurturing life*), its application extends to safeguarding oneself and one’s family, ensuring **ānshēn** (安身) and **ānjiā** (安家). **[As recorded in *Lǚ Shì Zázù* (吕氏杂录).]**

**Dōngpō** (东坡) **Sū Wénzhōng** (蘇文忠) **Gōng Suī** (公轼) received imperial orders to compose the stele for **Shàngqīng Chǔxiáng Gōng** (上清储祥宫). He wrote: *“I humbly examine the **Dàojiā** (道家) School, which originated with **Huángdì** (黃帝) and **Lǎozǐ** (老子). Its **Dào** (道, *Dao/Way*) centers on **qīngjìng wúwéi** (清静無為, *clarity, stillness, and non-action*), and its practice lies in **xūmíng yìngwù** (虚明应物, *illuminated responsiveness to things*). Its conduct is marked by **cíjiǎn bùzhēng** (慈俭不争, *benevolence, frugality, and non-contentiousness*), aligning with the *Yì* (易). It requires no deliberation, no thought. The *Lúnyǔ* (论语) states, ‘The **rénzhě** (仁者, *benevolent person*) is still, and thus attains longevity.’*
*From the **Qín** (秦) and **Hàn** (漢) dynasties onward, the **Dào** (道, *Dao/Way*) was first applied by **fāngshì** (方士, *adepts of the Way*), who introduced **fēixiān biànhuà** (飞仙变化, *transformation and ascension to immortality*), **Huángtíng Dàdòng** (黃庭大洞, *Great Cavern of the Yellow Court*), **Tàishàng Tiānzhēn** (太上天真, *Supreme Celestial Purity*), **Mùgōng Jīnmǔ** (木公金母, *Wood Master and Golden Mother*), and the worship of **Tiānhuáng Tàiyǐ** (天皇太乙) and **Zǐwéi Běijí** (紫微北极, *Purple Darkness and Northern Pole). Down to the present, all **dānyào qíji** (丹药奇技, *elixirs and esoteric techniques*), **fújù xiǎoshù** (符箓小数, *talismans, charms, and minor rites*) are attributed to the **Dàojiā** (道家). I have privately analyzed this: **Huángdì** (黃帝) and **Lǎozǐ** (老子) represent the **běn** (本, *root*), while the **fāngshì** (方士) teachings are the **mò** (末, *branch*).”*

In his *Cáotáng Jì* (曹堂记), he wrote: *“When **Cáo Cān** (曹參) served as **Qíxiàng** (齐相), the **Jiāoxī** (胶西) **Guāng Gōng** (盖公) was renowned for his mastery of **Huánglǎo** (黃老). When the people requested **Cáo Cān** (曹參) to implement his methods, **Qíguó** (齊國) flourished. Later, he applied the same principles to govern the world, and the world has since praised him as a **xián** (賢, *virtuous scholar).*”

「I became the governor of Jiāoxī (胶西) and came to know **Guāng Gōng** (盖公) as a man of **dàojiā** (道家) virtue. I sought his tomb and descendants but could not find them, and with a sigh, I pondered him deeply. Through his teachings, I imagined his character.

**Cáo Cān** (曹参) was a loyal minister of the **Hàn** (汉) dynasty, while **Guāng Gōng** (盖公) served as his teacher—this was indeed a grand affair. Yet the historians do not record how his life ended. Could it be that he was one of the **zhìrén** (至人, *perfected sages*) of old, who attained the **Dao** (道) and did not die? [*See this collection.*] Later, **Sū Zǐyóu** (苏子由) wrote after composing his own *Lǎozǐ Jiě* (老子解):

*"I was forty-two years old when I was exiled to Yúnzhōu (筠州). Though Yún was a small state, it had many ancient **chán shè** (禅刹, *Buddhist monasteries*). Monks from all directions gathered there. Among them was **Dàoguān** (道全), a descendant of **Nán Gōng** (南公) of **Huángbì Shān** (黄蘗山). His conduct was lofty, and his mind was open, so he delighted in following me as I traveled. Once, he spoke with me of the **Dao** (道), and I told him: ‘What you speak of, I have already found in Confucian texts.’

He replied, ‘This is **Fófǎ** (佛法, *Buddhist dharma*). How did the Confucians obtain it?’

I said, ‘No, I have indeed heard of the **Dao** (道), which Confucians do not possess. Why force me to falsely claim it? Still, it exists, though the world does not know it!’

The **Dao** (道) of **Rú** (儒, *Confucianism*) and **Fó** (佛, *Buddhism*) do not mutually illuminate one another, just as **Hú** (胡, *non-Chinese*) and **Hàn** (汉, *Chinese*) peoples do not understand each other’s languages. How then do you know it?

He said, ‘Try to explain its essence to me.’

I replied, ‘The book of **Zǐsī** (孔子之孙, *Confucius’ grandson*), **Zǐsī** (子思), states: *“The Doctrine of the Mean”* (**Zhōngyōng** 中庸). In *“The Doctrine of the Mean”*, it says: *“When joy, anger, sorrow, and delight have not yet arisen, this is called the **Zhōng** (中, *Mean). When they arise and are all in accordance with the **Zhōng** (中), this is called **hé** (和, *harmony).”* The **Zhōng** (中) is the **Dàběn** (大本, *great root*) of all under Heaven, and **hé** (和) is the **Dádào** (达道, *great Way*) of all under Heaven. To achieve **Zhōnghé** (中和, *central harmony*), the **Tian Di** (天地, *Heaven and Earth*) attain their proper positions, and all things are nurtured.

Is this not the **Dao** (道) of the **Rú** (儒), and what else could it be? Though the sources of speech may differ, the meaning is the same.»

全曰：「如何言之？」予曰：「六祖有言：不思善，不思恶，方是时也。孰是汝本来面目？自六祖以来，人以此言悟入者，太半矣。所谓不思善不思恶，则喜怒哀乐之未发也。盖中者，佛性之异名；和者，六度万行之总目也。致中极和，而天地万物生于其间。此非佛法，何以当之？」

全惊喜曰：「吾初不知也。今而后始知儒佛一法也。」

予笑曰：「不然。天下固无二道，而所以治人则异。君臣父子之间，非礼法则乱。知礼法而不知道，则世之俗儒，不足贵也。居山林，木食涧饮，而心存至道，虽为人天师可也。而以之治世，则乱。古之圣人，中心行道，而不毁法，而后可耳。」

全作礼曰：「此至论也。」

是时，予方解《老子》，每出一章，辄以示全。全辄叹曰：「皆佛说也。」

予居筠五年而北归，全不久亦化去。逮今二十余年也。

凡《老子》解亦时有所刋定，未有不与佛法合者。时人无可与语，思复见全而示之，故书之《老子》之末。

大观二年十二月十日，子由题。

又曰：予防南迁海康，与子瞻兄邂逅于藤州，相从十余日，语及平生旧学。子瞻谓予：「子所作《诗传》、《春秋传》、《古史》三书，皆古人所未至；惟解《老子》，差若不及。」予至海康闲居无事，凡所为书，多所更定。乃再录《老子》书以寄子瞻。自是防恩归北，子瞻至毗陵得疾不起。逮今十余年，竟不知此书于子瞻为可否也。

政和元年冬，得侄迈等所编先公手泽，其一曰：「昨日子由寄《老子新解》，读之不尽卷，废卷而叹。使战国有此书，则无商鞅、韩非；使汉初有此书，则孔、老为一；使晋宋间有此书，则佛、老不为二。不意老年见此奇特，然后知此书当子瞻意然。予自居颍川十年之间，于此四书复多所删改，以为圣人之言非一读所能了，故每有所得，不敢以前说为定。今日以益老，自以为足矣。欲复质之子瞻，而不可得。言及于此，涕泗而已。」

十二月十一日，子由再题。

眉山苏籀，颍滨文定公之孙也。记其遗言曰：「公为籀讲《老子》数篇，曰：高于孟子二三等矣。又曰：言至道，无如五千文。又曰：公老年作诗，云近存八十一章，注从道老聃门下，人盖老而所造益妙，录录者莫测矣。」

---

吕吉甫作《道德经传》，成以元丰元年表进于朝曰：

「臣恵卿言：臣闻庖丁奏刀，得养生于文恵；轮扁释凿，议读书于齐桓。志之不分，道或有在。臣诚惶诚恐，顿首顿首。

臣窃以大制散于智慧之伪，含生失其性情之初。爰有真人起，明至教，独推原于《道德》。盖祖述于典坟，是以鸡犬相闻，庄周指谓神农；上谷神不死，列子称为黄帝之书。究其微言，中有妙物，唯恍唯惚，视听莫得以见闻。不古不今，迎随孰知其首尾？失之其出，弥远至宝，秘于荆山而莫知悟之。不召自来，珠索之象罔而可得。轩辕、华胥之国，唐尧、姑射之山，皆极至游，遂臻泰定。此书之指，其诣不殊。

曹防师于盖公，而相齐国；孝文传之河上，而为汉宗。仅得浅肤，犹防康阜。夫唯俗学不识道真，徒见其文有异于诗书之迹，莫知其指。乃是皇王之宗，故闻不尚贤则谓遗之野而不收；不贵货则谓弃诸地而不用；谓绝学则无忧等于禽犊；谓绝圣则无法等于鸿荒。不知灵府之间，有若清眸之上，虽留金屑，亦翳神光。故令善恶之两遗，而极冲虚之一致。

兹难情度，冝使智迷，遂以允圣之信言，列于百家之珍说。发兹微学，宜属至神。伏惟皇帝陛下以高真之质，而出应君师；以妙本之余，而形为事业。蛊饬而庶政交举，革当而四方已孚。方将齐心服形，而捐治物养己之累，深根固蒂，以臻长生久视之门，同天下于华胥，见神人于姑射。深造其极，适丁斯时。

臣性维颛防，生足忧患，每思朝彻以解天弢，泛观以考其散殊，又损而期于吻合。维日不足，历年于兹。晚于斯文，忽若有得，即动而静，物芸芸而归根。由浊以清，中冥冥而见晓。遂以其意达之于辞，虽云自安，未知其可。窃谓至人之静鉴，实为学者之元凶。敢用冒闻，以占中否。

龙随章防，固难知其上；天马以智专，因可取于辨道。倘有一言之补，敢辞万死之诛。

所着《老子道德经传》，凡计四册。谨奉表投进，以闻。

臣恵卿诚惶诚恐，顿首顿首。

谨言，元丰元年正月日。

资政殿学士、通议大夫、定州路安抚使、马步军都总管兼知定州军州事及管内劝农使、上轻车都尉、东平县开国伯食邑八百户臣吕恵卿上表。」

【按李彦平先生遗书云：吕吉甫读《庄子》至参万岁而一成，纯遂大悟性命之理，故其《老庄》二解独冠诸家。】

The Master of Farming from Lu’s Mount Ertao said: Since the Qin dynasty, the learning of *xìngmìng* (性命) has not been practiced in the world, and the Dao/Virtue of the *Dàodé* (道德) has long been fractured. The scholars of the age, alas, are obscured by incomplete and one-sided books, daily sinking into the triviality of commentaries and annotations, thus losing their innate *xìngmìng* essence and failing to grasp the great purity of Heaven and Earth or the fundamental principles of the ancients. I lament this deeply.

For the *Dàodé* is what Guan Zhong used to sincerely cultivate the heart and what Laozi employed to sincerely harmonize intentions. Its profound and subtle meanings, its subtle and wondrous principles—there are many of them. They can awaken those obscured by learning, restoring their innate *xìngmìng* essence. Alas, for over a thousand years, they have remained veiled by the trivialities of commentaries. Thus, I have composed this *transmission* to illuminate what was obscured.

Though sages in the lower realms are many, those who recorded their teachings in the spirit of *Dàodé* were not solely Laozi. Rather, he was concise, while Liezi elaborated further, and Zhuangzi elaborated still more. Thus, my exposition combines the detailed accounts of Liezi and Zhuangzi, synthesizing them to ensure the *Dàodé*’s intent is not lost.

---

**Annotations in the text:**
- *Xìngmìng* (性命): "Nature-Life" (a key Neo-Confucian/Daoist concept blending innate human nature and vital essence).
- *Dàodé* (道德): "Dao/Virtue" (here emphasizing the *Dàodéjīng* as a text of moral-philosophical depth).
- *Ellipsis* in "眉山唐庚子西曰..." and "淮海秦观曰...": Quotations from later scholars (Su Shi’s *Mishan Ji* and Qin Guan’s views) inserted as marginal notes.

---

**Mishan’s Master Tang (Su Shi) said:** The world doubts that Laozi’s western journey was merely a pretense, claiming he had compassion, practiced frugality, and yet did not seek to lead the world. If he held to the Dao and wandered among people, what would he reject or pursue? This is utterly mistaken. Precisely because he was without exclusion, he could move anywhere—even among the barbaric and uncivilized. This is why he was Laozi.

**Huaihai’s Qin Guan said:** Ban Gu praised Sima Qian for his *Shiji*, yet criticized him for his "erroneous judgments against the sages." He claimed that in discussing the Great Dao, Sima Qian prioritized Huang-Lao over the Six Classics. Who would have thought that a man of Sima Qian’s vast talent and erudition would err so? In my view, this is not so. Sima Qian truly had insight and expressed it.

Mencius said: *"Rén* (仁) is what it is to be human."

「In summary, the *Dao* is the guiding principle, *virtue/power* (德) is the means to attain it, *rén* (仁) is the essence of humanity, *yì* (義) is its fitting application, and *lǐ* (禮) is its embodiment in form. The heavens are its unified expression.

When *Dao* is embraced, all is harmonious; when divided, it scatters. Indeed, *virtue/power* (德) encompasses *rén* (仁), *yì* (義), and *lǐ* (禮), while *rén* (仁) and *yì* (義) are but partial aspects of *virtue/power* (德). The Huang-Lao school values unity and despises division, thus regarding *Dao* as the foundation and the Six Classics as its practical application. In matters of cosmic harmony, it offers broad strokes; in matters of restraint, it provides meticulous detail. Hence, it emphasizes *rén* (仁), *yì* (義), and *lǐ* (禮) in practice. Sima Qian’s discussion of the Great Dao—prioritizing Huang-Lao over the Six Classics—is not without insight, is it?

He further said: History records that Cui Hao compared himself to Zhang Liang, claiming that his study of antiquity surpassed Zhang’s. But in my view, Cui Hao never matched the erudition of Xun Ji or Jia Yi, how could he dare aspire to Zhang Liang’s level? The former refined his own life with meticulous skill, while the latter applied his remaining talents to governing the world. Once his task was complete, he withdrew into seclusion—truly a follower of the Dao. He observed celestial phenomena and discerned the changes of the times to aid human affairs, mastering superficial details yet ignorant of their roots—truly a follower of the Yin-Yang school. Zhang Liang began his journey in Xiaopi, receiving teachings from the elderly man by the riverbank, and ultimately declared, *"I wish to abandon worldly affairs and follow Chi Song!"*—his methods were clearly rooted in Daoist practice. Yet Cui Hao, proficient in divination and numerology, spoke of the *Yinghuò* (荧惑) entering Qin and the comet extinguishing Jin, as well as the hare appearing in the rear palace and Yao Xing offering his daughter—these claims were most peculiar. When he was banished for advocating the *Zhuang* (庄) and *Lao* (老), he dismissed their teachings as fabrications—thus, his methods were clearly rooted in Yin-Yang alone. This is why they differ.

**Mount Huaihai’s Li Tai (李泰伯) said:** Han Yu remarked that the *Laozi* claimed, *"The Sage Laozi is a disciple of Confucius,"* and the *Buddha* also claimed, *"The Sage Laozi is a disciple of Confucius."* Han Yu, though small in stature, also said, *"My teacher has spoken thus."* The teachings of the *Buddha* I cannot fully examine, but the *Records of the Historian*’s account of the *Laozi*’s biography includes a query about *lǐ* (禮), and history is not entirely trustworthy. Even the *Analects* and the *Confucian Classics* contain errors.

**Mount Song’s Jing Yu (景迂) said:** Fu Xi, King Wen, and the Duke of Zhou praised the *Yijing* (易) after them, yet only the Sage Laozi understood its transformative flexibility, its yielding and extending. He valued softness and emptiness, adaptability and non-attachment, yet his earnestness in articulating these principles was fortunate indeed. This text’s preservation is a blessing.

He further said: Wang Bi’s annotations on the *Laozi*’s *Daodejing* truly captured the Sage’s learning, were they not? His interpretations follow the tradition of Yan Junping. Wang Bi stated that *rén* (仁), *yì* (義), and *lǐ* (禮) cannot function on their own but require *Dao* to apply them. Heaven and earth, myriad things each receive their share—how could this be limited to Laozi alone? All scholars must recognize this truth.

He further said: Wang Bi knew that the finest weapons are not auspicious, yet he treated victory as if it were a funeral rite—this was not Laozi’s teaching but rather an ignorance of the eternal. The Sage’s virtue lies in always aiding people, thus never abandoning them; always aiding things, thus never abandoning things. Only the Master of the Yellow River (河上公) and the ancient texts possessed this understanding. It was fortunate that Fu Yi could discern this truth.

**The Biqiuzi (碧虚子), Chen Jingyuan (陈景元), a disciple of Zhang Hongmeng (张鸿蒙), once compiled the *Daodejing* in the *Cangshu* (藏室) and annotated the *Zhuangzi*’s *Fang* (防) chapter, gathering the refined interpretations of ancient scholars and supplementing them with his teacher’s esoteric traditions. The work was presented to the Emperor during the *Xīníng* (熙宁) era and received imperial commendation. His annotations on the *Nanhua Jing* (南华经) covered chapter meanings and pronunciation, totaling over twenty volumes. These are now included in the *Daozang* (道藏).

**The Xicang (溪堂), Xie Shou (谢寿), in his *Tingji* (亭记), said:** When Confucius spoke of *rén* (仁) as longevity, when Laozi spoke of *"dying yet not perishing"* as longevity, and when the *Buddha* spoke of *"limitless life"*—the three sages, though their words differed, their meanings were the same. The *rén* (仁) of humanity exhausts the *xìng* (性); exhausting the *xìng* (性) means dying yet not perishing; dying yet not perishing means that their longevity could never be measured. Yet those who merely observe the hair turning white, the nails growing long, the return of tears and saliva to water, the warmth of breath to fire, and the movement of limbs to wind—these superficial signs—believe the person has truly died. They fail to see that the essence remains undiminished, never ceasing to exist.

「【As seen in the *Dao Xiang* (道乡) collection of Zou Zhonggong (邹忠公), Zou Hao (浩) notes:】
*"The door of the female* (牝之门)—taking it from my own body—is the nose (则鼻也). The nose is the passage through which breath (息) enters and exits. Gentle and continuous like the flow of the Dao, if one does not overuse it, then one’s breath is deep and profound (则其息深矣). Sun Shu Ao (孙叔敖) had a nose that seemed to breathe with effortless grace (鼻间栩栩然)."»

Zhuangzi said: *"The breath of the True Person (真人之息) surpasses that of the common person (以踵众人之息). The common person, when bent and yielding (屈服者), breathes with a rasping throat (其嗌言若哇); the one with deep desires (其嗜欲深者) has shallow vital essence (其天机浅)."*

The *Su Wen* (素问) states: *"Without the processes of ascent and descent (升降), there would be no growth, flourishing, aging, or death (非升降则无以生长化收藏). Without the processes of ebb and flow (出入), there would be no growth, flourishing, aging, or decline (非出入则无以生长壮老). Ascent and descent, ebb and flow—without vessels (器), these would not exist (升降出入无器不有). To value the preservation of constancy (常守) is to understand this, and only then does one know the Way of the Grain Spirit (谷神) in its eternal vitality (不死)."*

Again, it says: *"Empty your mind (虚其心), and your belly will naturally fill; weaken your will (弱其志), and your bones will naturally strengthen."*

---

「【As seen in the *Dao Xiang* (道乡) collection】 Zou Bo (邵伯温) said: Master Kangjie (康节先公) regarded Laozi’s *Dao De Jing* (老子) as the embodiment of the *Dao* (知易之体) and Mencius’ teachings as its application (知易之用). In his discussions on the *Zhongzi* (中子) text, he acknowledged the Buddha as a Sage of the Western lands (西方之圣人) without hesitation (不以为过).»

Western Pond (西塘) Zheng Xia (郑侠) said: *"I have heard that Laozi said: ‘Knowing contentment brings no disgrace; knowing cessation brings no peril.’ The *Yijing* (易) states: ‘The word *kàng* (亢) means knowing advance yet not knowing retreat, knowing preservation yet not knowing perishing, knowing gain yet not knowing loss, knowing growth yet not knowing decline. Only those who know advance and retreat, preservation and perishing, gain and loss without straying from the Dao (正)—only these are the *Sages* (圣人).’ Yet then, how can the principles of advance, retreat, preservation, perishing, gain, and loss not be harmoniously aligned? Why is it so difficult to know retreat and perishing, contentment and cessation, yet why do the Sages so earnestly praise and marvel at them?*

*"It is all one (是皆一也). Advance and retreat have their Dao (道), so advance does not falter and retreat is not difficult. Preservation and perishing have their Dao, so preservation does not bring joy and perishing does not bring sorrow. Advance, retreat, preservation, and perishing all return to the Dao: when cessation (止) is appropriate, one ceases; when action (行) is appropriate, one acts. Who does not see their harmonious alignment?*

*"Again, it says: The superior person, hearing the Dao, strives diligently to practice it (勤而行之). The middle person, hearing the Dao, finds it like a lingering presence—sometimes there, sometimes gone (若存若亡). The inferior person, hearing the Dao, laughs at it (大笑之). To not laugh is to not grasp the Dao (不笑不足以为道).*

*"Again, it says: The Dao is vast (惟道大), and thus it seems like both the unworthy and the worthy are encompassed within it (似不肖若肖). If one strives to emulate it (乆矣), yet only in a trivial way (其细也)—then, to emulate it is to emulate it."*

The nourishment of the common is small, for this reason.
The customs of the world nourish only the petty—thus the great are deemed unworthy.
These are all unfit to converse with about the Dao.

Again, it says: The Dao is vast, yet things are small—
how people scurry and yet perish!
The petty are burdened by things.

The Original (元) is the source of all goodness, reaching to the great.
To attain it yet not know its greatness is to align with the Dao,
yet differ not from the vulgar.
Thus the world calls my Dao great, yet unworthy—
for they scrutinize and compare, while I alone remain tranquil,
deeming the world insufficient.
Hence, my difference.
Thus I achieve greatness, possessing it without worldly measure.
Therefore, the vulgar and the inferior hear it and laugh—
only then may one guard against the Dao.
If one does not laugh, it is not the Dao.

Again, it says: Water benefits all things yet does not contend.
It dwells where others abhor—
thus it is guarded by the Dao.
Yet if it stagnates, it becomes foul and impure,
unfit even for washing one’s feet—
this is self-inflicted, not guarded against evil.

Those who seek to restore their nature take this as a mirror:
know that the clear and bright comes from one’s true nature,
while the turbid and chaotic arises from external influences.
Remove the stagnant and confused—
then clarity and brightness reside within.
Then, even if waves surge and mud stirs,
they cannot alter me—
for they cannot transform me.
Yet they gradually become clear and pure,
though none perceive it.
I act, yet none know my force.

Again, it says: Water’s nature is clear,
yet it emerges from the earth—
thus it is so turbid.
Slow it, and it becomes clear enough to reflect hair.
Human discernment is bright,
yet it arises from things—
thus it is so confused.
Slow it, and it shines like the sun and moon.
Before, turbid water becomes clear when slowed;
before, confused clarity returns when still.
Thus, the turbid and chaotic are always imposed from without,
while the clear and bright are inherent within.

Heaven and Earth unite to descend sweet dew—
no one commands it, yet it distributes evenly.
Rain and dew, though minute in the cosmos,
are yet evenly spread.
How much more so the human spirit!

Again, it says: Before the Three Dynasties,
there were no teachings of Confucius, Mencius, Laozi, or the Buddha.
The Sage encountered the Emperor, the Emperor the King,
and the King declined—
only then did the Three Teachings emerge,
merely to rescue the world from drowning.
I once composed *The Great Celebration Resident’s Preface*, saying:
The Resident’s foundation is Confucianism, with Confucius as its master,
yet I attained Laozi’s teachings to illuminate it.
[As seen in *Western Pond Collection*]
Leaf Dream said: The *Book of Changes* was abridged from Yao and Shun,
yet only reached Fu Xi, Shen Nong, Huang Di, and then Yao and Shun.
Thus, those before Yao were real—
the Six Classics remain, yet their origins are untold.
If one examines it with the heart, its saying is:
*The Book of Changes* requires no thought, no action—
still and unmoving, yet responsive, it communicates with all under Heaven.
Who but the most divine could attain this?
Only then does one know that Fu Xi, Shen Nong, Huang Di,
down to Yao and Shun, all transmitted this—
retreat to Laozi’s teachings, and nothing differs from *The Changes*.

Again, it says: The *Analects* records that Confucius compared himself to Laozi and Peng Zu.
Mencius followed Confucius—
Mencius, in Confucianism, did not diminish even by a hair.
His rejection of Yang Zhu and Mo Di,
his opposition to ritual excess like Qin and Qin’s tyranny,
exceeded even Jie and Zhou—
yet he did not reach Laozi’s depth.
Only when he spoke of *exhausting the heart, knowing nature, reaching to fate*
did Laozi’s intent become profound.
Thus, Laozi’s teachings were never abandoned by Confucius and Mencius.

Again, it says: Laozi’s teachings differ from Confucius’
only in their rectifying language,
yet they align with *The Changes*.
After Laozi, several hundred years passed,
and the Buddha emerged—
his words were even more remote and profound,
yet in essence, they reached the same truth as Laozi,
almost forming a complementary pair.
[As seen in *Scriptural Commentaries*]
Leaf Dream said: Laozi’s theory of *qi* advocates concentrating *qi* to achieve softness like an infant.
Mencius’ theory of *qi* speaks of the utmost greatness and rigidity—
nurturing it without harm, filling Heaven and Earth.
These two seem opposite,
yet from Laozi’s perspective, Mencius is abandoned,
and from Mencius’, Laozi is.
But in my view, the two are not truly opposed.
Scattered *qi* contends with things, becoming rigid;
concentrated *qi* turns inward, becoming soft.
Rigidity cannot overcome rigidity—
only softness can conquer rigidity.
Thus, concentrating *qi* is the means to achieve straightness.
Straightness, nurtured without external harm,
is how one maintains resolve without violence—
as Zengzi guarded his boundaries.
Yet when restraint accumulates to excess,
it returns to guarding.
Thus, straightness nurtured is the means to achieve softness.
Truly, the Dao’s ultimate essence is undivided.
[As seen in *Stone Forest Beneath the Rocks*]

Dong Si Jing said: Laozi’s Dao takes natural purity and non-action as its foundation,
using clarity and responsiveness without obstruction as its function,
practicing benevolence, frugality, humility, and non-contention as its conduct,
and opening the way for others without desiring to lead as its governance.
In governing self and others, it is supreme—
if applied, the governance of ancient times could be restored.
Yet in his era, the age favored artifice over simplicity,
and the pure and unadorned had vanished.
Laozi upheld the pristine Dao,
yet his time resisted him—
he could not be positioned to transform the world,
knowing the times were contrary.
Yet he never let the Dao perish without transmission—
thus, his teachings endured, though obscured,
and lingered in the strange customs of the Western lands.
His pity for his time and concern for posterity—
how profound!
Fortunately, this tradition did not vanish—
thus, in the Western Pass, I awaited the carriage in Donglu,
and the *Dragon’s Record* remains.

The Riverbank Master, Huangshi Gong, Le Su Gong,
and their disciples—
they could truly examine and embody the Dao.
If they confined its practice to themselves,
their world would be that of the Three Sovereigns.
Yet when the Dao was transmitted,
Emperor Gaozu practiced it as a gentleman,
Liu Bang relied on it as a minister,
and Cao Shen used it as a chancellor.
Truly, they restored a single lineage after the harshness of Qin—
ah, another verification!

Yet if one were to advance further still—
to emulate the Sage of the Primordial Age,
then one would truly be the Sage of the Primordial Age.

Some may see this text’s words and meaning,
vast and inclusive, yet fail to grasp
the moment of utmost subtlety and stillness,
the depth of profound emptiness and settled virtue—
the foundation for all governance and affairs.
Instead, they merely toil in dull obscurity,
habitually falling into complacency and neglect,
until they are criticized for empty detachment,
useless in practice.

Thus, the uninitiated may dismiss it—
alas, what a pity!

Some say its teachings are veiled in metaphor and hidden precepts,
so the opening chapter speaks of *wu* and *you*,
of the Two Dan, of divine *qi* and the interplay of water and fire.
To empty the heart and fill the belly—
this is the method of refining lead.
To master warfare and battle—
this is the art of extracting silver.
The character *chong* (冲), with water and center radicals,
symbolizes the true unity within the *qi*.
Thirty spokes converge at a single hub—
this illustrates the five *zang* organs,
each bearing the six *qi*’s essence,
and the correspondence to the moon’s phases and fire’s numbers.
Such meanings are now omitted—
why? Because nature must be self-realized,
while techniques require a master’s guidance.
If one truly embodies the precepts,
the precepts will already be complete within them—
then oral transmission, meticulous in detail,
leaves no room for doubt.
How could mere scribbles on paper suffice?

Moreover, this text upholds the essence of *Dao* and *virtue*,
expounding the Way of non-action,
transcending all forms, yet penetrating their core—
how could it tolerate the intrusion of trivial doctrines,
mixed with petty speculations?

Bai Juyi remarked:
*"The Yellow Emperor’s Five Thousand Words*
*speak neither of medicine nor immortality,*
*nor of ascending to heaven in broad daylight—*
*this is also a matter of debate."*

The Master of Shu, surnamed Ruo, styled himself "Laiyuanzi." During the Shaoxing era, he composed *An Exegesis of Laozi*, beginning with:

*"According to the Records of the Historian, Laozi served as the Keeper of the Archives. As the Zhou declined, he departed. Guan Yinzi [Emperor Jing of Zhou] said, ‘Master, you are about to withdraw—please write a book for me.’ Thus, he wrote the *Daodejing* in two sections, over five thousand words on the Way and virtue, before leaving. No one knows his ultimate fate. Because the Records mention ‘upper’ and ‘lower’ sections, later scholars divided it accordingly—upper on the Way, lower on virtue. But logically, the Way and virtue are inseparable; this division is mere vulgar speculation. I have removed it.

Moreover, some unknown person arbitrarily split the original text into eighty-one chapters, focusing only on diagrams, symbols, and yang numbers—all frivolous speculations offering no benefit to humanity. I have also discarded these.
