Many commentators exist, yet though they may grasp the principles, they fail in application; though eloquent in theory, they lack method. For instance, they claim *‘pìn’* (牝) means ‘mouth and nose’—this is incorrect in principle. They say *‘sǐ’* (死) refers to *nirvana*—this is incorrect in fact. Such reckless annotations, scattering words without foundation, stem from their failure to attain the Dao.

Confucius sought the Dao, yet since the Dao is formless, he grounded himself in virtue, making virtue the foundation. Once the foundation is established, the Dao arises naturally. How could one neglect virtue while pursuing the Dao? This is akin to passing a door without entering the chamber.

Many conflate technique with the Dao, mistaking filth for essence, dullness for purity, speed and slowness for nature, stillness for non-action, and abstinence from flavors for tranquility. They deem coming and going as natural, celibacy as purity—such people are unworthy of discussing the supreme Dao. As *Baopuzi* states in Volume Eight: *‘Though the Five Thousand Words originated with Laozi, they are but broad and superficial discussions. Even if one claims to understand its beginning and end, what evidence do they offer? These people speak only of chamber arts and alchemical elixirs, clinging to contrivance as their practice, their minds fixated on profit, lust, and measuring the Dao of non-action by worldly desires. Thus, they fail to grasp its essence—how much less can those beneath them?’*

Again, some elevate reckless action as the highest virtue, placing moral cultivation as secondary. To conceal one’s body, alter one’s form, transform appearances, and dwell in seclusion is deemed demonic. Confucius did not speak of supernatural feats or chaotic forces, for they yield no benefit. Laozi’s teachings on longevity and vision sought to advance the Dao, not cling to it exclusively. Even immortals dwelling on the Three Mountains remain earthbound; riding the *luán* (鸾) to court the Emperor does not elevate them beyond heaven. They linger between heaven and earth, enduring for ten thousand years—yet if they do not advance the Dao, what distinguishes them from the *dàchūn* (大椿, the great cypress)? This is not mere debate; it is a warning against fixating on alchemical techniques while neglecting the Dao.

Yan Gushan (Jiang Mao) remarked:
*"The Dao is elusive—how can it be spoken of? What we call the Dao is but a name; in truth, it is not the Dao itself. The Five Classics speak of it broadly, while Laozi, Lie Yukou, and Zhuangzi expound it in detail. Though their approaches differ, both elucidate the fundamental source of the Dao.*

*Someone asked: ‘Did Laozi compose the *Daodejing* as a single work, or was it compiled later?’ I replied: ‘This cannot be determined.’*

*I once encountered an ancient text of the *Laozi* in a private collection, differing slightly from the current version but sharing the same meaning. Only the order of passages varied, leading me to suspect later scholars fragmented it.*

*The Dao encompasses all things, yet the Five Thousand Words record only what can be spoken of as the Dao. To separate it into ‘Way’ and ‘virtue’ seems unjustified."*

I said: *"Though the terms differ, the essence is the same. Why distinguish between ‘Dao’ and ‘virtue’?"*

He replied: *"How can you say that? I have read the Five Classics and the Hundred Schools—whenever they speak of ‘Dao’ and ‘virtue,’ they distinguish between greater and lesser, earlier and later. This cannot be generalized without examination."*

I said: *"Zhuangzi lamented that later scholars, though skilled in parsing the Dao into trivialities, failed to grasp its weight. Do you not fear repeating their mistake?"*

He said: *"Let me hear your view."*

I replied: *"The Dao is formless—‘without’ is the Dao. Virtue arises from what one attains. The Dao is the path all share; virtue is what each cultivates for themselves. Take water as an analogy: the vastness of lakes and seas differs from the stagnation of a well, yet both are water. The torrent of rivers contrasts with the sluggishness of ditches, yet both are water. Water is the Dao—‘without’ is its nature. Lakes, seas, wells, rivers, and ditches each manifest it differently. This is how the real and the named diverge yet remain one.

*Scholars who study the Dao must refine their minds, attune their spirits, and balance excess and deficiency until self-sufficiency is achieved. Then virtue is complete, and standing is secure. Advancing virtue until it encompasses the Dao returns one to the original state.

Thus, the Dao is not excessive beyond virtue; virtue is not deficient without the Dao. When virtue is perfected, the Dao becomes hidden. The sage embodies the complete Dao; the virtuous person manifests the complete virtue. This is their distinction.*

*You ask: ‘The Dao is subtle and formless—how can virtue be seen?’*

*The Dao has no fixed form; virtue has a manifest nature—its success or failure is evident to mind and eye. How can it be unseen?*

*The Dao is without constant name; virtue has a defined substance. When separated, virtue can be named, while the Dao cannot. Hence, Laozi’s teachings, arising amid the fluctuations of his time, all point to the supreme truth. Though his words vary—beginning and end, yin and yang—they converge in their ultimate meaning. What need is there for such distinctions?*

*[Question on Virtue]*

*Again, he said: ‘What arises in the heart is inexhaustible; thus, what is commanded by the body never ceases. Death and rebirth, life and death—beginning and end cycle endlessly, like an unending wheel. Laozi spoke of birth and death: ten attain life, ten attain death, and ten perish in motion. Three threes make nine—speaking in tens, birth and death are one. What does this mean? It refers to those who grasp the vital breath.*

*The scholar who aspires to the Dao must cultivate the body to await it, act straight yet adapt flexibly, and endure to fulfill their destiny."*

「Though one may inquire thus, **[Question on Vital Breath]** he further said: ‘Some ask why Laozi, Lord Lieh, Zhuangzi, and Mencius—all ancient masters who attained the Dao—each sought to persuade later generations with their teachings, yet contradicted one another so sharply. Laozi said, *“I have a great affliction: my body.”* Lord Lieh replied, *“The inward observer takes trust from the body.”* Zhuangzi declared, *“My body is not truly mine.”* And Mencius asserted, *“All things are complete within me; by reflecting inward and being sincere, no greater joy exists.”* Some reject the body as an affliction, while others prize its completeness and take what is sufficient. Is this not mutual contradiction?

I say: You have not pondered deeply. You grasp their words but not the reasons behind them. The four limbs, hundred bones, five viscera, and six entrails—all are present and complete; this is called the *body* (形). Sight, hearing, speech, appearance, thought, and innate nature—these are also called the *body*. Yet though the name is one, the means by which they speak of the body differ. Laozi and Zhuangzi focus on its *form* (體), while Lord Lieh and Mencius emphasize its *nature* (性). When speaking of *form*, the four limbs, hundred bones, five viscera, and six entrails are what they mean—if they exist, there is affliction; if not, there is no affliction. Hence, they cannot allow it to exist. But what they call *non-existence* is not annihilation; rather, it means that all movements, words, and actions are imperceptible, and only *nature* remains. When speaking of *nature*, sight, hearing, speech, appearance, thought, and the unified principle—all are complete within the self. If one inwardly observes them, they can take what is sufficient: lofty yet grounded, vast yet intimate, discerning all without deficiency. Who could say such completeness is absent from me? Who could say such sufficiency is lacking in the body? Thus, Laozi and Zhuangzi’s teaching on the body seeks to make it non-existent, while Lord Lieh and Mencius’s teaching seeks to make it observed.

**[Question on Vital Breath]** He further said: ‘Some ask, what is *breathing at rest* (息)? I reply: It is the harmonious flow of yin and yang, balancing left and right, following the *zi* and *wu* cycles of waxing and waning. Its movement is like an unceasing wheel, its rotation like an endless loop—it intermingles with primordial *qi* (元氣), never resting day or night. This is what Laozi and Lord Lieh called *penetrating qi* (沖氣). If one knows how to guard *breathing at rest*, they know how to nurture *qi*. If they know how to nurture *qi*, they know how to enter the Dao. If they know how to enter the Dao, they can embrace the One and attain *chan* (禪) and *ding* (定), fixed in their singular purpose without deviation. Laozi said, *“Continuous, as if something exists, yet used without exertion.”* If one seeks this and understands it, they will gain insight.

**[Question on Qi]** He further said: ‘Confucius taught, *“Have no fixed intentions; have no self.”* Laozi said, *“When I have no body, what affliction can I have?”* Master Zhìchán (瞿昙) said, *“No eyes, no ears, no nose, no tongue, no body, no self.”* If all these exist, how can one make them non-existent? Truly, *non-existence* is the convergence of all virtues and the foundation of all teachings. If one can externally still all external influences and internally rest the mind in *non-existence*, they will align with the Dao. Its convergence is singular—how different is it from what is called *sitting and forgetting* (坐忘) or *facing the wall* (面壁)?

The *Clamber* (袤) character is Zhòng Zhǎng (仲長), a man from Sānqú (三衢). His self-given title was Yán Gǔshān (嚴谷山人). He cultivated his natural essence in his garden retreat and used his learning to instruct students. During the reign of Emperor Guāng (光廟), when he was a scholar-official in the Palace Academy, officials sought his writings on *Laozi’s Easy Classic* (老子通言). His response was: *“What is the value of Laozi’s teachings? He grasped the fundamental principles and could govern all things through the Dao—this is his strength. Though he prized *non-being* over *being*, and warned against worldly speech, it was not his weakness. Hence, the Emperor who followed Laozi’s teachings was Emperor Hán Wén (漢文帝), who ruled with great success. His governance largely entailed purifying the heart, reducing desires, and maintaining profound silence and simplicity to nurture the realm. This was not to neglect all affairs; rather, his foundation was firm, and his legacy enduring, because he understood its essence and applied its strengths.

As for the Western Jin (西晉), they heard his words but took *non-action* (無為) as mere governance without understanding its true nature. They thought it meant abandoning laws and measures, passively doing nothing, and expecting the realm to govern itself. Thus, the state’s principles collapsed, and chaos ensued. Wang Tóng (王通) remarked, *“The Jin’s virtue was in emptiness and stillness, yet their realm fell into disorder—this is not Laozi’s fault. They failed to grasp its essence and misapplied its strengths.”* My humble hope is that Your Majesty adopts the Dao’s fundamental principles as a model, follows the Han’s governance, and avoids the Jin’s neglect of affairs. Then, Laozi’s profound words and marvelous Dao will all be within Your Majesty’s application.

He also wrote *The Record of Virtue in the Palace Academy* (潛藩盛德錄), where one passage states: *“I once had the honor to converse with officials. Whenever the Dao was discussed, I took the *Easy Classic’s* (老子) passages on *Dao-instrument* (道器) and Confucius’s *lower learning, higher attainment* (下學上達) to compare and illustrate. The *Dao-instrument* and the *learning-attainment* can be grasped from both perspectives—like firewood generating flames, the lower form (下形) is necessary to manifest the higher Dao (上形之道). Without the lower instrument (下器), one cannot attain the higher learning (上學). Wood, though coarse, contains its own vitality—if it can produce flames, it can ascend. The *Six Classics* (六經) and the *Mencius* (論孟) speak much of *instrument* and little of *Dao*—they are like storing wood to seek flames. Laozi and Zhuangzi’s texts speak much of *Dao* and little of *being*—they are like saying the *Six Classics* have already spoken of the wood, so no need to repeat.”*

The Confucian scholars, in their pursuit of the Dao, have the *Six Classics*. It seems they need no aid from Laozi and Zhuangzi. Yet Laozi and Zhuangzi’s texts delve deeply into guarding and guiding the Dao, offering particularly precise and essential insights. If one can widely embrace these teachings, they will greatly benefit. One must not, however, be like the Jin scholars, who spoke of emptiness and stillness yet abandoned ritual, music, penal laws, and governance, claiming the realm could self-regulate. Such folly is unheard of—can there be flames without firewood?

**[Again]** He further said: *“Now, Daoists who study Laozi’s teachings abandon the Dao’s essence, speaking only of formulas, medicines, prayers, and exorcisms—how far they have strayed from the original intent! Only the Tang poet Bái Jùyì (白居易) could clarify this, as his verse states: ‘How much more so the *Dao-instrument* (道器) of the Yellow Emperor (黃元皇帝)—Laozi’s *Five Thousand Words*—speaks not of medicine, not of immortality, not of the sun ascending in white light to the heavens.’”*
The *Yellow Emperor* (黃元皇帝) refers to Laozi.

The Daoist tradition takes Laozi as its teaching ancestor, yet beyond the eighty-one chapters on purity, simplicity, and few desires, there is not a single word concerning how he might have fabricated empty claims about medicines, immortals, or prayers for exorcism and warding off evil. Respectfully heeding the sage’s admonition, I affirm that the full recitation of Bai Juyi’s poem only deepens my admiration for his understanding of Laozi’s essential truths.

**[Also found in this collection]**

Yongjia’s Zheng Bo-xiong (景望) said: The Master applied the Yellow Emperor’s teachings alongside Cao Can’s governance in Qi and was praised for it, yet scholars of the Confucian school often disparage the Yellow Emperor’s words. Why? I have often shut myself away, sitting in silence all day, careful in my movements, sparing in tastes, and keeping my heart serene and my *qi* balanced—no illness has ever intruded. I have not sought to preserve what is already complete nor dispersed what is lacking; I have not craved what I do not need. Thus, those who age and die find peace in simplicity. If this principle is applied universally, why should only the hundred-li realm of Qi benefit? The methods of Confucius and Mencius—how could they be anything but this? Yet why do scholars disparage the Yellow Emperor’s words? Truly, what the Daoists today speak of as "purity" is nothing but delusion beyond this.

**[As recorded in Master Zheng’s *Steady Words*]**

Xue Hui’s disciple, Xue Cai, compiled *Laozi’s Collected Explanations*, and High Master Gao Shusi wrote the preface. Laozi was born in Meng, and his writings speak of the Dao’s emptiness. Since the Han, none have traced its origins. Only the people of Liang have glimpsed a partial truth, and from this arose the later interpretations. Thus, the book of Liang’s people—Mr. Xue, the Censor—first pondered the Dao’s source, seeking harmony between heaven and humanity, reconciling all doctrines with measure. Laozi’s Way then shone forth in brilliant clarity. The book was completed in the ninth year of Jiaqing (嘉靖, 1529), in the year of the Tiger (庚寅). The preface states:

*"The Dao’s essence converges in unity, yet its expressions vary. The sages’ teachings align in sentiment but differ in words. In ancient times, Confucius’ disciples spoke of the Dao of heaven, and thereafter, none could hear it again. Yet the *Yijing*’s six dragons—beginning with ‘Do Not Use’ and ending with ‘Exhausted and Overreaching’—never reveal fortune or misfortune to people. When the nine lines appear, the six dragons have no head, and Confucius praised it: ‘The nine lines manifest the heavenly virtue—it cannot be the leader.’ The *Qian* hexagram’s nine lines reveal the heavenly pattern: firm yet yielding, the Dao of heaven. How does this differ from Laozi? Thus, I say: My understanding of Laozi is like seeing a dragon.*

*The scholar’s greatest flaw is ignorance of the unity between heaven and humanity. Ignorance of this unity makes their discussions of the sages shallow. From ancient times, who has surpassed Laozi in speaking of *ren* (仁), *yi* (义), *li* (礼), and *yue* (乐)? Yet he did not ignore heaven. Who has surpassed Confucius in speaking of *yin-yang* and *yang-yin*? Yet he did not ignore humanity. The sages taught differently, and scholars believed what they spoke; they doubted what they did not. Thus, those who study Laozi disparage Confucianism, and Confucians disparage Laozi. Knowing humanity but not heaven leads to folly; knowing heaven but not humanity leads to deception. How can one judge the sages with such partiality?

*Some say Laozi’s book is about nurturing life. The sage’s Way—internally nourishing the body to ensure longevity and externally ordering affairs to bring harmony—is the same for all sages. Their methods are of one thread. The authors are long gone; their echoes and bones have decayed. Yet their spirit lives on in their words. Xue’s *Laozi’s Collected Explanations* in two volumes is kept at home; I have copied it here.*

*Again, it is said: The Censor Xue, having secluded himself in Meng for a decade, devoted himself to learning. In the year of the Tiger (庚寅), he annotated *Laozi*, titling it *Collected Explanations*. I wrote this preface, and the book was printed. The Master’s intentions were not yet fully realized, so he revised it again in the year of the Hare (丁酉), and it was completed. Upon reading it, I found his interpretations profound, aligning with the Great Middle. Since the Han, no one has understood *Laozi* as decisively as this. Yet the world has no one to recognize him. Who, then, can understand the Master’s annotations?*

*The preface continues: When I was young, I read *Laozi* and thought it was merely the words of an ancient emperor or a noble, speaking of lofty matters beyond common use. Truly, if one secretly applies it, it suffices to cultivate governance and bring order. As I read further, I saw that its teachings harmonize with the sages’ Way without contradiction, applicable to the world. Yet its phrasing is often exaggerated, making it difficult to grasp. The unwise, desiring to believe, resort to supernatural explanations, and thus it was dismissed by Confucians as a book on longevity. His disciples clung to it, causing its words to fade into obscurity. The ancient sages all studied both self-cultivation and governance—some saw it deeply, others superficially, some purely, others mixed. Why must we abandon it entirely, making the world avoid speaking of *Laozi*? His book nearly vanished.*

*I harbored this thought until I met the Chancellor of Wu City, Master Wang Chunfu (王纯父). I asked him: Confucius and Laozi differed in speech—why? Master Wang replied: They both lived in the late Zhou, witnessing the flourishing of culture and eager to return to antiquity. Their words were passionate, and passion often leads to excess. Confucius himself said: ‘If a person lacks *ren*, how can there be *li*? *Li*! *Li*! Is it not about jade and silk?’—meaning that *li* without *ren* is mere empty ritual.*

*Thus, the differences between them are clear.*

The Chancellor of Wu City then said: *"Let us be frugal, and Laozi would reply: ‘Ritual is the thinnest veneer of loyalty and trust, yet the root of chaos. Thus, his words are so vehement that they drift far from Confucius’ measured speech. Therefore, the Master’s teachings are chanted as law for ten thousand generations, while Laozi’s is discarded as something else entirely.*

I kept his words hidden until later, when I served in Shanxi and consulted Master Cao Defang, the Prefect of the Capital. He said: *‘The sages speak of the Dao—just as people name the sky. The Chinese call it *Tian*, while the Xiongnu call it *Chenglei*. Is there truly a difference? Only their words vary. The sky does not know itself, yet people insist on naming it and debating its nature. Thus, the true scholar seeks only the Dao; all else may be forgotten.’*

Ah, how profound his words were! Both these masters delved deeply into Laozi’s thought. Now, examining the *Kaogong* commentary, I find it aligns with the *Yi*’s *Great Transmission*: *‘Though all paths lead to the same end, their ways diverge; though all thoughts converge, their expressions vary. Scholars, accustomed to hearing only differences, fail to see the unity beneath them. They rush to condemn what they do not understand—how could it be just Laozi alone who suffers this fate? Alas, it is difficult to speak of!*

If such a person arises in a hundred generations, they will know it themselves. What need have I for praise or blame? I record these two masters’ words and preface them with this account.*

Li Hongfu engraved Zi You’s commentary in Jinling, and inscribed beneath it:

*‘To eat until full—this is one thing. Southerners eat rice and find it sweet; northerners eat millet and find it sweet. Though one is south and one north, they do not envy each other. Yet if they were to exchange places, they would not reject it either. The Dao in Confucius and Laozi is like rice and millet to the south and north—though one may not envy the other, can it truly be discarded?’*"*

「What then? When one is truly sated, each finds their fill, but when one is truly hungry, there is no rejection. Indeed, I once studied in the north and ate at my host’s home. At first, I knew not its taste.

The cold was severe, and heavy snow fell for three days, cutting off all grain. Seven days passed in hunger, cold, and exhaustion—I gazed upon my host’s table with longing. My host took pity on me, cooked millet, and fed me. I devoured it without pause, unable to discern its nature.

Only after clearing the table did he ask, “Is this not rice and wheat?” I replied, “What strange delight is this?” My host laughed, “This is just millet and barley—no different from what you had before. It is only because you were so hungry that it seemed so fine, and only because it was so fine that you ate so much.”

Now, when you look back, you no longer imagine it as rice and wheat nor as millet and barley. I heard this tale and sighed deeply. If my understanding of the Dao were as desperate as my hunger for food, would Confucius and Laozi hesitate to choose me?

From that moment, I vowed to study the Dao tirelessly, day and night, without rest or food. Later, I obtained *Zi You’s* commentary on *Laozi’s* *Dao De Jing*, as well as *Jiao Ruo’s* commentary—many have expounded on *Laozi*, yet *Zi You* stands supreme.

*Zi You* cited the *Analects* of *Zhu Xi* (宋自明道以后), saying, *“Before the emotions of joy, anger, sorrow, and delight arise, they are called ‘the Middle.’ The Middle before they arise is the mystery of all things.”* Ever since *Zhu Xi*’s *Illumination of the Dao* (明道), each generation has passed down this teaching, instructing their disciples to observe the *qi* of their nature and ask, *“What is its state?”*

*Zi You* alone grasped the subtle meaning within the fragmented texts and broken passages, thus proving his mastery in elucidating *Laozi’s* profound teachings. His five thousand words shine forth like a bright sun—students cannot bear to part with them even for a day.

His commentary reveals the Dao in its entirety, and his intent is fully aligned with *Laozi’s*. He entrusted his meaning to *Zi Zhan*, who also understood it perfectly. Now, five hundred years have passed since *Zi You*, yet here I find this rare and extraordinary work again. Alas, only the truly hungry can attain such wisdom!

In the twelfth month of winter, the second year of *Wàn Lì* (萬曆), *Hongfu* inscribed this preface.

*Master Li Hongfu*, having already engraved *Zi You’s* commentary on *Laozi*, a year later composed his own two-volume commentary. He wrote in the preface:

*“I once read *Han Fei’s* commentary on *Laozi*—though it was not entirely without merit, I could not help but pity him. *Han Fei*, with all his talent, was ultimately executed in *Qin*—yet even in his flawed exposition of *Laozi*, was he not a man of great effort? How could it be said that he accomplished nothing?”*」

Now, the one employs softness and weakness while the other, strength and firmness—this one is bold in daring while that one is bold in not daring. The contrast between the square and the round, ice and fire, is already clear. Yet to claim that *Dao/virtue* and *de* (power) are the ancestral traditions of *Shen Buhai* and *Han Fei*—how can that be?

*Sū Zhīzhān* sought it but could not find it, so he forced an interpretation, saying:

*"The learning of Laozi weighs heavily on non-action and lightly on governing the world and the state. Thus, benevolence is insufficient for love, and ritual is insufficient for reverence. Han Fei obtained the means by which one lightly regards governing the world, and so he became ruthless, harsh, and merciless without hesitation. Alas! If this is so, then how can it be said that one cannot govern the world and the state with it?

Was Laozi’s learning truly like this? Laozi was not one who could govern but did not govern; rather, he was one who did not govern *because* he governed. Thus, those who truly cherish their own bodies do not govern their bodies, and those who truly cherish the world do not govern the world. All that the ancient sage-kings called benevolence, righteousness, ritual, and music were not the means by which they governed. How much less so all the systems of punishments, names, laws, and stratagems!

Therefore, his writings speak only of *Dao/virtue* and *de* (power) and do not speak of benevolence and righteousness—for even if benevolence were without action, it would still not be entirely without action, and righteousness, when put into practice, would be even more so. Thus, his *Way* is:

To take emptiness as the norm,
To take action as the guiding principle,
To take gentle submission and non-contention as the sovereign of all things,
To take delight in war and killing as a necessity,
To regard victory as not beautiful,
To regard retreat as advancement,
To regard defeat as achievement,
To regard fortune as misfortune,
To regard gain as loss,
To regard ignorance as wisdom,
To regard desirelessness as desire,
To regard namelessness as name—
Who would say that non-action is insufficient for governing the world?

The world has yet to know the benefits of non-action.

But then, why does *Han Fei* love it? He says:

*"To follow and achieve is the governance of the emperor-king.
To oppose and yet endure is the art of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi.
To follow and achieve is to govern with a heart that does not endure, enacting policies that do not endure—
Thus, when one follows events with benevolence and applies policies with flexibility, only then can one achieve universal harmony without coercion. Its effects cannot be demanded overnight.

To oppose and yet endure is to make the desirable unseen—
Thus, there is no governance that is not achieved, yet no heart can be discerned in its workings;
No subject is unsettled, yet no virtue can be praised in it—
Such is all there is to it.

This is the most easy, most subtle *Way*, yet it is what all those who seek quick results and profit revere.
But those who seek profit and results wish to emulate it and yet cannot—
Thus, they cannot endure desirelessness but endure killing;
They cannot endure self-restraint but endure inflicting it on others;
They cannot endure endurance but endure not enduring.

Scholars, failing to discern its source, doubt its origin and say, ‘The calamity of *Dao/virtue* is that it later became *Shen Buhai* and *Han Fei*.’

If this is so, then indeed *Dao/virtue* later became *Shen Buhai* and *Han Fei*.
Yet why not say that the calamity of benevolence and righteousness was fratricide and regicide?

Many schools of learning have existed since antiquity—
After one or two transmissions, they are lost, and their harm is incalculable. How few are there?

Alone, Laozi?

From this perspective, it can be said that *Shen Buhai* and *Han Fei* originally followed the intent of *Dao/virtue*—how can this not be granted?

My nature is rigid, and my *qi* suffers from excessive firmness, unable to restrain itself.
I enjoy reading *Han Fei’s* works, yet I dare not again let the *Dao/virtue* tradition bring about calamity.
But because of *Dao/virtue*, I deeply appreciate it and have composed this commentary and preface to reflect on my own words regarding *Dao/virtue*.

The gentleman’s name was *Li Zàifáng*, a native of *Wēnlíng*. He held office as the Prefect of *Yà’ān* before retiring.
*Laozi’s* *Yì* (Wing) Volume Three.
*Laozi’s* *Yì* (Wing) *Kǎo Yì* (Examination of Differences)."

「Among the ancient books that have come down to the ages and been altered by men, there are many—but none more so than Laozi. In the Kaiyuan annotation, it is explicitly stated that I alone differ from others in valuing "seeking food from the mother" (i.e., nourishment from the Dao/Way) over the two characters "there is no seeking." The additions made by later scholars are reckless and do not reflect the original intent of Laozi’s text. How numerous are these alterations?

Sima Qian wrote that Laozi’s book contained over five thousand words, but this was merely an approximation. Some have gone so far as to arbitrarily delete auxiliary words just to fit the five thousand count—this is the misfortune caused by Sima Qian’s single remark.

Master Xue Jun (采氏) compiled *Laozi’s* *Ji* (Collection) *and Jie* (Exegesis), separately creating a section titled *Kǎo Yì* (Examination of Differences) appended to it. However, the sources he consulted were only about ten in number. I have seen many more scrolls and volumes, with numerous discrepancies. What he arranged for me is already recorded in the orthodox text, while the rest he attached to the end of the volume, still calling it *Kǎo Yì*. Let those who love antiquity verify this.

**Chapter Two:**
*This evil is already complete*—all know that goodness is goodness,
So the presence and absence of things give rise to one another.
All things are born from this, yet it does not claim credit.
It accomplishes its work yet does not dwell on it.

**Chapter Three:**
*Only by not dwelling on it*—

**Chapter Four:**
*Empty* yet never full,
Chaotic yet profound,
Mingled yet clear—
I do not know whose child it is.

**Chapter Five:**
*Not bent* yet much heard.

**Chapter Six:**
The root of Heaven and Earth—

**Chapter Seven:**
Heaven and Earth endure long.
Heaven and Earth endure long because of this—
Thus they can long endure.
Is it not because they have no selfishness?

**Chapter Eight:**
Yet they do not contend,
Dwelling where others find fault—
Thus they are guarded by the Dao/Way.
The benevolent one is thus faultless.

**Chapter Nine:**
*Hold* and grasp, yet sharpen it—
Long preserve it, filling the hall,
Proud—
Work accomplished, name secured, yet withdraw.

**Chapter Ten:**
*Embrace* without letting go,
Can one be like an infant?
Nurture and cherish it.

**Chapter Eleven:**
*Consolidate*—

---
*(Note: Some passages are fragmentary due to the nature of the original text. The bracketed annotations indicate variant readings in different manuscripts.)*

**Chapter Thirteen:**
What is this called favor and insult as if they were a shock?
Favor is the lower; insult is the higher.
This is how I have great misfortune—
When I have no body,
What misfortune could I have?
Thus, one who values his body as the world
Can entrust the world with love.

**Chapter Fourteen:**
These three things—
Therefore they blend and become one.
Above and below guard each other—
*Ah!*—without form.
This is called **hunlong** [惚怳].

**Chapter Fifteen:**
To be simple is like being a guest;
To be open is like releasing something;
To be earnest is like being plain.
Empty—like a valley;
Turbulent—like mud.
Calm—like still water;
Slow—like clarity emerging.
Steady for long—
Thus one can wear out without renewal.

**Chapter Sixteen:**
Observe its return—
Calm, it is called "returning to the Way."
*Yunyun*—
Each returns to its root.
The public is the king; the king is Heaven.
Thus one can perish without danger.

**Chapter Seventeen:**
Do not know it yet love it;
Next, it is slighted.
Trust is insufficient, so there is distrust.
*You!*—how precious is your speech!
When matters are accomplished—

**Chapter Eighteen:**
Discarded are the filial and kind,
The loyal and upright.

**Chapter Nineteen:**
These three things are used as ornament—
Yet they are not enough.

**Chapter Twenty:**
*Ah!*—how vast and boundless it is!
Like receiving a guest,
Like ascending a spring platform.
*Ah!*—how fearsome!
*Yingying!*—how murky!
*Dun dun!*—how dim,
As if without end.
Different from others—
How precious is food!

**Chapter Twenty-One:**
*Hunlong!*—
Within it, there is form.
Its essence is very true.

**Chapter Twenty-Two:**
Straight—
Is this not an empty claim?

**Chapter Twenty-Three:**
Thus the gusting wind does not last a morning.
Who causes this? Heaven and Earth—
Those who follow **virtue** align with **virtue**,
Those who lose **virtue** align with loss.
Those who align with the Dao/Way—
The Dao/Way also delights in this.
Trust is insufficient.

**Chapter Twenty-Four:**
*Qi* [跂]—
In its relation to the Dao/Way,
Things may detest it—
Yet it does not dwell.

「But how should one act?」
To lose the root—
*[Ancient texts read "lose the foundation" or "lose the subject"]*

**Chapter Thirty:**
*[Sighing]*—
In its relation to the **Dao/Way**,
Things may detest it—
Yet it does not dwell.
*[The text reads "trust" (呴), but some versions render this as "exhale" (歔) or "cease" (噤)]*
*[The text reads "weaken" (羸), but some versions render this as "abrade" (锉) or "injure" (挫)]*
*[The text reads "load" (載), but some versions render this as "cultivate" (培) or "connect" (接)]*
*[The text reads "ruin" (隳), but some versions render this as "fall" (堕)]*
Thus—
After a great army, there must be years of calamity.
*[Ancient texts omit this clause]*
It is indeed so—
Taking strength—
*[The text reads "fruit" (果) twice, but some versions specify "fruit and not take strength" (果而弗强) or add "this is called" (是谓)]*
Not the **Dao/Way**.
*[Ancient texts read "non-Dao" (非道)]*

**Chapter Thirty-One:**
*[The text reads "the instrument" (之器), but some versions omit these two characters]*
*[The text reads "serene and tranquil" (恬淡), but some versions specify "serenity and simplicity as the highest" (以恬澹为上)]*
Victory without glory—
*[The text reads "glory" (美), but some versions omit "without glory" (不美)]*
To beautify it—
This is the delight in killing people!
*[The text reads "delight" (乐), but some versions specify "those who delight in killing people" (乐人之杀人者)]*
He who delights in killing people—
Cannot achieve his aims in the world.
*[Ancient texts read "cannot achieve his aims" (不可以得志), but some versions specify "cannot achieve his intentions" (不可以得意)]*

*[The text reads "words" (言), but some versions specify "words of governance" (防礼)]*
*[The text reads "many" (众多), but some versions render this as "the multitude" (之众)]*
*[Ancient texts omit "then" (则)]*

**Chapter Thirty-Two:**
The **Dao/Way**—
Though small,
The world does not dare to submit to it.
*[Ancient texts read "no one dares to submit to it" (莫能臣也)]*
*[The text reads "when" (矦王), but some versions render this as "if" (王矦) or "the multitude" (之众)]*
All things will naturally come as guests—
*[Some versions render this as "the world" (天下)]*
All things will naturally be balanced—
*[Ancient texts omit "indeed" (焉)]*
Like rivers and valleys
*[Ancient texts read "like" (由)]*
To
*[Some versions render this as "to the" (之与)]*
Seas and oceans.
*[Ancient texts omit "indeed" (也)]*

**Chapter Thirty-Three:**
He who knows others—
Is wise.
*[Ancient texts omit "indeed" (也)]*
*[The text reads "having strength" (有力), but some versions omit "having" (无有)]*
He does not lose his place.
*[Ancient texts omit "then" (止)]*

**Chapter Thirty-Four:**
*[The text reads "flood" (泛), but some versions render this as "overflow" (汜)]*
Not dwelling—
*[Ancient texts read "not named" (不名)]*
*[The text reads "nurture" (爱养), but some versions render this as "clothe and cover" (衣被)]*
It may be named as small.
*[Some versions render this as "it may be called small" (可名为小) or "it may be named as small indeed" (可名于小矣)]*
Returning to it—
*[The text reads "return" (归焉), but some versions specify "return to it" (归之)]*
It does not become the master.
*[Some versions render this as "it does not become the ruler" (知)]*
Because it does not exalt itself—
*[Ancient texts read "because it does not make itself great" (其终), but some versions omit "its end" (终)]*

**Chapter Thirty-Five:**
Seizing the great image—
*[Ancient texts omit "indeed" (者)]*
From the mouth—
*[Ancient texts read "from the words" (言)]*
Serene and tranquil!
*[Ancient texts read "serene and tranquil indeed" (淡兮)]*

**Chapter Thirty-Six:**
*[The text reads "withdraw" (歙), but some versions render this as "prevent" (防) or "gather" (翕)]*
Softness overcomes hardness,
Weakness overcomes strength.
*[Ancient texts read "softness overcomes hardness, weakness overcomes strength indeed" (之众)]*
*[The text reads "escape" (脱), but some versions render this as "abandon" (侻)]*
*[The text reads "country" (国), but some versions omit this character]*

**Chapter Thirty-Seven:**
*[The text reads "when" (矦王), but some versions render this as "if" (王矦)]*
*[The text reads "settle" (镇以), but some versions specify "settle it" (镇之以)]*
This will also not desire—
*[Ancient texts read "this will also not desire, indeed" (夫不欲)]*
Not desiring—
*[Some versions render this as "without desire" (无欲)]*
With tranquility.

**Chapter Thirty-Eight:**
The **virtue/power** of the superior—
Acts without action,
Yet nothing is left undone.
*[Ancient texts read "acts without action, yet nothing is left undone for the inferior" (无不为下德为之而无以为)]*
This is the origin of disorder,
The beginning of folly.
*[Some versions omit "indeed" (也)]*
Dwelling in its depth—
*[Ancient texts read "dwelling in its depth, not dwelling in its shallowness" (处其厚不居其薄)]*
Dwelling in its substance—
*[Ancient texts read "dwelling in its substance, not dwelling in its ornamentation" (处其实不居其华)]*

**Chapter Thirty-Nine:**
*[The text reads "when" (矦王), but some versions render this as "if" (王矦)]*
The world is sincere—
*[Some versions render this as "the world is upright" (正)]*
This is its completion.
*[Ancient texts omit "indeed" (一)]*
All things obtain one to live.
*[Some versions omit "indeed" (以) and "all things" (万物), rendering this as "the world obtains one to live" (天下得一以生)]*
*[Some versions omit the entire clause "All things obtain one to live" (万物得一以生), fearing it disrupts the flow of the text]*
*[The text reads "when" (矦王), but some versions render this as "if" (王矦)]*
Without completion—
*[Some versions render this as "without being called completion" (无以贵高)]*
This is to be feared and treacherous.
*[Some versions render this as "this is to be feared and treacherous indeed" (将恐蹶)]*
Self-praise—
*[The text reads "self-praise" (自称), but some versions specify "self-claim" (自谓)]*
Is this not so?
*[The text reads "is this not so?" (非欤), but some versions omit the question mark]*
*[The text reads "many" (数舆), but some versions render this as "many praises" (数誉) or "many carriages" (数车)]*
*[The text reads "without praise" (无舆), but some versions render this as "without carriages" (无车)]*
*[The text reads "like" (琭), but some versions render this as "record" (录)]*
Like jade—
*[Ancient texts read "like" (若)]*
Pure and clear!

**Chapter Forty-Five**
*Succumbing* and *bending*—
*[Ancient texts read "emptying" (冲) and "bending" (屈)]*
Pure and tranquil—
*[Ancient texts read "pure and tranquil" (清静) as the Dao/Way’s rectitude for all under heaven]*
*[Ancient texts read "as the Dao/Way’s rectitude above" (为天下正) or "as the Dao/Way’s rectitude with foundation" (有以)]*

**Chapter Forty-Six**
*[Turning back] warhorses to spread dung*—
*[Ancient texts read "to scatter" (播) or "to spread with carts" (以粪车)]*
No guilt is greater than *what one craves*—
*[Wang Bi’s version omits this line]*
No blame is greater than *desiring to possess*—
*[Han Fei’s ancient text reads "No blame is greater than *what one desires to possess*" (咎莫大于欲得)]*
Thus, *the sufficiency of contentment* is ever sufficient.

**Chapter Forty-Seven**
Without stepping outside, one knows all under heaven;
Without peering through windows, one sees the Dao/Way.
*[Ancient texts read "With stepping outside, one can know all under heaven; with peering through windows, one can see the Dao/Way" (出户窥牖下俱有可以)]*
The farther one goes,
The less one truly knows.
*[Han Fei reads "The farther one goes, the more one knows" (远下有者)]*
*[One version reads "without action, one arrives" (不行而至)]*

**Chapter Forty-Eight**
To study the Dao/Way—
*[Ancient texts add "the two are" (二者)]*
Yet *to diminish it*—
*[One version omits "it"]*
Without action, yet nothing remains undone.
*[Ancient texts read "Without action, there is nothing that is not done" (无为则无不为)]*
Thus, *those who take all under heaven*
*[Wang Bi omits "thus"]*
Are not sufficient to take all under heaven.
*[Ancient texts read "are yet not sufficient to take all under heaven" (又不足以取天下矣)]*

**Chapter Forty-Nine**
Virtue is accomplished—
*[One version reads "obtained" (得善); another omits "accomplished" (矣)]*
*[The text reads "惵惵" (惵惵), possibly "reverent and solemn" or "gathered and still"]*
*[Ancient texts read "歙歙" (歙歙), possibly "converged and tranquil," with "thus" (焉) appended]*
*[The text reads "孩之" (孩之), possibly "infant-like" or "innocent"]*
*[Lu and Wang Bi read "咳" (咳), possibly "cough" or a variant of "孩"]*

**Chapter Fifty**
*[The text reads "人之生动之死地" (人之生动之死地), possibly "When life stirs, it moves toward death’s domain"]*
*[Han Fei’s ancient text reads "民之生生而动动皆之死地" (民之生生而动动皆之死地), "The people’s very being and movement are all toward death’s domain"]*
The vitality of vitality—
*[Ancient texts append "thus" (也)]*
Why is this?
*[Ancient texts append "thus" (也)]*
There is no death’s domain.
*[Ancient texts append "thus" (焉)]*

**Chapter Fifty-One**
*[The text reads "夫莫之爵" (夫莫之爵), possibly "Yet none bestow titles upon it"]*
*[One version omits "Yet" (夫); another reads "莫之命" (莫之命), "none command it"]*
*Nurturing it,*
*[Ancient texts read "virtue nurtures it" (德畜之)]*
*[The text reads "亭之毒之" (亭之毒之), possibly "sheltering it, refining it"]*
*[One version reads "成之熟之" (成之熟之), "completing it, maturing it"]*
*[Ancient texts read "养之" (养之), "nourishing it"]*
*[Ancient texts read "盖之" (盖之), "covering it"]*

**Chapter Fifty-Two**
With a beginning—
*[Ancient texts append "that may be" (可)]*
Know its mother,
*[One version reads "obtain" (得)]*
And cling to constancy.
*[Ye reads "袭裳" (袭裳), possibly "inherit the robe" or "adhere to the form"]*

**Chapter Fifty-Three**
Yet the people love shortcuts—
*[Bi Xu, Sima, and Su Lin read "the people greatly love shortcuts" (民甚好径)]*
Wealth and goods overflow,
*[Ancient texts read "wealth and resources" (货财) or "resources and wealth" (资财)]*
Thieves proliferate like *reed-pipes*—
*[Ancient texts read "thieves and flatterers" (盗夸)]*
This is not the Dao/Way!
*[One version appends "thus" (也); Zhao Zhi Jian reads "thieves and flatterers are not the Dao/Way" (盗夸非道)]*

**Chapter Fifty-Four**
*[Han Fei omits "the good builder"]*
Their descendants’ sacrifices never cease—
*[Peng Zhi’s text appends "with" (以) before "世世"; Han Fei reads "their descendants, generation after generation, make sacrifices without interruption" (子孙以其世世祭祀不绝)]*
*[The text reads "修之于身" (修之于身), possibly "cultivate it in oneself"]*
Thus, it is true;
*[Zhao Zhi Jian reads "能" (能), "thus, it is able"]*
Thus, it is abundant.
*[Zhao reads "能有余" (能有余), "thus, it is abundant"]*
*[The text reads "修之于国" (修之于国), possibly "cultivate it in the realm"]*
How then?
*[Ancient texts read "What then?" (奚以)]*

**Chapter Fifty-Five**
*[The text reads "含德之厚比于赤子" (含德之厚比于赤子), possibly "The depth of virtue held is like that of an infant"]*
*[Ancient texts read "The depth of virtue held is compared to that of an infant" (含德之厚者比之于赤之也)]*
*[The text reads "毒虫" (毒虫), possibly "venomous insects"]*
*[Ancient texts read "蜂虿" (蜂虿), "wasps and scorpions"]*
*[The text reads "防" (防), possibly "ward off"]*
*[Ancient texts read "朘" (朘, *fǎn*), "repel"; Lu reads "全" (全), "protect"]*
This is *the utmost*—
*[Bi Xu, Su, and Cao omit "thus" (也)]*
Yet it is not *choked*—
*[One version reads "嗌不嗄" (嗌不嗄); Huang Mao Cai notes ancient texts omit "嗌" (嗌), later added from Zhuangzi’s style; "嗄" (嗄) means "gasping from blocked breath"]*
*[The text reads "曰强" (曰强), possibly "it is called force"]*
*[Ancient texts read "则强" (则强), "thus, it is force"]*
This is called *not the Dao/Way*.
*[Stele text reads "非道" (非道); ancient texts read "谓之不道" (谓之不道), "it is called not the Dao/Way"]*

**Chapter Fifty-Six**
The wise do not speak.
*[Ancient texts append "thus" (也)]*
They cannot be *warded off*,
Cannot be *harmed*,
Cannot be *debased*.
*[Ancient texts append "also" (亦) before each clause]*

**Chapter Fifty-Seven**
With rectitude—
*[Ancient texts read "governance" (政)]*
No affairs arise.
*[Da Zhen’s text reads "no turmoil" (无事作为)]*
How do I know all under heaven is thus?
*[One version reads "How do I know it is thus?" (吾何以知其然哉)]*
The people multiply in *skills and artifice*;
Strange things proliferate.
*[Ancient texts read "The people multiply in cunning and perverse deeds" (民多智慧邪事滋起)]*
They become ever more *flamboyant*.
*[Ancient texts read "滋章" (滋章), "ever more ostentatious"]*
I hold no desires, yet the people return to simplicity.
*[He Shang’s text appends "I hold no emotions, yet the people return to clarity" (我无情而民自清)]*

**Chapter Fifty-Eight**
*[The text reads "闷闷" (闷闷), possibly "obscured and obscured"]*
*[Ancient texts read "闵闵" (闵闵), "solemn and solemn"]*
*[The text reads "醇醇" (醇醇), possibly "pure and pure"]*
*[Ancient texts read "偆偆" (偆偆), "refined and refined"]*
Calamity! Fortune depends upon it.
Fortune! Calamity lurks within it.
*[Han Fei and ancient texts read "Calamity! Fortune depends upon it. Fortune! Calamity lurks within it" (祸兮福之所倚福兮祸之所伏)]*
The people’s confusion—
*[Han Fei reads "The people’s confusion—its duration is indeed long" (民之迷其日固久)]*
Its days are indeed long.
*[Han Fei reads "its origin is indeed long" (其故以久); one version reads "its duration is indeed long" (其日固久)]*
*[The text reads "刿" (刿), possibly "pierce" or a proper name]*
*[Zuan Wei reads "秽" (秽), "filth"]*

**Chapter Fifty-Nine**
Nothing is better than *frugality*—
*[One version reads "like" (如)]*
This is called *early service*.
*[Han Fei reads "with service" (以服); one version reads "again" (复)]*
*[The text reads "谓之重积德" (谓之重积德), "it is called *layered accumulation of virtue*"]*
*[Han Fei reads "is called *layered accumulation of virtue*" (是谓重积德)]*
Thus, none know its limit.
*[Huang omits "Thus, none know its limit" (莫知其极)]*
Deep roots, firm foundation.
*[Han Fei reads "deepen its roots, firm its foundation" (深其根固其柢); one version reads "蒂" (蒂) for "foundation"]*

**Chapter Sixty**
Govern the great realm—
*[Han Fei reads "Those who govern the great realm"]*
*[The text reads "烹" (烹), possibly "boil" or "cook"]*
Its spirit is not harmed.
*[Han Fei appends "thus" (也)]*
The sage does not harm it.
*[Han Fei reads "does not harm the people" (不伤民)]*

**Chapter Sixty-One**
All under heaven’s *crossroads*—
*[One version reads "all under heaven’s *female*" (天下之牝)]*
With stillness as *the lowly*—
*[Ancient texts read "because of its stillness, it is *the lowly*" (以其静故为之下也); Sima reads "with its stillness as *the lowly*" (以其静为之下)]*
Thus, the great *becomes the lowly*.
*[One version omits "thus"]*
*[One version reads "for *the lowly*" (为之下)]*

**Chapter Sixty-Two**
*[The text reads "奥" (奥), possibly "mysterious"]*
*[One version appends "thus" (也)]*
The good person’s treasure—
*[Sima and Cheng append "where" (所)]*
*[The text reads "加人" (加人), possibly "bestowed upon people"]*
*[Peng’s text reads "加于人" (加于人), "bestowed upon people"]*
Sitting, advancing this Dao/Way.
*[Ancient texts append "thus" (也)]*
Why is this Dao/Way thus honored?
*[One version reads "Why is this Dao/Way thus honored?" (何也)]*
Is it not because *of crimes that are pardoned*?
*[One version appends "that may be" (可)]*

**Chapter Sixty-Three**
*[The text reads "difficult" (难), possibly "difficult at its ease" (图难于其易)]*
*[Ancient texts append "at" (乎) after "difficult"]*
Things that the world finds difficult
*[One version appends "are difficult" (之下)]*
are made easy through light promises and frequent failure.
*[Ancient texts append "who" (者) after each]*
Yet they remain difficult.
*[Sima reads "by" (由) instead of "yet" (犹)]*
Thus, in the end, there are no true difficulties.

**Chapter Sixty-Four**
*[The text reads "easy" (易), possibly "easy to break" (易判)]*
Act before things exist.
*[Ancient texts read "act upon it before it exists" (为之乎其未有)]*
*[One version reads "hair" (毫) instead of "great" (豪)]*
Nine layers complete—
*[One version reads "nine tiers" (九层)]*
a thousand-*li* journey.
*[One version reads "a hundred-*ren* height" (百仞之高)]*
The Sage acts without action,
*[One version appends "therefore" (是以)]*
constantly guarding the unformed.
*[One version appends "its" (其)]*
Thus, there are no failed undertakings.
*[One version appends "thus" (也)]*
Returning to the common people—
*[Ancient texts append "with" (以) before "returning"]*
relying,
*[The original reads "support" (辅), corrected to "rely" (恃)]*
they dare not act.

**Chapter Sixty-Five**
Because of their many clever schemes,
*[Ancient texts read "because of their many cleverness" (以其多智也)]*
they are the ruin of the state and its blessing.
*[Ancient texts append "also" (也) after each]*
To know these two is to grasp the standard.
*[Ancient texts read "to always know these two is to examine the standard" (常知此两者亦稽式)]*
Only then—
*[Ancient texts append "therefore" (然后)]*

**Chapter Sixty-Six**
Because of their virtue, they are placed below.
*[Ancient texts append "thus" (也)]*
Because of their words, they are placed below.
*[Ancient texts read "must place them below by their words" (必以其言下之)]*
The people are not harmed.
*[Ancient texts append "thus" (也)]*

**Chapter Sixty-Seven**
Only the great seem unworthy.
*[The Qingyuan text omits this sentence]*
As for the trifling—
*[One version omits "as for" (夫)]*
treasure it and hold it fast.
*[One version reads "hold it fast and treasure it" (持而宝之)]*
*[One version reads "preserve" (保) instead of "treasure" (宝)]*
Because of their tenderness, they can be courageous.
*[Ancient texts append "thus" (夫)]*
*[One version reads "long" (长) instead of "long" (器)]*
*[Han Fei reads "matters" (事) instead of "vessels" (器)]*
Abandoning their tenderness and courage,
they die.
*[Ancient texts read "this is called entering the gate of death" (是谓入死门)]*
In war, they win.
*[One version reads "in formation, they are correct" (以陈则正)]*
With tenderness, they defend.
*[One version appends "necessarily" (必) before "with tenderness"]*

**Chapter Sixty-Eight**
Those skilled in being a warrior do not rely on force.
*[Ancient texts read "The ancients skilled in being warriors did not rely on force" (古之善为士者不武也)]*
Those skilled in defeating enemies do not contend.
*[Lin Xi reads "Those skilled in defeating war do not contend" (善胜战者不与)]*
*[One version omits "enemies" (敌)]*
This was the ancient extreme.

**Chapter Sixty-Nine**
In wielding arms, there is a saying:
*[Ancient texts append "thus" (曰)]*
"Hold no weapons and have no enemies.
*[Wang Bi reads "cast away" (扔) instead of "hold" (执)]*
Lightly regard enemies—
*[Ancient texts read "without enemies" (无敌)]*
guard against losing your treasure.
*[Ancient texts read "without enemies, then guard against losing your treasure" (无敌则防亡吾宝)]*
When armies clash, the compassionate will prevail.
*[Ancient texts read "When armies face each other, the compassionate will prevail" (抗兵相若则哀者胜矣)]*

**Chapter Seventy**
The world cannot know, cannot act.
*[Ancient texts read "yet people cannot know it, cannot act upon it" (而人莫之能知莫之能行)]*
They do not know me—
*[One version omits "thus" (也)]*
thus, I am honored.
*[One version reads "thus, I am the honored one" (则我者贵)]*
Wearing coarse cloth,
*[One version reads "wearing coarse hemp" (披褐)]*
*[One version appends "and" (而) after "cloth" (褐)]*

**Chapter Seventy-One**
Above, there is sickness.
*[Ancient texts read "Above, sickness!" (尚矣)]*
The Sage’s lack of sickness is because they treat sickness as sickness—
*[Han Fei reads "The Sage’s lack of sickness is because they do not have sickness—thus, no sickness" (圣人之不病也以其不病是以无病也)]*
thus, they are not sick.

**Chapter Seventy-Two**
Great awe has arrived.
*[One version reads "then great awe has arrived" (则大威至)]*
Do not be intimate.
*[One version reads "do not be familiar" (无狎)]*
Only the unwearied—
*[Wu Fangqing reads "only the unfamiliar" (夫唯不狎)]*

**Chapter Seventy-Three**
These two—
*[One version appends "know" (知) before "these"]*
*[One version is incorrect (非)]*
are subtle.
*[One version reads "open" (坦), another "silent" (默)]*

**Chapter Seventy-Four**
The people do not fear death.
*[One version appends "constantly" (常) after "people"]*
If I can seize and kill them, who dares—
*[Ancient texts append "thus" (也) after "dares"]*
*[One version reads "could" (岂) instead of "if I can" (吾得)]*
—to take my place as executioner?
*[One version reads "husband" (夫) instead of "to" (而)]*
This is like substituting for the master carpenter to hew—
*[One version appends "it is said" (谓) after "this"]*
few will avoid injuring their hands.
*[One version reads "few have" (希有) instead of "few" (希)]*
*[One version omits "their" (其)]*

**Chapter Seventy-Five**
The people’s hunger—
*[Ancient texts append "thus" (者) after "hunger"]*
it is because taxes are too heavy.
*[One version omits "thus" (也) after "heavy"]*
Because of their excessive pursuit of life,
*[One version reads "because of their excessive seeking of life" (以其上求生之厚)]*
only those who do not value life—
*[One version appends "thus" (夫) before "only"]*
*[One version appends "noble" (贵) after "life" (生)]*
—value life.
*[The Zhenghe text appends "thus" (也) after "life"]*

**Chapter Seventy-Six**
Grass and trees,
*[One version prefaces with "all things" (万物)]*
the tender and fragile,
*[One version reads "weak" (弱) instead of "fragile" (脆)]*
*[Ancient texts append "thus" (也) after "fragile"]*
strong arms,
*[Ancient texts append "thus" (者) after "arms"]*
break the strong and place them below.
*[Ancient texts read "thus, the firm and strong are placed below" (故坚强处下)]*

**Chapter Seventy-Seven**
Is it like drawing a bow?
*[Ancient texts read "Is it like one who draws a bow?" (其犹张弓者欤)]*
*[Shao reads "by" (由) instead of "like" (犹)]*
Supplement it—
*[One version reads "give it to" (与之)]*
who can have excess to nourish the world?
Only those of the Dao.
*[Ancient texts read "who can reduce excess to nourish the insufficient in the world? Only those of the Dao, is it not so?" (孰能损有余而奉不足于天下者其唯有道者乎)]*
When the work is done, they do not dwell upon it.
*[One version reads "when the work is done, they do not reside" (功成不处)]*
Seeing the virtuous—
*[One version omits "thus" (耶)]*

**Chapter Seventy-Eight**
Nothing in the world is as soft and weak as water,
*[One version reads "nothing is as soft and weak as water" (天下柔弱莫过于水)]*
yet it can overcome them all—
*[One version reads "prevails" (胜) instead of "overcome" (先)]*
because there is nothing to replace it.
*[One version omits "thus" (以)]*
The soft overcomes the hard,
the weak overcomes the strong.
*[One version appends "thus" (故) before "no one"]*
No one does not know this,
yet no one can act upon it.
*[Ancient texts read "yet no one can act upon it" (而莫之能行)]*
Thus, the Sage says:
*[One version reads "therefore" (是以)]*
*[Ancient texts append "thus" (之言)]*
This is called "inverted action."
*[Ancient texts append "thus" (也) after "inverted"]*

**Chapter Eighty**
Let the people possess tenfold or hundredfold implements,
yet not use them.
Let the people cherish death so deeply
that they do not flee afar.
Let them find their food delightful,
their customs secure,
their occupations joyous.
Let their music resound,
and though they reach the utmost,
they do not visit one another.

**Chapter Eighty-One**
The virtuous do not argue;
those who argue are not virtuous.
Do not accumulate—
the Dao of Heaven.
